# Crosstalk

# Chapter 1

Jet could have put the Bebop into the lot behind Mama Yun's half asleep. Some days he had. The angle lived in his hands now: clear the crooked water tower, slip past the laundromat vent that coughed steam over the alley, nose down between the noodle shop's back wall and the junk dealer's fence, then settle with a soft touch before the port stabilizer kissed the old yellow line somebody had painted there years ago and never repainted. It was one of the few things in his life that still came back the same every time.

The bridge smelled like old oil, cold metal, dog, and the scorched peppers he'd welded into last night's pan. Outside the front glass Mars was going dull with evening, the sky that bad amber-pink it always wore like a bruise. A red fuel light kept winking from the panel at him. It wasn't urgent yet. It still sat there like bad news.

"I'm telling you," Faye said from behind him, "if Captain Sleepy over there hadn't decided he was the only one allowed to get close, we'd have had the bastard."

Spike had one boot braced against the console and looked exactly like a man who had nothing to do with anything. "You fired too early."

"I fired because you drifted."

"I didn't drift."

"You drifted right past him."

Jet kept his eyes on the alley mouth threading up beneath them. "Could both of you shut it for ten seconds."

"I'm not the one talking," Spike said.

Faye made a sound in the back of her throat. "That's because you think looking bored counts as a defense."

Ed was upside down in the doorway to the bridge, knees hooked over the frame, goggles hanging off one ear. "Deimos was lumpy," Ed announced. "Very bad potato moon for straight line zooming."

"Thank you, Ed," Jet said. "At least somebody on this ship can identify terrain."

"Jet also identified terrain," Ed said. "Jet identified it loudly."

Ein sneezed from under the co-pilot chair. Red dust puffed off his muzzle and drifted through the strip of light at his feet.

The alley behind Mama Yun's slid into view.

Something was in his spot.

Jet's hand tightened on the yoke before he'd even finished seeing it. The lot was small enough that anything parked there changed the whole shape of it, and this ship changed more than shape. It sat low and clean where the Bebop should have been, a dark wedge with the engines cold and the hull reflecting the noodle shop's back sign in a red broken stripe. No cargo pods. No rust blooms. No tarp over the port intake. Nothing local about it. It looked like it had landed by mistake in the middle of a neighborhood argument.

"What the hell is that?" Jet said.

Spike leaned forward a little, which for Spike was practically a sprint. "Not ours."

"Oh, good," Faye said. "I thought you'd finally bought something with actual lines."

Jet brought the Bebop around once, low and slow, trying to find a pilot in the windows or a service light on the hull or any sign that somebody was about to wave apologetically and move. Nothing. The ship was dark. The alley was dark. The old refrigeration unit behind the shop thumped and rattled in the same rhythm it always did, like it took personal offense at being kept alive.

He hit the local hailing frequency. "Docked vessel behind Yun Noodle. Identify and respond."

Static.

He did it again, slower, because half the idiots on Mars thought they could ignore authority if it sounded tired. "Docked vessel behind Yun Noodle, you're occupying a private arrangement. Respond."

Nothing.

Faye craned around the back of his chair. "Private arrangement. Listen to you."

"It's private."

"It's a dirt lot behind a noodle shop."

"It's my dirt lot behind a noodle shop."

Spike looked out at the dark ship with his usual low-watt calm. "Maybe they're dead."

Jet shot him a look. "Don't start."

"I'm not starting. If they're dead, we can move it."

"Great," Faye said. "Let's add corpse disposal to the evening. That'll help the budget."

Ed dropped from the doorway, landed barefoot, and padded to the console like gravity had been an opinion all along. "Sleepy ship says nothing. Sleepy ship is all zipped up."

Jet circled a second time. The fuel light blinked again, meaner now. He knew exactly how much each pointless minute cost because he'd bought the last tank himself in person, cash over a counter, with the kind of smile a clerk used when the amount on the screen was small enough to pity. He took the Bebop up and away before anger could make him stupid.

"Paid dock," he said.

Faye's groan filled the bridge. "No. No, come on."

"You want to hover all night?"

"I haven't paid shit for that spot and I still want it back."

"It belongs to me by labor," Jet snapped. "I fixed that refrigeration unit three times this month."

Spike settled back. "Four."

Jet glanced at him. "What?"

"You fixed it four times."

"That's not helping."

"I know."

The paid lot was exactly two blocks east and felt farther because everybody in Tharsis knew it existed only for people whose luck had already run out. A rusted light pole leaned over the entrance. The payment kiosk was bolted to a concrete island and wore three layers of old transit stickers under a skin of dust. Someone had scratched NO CREDIT NO EXCUSES into the metal under the screen.

Jet put the Bebop down with more force than the landing gear deserved. The hull shuddered. Somewhere aft, something in the galley clanged in protest.

No one moved at first.

Then Faye said, "Maybe it'll be cheaper if we look pathetic in person."

"We look pathetic for free," Jet said, unbuckling. "It hasn't helped yet."

The kiosk wanted cash.

Of course it wanted cash.

Jet fed it the folded notes from the envelope he kept clipped under the bridge panel. The screen ate each one with a cheerful chirp that made him want to put his cybernetic fist through the glass. The total on the corner kept dropping, not enough. Still not enough. He checked his pockets, already knowing. Two coins. One washer. A screw.

"That's not a coin," Faye said.

"I'm aware."

She stood there hugging herself against the cold, mouth pinched, then dug into the pocket of her yellow shorts and slapped three small woolong coins into his palm. "I was going to use those for cigarettes."

"You don't smoke."

"I was going to start."

Spike came down the ramp slow as weather and set one coin beside the others. "Found that in the couch."

Jet looked at it. "You've known there was money in the couch."

"I knew there was something in the couch."

Ed patted down a dozen pockets as if a full salary might have migrated into the coat nobody remembered Ed wearing. A transit token, two bent paper clips, a dead battery, and half a candy wrapper appeared on the kiosk shelf.

"Close," Ed said.

Ein put one paw on Jet's boot and looked up with an expression that said he regretted having no currency.

Jet fed the coins in. The machine accepted all of them, thought about its own cruelty for a moment, and printed a receipt nobody needed.

"There," Faye said. "Excellent. We're officially parked with all the dignity money can buy."

"Move," Jet said, and pulled the receipt free before the wind could take it.

They walked back through the thinning evening with their collars up. Dust skated along the curb and collected in the corners of cheap neon signs. Ship traffic muttered overhead. The cold on Mars always felt dry enough to sand skin off the knuckles, and Jet could already feel it splitting the cut beside his thumb open again.

Mama Yun's front window was fogged with broth steam. Somebody had taped a handwritten special to the glass and crossed out the word beef without bothering to replace it with anything. Inside, fluorescent tubes buzzed overhead. The place was narrow, hot, and smelled exactly right: stock, peppers, old fryer oil, dish soap, damp coats. Jet felt his temper sharpen when he saw the back door from the counter. Two steps through there and the lot should have been his.

Mama Yun was behind the register laying strips of orange peel on a square of wax paper. She dried them out and threw them into tea or broth or whatever else needed more life than the market could afford that week. She looked up, saw Jet's face, and did not even bother asking what had gone wrong.

"Don't start hollering," she said. "I just got the baby next door to sleep."

"There's a ship in my spot."

"There sure is."

"You noticed."

"Jet, the lot's behind my shop. I notice the lot."

Faye slid onto a counter stool as if she had been walking for three days. "Please tell me you're about to say you don't know who owns it and we can all do crime."

Mama Yun snorted and turned her orange peels over with a finger. Her nails were cut square and stained dark from soy and tea. "No crime in my shop."

"Outside your shop, then," Faye said.

"Still no."

Jet planted both hands on the counter. The sticky laminate tugged at his palm. "How long has it been there?"

"Three days."

He stared at her. "Three days."

"Maybe a little more. Came in before dawn. Woke up my whole back wall."

"And you just let it sit there."

She finally looked at him over the top of her glasses. "A pilot paid for the lot."

"Paid who."

"Me."

"For how long."

"Six months."

For a second the shop noise thinned out around the words. The hiss from the pot, the clatter of bowls, Faye breathing through her teeth, even Spike shifting his weight by the door. Six months. That killed any hope this was a misunderstanding or some idiot stopping to piss and buy noodles. Six months meant planning. Six months meant someone had looked at that stupid patch of cracked dirt behind a noodle shop and decided it was worth money.

"Six months," Jet said again, because repetition was the only thing standing between him and taking this personally in public.

"Cash," Mama Yun said. "Up front. Exact. Clean bills. Kept the helmet on, too, before you ask, so no, I don't know the face."

"You took money from somebody wearing a helmet inside."

"I take money from anybody whose money spends."

"That was my arrangement."

"Your arrangement's with my refrigerator. The refrigerator's working."

Jet opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again. "Mama."

"Don't Mama me. If your spot means that much, next time pay six months."

"With what?"

That got a shrug out of her. Not cruel. Just Mars. "Catch your bounty."

Spike pushed off the doorframe. "That part didn't happen."

"I had noticed," Mama Yun said.

Faye barked a laugh. Jet wanted to be mad at her too and did not have the spare energy.

"Did the pilot say anything else?" he asked.

"Asked if the lot stayed quiet. Asked if anybody messed with ships behind my place. Asked if there was a mechanic nearby for refrigeration. Asked that part twice, like it mattered." She pointed a soy-stained finger at the stools. "Sit down. I've got noodles. You can be angry and eat at the same time. Lots of people do."

Jet looked at the back door again, as if the dark ship might take the hint and remove itself. Nothing moved but steam and the hanging fly strips.

Faye was already seated. "I'm not leaving without food. If we're this poor, I want to be poor sitting down."

Spike shrugged once and took the stool beside her.

Ed had found the end stool and was staring at the back window, head tilted, as if the ship might start talking if everybody else got quiet enough.

Jet stayed standing another beat because standing made it feel like he still had options. Then he sat, because one of those options now involved getting food into Ed and Ed to a terminal.

Mama Yun slapped bowls down in front of them with the efficient contempt of a woman who had kept hungry idiots alive for too long to be impressed by any of them.

There was, of course, no beef.

* * *

The broth was hot enough to burn a line down Jet's throat and thin enough that he could see the bottom of the bowl through it when the noodles shifted. Pepper strips floated on top like they were apologizing for something. Mama Yun had given them extra scallion, which was how she handled pity when she could not stand to show it plain.

Faye poked at the bowl with her chopsticks. "This is decorative."

"It's dinner," Jet said.

"Dinner's supposed to have something dead in it."

"You want something dead, go marry a butcher."

"With what dowry? We just parked it."

Spike ate like a man who had no quarrel with scarcity as long as it arrived in liquid form. That always got under Jet's skin. He was the one who had to count the noodles and hear the pan come up light, and Spike still managed to make hunger look like weather. "Could be worse."

Faye turned on him at once. "Don't do that."

"Do what."

"That thing where you act like deprivation is a hobby."

"I'm eating noodles."

"You're performing noodles."

Jet rubbed a hand over his face. The broth had put heat in his stomach and nowhere else. The rest of him still felt like cold metal and exact change. "Can we get through one bowl before the postmortem."

"There's no postmortem," Faye said. "There's thirty thousand woolongs sitting on Deimos because somebody had to fly it solo."

Spike didn't look up. "Your line was already broken."

"My line was fine till you flew through the middle of it."

"It had a gap."

"It was a herding pattern, not a goddamn gap. And the bounty went right out behind you."

Jet pointed his chopsticks at both of them. "That thirty thousand was going to be fuel and two months of the spot we just lost. I want you to think about that while you're eating soup you can see through."

Ed slurped noodles, thoughtful. "Bounty man was wiggly."

Jet almost laughed at that. Ed could sand an argument flat in three words if the mood struck.

"Wiggly doesn't pay rent," Faye said.

"Nothing pays rent," Spike said.

Jet let the chopsticks drop to the rim of his bowl. "Fine. Deimos was a mess. Now tell me why a stranger pays six months for quiet and asks after refrigeration."

Mama Yun came down the counter with the stock pot in both hands. "Because the stranger wanted refrigeration."

"That's almost insultingly helpful," Faye said.

"I heard the cold loop kick twice after sunrise," Mama Yun said. "Not cabin air. Lower down."

Spike looked up at that. "Cargo hold."

Ed let a noodle strand hang from the chopsticks and cocked Ed's head toward the back window. One goggle lens hung down over one eye. "Freezer fish. Public papers say pleasure boat. Buried service papers say belly coils got swapped for freight-cold ones, little ones. Not whole-hauler cold. Locker cold."

Jet felt his appetite go flat. Quiet, pre-paid, locker-cold. The shape of the evening was getting worse faster than he could name it.

"Or a body," Faye said. "Let's keep our options broad."

"Medicine," Jet said. "Samples maybe. Flowers if somebody's got money to waste."

"Flowers don't land before dawn and keep the helmet on," Spike said.

"Neither do corpses if they're polite," Faye said.

Mama Yun tapped the back of Faye's stool with the pot handle. "Don't talk about thawing bodies over broth."

"Then stop renting my lot to possible corpse people," Jet snapped. The words came out hotter than he meant. He scrubbed a thumb over the bowl's cracked glaze and looked at Ed. "You hearing this."

Jet blinked. A tight little drop opened under his ribs. He had meant to feed Ed first, let the whole thing stay stupid till morning. "You got that already."

"Shop has net. Net has crumbs."

Faye leaned back. "So it's lying about what it is."

Spike said, "Or about what it's carrying."

"Same thing if you're poor enough," Faye said.

That got closer to the bone than Jet liked. He looked back toward the steamed window. The ship changed shape again in his head. It wasn't some smooth rich bastard parking for the view anymore. It was a quiet hull with a freight-cold belly, sitting in his place and pretending at leisure. "Nobody opens it tonight."

Faye looked at him. "Since when."

"Since I don't know if it's holding meds, meat, or somebody's dead aunt. We crack a line because you're curious, we buy the problem."

"You say the sweetest things."

"Ed," Jet said, "when we get back, pull everything on that ship that isn't painted on the outside."

"Ed peels fish stickers."

Mama Yun topped off Ed's broth first. Ed had somehow already finished half the bowl and was feeding noodles under the counter to Ein one strand at a time when Mama Yun wasn't looking and in fact when she was. She ignored it with the resignation of somebody who had once decided dogs did not belong in the shop and then run out of energy for principles.

The sight of Ed sneaking noodles to the dog gave Jet one thin second of relief. The universe could still waste his money and leave small stupid things in place.

"Anybody get off it," Jet asked.

"No."

"Anybody go near it."

"No."

Spike said, "So whatever's in the hold, it can wait."

Jet wanted to buy that and couldn't. The cold-loop detail kept catching on his thoughts like a fishbone.

"That doesn't improve the corpse theory," Faye said.

"There's no corpse theory," Jet said.

"There sure is now."

"There isn't if you want dessert," Mama Yun said.

There it was. The true shape of it, ugly and stupid and impossible to make dignified. The paid lot was not ruinous by itself. Nothing ever was. It was one more leak in a ship that lived by leaks: fuel, ammo, noodles, filters, parts, docking, fines, coffee, laundry, cards, cigarettes Faye claimed not to smoke, dog food, whatever Ed had once ordered off a forgotten network account and insisted was educational. They never went broke in one clean gesture. They went broke by abrasion.

Jet took another mouthful of broth and tasted salt where the skin on his thumb had split. The spot behind the shop had never been just convenience. It was no meter and no questions and a walk of six seconds from the back ramp to hot food. It was Mama Yun yelling if somebody touched the Bebop. It was a place to come back to that had not been sold out from under him by some office or auction or bureaucrat with a stamp. He had built that spot the only way people like him got to build anything permanent, by fixing a bad compressor again and again until a woman with a noodle shop started saying your ship instead of that ship.

Spike must have seen some version of the thought on his face, because he said, quieter, "Maybe they leave tomorrow."

"Ships with freight-cold guts don't prepay six months to leave tomorrow," Jet said.

"People pay for bad plans all the time," Faye said. "You've met gamblers."

"You've been gamblers," Jet said.

"Yeah, and look how happy it made me."

Mama Yun set a small dish of pickled greens between them. "Don't pretend none of you chooses this life every day."

Faye snorted. "Some of us aren't choosing. Some of us are being extorted by history."

"You still want the greens or not."

Faye took some. "I didn't say no."

Spike reached for the same dish at the same time and got his hand slapped away with Faye's chopsticks.

"Get your own."

"It's in the middle."

"I called it. Dibs."

"You've called dibs on everything tonight."

"Shut up."

Jet should have cut it off. He knew that. Stress made his voice louder and his sentences longer and his patience thinner than paper, which was one reason the crew got away with so much until they did not. But there was relief in hearing them reduce the thing in his spot to flowers, corpses, and pickled greens. If they could still joke it down to size, then maybe it was still small enough to live with.

Ed, though, had stopped paying attention to the bowl.

Jet noticed because Ed was almost never pointed in only one direction. Usually there was motion leaking out somewhere, a foot kicking, fingers drumming, eyes slipping off after any new sound like they'd been personally invited. Now Ed sat hunched on the stool with noodles cooling untouched and stared through the steamed back window toward the lot.

"What," Jet said.

Ed didn't look away. "Ship is counting feet."

"That's creepy even for a ship," Faye said.

Ed tapped two fingers on the counter in a thin patient rhythm. "Ground feet. Landing struts keep checking load, checking load, checking load. Little dock-watch running on its own sleepy battery. Anybody nudges the hatch, sleepy ship tattles somewhere else."

Spike glanced over. "Proximity alarm."

"Quiet one," Ed said. "Quiet till it isn't."

Faye tipped her head back. "So crime got paperwork and a snitch."

Jet looked toward the back door again. The ship changed shape yet again in his head. Not just a liar. Not just a cold-belly freighter in a leisure coat. Something set to feel the ground under it and complain if the ground changed. "Good. Then nobody touches it tonight. Not a finger."

"Romance isn't dead," Faye said.

"Can you get a name?" Jet asked.

"Ed can get all the names. Factory name, registry name, maintenance name, secret little internal network name if the owner was lonely." Ed slurped broth, then brightened. "Ed will look."

"After you eat," Jet said.

Ed stuffed the rest of the noodles into Ed's mouth in one improbable, legal gesture and kicked both feet in the air once to show compliance.

Faye stared. "That's disgusting."

Ed swallowed. "Efficient."

"No," Faye said. "Disgusting first."

The talk loosened after that, but it did not go back to Deimos. Faye wanted to know whether a ship that clean with a freight-cold hold and a tattletale alarm was worth robbing. Spike said if she had to ask, she couldn't afford it. Jet said if either of them touched the hull before Ed finished digging, he would break their hands. Mama Yun told them all to either order tea or get out because the counter had to turn. Nobody ordered tea. They stayed until the bowls were empty and the heat from the broth ran out of them and Mars reminded them it was waiting outside.

On the way back, Faye walked with her hands jammed under her arms and muttered a running list of the things she hated: cold weather, rich strangers, exact change, men who flew like they had spare parts, noodles with no damn beef in them, and anybody who wore a helmet indoors. Spike let most of it slide past him. Once he said, "You forgot parking." Faye told him to die carefully. Ed skipped ahead in erratic diagonals, then ran back, then stopped in the middle of the sidewalk to study the glow from the occupied lot with Ein under one arm like a stolen loaf of bread.

Jet kept looking too. Every angle on the ship offended him more. It didn't belong there in any sense that mattered. The practical part of him knew it, the weight-and-silence part knew it, the part that kept coming back to the same corner of the world and expected it to know his shape.

By the time they climbed the ramp back into the Bebop, the ship's heat had dropped enough that the deck plates carried the cold through Jet's boots.

He hated the paid lot. He hated the receipt in his pocket. He hated that the back window over the galley looked onto a cracked lamp post instead of the steam vent behind Mama Yun's.

He hated, most of all, that he was going to sleep angry over parking and wake up still poor.

* * *

Night on the paid dock had a different sound than night behind the noodle shop. Mama Yun's lot carried broth steam, dishwater, the slap of back doors, somebody arguing in the apartments upstairs, the compressor kicking on like a cough. The paid lot had a security lamp buzzing over concrete and the faint grind of a meter somewhere inside the kiosk, measuring hours into money. It sounded like being charged for breathing.

Jet couldn't settle.

He took apart the galley cabinet latch that had been sticking since last week and spread the pieces out on a towel at the table because his hands needed something to do that wasn't strangling strangers. The screw heads were soft with age. The spring inside had lost tension. He cleaned each bit anyway, wiped the old grease off, put on fresh, checked the hinge alignment, took it apart again. The cabinet still held shut if you lifted it a fraction and pressed.

Spike had drifted to the common room couch with one knee up and a cigarette tucked behind his ear unsmoked because Jet had threatened to toss him out the airlock if he lit up near the fuel filters again. Faye was on the far end in her boots, not quite lying down, not quite sitting, flipping a battered deck of cards from hand to hand without looking at them. Every third throw she lost one and swore at the floor. Ein had wedged himself under the table where the heating vent still worked best.

Ed sat cross-legged at the terminal in the corner, back bent, hair haloed by monitor light. The screen threw pale columns over the walls and the nearest window. It made the room look thinner than it was.

"What've you got," Jet said without looking up from the latch.

"Dead mechanic," Ed said. "Retrofit trail runs through a repair outfit that dissolved two years ago. Company graveyard says dead. Work order says fresh sealant last month."

Faye flicked a card, missed the deck, and let it lie where it landed. "Maybe it's a honeymoon."

"In that alley?" Jet said.

"People have started marriages on less."

Spike closed his eyes. "You'd know."

Faye's card snapped a little louder between her fingers. "What's that supposed to mean."

"Nothing."

"You always say nothing when you mean something annoying."

"Saves time."

Jet fitted the cleaned spring back into the latch. His temples had started to pulse in time with Faye's cards hitting the table. "Can we have one hour. One hour where nobody takes the bait every time somebody breathes."

"He doesn't have to make it sound like bait," Faye said.

"You don't have to bite it."

"You say that like you don't."

Jet looked up long enough to glare at both of them equally. "I'm trying to repair something."

Spike opened one eye. "That thing's been fine for three years."

"It sticks."

"Only if you pull it wrong."

"Everything works if you use it exactly right, Spike. That's not the point."

The words came out sharper than the latch deserved. He was talking about the cabinet and not talking about it at all.

Faye barked a short laugh that died quickly, not because anything sad had entered the room, just because they were all too tired to keep a laugh alive unless it was earning its keep.

Jet set the latch down. The galley smelled faintly of soap and old broth. The receipt from the kiosk sat under the sugar tin because he'd shoved it there and not thrown it out yet. He could feel it in the room like an unpaid insult.

Ed made a little humming noise and pulled another window across the screen. Data reflected in Ed's goggles, scrolled past, changed shape. Ed worked like some people played piano, not because the comparison was poetic but because the hands and the ears seemed to be doing different jobs while the rest of the body kept time.

"Can you get me the contractor on the retrofit?" Jet asked.

"That was the contractor," Ed said. "Paper trail says yes. Actual trail says no."

Spike opened one eye. "Shell company."

"Conch shell maybe," Ed said.

Faye flicked another card. "So our parking thief bought six months of dirt and used ghost mechanics."

Jet could see the invoice trail already, one dead repair shop stamped over another. Mars loved a ghost as long as the ghost paid cash.

"Or borrowed the ghost," Spike said.

Jet set his jaw. "Can we move it?"

"Physically or legally or sneakily?" Ed said.

"The easiest one."

"Quiet-sneaky is gone," Ed said. "Little dock-watch under the struts is real. Shift the landing gear, open the hatch, maybe even bump the nose wrong, it chirps off-pad."

"So crime's back to loud," Faye said.

"I wasn't asking you."

"You should ask me things. I've got plenty of loud criminal ideas."

"That's never been in doubt."

The stupid part was that he pictured it anyway: tow clamps biting the stranger's hull, alarms climbing all over the block, the Bebop dragged into one more ugly scene over a patch of dirt. Poverty made idiots by inches, and he could feel the next inch waiting.

Ed stretched both arms over Ed's head without taking eyes off the screen. "Big ship could maybe tug little ship if little ship was sleepy enough and if nobody had alarms and if Jet wanted a tiny war."

"I don't want a tiny war," Jet said.

Spike put the cigarette in his fingers and turned it without lighting it. "What kind do you want."

"The kind where a stranger gets out of my spot and I don't pay for it."

"That's not a kind of war."

"It's on Mars."

That got an actual smile out of Spike, quick and reluctant. Faye saw it and looked offended on principle.

"Tomorrow," Jet said, more to himself than to anyone, "I'm not asking who saw it land. I'm asking who sold a stranger coolant, seals, or a quiet repair. Ships don't keep themselves cold by wishing."

Talking plans steadied him a little. Coolant, seals, invoices, faces. Those were things a man could go lay hands on.

"Or nobody answers," Spike said.

"Then somebody lies with details," Jet said.

"Quiet repair still sounds fancy," Faye said.

"Rich doesn't mean fancy."

"It means rich enough to be annoying."

Jet checked the hinge alignment against the cabinet door. Still off by a hair. Good. A hair was something he could fix. He reached for the screwdriver again.

The Bebop ticked around them as the outer hull cooled. Life support hummed. Somewhere down the corridor a loose panel rattled once and settled. Ed's keyboard clicked in shifting bursts. Outside, a ship came in high over the district, engines thinning out as it looked for somewhere better than the lot they were stuck in.

For a while the room relaxed into the kind of near-silence only people who lived on top of each other could manage. It wasn't peace. It was the familiar truce. Faye kept throwing cards and gathering them. Spike watched the window. Jet worked the latch. Ed dived deeper.

"Huh," Ed said.

Jet did not look up immediately. On the Bebop, huh covered a lot of ground. Burned toast. Missing socks. A government data breach. Ein doing something with a shoe that wasn't technically illegal. "What kind of huh."

"Round huh."

"That's not a kind."

"Now it's a kind."

Faye flung another card. "Can the round huh wait till morning."

"Morning's still round," Ed said, distracted.

Jet reattached the latch to the cabinet and tested it. It clicked shut smoothly for the first time in a week. He should have felt better. Instead the small success only made the real problem look pettier and therefore meaner.

Ed's work-song sheared off halfway through a note.

That got his attention.

Jet looked over. Ed was folded over the terminal with one hand on the keyboard and the other hovering above the trackpad. On the screen, lines of registry data had given way to a pulse chart of the ship's outgoing dock ping stretched across several hours, with an older, blockier archive pane crouched underneath it.

"Ed?" Jet said.

No answer.

Spike sat up a little. Faye lowered the cards.

Jet pushed back from the table. The chair legs scraped. "What'd you hit."

Ed's fingers twitched once and opened a narrower pane inside the first. More strings. More numbers. Rows of timestamps stacked beside the pulse map. Each public ping rose clean and even. Under every ninth ping sat a second faint three-beat hitch, identical every time, like another signal was hiding inside the first one and clearing its throat on a delay. Jet didn't know the old code, but he knew a civilian ship was not supposed to answer roll call twice.

"I said what'd you hit."

Ed turned then.

Jet had seen Ed excited, smug, hungry, feverish, upside down, asleep in a vent, laughing so hard no sound came out. He had seen Ed faking seriousness to torment adults and real seriousness only once or twice, brief as weather. This was neither. The grin had not made it back from the screen. The eyes looked fixed to something far behind it, something only Ed could see yet.

"I was checking how it said hello," Ed said softly. "Public ID pings the dock once. This thing pings the dock once and something older once underneath every ninth cycle. Same three-knock rhythm every time. Older than the registration by a lot."

Spike was already on his feet. "Wrong how."

Ed looked at him and not at him. "I don't know who the second hello is for yet."

"Jet," Ed said, and now even the voice had lost its bounce. "Come here."

Jet was already moving. Whatever Ed had found, it had shoved parking clean out of the room. On the screen, under the clean white rise of the public ping, the second answer came back late again: three blunt knocks tucked under every ninth cycle.


# Chapter 2

The Bebop had a stretch of time that was not night anymore and not yet morning either, a slack ugly hour when the pipes ticked in the walls, the air recycler breathed dust-dry air through old filters, and Jet's first pot of coffee always smelled a little burned because the burner ran hot on the left side. Ed liked that hour. Adults were slow in it. Systems were not.

Ed had been awake through most of it, or maybe all of it. Sleep had happened at some point on the couch with Ein against Ed's ribs, but the second hello under the dock ping had kept making a scratchy little noise in Ed's head, so Ed had rolled off the cushion before the lights fully changed and crawled back to the main console. The dark ship in their docking spot was still clean on the public registries. The registration was clean. The tax records were clean. The maintenance history was clean. It was too clean, as if somebody had taken a rag to the fingerprints.

Ed liked fingerprints.

Three archive windows hung over the main screen in layers: bounty board, old freight liens, and a deep crawler that smelled in Ed's mind like old cardboard and forgotten passwords. The same three-knock handshake pattern was still there under the public ID, a tiny wrong rhythm buried in the outgoing ping. Anybody digging deep could hear it plain enough. The code was old enough that the modern systems treated it like dust.

Ed loved dust too.

"Aha-ha," Ed said softly to the console. "Crunchy little signal biscuit. Ed bites, biscuit breaks, inside is another biscuit."

Ein lifted his head from the floor, watched Ed for a moment, then put his chin back down.

Behind Ed, the ship went through its usual half-dead dawn routine. Jet banged a cupboard door harder than necessary. Water ran in the galley sink. Something hissed in a pan. Faye, who had slept on the couch without meaning to and would deny it if asked, dragged the blanket over her face and made a noise that meant everybody should die quietly. Spike sat at the table with one ankle over one knee, cigarette unlit between his fingers because Jet had bitched about smoke before coffee again. He looked as if he had been awake for hours or for three minutes. With Spike it was always hard to tell.

Ed found the record on the fourth cross-reference.

It surfaced as a stale old line item with the edges frayed by time, sitting in a government archive that had not expected to be loved this hard. No warning stamp showed. No alarm sounded.

ARCHIVED. DORMANT. CLAIM STATUS EXPIRED.

SUBJECT: TAKARA, HOSUKE.

OFFENSE CLASSIFICATION: PETTY LOGISTICS FRAUD.

The date was old enough to have dust on it. The reward was a joke. The attached description was not.

Ed sat up so fast the chair rolled back and hit the bulkhead.

"Jet-person!" Ed shouted.

Faye groaned under the blanket. "If this is another weird plate of numbers, I'm killing you."

"This is a weird plate of numbers with a man on top. Very important garnish."

Jet did not turn right away. He had the coffee pot in his right hand and half an eggshell in the other, big shoulders filling the galley space. Burnt grounds ringed the hot plate under the burner. "If it doesn't pay, it can wait."

"It pays in mystery money."

"Then it definitely can wait."

Ed was already skittering barefoot across the deck, one hand on the edge of the console so the screen followed. "No waiting. Waiting makes the data sad."

Spike glanced over before anyone else did. His eyes moved once across the page, then came back slower. "What's Hosuke Takara?"

Jet turned then.

Ed saw the change before the adults started doing adult things to cover it up. The room altered. Sound tightened around the screen. Jet stood with the coffee pot in his right hand and the eggshell crushed into the other palm and did not seem to feel either one. Faye pushed the blanket down enough to squint at the screen. Spike stopped tapping ash from a cigarette he had not lit.

"Old sleepy bounty," Ed said brightly, because bright was still correct. "Dormant, archived, crunchy around the edges. Triggered on the ship's signal pattern when Ed dug deep enough. Look look look, the little description man has Jet-face."

Faye sat up. Her hair had gone flat on one side and mean on the other. "What?"

Ed turned the screen toward her with both hands. The file had a cheap age progression attached, some underfunded database clerk's best compromise between photo and sketch: young male, Mars-born, thick through the shoulders, dark beard shadow maybe and maybe not. The cheek was smooth where Jet's scar would be later. Both arms were flesh in the image. The mouth mattered, though, and the ears, and the heavy blunt shape of the brow. Time and trouble had changed the face, but not enough.

Faye blinked once, then let out one short laugh that died almost at once. "Oh, hell."

Jet set the coffee pot down with too much care. "Move it back."

Ed did not. "Jet-person had a secret name. Hosuke Hosuke Hosuke."

"Ed."

Jet did not raise his voice. That was what made Faye look at him properly.

She swung her legs off the couch. "No, hold on. Is that you?"

Jet wiped his hand on a towel that already had grease on it. A ribbon of egg white slid from his thumb to the floor. "It's an old file. You know how bad those archives are."

"That isn't an answer."

"It means the archive is bad."

Spike leaned back in his chair, eyes still on Jet instead of the screen. "Looks a little like you."

Jet's jaw moved once. "A lot of people look like me."

"That's unfortunate for them," Faye said. She stood and came closer, arms folded against the cold. "Hosuke? Seriously? That's what you came up with?"

"Who says I came up with anything?"

"The part where you look like the file says that."

Ed made the screen larger. "Also height variance within acceptable range, ear geometry match probable, shoulder width match probable, facial ratio match probable. Eyebrow grumpiness identical."

"Eyebrow grumpiness isn't a metric," Faye said.

"Ed can make it one."

The eggs in the pan started to burn. Jet smelled them before anybody else did and still did not move. His face had gone fixed in a way Ed did not usually see. He did not look angry. Angry-Jet was loud and busy and came with hands. This was statue-Jet, the kind where all the sound happened around him and none of it got in.

Spike finally reached over and turned the burner off.

Jet looked at him like he had forgotten the pan existed.

Faye's voice lost some of its edge. The mockery drained out of it and left her watching him straight. "Jet."

"It's nothing," he said.

The lie sat in the galley with the smell of scorched egg.

Ed looked from one adult face to another. They all did this, the saying-one-thing-with-their-mouth and another-thing-with-their-necks and shoulders and the way they held objects too long. Ed was good at the second thing and bored by the first. "If it was nothing, the room wouldn't be making that room-noise."

Faye snorted. "The what?"

"The tight noise."

"That's not a real thing," Faye said.

"Sure it is," Spike said.

Jet took the console from Ed then, not rough but not gentle either, and stared down at the file. ARCHIVED. DORMANT. PETTY LOGISTICS FRAUD. The words were stupidly small for how hard they hit. He had not seen that name in years. He had not heard it spoken out loud in longer than that. He had handed over more of his life than he liked when he joined ISSP, because they wanted everything, every juvenile charge that never stuck, every alias, every address, every stupid trick a seventeen-year-old thought was clever. He had signed forms. Sat in cold offices. Told the truth where it could be checked and lied where it could not. Then he had spent years pretending those papers had gone into a hole and stayed there.

Nobody on the Bebop had any business knowing the name on that screen.

Nobody off the Bebop should have been able to pull enough detail to pin the file to him either, not now. Not after all this time.

Jet closed the archive window. Ed made an offended sound from deep in the chest.

"Hey."

"Enough," Jet said.

"You can't close mystery."

"Watch me."

Faye planted a hand on the table. "You don't get to do that. Not after yesterday, not after some creep parks in our spot and your—"

"Here's what happens." Jet's voice came up enough to fill the galley. He looked at Ed, not Faye. "File stays open. You watch. Passive only — don't send anything, don't ping anybody, and don't scratch the outside of any system that might scratch back. Anybody on this ship reaches past local on this, I pull the antenna myself."

Faye's mouth stayed open for a beat, then closed.

Jet exhaled through his nose. He could feel Spike watching him and hated that more than Faye's needling, because Spike watched quiet. Faye liked noise. Noise was workable. Quiet meant the other man was filing things away.

Ed hugged the console to a narrow chest. "Ed is always passive. Like a very attentive rock."

"You know what I mean."

"Yes." A beat. "Jet-person knows Hosuke."

Jet bent to pick up the towel, the dropped shell, anything that let him put his face somewhere else. "Finish your sleep, kid."

Faye didn't push again. Not because she was done. Because Jet had skipped the part where he denied it and gone straight to building a perimeter, and the perimeter told her more than the denial would have.

He moved around them because movement was easier than staying put. He dumped the ruined eggs in the recycler, rinsed the pan, and started over. Practical work went better than standing there. The burner clicked. Oil shimmered. His cybernetic fingertips sorted the cracked shell from the rest of the trash with mechanical precision that felt a little obscene under the circumstances. He knew the shape of his own evasion while he did it and kept doing it anyway.

Behind him Faye muttered, "This ship is starting to feel crowded," which was not really about bodies.

Spike said nothing for a moment. Then, casual as dry toast: "You want me to take another look at the signal?"

Jet kept his back turned. "Do whatever you want."

"He will," Faye said.

Ed, who had already gone back to the console, announced to nobody in particular, "Hosuke Takara liked freight labels and did not like tax compliance."

"Shocking," Faye said.

Jet cracked another egg too hard. Yolk ran between his fingers, warm and slick, and for one stupid second it felt like being seventeen again, standing in a freight office with forged manifests in his jacket and no plan past the next hour. He rinsed his hand until the yellow was gone.

When he looked up, Spike was still in his chair, but the laziness had thinned out of him. He had that distant, listening look he got around trouble, as if somewhere a floorboard had creaked and only he had heard it.

Jet knew that look. He knew it almost as well as he knew his own.

That was worse than the file.

No one said much while the second round of eggs cooked. Faye lit a cigarette anyway and ignored Jet's glare until he shoved an ashtray toward her. Ed hummed to the database, making little corrections under breath as the crawler walked deeper. Spike stood, finally, and wandered toward the corridor with one hand in his pocket.

"Where are you going?" Jet asked before he could stop himself.

Spike half-turned. "To look at the signal."

"With what?"

"A receiver."

"That's specific."

"You'll live."

Faye looked from one to the other. "Everybody's being weird."

"You're weird all the time," Spike said.

"Yeah, and now you're catching up."

He left before she could throw anything.

Jet plated the eggs nobody wanted very much and told himself that if Spike had found something, Spike would say it. That was not true. It had never been true. The only reason the ship worked at all was that they all respected a line none of them ever named. You could share meals, fuel, bruises, bad luck. You did not put your hands in the locked drawer somebody kept in the back of their head.

This morning the drawers had started opening by themselves.

* * *

By midday Jet had a screwdriver in his hand and no idea how long he had been staring at the same junction box.

The latch on the under-sink panel in the galley had been sticking for weeks. It did not need him now. Plenty of things on the Bebop needed him now. The starboard water recycler gave a cough every ten minutes. The short-range radar had a dead strip on the left side. The shower drain in Faye's bathroom backed up if she used too much conditioner, which she denied even while the clogs came out dark with it. Any of those would have been honest work. Instead he had picked the latch because it was close and small and would fit under his hands.

The screws sat in a neat line on the counter by size. The habit embarrassed him a little. So did the fact that he had done it anyway.

Through the open galley he could hear Ed talking to the ship's computer in three registers at once and getting just enough useful output back to count as conversation. Faye had gone out after complaining there was nothing edible on board except eggs and she was not interested in another egg in her life. Good. One less person in the room. The Bebop smelled like hot metal, stale pepper oil, and the cold broth Jet had splashed on his sleeve at breakfast.

Outside the nearest porthole, Mars light came in wrong-colored and thin. Beyond the paid docking lot, two blocks over, their real spot sat behind Mama Yun's with that dark sleek ship parked in it like an insult that had learned manners.

Jet pushed the screwdriver into the latch plate harder than he meant to and stripped the edge of the screw head.

"Nice work," he muttered.

The answer came from behind him.

"You talking to the latch or yourself?"

Spike stood in the galley doorway with a portable receiver tucked under one arm and the Bebop's old maintenance headset dangling from one hand. He looked exactly like he always looked: suit hanging wrong because he'd slept in it, hair doing whatever it wanted, face unreadable unless you knew him. Jet knew him. That was the problem.

"Both," Jet said. "What'd you rip that receiver out of?"

"Storage."

"Storage isn't a system."

"Depends how you wire it."

Jet set the screwdriver down. "You need something from me?"

"No."

"Then why are you hovering?"

Spike lifted the headset a little. "Making conversation."

"Don't start now."

For half a second Spike almost smiled. Then it was gone. "Touchy."

Jet wiped his prosthetic hand on a rag though it was clean. "You got somewhere private to do that, or are you going to let the whole ship listen?"

"Swordfish bay."

"Fine."

Spike moved on without haste, but not with his usual drag either. The difference was small. Jet caught it anyway. The receiver's loose cable knocked once against the bulkhead as Spike disappeared down the corridor.

Jet looked at the stripped screw, at the open panel, and then toward the hall.

He picked up the screwdriver, put it down, and followed.

He did not go all the way. He stopped at the mouth of the hangar passage where he could see without being seen unless Spike turned around. The bay lights were dimmed to save power. Swordfish II sat in the cradle with its nose angled toward the launch doors, all clean lines and old violence. Spike climbed onto the access ladder, set the receiver on the rim of the cockpit, and plugged into the ship's comm package with the ease of somebody who had done a lot of illegal things on very little sleep.

Jet should have walked away then. He knew it. Instead he leaned a shoulder against the cold wall and listened to static bleed faintly from the headset speakers.

Spike tuned by hand.

Ed worked like a net diver, all at once, throwing hooks into twelve systems and seeing what bit. Spike worked like a man cracking a safe in a building where people had died, with slow turns and patience. He stripped the public ID signal away first, then the registry wrapper, then the ordinary maintenance handshake under that. What came through next was thin and ugly, a pulse with too much space between beats.

To Jet it sounded like garbage.

To Spike it sounded like a dead man clearing his throat.

He went still on the ladder, one hand on the edge of the cockpit. The bay suddenly seemed colder. That old handshake belonged to a dead freight language: two short bursts, a hold, carrier wobble, route-auth riding under the public ping. He had spent years hearing versions of it through cheap earpieces in back offices above warehouses, in freight yards that smelled of oil and rat poison, in transit shuttles with false flooring and crates strapped down badly. Messages came wrapped in ordinary traffic, invisible if you did not know the rhythm. Cargo would go here. Escort would go there. Somebody would change the route. Somebody would burn the ledger. A whole supply chain had moved on sounds like this.

And tucked inside the handshake, ghosted in an old auth string no one should still be using, was the name he had used because it kept his real one off paper.

Lin Shao-Feng.

The name sat there as a scuff in the code, half-erased but legible enough, where the route key identified which set of hands the shipment belonged to.

He did not feel much when things got bad. That was one of the few dependable facts of his life. His pulse did not race. His breath did not catch. The world simply flattened out. Unnecessary details dropped away. The stupid little hangar on the Bebop became ladder, receiver, signal, door.

Someone had found an old syndicate logistics handshake, configured a ship to echo it on purpose, and parked that ship within arm's reach of him.

If any remnant node of the network was still listening, if any of the old scavengers or successor crews still ran watchers on dead frequencies, then the moment the Bebop had come within range their location had been handed to the past like a calling card.

Spike pulled the lead loose so fast the headset snapped with static.

The sound carried down the corridor. Jet straightened away from the wall before Spike turned.

He waited a beat, then stepped into the bay as if he had only just arrived. "You break it?"

Spike looked down from the ladder. His face had gone blank in a way Jet had only seen a handful of times. Blank on Spike was not calm. Blank was blood drained off the room.

"No," Spike said. "Just old hardware."

Jet let that sit there. "That's what it is?"

"Yeah."

It was a bad lie. Spike almost never told lies poorly. It was bad because Jet knew what Spike looked like when he had stubbed a toe, when he'd missed a shot, when he'd lost money, when he'd decided not to care. This was none of those faces.

Jet climbed halfway onto the ladder beside him and looked at the disconnected receiver. The tiny screen showed only static hash and a dead line where the data had been. Whatever Spike had seen, he had already killed it.

"You want to tell me something?" Jet asked.

Spike coiled the lead around his hand once, neatly. "Do you?"

There it was.

Jet could have answered. He could have said yes, there was an old bounty file with a dead name attached to it and somebody had reached into paperwork that should have stayed buried. He could have said he did not know who had done it and that was what scared him. He got as far as opening his mouth.

"There's a—" He stopped.

Spike waited.

"No," Jet said. "Forget it."

"You didn't say anything."

"Right. So forget it."

Spike pulled the headset cord taut between his hands, held it, let it go. "Kill the Bebop's outgoing ping."

Jet blinked. "What?"

"The maintenance handshake. The standard docking broadcast. Kill it."

"Every ship in the lot runs that. If I shut it off, port authority flags us inside a day."

"Tell them it's a malfunction. You're good at that."

Jet stared at him. Spike was not a man who gave orders on the Bebop. Spike barely gave suggestions. He drifted through rooms and let other people make decisions and then did whatever he'd planned to do anyway. This was different. This had edges.

"What did you hear?" Jet asked.

Spike tucked the receiver under his arm. "The ship out there is carrying something under the public signal. Old. Shouldn't still exist." He paused, and the pause was where the rest of the information lived. "If our ping is answering it on default, we're having a conversation we didn't start."

That was more than Spike volunteered about anything. Jet could feel the shape of what was missing — the part Spike was keeping behind his teeth — but the part he'd given was enough to make the bay feel smaller.

"Tell Ed too," Spike said. "Passive watch only. No crawling the outside of that ship's systems."

"You're not going to tell me what you heard."

"I told you what to do about it."

Jet almost laughed. It would have come out ugly, so he didn't bother. "Hell of a morning."

"It's still early."

He headed back toward the corridor. Jet turned enough to watch him go. Spike had a cigarette in his mouth now, but it stayed unlit all the way out of the bay.

That more than anything told Jet how bad it was.

He waited until Spike's steps faded, then looked at the cockpit rim where the receiver had sat. Bare metal. Spike hadn't left anything behind.

Mutual concealment was the cheap truce the Bebop ran on. Do not ask unless you want to answer. Do not pry unless you are ready to be pried open in return. It had worked because the past stayed out where it belonged. Today it had come aboard in pieces, and Jet could feel the whole ship listing around the weight of what nobody was saying.

He went back to the galley and spent twenty minutes fixing the wrong latch.

* * *

Faye told herself she left the Bebop because there was no decent food on it and because Jet, once he got spooked, started breathing responsibility into every room until the air went stale.

That was not the whole reason, but it was enough to put shoes on.

Mars cold hit through the thin fabric around her legs the minute she stepped off the gangway. The paid docking lot was a slab of patched concrete bordered by cargo fencing and one flickering light pole that made everybody look jaundiced. Dust had gathered in the seams of the pavement overnight. It always did. Red grit in the grooves, red grit in the cuffs, red grit on the toe of her boot by the third step. Two blocks away, behind Mama Yun's, the ship in their usual spot caught the amber daylight along one smooth flank and gave nothing back.

Faye slowed without meaning to.

Up close the thing annoyed her even more. It was too sleek for the neighborhood and too well kept to be honest. The hull lines had been modified by somebody who knew what they were doing. She saw no obvious registry decals and no streaks of burnt fuel around the aft vents. Somebody had wiped this ship down by hand. The Bebop wore its life on the outside. This one looked as if it had filed an appeal.

She walked a half circle around it and saw nothing through the dark canopy. There was no pilot and no movement, just her own reflection stretched thin and mean in the glass.

"Creep," she muttered to the ship, and went inside.

Mama Yun's noodle house held heat the way cheap places did, by never fully letting go of it. Steam filmed the windows. The fluorescent light above the counter buzzed like a trapped fly. The place smelled of broth, soy, pepper oil, old bleach, and wet coats. A lunch rush had not started yet, but the stools were warm from whoever had been there before. Somebody had scuffed a muddy boot print into the floor by the door and tracked it halfway to the register.

Mama Yun herself was nowhere in sight. A grease-pencil shift card clipped to the register had `YUN` scratched out and `MIRIAM - LUNCH` written hard over it. Behind the counter stood a woman Faye did not know, mid-fifties maybe, sleeves rolled to the elbow, hair pinned up with the sort of metal clip that looked as if it had outlived two marriages and a kitchen fire. The woman was already telling the cook, for what sounded like the third time, that Mama Yun's nephew had opened his palm on a prep slicer and she was only here until Yun got back from the clinic. She had tired eyes and the Ganymede roundness in the vowels when she called back an order. A squat salt shaker rode in the pocket of her apron and, before she even tasted the broth she'd poured for herself, she shook a white storm into it and muttered, "Mars people sweeten everything. Cowards."

That, Faye liked on sight.

The woman noticed her noticing and shrugged. "What? They do."

"You're working in the wrong place to say that out loud."

"I rotate. They can fire me from one shift, I'll irritate somebody else by dinner." She set a bowl down in front of an older man at the end stool and jerked her chin at Faye. "You want something or you just came in to judge the decor?"

Faye slid onto a stool. "Cheapest thing on the menu."

"That's noodles."

"Then noodles."

"You people from the ship all order like you're being insulted by existence."

"We are."

The woman snorted. "Fair enough. Service work gives you a memory for faces and cheap men. Same muscle, I think."

Faye leaned her elbows on the counter and tried not to think about Jet's face that morning, about Ed chanting Hosuke like a nursery rhyme, about Spike doing that awful quiet thing he did when his mind was somewhere he'd rather set on fire. She looked instead at the kitchen pass, at the old jar of pickled peppers, at the thin crack in the mirror behind the shelves where bottles of cheap liquor sat waiting for nighttime.

The waitress set down a bowl and the salt shaker in one move. "There. Burn your mouth and complain less."

Faye took the chopsticks. Steam hit her face. The broth smelled good enough to make irritation seem decorative.

The waitress had already turned away, then slowed and looked back with the stubborn concentration of somebody dragging a face up from old years.

"Hold on," she said.

Faye looked up. "What."

The woman squinted at her the way service workers did when dragging a memory up from under ten thousand interchangeable faces. "Do I know you?"

Faye's first answer arrived from habit. "No."

"Maybe not now." The woman wiped her hands on the apron and leaned one hip against the counter. "Ganymede."

Faye kept the chopsticks poised over the bowl. "Never been."

"Golden Ox."

Something in the name made her pause, not because she recognized it but because the waitress did not sound like she was guessing anymore.

"High room," the woman said. "Table by the rail. Blue dress. Low cut enough that the wool trader with the rings couldn't stop looking where he wasn't supposed to. I was on that floor half the night because the bastard never tipped and management never threw him out. Then you cleaned him out and walked with half his cash."

Faye smiled the way she smiled when men got fresh and she wanted them to regret it later. "Lady, that's flattering, but it wasn't me."

"It was you."

"No."

"Honey, I carried drinks to that table all night. I know who it was."

Faye shook her head and went back to the bowl, because dismissal worked on most people. The chopsticks slipped in broth-slick fingers. She got a mouthful of noodles anyway and they burned the roof of her mouth bad enough to make her eyes water.

"You had a laugh," the waitress went on, uninvited. "Small, through your nose, like you were already counting his money while he kept talking. You don't do it now. Or maybe you do and I haven't said the right thing yet."

Faye chewed. Swallowed. "You've got the wrong woman."

"Then you have the same face, the same voice, and the same bad habit."

Faye set the chopsticks down.

"What habit."

The waitress touched two fingers to her own collarbone, just under the throat, quick as a tic. "That. Every time you were bluffing or deciding whether to bluff. Right there. Like you were checking if the bones were still where you left them."

Faye's hand moved before she could stop it.

Fingers to skin. The little notch at the base of the throat. Warm from broth steam.

The waitress saw it and her expression changed from effort to certainty. "See?"

Faye dropped her hand to the counter as if the skin had stung her. The stool suddenly felt too narrow. The shop felt too hot. By the years the woman was talking about, Faye should have been nowhere. She should have been a body in a freezing unit, then paperwork, then interest compounding on debt she had not chosen. She knew that much even if most of her life before waking was still a locked room. The dates alone made the whole thing impossible.

It was impossible and happening anyway.

"No," Faye said, and heard how flat it came out.

The waitress frowned. "What do you mean no?"

"I mean—" Faye's hand was still near her collarbone. She dropped it to the counter. "When was this."

"When was what?"

"The Ox. The table. When."

Miriam's frown shifted from confused to calculating. "I don't know, eleven years ago? Twelve? Year they shut the Ganymede interchange. No — year after. I remember because the ferries were already screwed up."

Eleven years. Twelve. Faye did the math before she could stop herself and the math didn't work. Eleven years ago she'd been frozen solid in a cryo-unit, accruing debt, accruing nothing, a body with a name on a clipboard and a temperature instead of a pulse. The dates were broken.

"That's not—" She shut her mouth. Tried again. "I wasn't anywhere eleven years ago."

"Honey, everybody was somewhere eleven years ago."

"Not me."

It came out stranger than she'd meant it to. Miriam's face changed — not sympathy, not disbelief, something flatter. The look of a woman who'd worked enough counters to know when a conversation had stepped off a ledge it hadn't meant to.

"All right," she said. "All right. I'm telling you what I saw. You do what you want with it."

Faye looked down into the broth. Oil swam red on top. A slice of scallion bobbed against the rim. Her reflection in the liquid was broken by steam.

"What was your name?" she asked before she could decide not to.

"Miriam."

"You sure about this, Miriam?"

"About your order? No. About Ganymede? Yeah."

Faye almost smiled at that and hated her for earning it.

Miriam folded a towel with careful square motions. "I remember because that man had been stiffing the floor staff for months, and watching him lose made the whole place nicer for one night. I remember because your dress was blue enough to hurt my eyes under those lights. I remember because after, when everybody else acted like servers were furniture, you looked right at me when you asked for the check. And you tipped."

"That sounds sentimental."

"It wasn't. You still looked mean."

Faye let out a short breath, close enough to a laugh that Miriam noticed and gave a tiny, grimly satisfied nod.

"See?" Miriam said.

"That doesn't prove anything."

"No. But it happened."

Faye stared at the salt shaker tucked in the apron pocket. Cheap plastic, cloudy from years of use. One side had cracked and been taped. She had the stupid urge to grab it and throw it at the mirror just to hear something break cleanly.

Instead she said, "You rotate here a lot?"

Miriam lifted one shoulder. "When they need hands."

"You worked the Ox how long?"

"Long enough. Why?"

"Just asking."

"Uh-huh."

Miriam was not intimidated, which made lying to her less fun. Faye reached for the broth, missed the bowl on the first pass, and caught the paper cup instead. The thin waxed sides crumpled a little in her grip. That made her furious at herself in a different way.

Miriam watched that too and went gentler than Faye wanted. "Look, maybe I got the place wrong. Could've been a different room. Could've been earlier, later, who knows. But I didn't get the face wrong."

"People get faces wrong all the time."

"Sure. But they don't usually get the little things wrong."

The kitchen bell rang. Miriam looked back, then to Faye again. Work came first. Memory came second. "You want me to pack that?"

Faye had eaten four bites at most. Her mouth still tasted of hot broth and metal.

"Yeah," she said.

"Fine."

Miriam took the bowl away. Over her shoulder she said, "If you think of the place later, don't come back in here acting like I made it up."

"I don't act like that."

Miriam gave a dry little sound. "You act like a lot of things, honey."

Faye waited with both hands wrapped around the paper cup of water until the noodles came in a carry box. Miriam set the box down, then tucked two packets of chili oil on top as if this were the most natural end to the conversation in the world.

"On the house?" Faye asked.

"No. Mama Yun will put it on Jet's tab and he'll complain at me next week."

"That sounds right."

"Mm."

Faye picked up the box. "You really hate Mars broth?"

"I hate what Mars does to broth. Different sin."

That was specific enough to be true. Faye nodded once, then left before the woman could say Golden Ox again.

Outside, the cold had sharpened. Wind moved dust along the curb in low red snakes. Faye took two steps, stopped, balanced the noodle box on one palm, and dug the nails of her free hand into the heel of the other until the sting cut through the churn in her head.

The cold bit her fingers through the cardboard. Miriam's certainty kept pace beside her, step for step.

She swore softly and adjusted her grip on the box.

By the time she got back to the Bebop, each footfall seemed to ring farther through the hull. Ships were metal and wiring and other people's smells. Still, she climbed the ramp with the feeling that somebody had been through the rooms and put each object back exactly where it belonged.

The common room held all of them and none of them, which was how the Bebop did family on bad days.

Ed lay upside down on the couch with the console balanced on shinbones, red hair hanging toward the deck, muttering to the bounty archive. On the screen Hosuke Takara's dead file sat beside three other government records in a loose electronic circle, and a narrow telemetry strip crawled under them with `PASSIVE WATCH // UNKNOWN SHIP` stamped in one corner. Every few seconds Ed enlarged one detail, frowned, and shrank it again.

Jet knelt by the galley cabinet with the under-sink panel open, shoulders bent over a latch that had probably wronged him somehow. The line of screws by size on the counter told Faye he had been at it a while.

Spike stood by the far window with a cigarette in one hand and a portable receiver in the other. The cigarette had burned nearly to the filter without him seeming to notice.

Nobody looked up right away.

That was the real bad sign.

Faye kicked the door shut behind her with more noise than necessary. "If anybody touched my noodles, I'll shoot the cheapest one first."

Ed swung farther upside down to peer at the box. "Noodle delivery achieved."

Jet said, "Put them on the table."

Spike glanced over then. "Everything okay?"

It was such an ordinary line that she almost told the truth just to spite it.

Instead she set the takeout down and peeled off her coat. "Waitress at Yun's thinks she knows me."

Jet's screwdriver stopped. Just for a beat.

"From where?" he asked, too carefully.

Faye shrugged like the answer did not matter. "Some gambling room on Ganymede."

Spike's gaze stayed on her a moment longer than he usually let it. "You ever been to Ganymede?"

"No."

"Then she's got the wrong face," Jet said.

Faye looked at him. He went back to the latch.

Maybe he believed that. Maybe he just wanted a problem he could tighten down with a screwdriver. She understood the instinct well enough not to push.

"Yeah," she said. "Maybe."

Ed had gone quiet, which on Ed lasted about three seconds. Then Ed rolled partly upright and jabbed at the console. "Pattern overlap is increasing."

"What pattern," Faye asked.

Ed waggled the console. "The mystery pattern. Hosuke pattern. Sneaky ship pattern. Faye-person noodle pattern. Ed made a new noodle file. `GOLDEN OX / GANYMEDE / MIRIAM maybe.` Bebop has many patterns."

"That isn't helpful."

"It is extremely helpful. Maybe later."

Spike crushed the cigarette out in the ashtray and set the receiver on the sill facedown. "Keep it local, Ed."

Faye looked from him to the screen. "Why's everybody acting like this thing can hear us think?"

Spike did not look at her. "Because we don't need to knock on its door to know it's there."

Jet slid the latch back into place and tested it once. The cabinet door closed smooth as if it had never stuck.

It was a small, stupid success. He did not look happier for it.

He wiped his hand on the rag and looked at Ed. "Set a proximity alarm on the ship too. If it powers up, if a hatch opens, if somebody so much as breathes hard near it, wake all of us."

Ed's head came farther off the couch. "All of all?"

"All of all."

"Alarm gremlin deployed," Ed said, and the console windows shuffled. The passive-watch strip widened. A fresh local note field bloomed beside the old bounty file, tagged with Miriam's name and Ganymede in Ed's uneven capitalization.

A dry bureaucratic chirp came from the console. One of the archive panes refreshed, the old dormant line blinking once before harder letters stamped themselves across the screen.

"Oops," Ed said, interested rather than alarmed. "Sleepy bounty woke up."

`WARRANT STATUS: REACTIVATED.`

Faye took the noodles to the table and sat. The box warmed her hands through the cardboard. Across from her, the common-room screen held Hosuke Takara in bad old resolution. Beside him sat Miriam's new note window, the bright reactivated warrant line, and the crawl of the passive watch. By the window the receiver reflected a thin strip of Mars light. Under the sink, Jet sorted the last two screws and put them away as if the order of tiny metal objects might still count for something.

No one said what they had actually found, not all of it.

The air recycler hummed. The hull ticked as the temperature dropped outside. Somewhere down the corridor, water knocked once in the pipes and stopped. Faye opened the noodle box, lifted the chopsticks, pressed her knuckles flat to the table until the tendons stood out, and ate while Hosuke Takara stayed lit on the screen and the ship listened for the stranger outside.


# Chapter 3

The name on the common-room screen had been dead for nineteen years. Jet had kept it that way long enough to start believing it had finally done the decent thing and stayed dead.

The night before, Ed had thrown it there in bright bounty-office lettering, cheerful as a game show. `HOSUKE TAKARA`. Dormant warrant. Reactivated. The face attached to it had been a cheap age progression built from buried data, but it had still hit hard. Same brow. Same nose, set a little wrong from when he was sixteen and took a pipe to the face over a crate of stolen filters. Same thick neck he had hated as a kid because it made him look older until he needed to look older.

He had been holding the coffee pot when Ed found it. He remembered that part more clearly than anything anybody said. The steel handle hot in his right hand. Burnt grounds on the plate. The hum of the Bebop under his boots. Faye's one short laugh because the picture looked enough like him to be funny for half a second before it stopped being funny at all.

By morning the Mars cold had gotten into the hull. The ship smelled like stale oil, old coffee, dog, and the peppers Jet had fried the night before. Ed and Ein were asleep under the table in a pile of cables and blankets that looked accidental and never was. Spike was somewhere awake or pretending not to be. Jet pulled on his coat, took the tram into Tharsis, and didn't tell anybody where he was going.

Hosuke Takara had never been an arrest record. That was what made the bounty wrong in a way simple bad luck wasn't. There had been no booking, no prints under that name, no mug shot hanging in some dusty juvenile database. At seventeen he had been one more Mars settlement idiot in a fake work jacket, moving freight that wasn't his for men who preferred not to be seen touching it. When somebody on a loading dock asked his name, he gave them one. When a manifest needed a hand that didn't point back where it should, he gave them that too. Then he got older, got tired, got meaner in a more organized way, and years later sat in a fluorescent ISSP interview room and disclosed enough of it to get ahead of the background check. The only honest place Hosuke Takara still existed was in the sealed vetting file Jet had signed with his real name at the bottom.

If that file had been opened, this wasn't random. It wasn't Ed stumbling over some lost warrant in the deep end of the net. Somebody had gone looking through drawers only ISSP was supposed to have keys for.

The tram let him out two blocks from the Mars district office. Tharsis was halfway between waking up and giving up on it. Shop grates rattled open. A produce vendor hosed red dust off crates of bruised greens grown under lights somewhere farther north. A pair of sanitation drones whined past on their rails, brushing yesterday's wrappers into neat gutter piles. The sky above the settlement dome had that washed-out rust color Mars wore when the weather was cold and the dust stayed low. Jet walked with his collar up and his hands in his pockets, shoulder turned against the early wind blowing down the avenue.

The ISSP building looked exactly the way underfunded authority always looked: too much concrete, not enough glass, and a front seal that had been polished recently enough to count as morale. The security arch at the entrance still chirped half a note sharp. He remembered that because the sound had annoyed him every day for years and no one had ever fixed it. The desk cop at the front glanced up, saw who it was, and did the little face people did when an old coworker showed up after leaving ugly: mouth flattening, shoulders tight, eyes already measuring distance. Cautious, like grief you had to keep passing in the hallway.

Jet signed in with `J. Black`. The stylus dragged on the pad. For one stupid second he had the urge to write the other name just to see what the machine would do.

He took the stairs instead of the lift. Habit. Also the lift smelled like wet wool and vending-machine soup. On the second floor an admin clerk he half recognized came out of records carrying three binders and stopped dead when she saw him. Her hair was cut blunt at the jaw now; it used to have been down past her shoulders. Jet couldn't remember her name. She couldn't decide if she wanted to nod. In the end they both kept moving like adults in a small institution.

Donovan had been kicked sideways out of field work years ago and landed exactly where men like Donovan always landed if they didn't quit first: behind a desk with more access than glamour. He sat at the outer records station in shirtsleeves, uniform jacket hanging off the back of his chair, reading glasses low on his nose. A half-finished roll of chalky antacid tablets sat beside the terminal, and he kept smoothing the foil backing flat with his thumb until it shone. Jet remembered Donovan as a broad man with a wrestler's neck and a smoker's laugh. He was still broad. The laugh had gone somewhere else.

Donovan looked up, took in the coat, the beard, the fact of Jet in his office at eight in the morning, and leaned back hard enough to make the chair complain.

"No," he said.

Jet pulled the spare chair out anyway and sat. "I didn't ask yet."

"You didn't need to. If you're here this early, it's either money or trouble, and you don't come to me for money."

"I need a quiet pull."

Donovan pinched the bridge of his nose, then took off his glasses and polished them on the hem of his sleeve. It looked less like cleaning and more like buying time. "Quiet pulls are the loudest kind."

"You owe me."

"I knew you were going to say that." Donovan put the glasses back on. "Every time I see you, I remember that one thing and not the eight times I covered your ass for free."

"Then call it balance."

Donovan gave him a long look. "What am I pulling?"

Jet kept his voice level. "Dormant bounty. Name is Hosuke Takara."

The foil stopped moving under Donovan's thumb.

That was enough. Jet felt it low and ugly, the old cop instinct that measured a room by what went still in it.

Donovan didn't answer right away. He swiveled back to the terminal, typed with two fingers, waited, typed again. The records light washed his face a sick blue. Jet could hear the office around them waking up in layers: printers stuttering, phones starting, somebody down the hall laughing too hard at something not funny.

"Who gave you that name?" Donovan asked.

"It found me."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one you get."

Donovan exhaled through his nose. "You always did know how to make a favor feel like paperwork."

"Can you pull it or not?"

"I can pull anything. Question is whether I want the audit trail." He kept typing. More windows opened. Jet caught glimpses of archive flags, access trees, old agency codes he hadn't seen since he turned in his badge. Donovan's mouth tightened. "Well. Hell."

"What?"

"You weren't kidding."

"I don't joke before coffee."

"That's one of the reasons people liked you so much." Donovan turned the screen a few degrees. Not enough for the room. Enough for Jet.

The bounty shell sat open in a nested archive window, ugly and thin. There shouldn't have been a shell at all. That was the first wrongness. The second was the source path hanging off it in a list of attached material: sealed vetting summary, intake physical markers, juvenile association note, cross-reference restrictions. It wasn't all the file, but it was enough. Enough to build a body out of. Enough to tell a bounty board this ghost had weight.

Jet felt the back of the chair through his coat like a shove.

"You see it?" Donovan said quietly. "This wasn't some public scrape. Somebody reached down deep."

Jet read the access stamp again, hoping on some dumb level that his eyes were giving him trouble. They weren't. "Who authorized it?"

Donovan clicked into the sighting report. "That's where it gets strange."

"Strange's been here since yesterday. Keep up."

"Three weeks ago the system got a live sighting submission on Takara. Standard format, enough fields filled to wake the shell. The submission came through on a protected source block." He clicked once more, jaw hardening. "Credentials attached to the report belong to a confidential informant."

Jet watched the next line populate.

`KOZUKI, DAICHI`

The name landed flat. No face came with it. No voice, no alley, no old debt.

"Who the hell is that?" Jet asked.

Donovan glanced at him, saw the blank on his face, and seemed almost disappointed by it. "Dead informant. Mars side. Been dead a long time."

"Then the credentials are cloned."

"Maybe. Maybe not." Donovan leaned back and reached for the antacid roll without looking at it. He dry-swallowed two tablets, grimaced, and kept going. "The codes authenticated deep enough to touch sealed material. That's the part I don't like. Spoofing a source block is one thing. Riding it into internal vetting is another. Somebody either had help from inside or somebody on the inside left a hell of a door unlocked."
