Jet kept his eyes on the screen. His own younger life sat there in pieces he had once handed over under fluorescent light because honesty had seemed cheaper than getting caught in the lie. He remembered that interview room now with an almost physical clarity. The cracked vinyl chair. The bad coffee. The lieutenant from Personnel who'd let him talk and talk until he started helping the questions along. Prior aliases. Prior uncharged activity. Prior associations of note. Jet had turned freight theft into "informal transport work." He had turned using false manifests into "document irregularities." He had cleaned it up because he wanted the badge. ISSP had taken the cleaned-up version, put it in a sealed file, and called him suitable.

Suitable. Christ.

"How are Kozuki's codes still live?" he asked.

Donovan snorted once. "Because this place never kills anything all the way. Because records don't get budget and dead informants don't get advocates. Because somebody paid to keep a line breathing. Pick your flavor."

"Which one are you leaning toward?"

"The one that keeps me from sleeping?" Donovan folded the antacid foil in half, then in half again. "Deliberate. Accidental neglect doesn't usually thread a needle this fine."

Jet looked at the timestamp on the report. Three weeks. Before they ever saw the ship behind Mama Yun's. Before Ed tripped over the bounty. This had been waiting.

"Can you open Kozuki's file?"

Donovan didn't even pretend to consider it. "Not for you."

"I asked, didn't I."

"And I'm answering." Donovan tapped the screen with one blunt finger. "I can tell you the man existed. I can tell you his access should've been burned when his status closed out. I can tell you somebody used that access to reactivate Takara and pull sealed background material connected to the identity. Anything beyond that, I stop doing you a favor and start donating my pension."

Jet said nothing.

Donovan studied him a moment. Under the office lights the man looked older than fifty, not in the face so much as in the posture. Records work had given him a permanent forward lean, like the files were always trying to pull him in. "You really don't know the name."

"No."

"Then that's worse."

"How do you figure?"

"Because if this was simple blackmail, they'd use a name you knew." Donovan nudged the turned screen with two fingers until it faced himself again. "This looks like somebody building a trail. For you, maybe. For somebody else watching, maybe. I don't like it."

"Join the club."

Donovan's mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "You want my advice?"

"Not especially."

"Too bad." He hooked his glasses off again and rubbed the bridge of his nose with thumb and forefinger until the skin went pale. "Keep this small. If you start asking for dead-source files by name, somebody upstairs notices. If somebody upstairs already expects you to ask, then maybe that's the point."

"You're saying let it sit."

"I'm saying don't swing a hammer at a wall if you haven't checked what's wired behind it."

Jet pushed back from the chair. "You got me the timestamp and the name. That's enough."

Donovan looked offended on principle. "No, it isn't."

"It's what I have."

Jet stood. Donovan stood too, slower, as if his knees had opinions. For a second they were two ex-cops in a room full of computers pretending the years between then and now had been orderly.

"Jet," Donovan said.

He stopped.

Donovan looked past him to the open office, then back. His voice dropped. "If this touches your vetting file, somebody knew where to look. That means they knew the gap between your real name and the other one. That's not broad-net stuff. That's specific."

"Yeah."

"You going to tell the people on your ship?"

Jet thought of Ed's wide excited face, Faye's fast sharp mouth, Spike leaning in the doorway with one shoulder and that expression that said he was paying attention no matter how bored he looked. Thought of the name sitting on the screen. Thought of his own past written up in institutional language, polite as a funeral notice.

"Not yet," he said.

Donovan didn't approve. He didn't disapprove either. He just looked tired. "You always did believe in timing more than honesty."

"That's why you let me buy the first round and not the second."

"Get out of here." Donovan's hand closed over the antacid roll again. "And if anybody asks, you came by to complain about bounty payouts like every other washed-up cowboy."

"Wouldn't be a lie."

"No," Donovan said. "That's why it'll work."

Jet left by the side stairwell this time. The front felt too exposed. Halfway down he stopped with one hand on the rail and looked at his reflection in the narrow wired-glass window cut into the landing door. Broad shoulders. Scar on the cheek. Beard gone gray at the edges whether he liked it or not. No trace of Hosuke unless you knew where to pry. Maybe that had been the mistake, all these years. Thinking a thing wasn't visible unless it was obvious.

Outside, the wind had picked up. Dust skimmed low along the curb in red sheets. Jet put his head down and walked without deciding where.

***

He should have gone back to the Bebop.

Instead he kept walking.

Tharsis had changed the way working districts always changed when money touched them wrong. One block got cleaned up enough to charge triple rent for noodles and old wood furniture shipped in from nowhere anybody sensible had ever lived. The next block still had patched pressure doors and busted pavement plates that clanged when trucks rolled over them. New signage glowed over old concrete. Cheap prayer strips flapped above mechanic bays. Someone had painted a mural of a blue sky on the side of a warehouse and the dust had already chewed through it in brown streaks.

Jet knew the district by slope as much as by sight. He knew where the street dipped toward the drainage grates after a recycling flush. He knew which alley mouths funneled wind hard enough to sting your ears in winter. He knew where security cameras used to have blind spots, though half of those corners now held coffee stands or vending walls or somebody's expensive idea of neighborhood revival. His body kept choosing routes before he thought about them. Left at the shuttered freight office. Across the narrow footbridge over the service trench. Down the block where the old machine shop had become three tiny storefronts selling handmade soap, repair charms, and imported tea in tins more expensive than a meal.

Hosuke Takara had learned these streets fast because learning them had paid. Not big money. Never that. Money enough to get a room with a door that shut and food that wasn't from a vending rack every night of the week. Money enough to pretend he wasn't one missed job from being back on a floor somewhere with his boots on because getting robbed in your sleep happened if people thought you had anything worth taking.

He had not been a glamorous criminal. That was a story other men told about their youth after enough drinks. At seventeen he moved whatever fit in his arms or on the back of a borrowed cart. Broken seal replacement strips. Unlicensed filters. Cases of cheap ampoules with the labels scraped halfway off. Bootleg chips. Once, a crate of handguns so badly packed he could hear metal clacking every time the cart hit a seam in the pavement, and he had spent the whole route waiting for the thing to tip and dump pistols into the street. He never carried what bled warm and he never carried live cargo. That had been his line then, stupid and self-serving and very important to him. A kid's line. The kind that let you keep thinking there was a cleaner version of the same work just around the corner.

There usually wasn't.

He passed a corner where there had once been a card room above a salvage shop. The salvage shop was still there, smaller and meaner. The card room had been turned into family housing; tiny shirts hung behind the safety mesh on a second-floor balcony that used to be full of men smoking over bad hands. A little girl in a school sweater stood on a stool behind the mesh watering a plant that had no business surviving Mars air even under a dome. She frowned at the plant with the grave concentration of someone taking orders from an unseen adult. Jet found himself slowing to watch her, then moved on before the habit could turn into sentiment.

He had spent years telling himself there had been stages. First the kid with the alias and the cargo scams. Then the cop. Then everything after. He liked the shape of that story because it gave him lines to point at. Here, this ended. Here, this started. Here, I got better. Here, I lost the right to say that. It had the clean feel of something filed properly.

Walking those streets, he could feel the lie in it through the soles of his boots.

The cop version of Jet had used everything Hosuke learned. Every damned thing. He had known which foreman lied with his shoulders and which one lied by getting too offended. He had known how stolen goods moved when nobody wanted them seen. He had known which teenagers on bikes were lookouts and which were just killing time because he had been the first kind and pretended to be the second. ISSP had called that street knowledge, practical experience, instincts. They had put a badge over it and called the whole package an asset.

He turned onto Mercer Row without thinking about it and then stopped because Mercer Row shouldn't have existed anymore under that name. The old sign had been stripped, but the bend in the street was the same, a narrow dogleg around a building that had once leaned so hard everybody swore it would come down in the first dust storm and then never did. Jet looked at the front of that building now and had to stand there a second to make his eyes accept it.

Blue plastic paneling.

Big square windows.

Warm white light.

A laundromat.

Mars had taken his old stash hole and filled it with washers. That figured.

The place had been a dead freight office when he knew it, half-abandoned except for the back room and the upstairs closet where hot cargo could sit a night or two while whoever owned it got their story straight. He had slept in that closet once, coat rolled under his head, one hand on a crate of stolen processors because the man paying him said if the goods walked the debt was his. He remembered the smell of mildew and old insulation. He remembered rats in the wall. He remembered thinking he was doing what adults did.

Now the front window was full of washers turning white sheets in calm perfect circles.

He let out a breath that almost became a laugh and didn't. The wind cut around the corner and shoved dust at his shins. After a second he opened the door and went in.

Warm wet air hit him first. Soap. Hot metal. Damp cotton. The dryers along the far wall thumped in staggered rhythm, a rough backbeat under the softer churn of the washers. A machine buzzed to signal the end of a cycle and nobody rushed it. Jet stood just inside the door longer than he needed to, letting the warmth work into his coat.

A woman at the folding counter glanced up from a mystery novel laid spine-broken beside a basket of towels. She looked around sixty, maybe older, with one bright green hair roller still clipped over her bangs like she'd forgotten it halfway through getting ready and decided life could deal with it. Her slippers had rabbit faces on the toes, long ago walked flat. She took one look at Jet's empty hands and said, "If you're waiting on somebody, the blue chair by the change machine's cracked."

"Not waiting," Jet said.

She shrugged like that wasn't her problem either way and went back to her book.

He moved farther in. The bones of the place were still there if you knew where to look. The support column near the middle of the room. The shallow rise in the floor where old conduit ran under cheap tile. The back wall, set a little off square because the original build had been done fast and bad. They had painted over everything, skinned it, scrubbed it, lined it with white machines and plastic bins. Still the space pressed the same way. Still narrow where it mattered. Still made a big man feel one size too large for it.

Jet sat in a molded chair by the window. Not the blue one. The chair flexed under his weight and gave back just enough to be annoying. Across from him a father in work coveralls folded children's clothes with surprising delicacy, smoothing each tiny shirt flat before stacking it. His left thumbnail was painted silver, sloppily, the way kids did it when they were practicing on a patient adult. He caught Jet looking, grinned once without embarrassment, and kept folding. Somewhere in the back, behind the service door, a radio played old jazz through static bad enough to turn brass into sand.

Jet leaned forward with his elbows on his knees and looked at the back wall.

The closet had been upstairs. Gone now, probably knocked through when they renovated. The stash cavity had been behind a false panel near what was now the detergent vending rack. He could still place it. Four feet up from the floor, six inches left of the column. He had hidden crates there, envelopes there, once a pistol wrapped in shop rags for a man who came back for it two days later smelling like blood and antiseptic. He had never asked what that had been about. Not because he was brave. Because he wanted to get paid and go.

Daichi Kozuki.

The name sat in his head with no hooks in it.

If Jet had known him then, he couldn't find the memory. There were men from that time he still remembered by the shape of their boots or the stink of their cologne, but the name did not ring against anything. That bothered him more than it should have. A dead stranger's codes had reached into ISSP and dragged Hosuke up by the collar. A man Jet had never heard of had more access to that old life than anyone on the Bebop did.

The woman at the folding counter turned a page. The dryers kept their beat. Outside the window people moved through the cold with their shoulders up, plastic bags bumping their legs, breath faint in the dome air.

He thought about the vetting interview again, and about the thing he had never put in it. Not the alias. Not the freight jobs. Those he'd admitted in softer words. He had left out winter on Mercer Row, third year in ISSP, when he caught a dock runner named Nilo with two unlicensed filters and a knife too cheap to open smooth. Jet walked him into the freight-office stairwell, shut the fire door, and told him possession could become conspiracy if the report was written the right way. Then he told him he knew the family was already two notices behind on rent and that inspectors liked making examples out of women who worked night shift and missed forms. Nilo bit through his lip hard enough to bleed on his own cuff. Before the heat pipe quit banging, he gave up the back room over the salvage shop.

The raid pulled three handguns, a ledger, and one man slow enough to still be upstairs when the door came off. Jet wrote `COOPERATIVE SOURCE` on the paperwork and watched the language sand the edges off what had happened in the stairwell. He took the clearance rate, the nod from his lieutenant, the groceries it paid for. He never went back to tell Nilo what happened to the room after.

If Daichi Kozuki meant anything, Jet wasn't going to find it by handing the name to Ed and lighting up the net for whoever had laid the trail. Men from that life stuck to places. To corners, welded doors, back stairs, bars that changed owners and never smell. Daylight first. Questions later.

The father with the silver thumbnail finished his folding, called toward the machines, "Mina, your socks," and a girl of about five trotted over in blinking light-up shoes that flashed blue with every step. She held up one damp red sock like a flag of surrender. The father took it, laughed under his breath, and tucked it into the stack. Simple. Ordinary. The kind of moment Jet used to think happened in another country entirely.

The blue chair by the change machine gave a small protesting crack as somebody ignored the warning and sat in it. The sound pulled a short bark of laughter out of the woman with the hair roller. She looked over the top of her book at Jet as if to say she'd told them. Jet almost smiled back.

He stayed through one full washer cycle. When the end buzzer went off, no revelation came with it, just the flat electronic chirp and the father hauling his clean clothes toward the door. That was enough loitering for one day.

When he finally stood, his knees complained. He rolled one shoulder, then the other, and went to the door.

"You leaving already?" the woman at the counter asked, marking her place in the book with a grocery receipt.

"Yeah."

"Mm. You sat here a whole cycle without putting anything in." She looked at him over the book, not unfriendly. "I thought maybe you were waiting on a dryer."

"Just thinking."

"That'll do it." She reopened the book. "Don't use the blue chair if you come back."

Outside, the cold hit harder for the warmth he'd just left. Jet stood a second on the sidewalk with the laundromat light behind him and the district in front of him, old street under new signs, and let the name turn over once more.

Daichi Kozuki. Still nothing he could hold.

He turned toward the paid dock and started back, already sorting the old Mars map in his head by what daylight might still shake loose.

***

By the time he reached the Bebop, the settlement lamps around the lot had come on. The paid dock always felt temporary, no matter how many times they used it. Too exposed. Too narrow. No Mama Yun in the back door yelling about broth. No smell of stock and peppers drifting out into the cold. Just a fenced strip of concrete, a grudging electrical hook-up, and a kiosk that printed fee receipts like insults.

Jet had stopped at a market on the way and bought what they could afford without hating themselves more than usual: a head of cabbage, two onions, a bag of noodles, and a packet of protein scraps honest enough to call themselves scraps on the label. He climbed the ramp with the paper sack tucked under one arm. The ship answered him the way it always did: a hollow clank through the gangway, a faint shift in balance, life support humming under everything.

The common room lights were on low. Ed was upside down on the couch with a terminal braced against bony knees, hair hanging toward the floor, Ein curled in the angle of the cushions like a loaf of bread with ears. Somebody had tied a strip of red repair tape around one of Ed's big toes for no reason Jet could see. Faye sat at the table in one of Jet's old thermal shirts under her yellow jacket, using the edge of a playing card to scrape black nail polish off one thumbnail. Spike leaned against the galley hatch with a cigarette tucked behind one ear and a bowl in his hand he had apparently been too lazy to return to the sink.

Everybody looked up.

Faye was first. Of course she was. "Nice of you to come home before we had to sell the ship."

Jet set the grocery sack on the counter. "You'd get a bad price."

"Probably. It's filthy."

Ed flopped the terminal closed with both feet and righted in one loose movement. "Jet-person brings cabbage. Cabbage means either dinner or emotional avoidance."

"Could be both," Spike said.

His tone was flat enough to count as neutral with anyone else. With Spike it meant he had noticed the dust on Jet's coat, the set of his shoulders, the fact that he had been gone all day and come back with groceries because ordinary actions covered irregular ones better than explanations did.

Jet unpacked the sack and spread the groceries under the counter light: cabbage, onions, noodles, and the packet of protein scraps he'd talked himself into buying on the way back. Even the vegetables looked worn out. "Anybody eat yet?"

"No," Faye said. "I was waiting for our fearless captain to tell us whether he found out why his face is on a bounty under some sad little made-up name."

Jet reached for the knife. "Then you're still waiting."

Faye snorted. "Oh, that's useful."

Ed swung off the couch and landed barefoot on the deck plates. "Was it a clerical ghost? Ed likes clerical ghosts. They have such good secret hallways."

"It was ISSP," Jet said. He split the cabbage, flattened one half, and started cutting. The knife hit the board in steady dull knocks. "Somebody got into sealed background material and hung a bounty shell on it with dead credentials."

That got the room a little quieter.

Spike shifted his weight off one shoulder. "Dead whose?"

Jet kept his eyes on the cabbage. "Old source out of Mars."

Faye stared at the back of his head. He could feel it. He kept cutting.

Ed leaned both hands on the counter and craned to watch the knife work. "Can Ed see the credential trail? Ed can make friends with dead things in the database."

"No."

"Why not?"

"Because I said no."

Ed rocked back, considering that. "Strong legal argument."

Faye tossed the scraped nail polish flake toward the trash and missed by a foot. "If somebody in ISSP is messing with you, that's crew business, whether you like it or not."

Jet chopped onion now, harder than he needed to. The smell opened sharp in the room. "Right now it's a sealed file somebody cracked."

"Which is still crew business."

Jet turned, knife still in hand but pointed at the board. "And that's why nobody goes near Mars records from this ship tonight. No public terminals, no source files, no asking around the dock. Whoever did this knew their way around internal records better than I do from the outside. So we eat, we sleep, and tomorrow we decide what gets touched."

There was a beat.

He heard how hard his own voice had gone and hated it, not because it was loud but because it sounded like a man trying too hard not to sound rattled.

Faye heard it too. Her eyes narrowed. One hand went, quick and unconscious, to the notch at her collarbone before she caught it and folded both arms instead. "Wow. Fine. Keep your secrets. Everybody else is having a great time with theirs too."

Jet set the knife down. "I didn't say that."

"No, you just said shut up and eat cabbage."

"That's not what I said."

Spike finally stepped away from the hatch. "Leave it."

Faye looked at him. "Oh, now you care?"

"Not really." He put the bowl in the sink at last. "But he's right about one thing. If ISSP still has live dead-source access floating around, stomping on it tonight is stupid."

Jet looked at him then. Spike's face gave him nothing useful, only that familiar nearly-bored stillness. The way he backed off carried recognition in it, like Spike saw the shape of a man holding something back because he had his own hands full of the same posture.

Ed, who hated static tension mostly because it interrupted whatever else Ed wanted to do, tilted the head sideways and asked, "So dinner first?"

"Yes," Jet said.

"Then Ed votes noodles."

"Ed votes noodles every night."

"Because noodles are good."

Ein barked once in agreement or opportunism.

That broke it enough. Faye rolled her eyes so hard it almost counted as labor and sat back down. "If this dinner has any surprises in it, I'm suing."

"You can't afford a lawyer."

"I can't afford whatever's in that packet either."

"Then chew carefully."

They were back on the old track now, laying cheap jokes over the sharper thing underneath because nobody wanted to pry it up yet. Jet got a pan hot. Oil hissed. Onion hit metal. The smell went sweet in a hurry. Ed climbed back onto the couch, terminal open again, mumbling to Ein about zombie passwords and delicious encryption. Faye resumed murdering her thumbnail with the card edge. Spike drifted to the porthole and stood there looking out toward the settlement lights, one hand in his pocket, the cigarette still unlit behind his ear.

Jet dumped in the cabbage, then the protein scraps. They browned at the edges if you were generous with the term. The knife work steadied him. So did the ordinary sting of cheap onion in the eyes, the heat off the pan, the hard little problem of making dinner from not enough. He could do that. He had always been able to do that.

"So," Faye said after a while, less sharp now, almost casual. "This Hosuke guy. Somebody you knew?"

Jet scraped the pan. "Old dirt."

Faye opened her mouth, then shut it. Maybe she was tired. Maybe her own day had left bruises in places she didn't want pushed. She flicked the playing card across the table instead and watched it spin to a stop.

Spike didn't turn from the porthole when he spoke. "You want me to ask around?"

The offer was quiet enough that Faye and Ed both missed what sat under it. Jet didn't. Spike never volunteered curiosity without a reason.

"No," Jet said.

"All right."

That was all Spike gave him: a flat all right and the space after it.

Jet had spent enough years reading reluctant statements to know when one carried its own omissions. Spike let it drop with professional courtesy, the kind men used when they were standing on different sides of the same fog bank.

The noodles went in. Steam climbed up and fogged the cabinet for a second. Jet stirred, tasted, added pepper, decided salt would only remind them how cheap the protein was. Behind him Ed laughed at something on-screen. Faye asked if there was any beer. Jet said no. She asked if there was hidden beer. Jet said especially no. Ein hopped down and stationed himself beside the stove with the patient moral certainty of a dog who had never cooked once in his life and still believed the meal depended on him.

When Jet set the bowls out, no one said thank you. They almost never did. Faye blew across hers and burned her tongue anyway. Ed slurped with total concentration. Spike ate leaning on the counter, looking like a man too tired to sit down all the way.

From the corridor it might have passed for normal: four people with bowls in their hands, making cheap remarks over dinner because that was easier than saying what had them by the throat.

Up close the whole room was off.

Faye kept losing the room for half a second at a time, eyes going somewhere else and coming back meaner. Spike's attention kept hanging up on the dark outside the porthole. Ed was halfway through the bowl when the terminal on the couch chimed and bloomed blue against the cushions. A Mars cache handshake spun up in one corner of the screen, quick as a reflex.

Jet crossed the room before he'd decided to move. He planted one wet hand on the lid and snapped the terminal shut. Plastic cracked loud in the common room. Broth slopped over Faye's fingers. Ein stood up so fast his bowl skidded.

"Jet," Faye said.

"I said not tonight."

Ed stared at the closed terminal, then up at him. The kid's face went abruptly still. "I hadn't opened Mars yet."

Jet kept his hand on the lid a second too long. "I know. Leave it closed."

Nobody picked a bowl back up right away. Spike watched Jet over the rim of his own, unreadable. Faye wiped broth off her knuckles onto a napkin and didn't make the joke sitting right there for her. Ed slid the terminal under the couch without another word. That silence sat in the room heavier than all the noise had.

Faye set her empty bowl aside first. "This would've been better with mushrooms."

"Everything's better with mushrooms."

"That's the smartest thing anybody's said for five minutes."

Jet gathered bowls for the sink. Water knocked in the pipes when he turned the tap. No one reopened the terminal. Behind him, Ed scratched one finger behind Ein's ear and said nothing at all.

Spike finally lit the cigarette and cracked the vent. Cold air threaded in around the smoke, carrying dock-lot dust and the faint metallic smell of evening. "Tomorrow then," he said.

Jet looked over his shoulder once. Spike was watching the smoke get pulled toward the vent, face unreadable. Faye had her chin in her hand again. Ed was still crouched by the couch, not hacking, not talking, one palm on Ein's back.

The Bebop ticked in the cold, and nobody went near Mars that night.


# Chapter 4

They hadn't gone near Mars that night. Daylight was a different bargain.

Spike started with the back stair behind the Chou arcade. It was gone, replaced by a freight elevator painted with black-and-yellow hazard stripes. Mars had a way of paving over old sins with useful things. The vent cavity behind the pawn broker on Ninth had been filled with pink sealant that bulged through the grate like cheap sausage. The room above the machine-parts wholesaler, where Spike had once slept through an afternoon with a pistol under his hand and dried blood cracking at his ankle, had become a tutoring center. Paper planets hung in the window. One had peeled loose and stuck to the glass from static, Saturn turned on its side over Tharsis.

He kept checking.

He checked the old cigarette machine with the false back. The machine was gone and the wall behind it had been tiled over in white squares already going yellow at the grout. He checked the drain trench behind a shuttered bathhouse where message capsules used to sit in the third slot down. The whole block had been torn open for a utility line. Rebar stuck out of a trench full of pale dust and plastic cups. He crossed into the older freight alleys and found one steel door welded shut, another with three fresh locks on it and a remnant gang tag sprayed across the frame in silver loops. It wasn't Red Dragon. It was something newer, with the same cheap paint and the same threat.

The wind came down the loading lanes with red grit in it, hissing over the concrete. By noon the sun had thinned to a dirty coin behind the settlement haze, and the yards were all sound and pressure: forklifts whining in reverse, pallet jacks clattering over seams in the slab, the low thump of cargo pods settling into magnetic cradles. A woman in insulated coveralls smoked behind a crate stack and read a paperback with the cover folded off. A loader drone rolled past Spike's shin, bumped his ankle, corrected, and kept going. Nobody looked at him twice. That was good. It should have stayed good.

The last place on his list sat at the edge of the district where the warehouses gave way to older lockups and bonded storage. The sign over the corridor still read KESSLER FREIGHT ANNEX in half-dead blue tubes. Two letters buzzed and failed, came back, failed again. He had used the locker under a logistics shell company that no longer existed and a routing number nobody had said out loud in years. It should have been dead with the rest of them.

The hall inside smelled like cold dust, old coolant, and the bleach the Annex used when it wanted to suggest cleanliness without paying for repairs. The floor was pitted concrete. The ceiling strips flickered in alternating rows, so that every few steps the corridor changed color on him. His locker waited at the far end behind a roll gate and a scarred biometric pad with a cracked corner.

He stood there for a second longer than he needed to.

Memory had nothing to do with it. Memory was cheap. Mars sold it by the block. He paused because the thing in front of him was supposed to be impossible. A ship had parked behind a noodle shop, sent the wrong handshake into the air, and now an old storage box was still sitting here ready to know his hand.

He set his palm on the reader.

The pad warmed, clicked, and held him there with a soft mechanical hum while it compared him to a version of himself that should have been dead. Then the lock thunked open.

Spike pushed the gate up halfway and ducked under. The air inside was colder. There were no windows and no circulation beyond the tired vent rattling somewhere behind the wall. Shelves lined both sides, metal flaking to orange at the bolts. The contents were what he remembered because he had arranged them not to matter: routing slips in sealed sleeves, old frequency crystals, two stripped-down comm relays, a packet of counterfeit transit seals gone stiff with age, a canvas tool roll, three paper cartons of manifests bound with wire.

There was nothing dramatic in it, and nothing worth nostalgia.

What bothered him was how undramatic the disturbance was.

Dust lay over the shelves in a thin even skin except where one carton had been opened and retied. The wire wasn't twisted the way he'd left it. The papers inside had been thumbed and settled again with care. Whoever had come in here had not tossed anything, had not stolen anything obvious, had not left the mess of a nervous searcher. They had known what they wanted before the door finished opening.

Spike crouched and pulled the carton free.

The moved manifest sat on top instead of buried halfway down where it belonged. He knew that because he had filed them by route weight and rotation, and this one came from the Tharsis-Hellas run in a quarter when the shipments had gone out under agricultural hardware, then mining parts, then whatever other lie fit the customs window. He slid the paper out of its sleeve.

The page had gone soft at the folds. The cargo line items were still crisp enough to read: ceramic barrel sleeves, receiver housings, spring packs, and three insulated cases under a medical rider appended at the last port call.

The rider was what had been marked. Somebody had circled the case numbers in dark pencil and written in the margin, in a hand neat enough to make the letters look calm:

She needed this to live.

Spike read it again because there was no point in pretending he had misread it the first time.

The attached rider carried pharmacode strings and a clinic destination he didn't recognize, out near the southern rim where Tharsis freight started thinning into Hellas feeder roads. The notes field named immune-suppression maintenance, post-transplant, and temperature sensitive handling. They were anti-rejection drugs, the kind of cargo that traveled in padded cases with extra seals and got unloaded fast because if the cold chain broke the shipment turned to expensive trash. He could remember signing off on the rider now that it was in front of him. He didn't remember the place or the cases. He remembered only the irritation of somebody jamming medical freight onto a weapons line at the last minute because the outbound window was closing and the books had to balance.

Back then it had all been weight, timing, bribes, route safety, who could be trusted not to skim, and who had to be overpaid because they were stupid enough to need it. Cargo stayed abstract until it left his ledger.

She needed this to live.

The note made the whole page smaller and meaner. It read now like a hand around somebody's throat, opening and closing according to schedule.

He checked the rest of the carton. No other notes. No names. No message addressed to him except the one that wasn't addressed at all and had still found him exactly.

Spike put the manifest aside and turned to the lock assembly on the inside wall. The biometric unit fed into an older service housing with a removable plate. Two screws showed fresh scrape marks. The marks were slight, but they were enough. Somebody had pulled the plate, bridged the maintenance leads, and let the system believe it was being serviced. Old syndicate infrastructure loved maintenance overrides. It had never trusted the same men twice if one wire could replace them.

He should have expected that. He hated that he hadn't.

On the shelf above the manifests sat one of the comm relays, a black brick no bigger than a lunchbox. He took it down, thumbed the latch, and waited while the dead screen thought about whether to betray him. The relay woke on the third try with a phosphor glow that made the locker look even more tired.

Packet traffic populated line by line.

Automated route checks came first, then invalid node requests. A broker query stamped from a carrier front in Noctis came next, then another from a name he didn't know, then another. Most of them bounced, but not all. The old band had listeners on it. There weren't many, but there were enough.

At the bottom sat the one that mattered: AUTH SIGNAL CONFIRMED / LIN SHAO-FENG NODE RESPONSE REQUESTED.

It was timestamped yesterday.

Spike stared at the screen until the letters stopped looking like letters and turned back into trouble.

There it was: somebody had rung the bell and a few scavengers had lifted their heads.

He killed the relay, pulled the battery, and set the battery on the concrete. The pistol came into his hand without a decision. One shot in the cramped locker cracked so hard the vent rattled and dust dropped from the ceiling. Battery acid leaked black and sharp across the floor.

He stood still and listened.

The corridor kept being a corridor. The fluorescents hummed. A cart wheel squeaked somewhere far off. No boots ran his way. The Annex had probably written gunfire into the overhead as acceptable loss years ago.

Spike wiped the relay down with the inside of his sleeve, took the manifest, and reshelved the carton as close to its old angle as he could get it. It didn't matter. Whoever had wanted him to find the paper had already done the work. Still, habit was habit. He lowered the roll gate, waited through the lock cycle, and walked back down the hall with the folded manifest inside his jacket, the paper stiff against his ribs.

Outside, the freight lanes looked even more ordinary than before. A man with a lunch bucket argued with a dock clerk over unloading priority. Somebody laughed from the back of a flatbed. The smoking woman had turned a page in her paperback and switched the cigarette to her other hand without looking up. A ship lifted from a nearby pad and washed the alley in heat and grit, hot enough to sting through Spike's shirt.

He turned his face away from the exhaust and thought about a woman he had never met waiting on a shipment routed between rifle parts.

Maybe she got it in time. Maybe she didn't. Maybe the note meant the shipment had arrived and that had been the good part, the only good part. Maybe it meant the opposite. The sentence didn't say. That was another thing about it. Whoever had left the paper knew exactly how much to tell him. Just enough to drag a body into a column of numbers and leave it there.

He took the long way back to the paid dock. Twice he changed streets because the old reflex was awake now, the one that counted reflections and doorways and men leaning too long in one place. He passed a storefront screen tuned to bounty listings and saw a dozen faces scroll by in blue police light. None of them were his yet.

By the time the Bebop came into view above the adjoining roofs, the wind had turned colder. The ship sat where Jet had been forced to pay for it, ugly and familiar and home in the way Spike never let himself phrase even in his own head. Somebody had hung laundry on a neighboring line between two service poles. A small shirt snapped in the dust-laden air like it was trying to get free.

Spike climbed the gangway with the manifest in his pocket and the old dead name walking half a step behind him.

* * *

The Bebop smelled like fried peppers, engine grease, wet dog, and the detergent Jet bought in bulk because it was cheaper even though it never really got the socks clean. Hull metal ticked under the afternoon cold. From somewhere down the spine of the ship came the hollow bang of a panel being put back where it belonged by force rather than persuasion.

Jet was at the galley table with the drip manifold from the coffee maker laid out in pieces on a towel. His cybernetic hand held the housing steady while his right teased mineral scale out of the valve with a pick. He looked up once when Spike came in, then back to the part.

"You been out all day," he said.

"Looks that way."

"That answer's a waste of my time."

"Then don't ask."

Jet snorted once through his nose. It wasn't amusement, just air.

Faye was stretched along the couch with one boot on, one off, scraping old polish from her thumbnail with the corner of a playing card. The card back was bent soft from use. She did not look up either, which meant she was listening hard enough to hear fabric move.

"He went for a walk," she said. "Maybe he found enlightenment. Maybe he found a hot dog."

"On Mars?" Jet said.

"Fine. A terrible hot dog."

Ed had colonized the main console. Bare feet tucked under one leg, goggles on forehead, a nest of wires around the terminal like colorful noodles. Ein lay under the chair with his chin on his paws and one ear turned toward the room. The monitor glow painted Ed's face green and gold by turns as different maps rotated through.

Spike moved past them toward the sink. His jacket felt too heavy for a sheet of paper. He opened the tap, drank water that tasted faintly of metal, and let the cold sit on his tongue.

He could feel Jet deciding whether to ask another question and deciding against it. That almost bothered him more than if Jet had pushed.

The silence stretched into one of the ship's ordinary silences, full of noises that only counted as silence because everybody knew them. Life support murmured. A fan with a bad bearing whined. The tap dripped because Jet still had the coffee maker in pieces and had used the wrong wrench on the faucet last week. Faye flicked dry polish into an ashtray. Ed hummed something tuneless to the rhythm of typing.

Then Ed stopped humming.

"Sneaky ship lifted its feet," Ed said.

Jet's pick clicked against the manifold. "What?"

Ed's hands were already moving. A transit grid spun onto the main screen, then a topographic map of Mars in dirty reds and amber contour lines. A bright point blinked over Tharsis, left the settlement, and drew a line south.

"There. There there there." Ed jabbed the screen with one finger and grinned without looking away from it. "No more noodle-house nap. It's going zoom-small, not zoom-fast. Surface skim. Ooo, low. Very low. Show-off low."

Faye sat up with her card still in hand. "How low?"

"Low enough to kiss the dust and maybe get a mouth full of rocks if the pilot sneezes." Ed tilted the map, expanded the track. "See? Not orbit. Not even pretending orbit. Just scooting. Scoot scoot. South."

Jet pushed back from the table. The coffee maker part rolled, hit the lip of the towel, stopped. "Toward where?"

Ed flattened the route with a flourish. "Big bowl. Hellas basin. Maybe rim settlements first, maybe not. Ship's taking the nice long slide down."

That got Spike's attention in a different way than the line on the screen should have. Hellas sat south and empty in the way only Mars could manage, huge and raw and scored with the kind of terrain small operators loved. Old waystation country. Old supply roads. The same belt a lot of Tharsis freight had used when it wanted weather and distance doing part of the work.

He set the cup down.

Faye looked from the screen to Spike and back again. "You know that face you make when you pretend you're not making a face?"

"No."

"You should. It's annoying."

Jet ignored both of them. He came around the table wiping his hands on the towel, leaving pale crescents of limescale dust across the fabric. "Can you hold the signal?"

Ed made an offended sound. "Ed can hold a signal, hold a sandwich, hold many things. Sneaky ship is practically helping. Ping ping ping. Very polite."

"Polite," Faye said.

"Mm-hmm. If sneaky ship wanted to hide, sneaky ship would stop blinking and tuck under traffic. But it's not doing that. It's doing this." Ed waggled her fingers over the southbound line. "Come see me dance."

Ein gave one soft bark without lifting his head.

Spike took a step toward the screen. The track was too clean. It wasn't straight. It was smarter than straight. It curved around enforcement corridors and skimmed commercial lanes just enough to blend if somebody took a lazy look. Whoever flew that ship knew Mars traffic and knew how visible they wanted to be.

The line on Ed's screen wasn't exact, but it ran close enough to the corridor stamped on the folded manifest in his pocket to make the paper feel heavier.
The wrong band was still open. The ship wasn't just going south. It was going where the paper pointed.

That was worse than panic. Panic ran hot. This had been planned.

Jet folded his arms. "Fuel says we don't go tearing across half the planet because Ed thinks a transponder has good manners."

"Fuel says a lot of things," Faye said. "Mostly 'you're broke.' We know."

"That's useful. Thanks."

She tossed the playing card onto the table. "You're welcome."

Ed was still zooming the map in and out, following branching routes that weren't there yet but might be. "Sneaky ship changed altitude twice after leaving Tharsis. Little test wiggles. Maybe checking if anyone is staring. Ed is staring. Hello."

Spike heard himself ask, "Any stops?"

Ed glanced up at him. "Not yet. Why, Spike-person? You have a vacation cottage in Hellas?"

"No."

"Shame. It sounds windy."

Jet's eyes flicked to Spike anyway, brief and measuring. Yesterday Jet had come back from wherever he had gone with the look of a man carrying a brick under his coat. Today Spike knew he was wearing the same outline himself.

Nobody said that part.

Faye stood and crossed to the console barefoot on one side, boot heel striking the deck on the other. "Pull the last six hours."

Ed obeyed without comment. The track expanded behind the ship's current position, a slow unspooling away from Tharsis. It was long enough to say the pilot had chosen the timing, and late enough to say she had waited for something.

For him to check the locker? For the note to be found? Maybe that was giving the whole thing too much shape. Maybe it wasn't enough.

Jet was watching the screen and not the crew, which usually meant he was thinking harder than he wanted anybody to know. "If it keeps going south, we lose it in the basin clutter."

"If?" Faye said.

"If." He looked at her now. "We're not taking a pleasure trip because somebody likes riddles."

She laughed once, flat and short. "You think this is a riddle?"

Spike could feel the room starting to tighten, the way it did before a fight that hadn't admitted it was a fight. The ship's hum seemed louder. Even the loose vent over the stove had shut up to listen.

Ed, oblivious or pretending to be, put two fingers on the screen like a conductor readying an orchestra. "Bebop could go whoooosh."

Jet pointed at the map. "Bebop could also burn fuel we don't have, landing fees we don't have, and food money we don't have."

"We don't have a lot of things," Faye said. "That's not new."

Spike slipped the folded manifest deeper into his pocket and tasted battery acid memory at the back of his throat.

Hellas waited to the south.

If the ship reached the old lanes and kept singing on that band, whatever was left of the network would not stay theoretical for long.

Jet straightened. "All right. Bridge. Now."

Nobody argued with the order because they were already moving.

* * *

The bridge lights came up in tired strips as Jet dropped into the pilot's chair and keyed the systems awake. Outside the forward glass the paid dock's service mast leaned at an angle that looked unsafe but had probably held for twenty years. A loader in orange coveralls crossed the yard below with a crate balanced on his shoulder and never looked up. The city went on making a living while the Bebop decided whether to spend what little it had left.

Spike stood behind Jet's right shoulder with one hand on the back of the copilot seat. He kept his weight easy, casual if you didn't know him. His stillness said otherwise. Faye took the side console and tucked her bare foot under her opposite knee while she pulled the other boot on all the way with short irritated jerks. Ed swiveled between stations because sitting in one place seemed beneath Ed's dignity. Ein trotted in, circled once under the nav bench, and settled where everybody would have to step around him.

Jet brought up the fuel numbers and cursed under his breath.

"Say it louder," Faye said. "Maybe the tanks will get embarrassed and fill themselves."

"We're short for a basin run and back if we have to do any chasing once we're there."

"Then don't miss."

"That's your whole contribution?"

"No, I can be worse."

Jet jabbed a finger at the screen. "Dock fees, fuel, filters clogged with half of Tharsis because we had to park in that dump, and now this. I want one reason to go that isn't curiosity."

Spike said, "You already have one."

Jet did not turn around. "Make it concrete."

There it was.

Spike looked at the southbound track on the secondary screen instead of at Jet. That was easier. "That ship's broadcasting on an old syndicate freight band. I checked one of the old storage boxes. Somebody had been in it before me and woke a relay. The band answered."

That got a full turn from him. "What box?"

"One of mine. From before."

Faye gave a dry little laugh. "That's nice and vague. Very in character."

Spike ignored her because if he looked at her he might say too much or the wrong thing. "It's clear enough. The traffic's live. If she keeps singing on it through Hellas, more people are going to hear her."

Jet pushed back from the controls hard enough that the chair motor whined. "And you were going to mention this when?"

"I just did."

"After you spent all day off-ship. After you came back and stood in my galley drinking water while the rest of us sat here with our thumbs up our asses."

"Yeah."

Faye looked from one to the other and shook her head once, disgust mixed with recognition. "Oh, good. This again."

Jet was already on his feet. Under stress he got bigger, louder, more solid in the room, and the bridge was not built for him to stand up angry in it. "No. Not 'this again.' I'm done with people on this ship telling me just enough to make the next bad decision and keeping the rest in a drawer."

Spike felt the folded paper press against him when he breathed. "You asked for a reason. I gave you one."

"You gave me the edge of one."

"It's enough to move."

"For you."

Ed had gone very still in the middle of swiveling. That was more noticeable than if Ed had screamed. Ein lifted his head.

Faye leaned back in her seat and crossed her arms. "He's not wrong that the signal matters."

Jet shot her a look. "That's surprising."

"Don't flatter yourself. I'm not taking his side. I'm taking my own."

"Which is?"

"Which is I'm not—" She stopped. The words caught. When she started again, her voice had gone fast and thin around the edges. "That woman at Mama Yun's. She knew my face. Said she'd seen me on Ganymede, years I wasn't there. Same name. Same—"

She looked at the front glass instead of at either of them.

"I was ice," she said. The line came out lower than the rest. Her mouth tightened around it before she kept going. "So I don't know what that is. But I'm not sitting here while you two take turns not telling each other things."

Jet's mouth tightened. "You could've said that yesterday."

"So could you."

Jet let that sit. His jaw moved once. Then his eyes came back to Spike. "And you want to go because old syndicate traffic makes your skin crawl."

"I want it dead again before more people answer."

"That's not all of it."

"No."

"Also it's a chase," Ed said. Nobody had asked.

Jet looked at her. "Ed—"

"Because sneaky ship parked in the good noodle place and then booped all the old human buttons and now it's going south-south-south like a little arrow with manners. If somebody does all that and then leaves breadcrumbs, Ed wants to see the loaf."

Faye blinked. "The loaf."

"Yes. The loaf."

"That's not a reason. That's a breakfast metaphor."

Ed considered this. "Ed likes breakfast too."

Against his own will, Spike felt the corner of his mouth move. It died fast.

Jet rubbed at his beard with the heel of his hand. "You all hear yourselves, right? One live band, one impossible waitress story, one kid who wants a chase, and I'm supposed to pour our last fuel into Hellas because that sounds sensible."

"No," Spike said. "You're supposed to do it because you have your own reason."

Jet looked at him flatly. "Don't tell me what my reasons are."

"Fine. Then say them."

The room changed shape again. Pressure pulled inward until even the bridge felt smaller.

Jet's gaze dropped to the deck, then to the fuel screen, then to the southbound line. "Because we lose the trail if we sit here."

"That's logistics," Spike said. "Not a reason."

Jet's jaw worked once. When he spoke again it came rougher, stripped down. "Somebody used a dead informant's code path to—" He stopped. "There's a bounty on me that shouldn't exist. It came through dead channels. That doesn't happen by itself."

Faye's head turned.

That was not the whole answer. Spike could hear it because he was carrying one of his own. Faye heard it too. Her expression said so, sharp and ugly and not surprised.

"Right," she said. "Great. So we're all doing that."

No one contradicted her.

Outside, a cargo hauler lifted from the neighboring pad and rattled the bridge glass. Red dust blew across the yard in a wide sheet, blurred, settled. Through it Spike could just make out the roofline beyond the warehouses and, past that, the block where Mama Yun's sat. Their usual spot behind the shop would be empty now. The one dependable place on Mars, empty because a stranger had chosen to make empty part of the message. Spike couldn't stop seeing the gap as bait. Whoever was ahead of them knew what counted.

Jet dropped back into the chair and keyed the engine preheat without announcing a decision. Fans spun up deep in the hull. The floor took on the faint vibration of waking machinery.

The hum came up through Spike's shoes. Once Jet warmed the engines, the argument was over in the only way that mattered. They were going.

Faye heard it and smiled without humor. "There's that."

"This isn't agreement," Jet said.

"Then what is it?"

"This is me refusing to lose the trail while the three of you circle yourselves."

"Touching," Faye said.

"Put your boot on."

"It's on."

He glanced at her feet. "The other one."

She looked, swore, and jammed her foot into it. Ed laughed so hard the chair spun halfway around.

The first stage thrusters coughed alive. Somewhere aft, clamps disengaged with a deep metal thud. The Bebop shivered in place.

Movement should have eased him some. It didn't. South had stopped being a line on Ed's screen and turned into the next hour of their lives.

Jet pointed without looking. "Ed, give me the cleanest line south that still lets us duck settlement enforcement."

"Mm-hmm. Sneaky but legal-ish."

"Legal."

Ed sucked air through teeth. "That's a narrower tunnel."

"Find it."

"Found it already."

Jet kept going. "Faye, strap in or don't, but if I've gotta scrape you off the port bulkhead again I'm charging rent."

"Add it to my tab."

Jet saved Spike for last. Of course he did. The others got assignments. Spike got the question behind the question.

"Spike."

"What."

Jet's hands settled on the controls. "If this band is as bad as you're saying, you tell me the second it gets worse."

He would not tell Jet everything. He would not tell him the name, the note in his pocket, or about the shot battery bleeding across the locker floor.

"Yeah," Spike said. "I'll tell you."

It came out close enough to truth to pass if you weren't looking directly at it.

The dockmaster on the local channel started yelling about departure sequence. Jet cut him off with a code string and a promise he did not mean about filing clean. The Bebop rose slow and heavy from the paid berth, casting a wide shadow over the concrete lot and the crooked service mast and the man below still carrying his crate. Dust rolled outward in red sheets. Laundry snapped itself horizontal on the line between the poles.

Tharsis opened under them in layers of roof metal, transit lanes, rust streaks, greenhouse glass gone cloudy at the edges. For a second Spike saw the alley behind Mama Yun's, the empty slot, and steam leaking from the noodle shop vent into the cold. Then the ship banked and the settlement fell away behind them.

South, the land roughened and widened. The map on the bridge screens redrew itself around basin contours and old roads half lost to dust. The blinking point ahead kept moving with patient confidence, not fleeing, not waiting either, and making them come.

Jet set the course.
By the time they reached the basin, the light would be going bad.

Faye strapped in at last, muttering to herself.

Ed hummed over the nav board and fed route updates to the system in little bursts of delight.

Ein braced his paws and sneezed when the cabin pressure shifted.

Spike stood behind them with one hand on the seatback and the folded manifest against his chest and watched the southbound signal burn clean on the screen. There were four people on the Bebop, one ship ahead of them, and four different reasons to follow.

From up here Mars looked empty enough to hide anybody.

That was the problem. It looked that way right before it found room for the past to climb back in.


# Chapter 5

The Hellas basin opened under the Bebop like somebody had hit Mars with a bottle and left the glass where it fell.

Red canyons split the ground in long crooked seams. Shadow sat in them thick and blue-black. On the rims, settlements clung to the rock in little rusted clusters of tanks, sheds, antenna poles, and dock lights that blinked through the dust as if they were surprised to still have power. The sky above all of it had that thin bad color Mars did at the end of the day, peach where the light caught and sick brass where it did not.

Jet hated chasing anything down here in a ship this size.

He sat forward in the pilot's chair with his good hand on the throttle and his metal one wrapped around the yoke, feeling every complaint the Bebop made through the frame. The trawler was built to endure, not to dance. She could take damage and keep flying. She could haul mass. She could drift for days. What she could not do was snake through smugglers' cuts in the southern basin after a custom ship that weighed maybe a quarter of what she did and moved like it had no furniture inside.

On the monitor over Ed's head the target ping slipped between canyon returns, reappeared, slipped again.

Ed had one foot on the console and the other up against the back of the chair, folded wrong in a way that made Jet's shoulder ache just looking at it. Ein stood with his front paws on the edge of the station, ears up, barking every time the ping jumped.

"Ed has the sneaky fish," Ed said. "Sneaky fish is going south-southish and then a little bite bite to the left. No, the other left. Mars left."

"There isn't a Mars left," Jet said.

"There is now."

Spike leaned in the bridge hatch with one shoulder against the frame, hands in his pockets like they were discussing dinner instead of dropping into canyon country with half a tank and no plan anyone had agreed on. He had his flight suit tied at the waist. The sleeves hung behind him.

"She's baiting us lower," he said.

Jet did not look around. "I know that."

"Doesn't sound like it."

"If you know so much, get in your ship."

"That's the first smart thing you've said in ten minutes."

Faye, strapped into the jump seat behind Ed's console, let out a short laugh that had no amusement in it. She had one boot up on the restraint bar and one hand on the zipper of her red jacket, already halfway out of it so she could get to the Redtail faster.

"Please launch him," she said. "If I have to listen to this for the whole chase, I'm opening the air lock."

Jet glanced at the fuel readout and felt his jaw go tight. Every dip and climb in the canyon wall cost them. Every hard correction cost them. The numbers kept stepping down in brisk little increments, cheerful as a cashier. He thought about the paid docking spot on Mars, the last woolongs already gone, Mama Yun's noodles eaten down to broth and memory. He thought about the ticket they could not afford before they had even gotten it. His hand tightened on the yoke until the metal joints in his left fingers clicked.

"We're not launching until we have a vector," he said.

Spike pushed off the hatch. "Then we're not launching."

"Jet-jet-jet," Ed sang. "Vector is a friend word. Vector is a shape word. Vector is also right there." Ed slapped a point on the screen with the flat of a hand. "There!"

The target ship broke from the clutter for half a second, a silver dart against the shadowed wall of the canyon, then cut hard between two stone teeth and vanished again.

Jet swore under his breath and rolled the Bebop after it. The whole ship answered half a beat late, a tired bulk shift that made the deck plates groan. Loose tools rattled somewhere below. Ein skidded, corrected, barked harder.

"That's it," Faye said, already out of the seat. "You can do your careful thing up here. I'm taking the Redtail."

"No."

"You don't get to say no."

"I just did."

She was moving before he finished. Spike was gone too, footsteps already hammering down the corridor toward the hangar. Jet cursed both of them now, out loud.

"Ed, keep the transponder on main screen. Full spread. I want terrain overlays and settlement traffic."

"Settlement traffic is full of boring workers and one singing truck and a medical courier that is in a hurry because of poop," Ed said.

Jet turned his head. "What?"

"Medical courier. Biological waste."

"Just put it up."

The bridge screen bloomed with colored lines and tags. The canyon narrowed ahead, then opened into a wider bowl where old mining access roads scarred the rock and a scatter of commercial signals drifted in and out of registration. The mysterious ship was cutting toward the bowl's western lip.

Jet hit the intercom. "Hangar, respond."

Spike's voice came back with engine noise under it. "Here."

Faye's cut right across his. "Obviously we're here."

"Listen to me for five seconds," Jet said. "Spike, take east wall high and get ahead of her. Faye, you hold west and don't cut low unless I say so. We box her into the bowl and force her up."

"That's too slow," Spike said.

"No, what's too slow is this ship you're trying to make pirouette," Faye said. "If I stay west, she just dumps into the undercut and I'm looking at rock."

"If you two would stop talking and fly-"

"I'm the only one up here flying a brick."

The comm cracked with the metallic thump of the Swordfish launch. On the starboard camera, Spike's mono-racer shot out of the Bebop's underside in a clean blue flare, dropped once, then knifed away along the canyon wall. A second later the Redtail punched free after it, round and red and faster off the line than either of them had a right to be in a situation like this.

Jet felt the old familiar mix of relief and irritation. The ship always got quieter when they left it. It always got more expensive too.

"You have your lanes," he said. "Stay in them."

"Sure," Faye said, in the tone of somebody already doing the opposite.

Ed hunched over the console. On the screen, the three friendly signals spread and the target signal darted ahead like a fish under dark water.

"Sneaky fish likes the zipper place," Ed said.

"The what."

"The zipper place. The canyons zip and unzip. See?" Ed made a fast opening motion with both hands. "There's a seam. If sneaky fish goes seam, Spike-person should go chomp and Faye-person should go whoosh and Bebop should go oom."

Jet looked at the map. There was, under the noise and Ed's nonsense, an actual route. A side cut split off the main canyon, ran blind behind a ridge, then met the bowl from the west. If the target used it and Faye stayed where Jet had put her, Faye might catch the exit.

"Faye, west seam, now."

"I'm already there."

"No, you're not."

"Jet, I can see the seam."

Spike came in flat over comms. "She's not at the seam."

"I said I'm taking the shortcut."

Jet felt the back of his neck go hot. "Don't take the shortcut. Faye, hold your lane."

She was laughing again, breathless this time with thrust and velocity. "You want her or you want a lecture?"

The Redtail's signal slashed off the screen into a lower cut Jet had not assigned. At the same moment the target disappeared into the same mesh of ravines.

"Damn it."

The next thirty seconds became pure noise.

Ed called out numbers, then colors, then numbers again, correcting himself so fast Jet had to choose which version to ignore. Spike said, "She's dropping," and then, "No, that's the wall," and then nothing at all because he was busy. Faye said she had visual, then said no she did not, then swore hard enough that Ein barked in offended sympathy.

Jet shoved the Bebop through the main channel and tried to keep the spread in his head. The canyon walls crowded the windows, red rock close enough that he could see old blast scoring from the mining years, black streaks where heat had licked stone, faded warning paint on one jut of concrete that had been part of something before the something rotted away.

Then a new signal burst onto the traffic band and a prox alarm went shrill.

"Unregistered pursuit craft, check your godsdamn vector!"

The face that popped onto the emergency screen belonged to a woman in a patched orange thermal cap with frost-bitten cheeks and a cigarette filter tucked behind one ear. Little green toy dinosaurs were taped across her dashboard in a line above the instruments. Behind her shoulder, through the ore hauler's dirty side glass, Jet could see thirty tons of raw hematite in a magnetic cradle and the mouth of the low cut Faye had chosen.

"Lady, move," Faye snapped.

"You move. I got weight under me, sweetheart."

Jet caught a flash of the Redtail on his lower camera, too fast, too close, red hull skimming past the blunt nose of the ore hauler with less than a meter between them. The hauler pilot let out a stream of abuse that clipped as her automatic collision system shoved her upward. The Bebop's own board lit with a traffic warning and an enforcement tag.

"Faye!"

"I missed her, didn't I?"

"That isn't the point."

"It sort of is."

The target signal jumped again, clean and infuriating, out of the mess Faye had made and into the wider bowl ahead. Jet shoved the hauler and Faye out of his mind because if he stayed with them the whole chase was gone. He rolled the Bebop hard enough that a storage latch banged open somewhere behind him and a pan clanged across the galley.

"Everyone listen," he said, too loud. "She's headed for the rim station. Spike, get above the fuel tower. Faye, back to west. Ed, if you say zipper again I swear to God-"

"Refueling place! Blinky tank village! Three old fuel bladders and one coffee machine with a broken milk arm!"

"How do you know that?"

"Public maintenance logs. Also the camera sees it."

The canyon spat them into open air. Ahead, welded to the cliff like an afterthought, hung a refueling stop: three spherical tanks banded in peeling white paint, a service platform on crooked stilts, two docking arms, and a web of pipes and maintenance catwalks clinging to the rock. Local craft hovered in approach lanes with their lights on. Wind dragged dust in long red sheets under the platform.

The target ship was arrowing straight for it.

Jet felt the shape of the thing before he named it: crowd, cover, more signals to hide in, more rules to break.

"Spike, now."

"I'm moving."

"Faye, west side."

"Yeah, yeah."

But he could hear it already in all three voices. The chase had gone past plan. The canyon had eaten the neat version. What they had now was appetite and noise and the other pilot still half a step ahead.

Jet drove the Bebop toward the station anyway, because the alternative was giving up and flying home broke.

* * *

By the time Spike cleared the Bebop and dropped fully into the Swordfish's narrow cockpit, the fight had simplified in the way good fights sometimes did. There was the ship in front of him, the walls to either side, and a clock measured in fuel and bad decisions.

Everything else could stay background for a minute.

He strapped in with one hand, thumbed the Swordfish hotter, and let the mono-racer fall into the canyon's throat. The controls answered him cleanly. That helped. Down here the Bebop sounded strained over the comm, all bulk and effort. The Swordfish only sounded alive.

The mysterious ship did not fly like a smuggler in a panic. That was the first thing.

It did not wobble or overcorrect or waste motion on anything showy. It flew like somebody who had been inside these cuts before and knew exactly how much room there was between wing and stone, working in short bursts and tiny corrections, with no nerves visible from the back end of it. If Spike had not known better, he would have said the pilot had laid this route out in advance down to the meter marks.

Maybe she had.

Jet's voice hit his headset again. "East wall. Get above her."

"Working on it."

"If you hit the station approach, don't fire. I don't need Mars traffic pinning attempted manslaughter on top of whatever they already have."

Spike watched the ship ahead skim under an outcropping, disappear behind a conveyor skeleton, then flash back into view in the reflected light off the basin wall.

"That'd imply I was going to miss," he said.

Jet made a sound that might have been a curse or a laugh dragged backward. It was hard to tell with static.

Faye cut in. "If anybody's firing, it's me."

"You almost clipped a hauler."

"I didn't, though."

"Almost got logged."

"So it got logged."

Spike tuned them down a notch and let their argument flatten into noise. It sat better that way. He leaned the Swordfish toward the east wall and took a cut Jet's bigger ship could not use, knifing between two rusted survey pylons that still had faded terraforming company logos on them. The pylons flashed by close enough that the Swordfish's right fin picked up a scraping scream. He barely felt it.

Old infrastructure kept surfacing in the basin if you knew how to look: stub tunnels, dead service roads, freight tags weathered half off rock faces. Before the syndicate routes had become syndicate routes, a lot of this had belonged to companies that thought they were going to turn Mars into a clean profitable miracle and then ran out of money halfway through. The syndicate had just learned how to use ruins better than accountants did.

The thought came and went. He did not let it sit.

Ahead, the target ship cut through the refueling station traffic pattern without asking anyone's permission. A hopper hauling drill heads lurched off course. A maintenance tug flashed hazard lights and spun slow in the wind wash. The station itself looked mean and underpaid, patched plates over old punctures, one docking arm painted a different color from the rest because somebody had replaced it from salvage. Over the service platform hung a sign that had once said FUEL and now mostly said UEL.

A local controller came over the public band, voice raw and furious. "Unknown racers, you are in a restricted commercial lane. Reduce speed or clear the station."

Spike ignored that. So did everybody else.

He came in above the platform, dipped the Swordfish's nose, and for three seconds the angle opened.

The other ship rolled under him so close that radar stopped mattering and all he had was glass and metal.

He saw the cockpit.

A young woman sat inside, dark hair yanked back, no helmet, one sleeve shoved to the elbow. Her hands were bare on the controls, knuckles nicked like somebody who used tools more than guns. She was looking straight through the turn, not at him, not hunting him with her eyes, because she did not need to. She had already done the math. The set of her mouth was wrong for fear. She was not smiling, exactly, but there was a looseness there, something almost amused, as if the whole ugly noisy chase had confirmed a suspicion she had been carrying.

Then she glanced once, quick, and their canopies aligned.

It was not panic or surprise. If anything it looked like recognition, though that made no sense.

And then she was gone past him.

Spike pulled the Swordfish hard enough that the harness bit into his ribs. The mono-racer came around in a screaming arc over the station platform. Beneath him, he caught a flicker of people scattering: a fuel tech in an oversized brown coat dropping a wrench and running crooked because one boot sole had peeled loose; two loaders diving behind stacked canisters; a kid on a service ladder frozen for exactly one stupid second before some adult yanked him down by the back of the jacket. Somebody's coffee exploded across a railing.

He had the target now, not on lock but in sight, and for him that was better.

"I see her," he said.

Jet came back instantly. "You see who?"

"Pilot."

Faye's voice sharpened. "Well?"

The other ship cut between the fuel bladders and the cliff face, too tight for anything but a small craft. Spike followed. The station's pipe maze whipped past his canopy in fast silver flashes. The ship ahead slipped under a service gantry, rolled once, and dropped toward a dark slit in the canyon wall that had not been on the public map.

It was an old maintenance access, narrow and probably bored back when the station serviced mining crawlers instead of whatever scraps kept it alive now.

Spike lined up anyway.

That was when the chaff came.

The other ship kicked something out of its belly, not a missile, not a mine. A cluster of cheap canisters burst in the wake and turned the air into a glittering storm of metal trash: shredded foil, reflective insulation strips, filings that lit under the Swordfish's sensors like fireworks, sliced-up cargo tags, little stamped maintenance tabs that hit the canopy like hard rain. His targeting screen went white, then red, then bloomed with three false returns stacked on top of each other so fast the system started arguing with itself.

"Shit."

The Swordfish shuddered as the debris hit. Static crawled the left side of his display. Something ticked sharp against the intake.

He could still see the tunnel opening ahead, a dark rectangle in the cliff with old hazard paint around the frame and half the letters of MAINTENANCE worn down to ghosts. He drove toward it on instinct and got within a breath of the mouth before scale caught up with him. The tunnel was there, and he was not fitting through it if he wanted to keep the wings.

He yanked back. The Swordfish screamed, nose up, engines biting at dirty air. The cliff wall rushed under him so close he could see old rock bolts sunk into the concrete lining, rust blooming around every one. A line of dead utility lamps whipped past beneath his belly.

The mysterious ship vanished into the dark.

Spike leveled out above the station with his heartbeat somewhere unhelpful and the smell of hot circuitry in the cockpit.

"Spike?" Jet said.

He looked at the ruined display, the tunnel mouth, the empty air where the other ship had been.

"Lost visual," he said.

"You what?"

"Tunnel."

Faye swore. Jet started talking at the same time, louder, but Spike barely heard either of them because his board was still sorting through false returns and one of them had already broken west, fast, deeper into the settlement approach grid.

Maybe it was her.

Maybe it was not.

He locked onto the cleanest echo and went after it because that was still better than hovering in place looking stupid.

* * *

On Ed's screen the chase turned into singing.

It was not real singing, not the kind you heard with ears. It was numbers singing. Traffic pings bounced in one color, station beacons in another, commercial IDs in flat boring little notes, and the sneaky fish signal kept skipping through them all with a bright mean trilling line that Ed liked very much. It had personality. After Spike called lost visual, nobody on the Bebop had eyes on the real hull anymore. They had signal ghosts, public cameras, and whatever the station grid felt like telling them. Then the chaff bloom hit and the song broke into a whole band of bad trumpets.

"Oho," Ed said.

Jet had one hand on the main console and one on the overhead brace now, standing because sitting was not mad enough for him. The Bebop had pulled clear of the station by a few hundred meters and was yawing slow while he tried to keep track of Swordfish, Redtail, local traffic, and the shape of the canyon routes beyond the rim.

"What is it?" he snapped.

"A mess!"

"Useful, Ed."

"The mess has a shape, Jet-person. Ed is looking at the shape."

Ein barked twice at the monitor, front feet dancing.

Faye blew in over the comms. "I've got a signal westbound. Fast. Same tag. No visual. Spike, are you on it?"

Spike answered a beat later. "Different angle."

"Whose angle?"

"Mine."

"That clears it up."

Ed leaned closer, face inches from the screen. The false returns were burning off now, dropping away one by one as the targeting systems sorted trash from metal from moving ships. Under that, something bothered him, not in the way danger bothered him but like a wrong note that insisted it belonged in the song.

One signal kept staying too neat.

The sneaky fish had been wiggly from the start. Real pilots were wiggly. Even very good ones. They overlaid with weather and rock shadow and little engine moods and tiny bursts of impatience. This one now had the right code and the right shape and none of the wrongness of a living hand on a stick. It went exactly where a signal should go if it wanted to be chased.

Ed grinned.

"Fake fish," Ed said.

Jet turned. "What?"

"Not ship fish. Pretend fish. Cargo fish in a wig."

"Ed."

"No, yes, listen-listen." Ed stabbed three spots on the display. "Here is tunnel mouth. Sneaky fish goes in and stops singing. Here, one blink later, fake fish wakes up westbound on a boring freight line with the same code and no elbow. Split happened there. We lost the real ship and kept chasing the beacon. This one is a beacon riding piggyback. Maybe drone. Maybe box truck with a lie taped to it."

There was a pause on the comm long enough for wind static to fill it.

Then Faye said, "You could've opened with that."

"Ed just found it."

Jet hit the channel hard enough that it clicked. "Faye, visual confirm."

No answer came for two seconds. Then three.

When Faye came back, she came back fast, stepping on Jet's next breath.

"It's a freight drone."

Jet shut his eyes once.

On the Redtail feed, which Ed pulled to the side screen without being asked, the drone waddled into view from behind a stack of ore bins. It was an ugly square-bodied commercial mover painted municipal yellow once and dust-color now, with two cargo claws tucked under it and a blinking transponder beacon hose-clamped to its dorsal frame. Somebody had run the wiring by hand. Yellow insulation tape flapped in the slipstream. The beacon was even braced with a strip of cut gasket rubber to keep it from rattling loose.

It was mechanic work, fast and practical.

"Oh, that's nice," Ed said with genuine admiration.

Faye's ship swept past the drone and kicked it sideways in the wash. The little freight unit bobbed drunkenly and kept following its dumb programmed route, signal blazing proud as a lighthouse.

"How long?" Jet asked.

Ed checked automatically and jabbed the log with one finger. "Twenty-one minutes, fourteen seconds since the split. Tunnel-mouth split. Real ship goes dark there, fake fish starts west there, adults start embarrassing themselves there."

Nobody said anything for a moment.

Then Spike, still farther out in the basin, said, "That tracks."

Jet looked like he might put his fist through the console. "No, it doesn't track. What it does is tell me we've been chasing a damn delivery cart through half a settlement while the real ship-"
