The bridge speakers chimed with an incoming priority notification. It was not an emergency. It was worse, bureaucratic.

A red icon unfolded across the main screen: MARS CIVIL TRAFFIC AUTHORITY.

Ed made a delighted noise. "Mail!"

"Don't open it," Faye said.

"Ed is opening it."

The notice filled the screen in legal gray blocks and aggressively polite font.

MCA ENFORCEMENT ACTION: RECKLESS FLIGHT WITHIN COMMERCIAL SETTLEMENT CORRIDOR

ASSESSED PARTIES:
BEBOP REGISTRY MARS-TEMP-447A
SWORDFISH II AUXILIARY CRAFT
REDTAIL AUXILIARY CRAFT

VIOLATIONS INCLUDED UNAUTHORIZED LOW-ALTITUDE VECTORING, HAZARDOUS INTERFERENCE WITH COMMERCIAL HAULER 6H-88, FAILURE TO RESPOND TO TRAFFIC CONTROLLER INSTRUCTION, AND CREATION OF A DEBRIS HAZARD WITHIN FUEL SERVICE AIRSPACE.

TOTAL PENALTY ASSESSED: 8,000 WOOLONGS.

PAYMENT DUE: IMMEDIATE.

Late fees accrue at midnight local settlement time.

Attached below, in much smaller text, was a video still of the Bebop angled stupidly over the station like a guilty building.

Ein barked at it.

"Well," Ed said. "That is very official."

"Delete it," Faye said.

"That's not how fines work," Jet said.

"How would you know? You're not paying it either."

"If we don't pay it, they flag the registry."

"We barely have a registry."

Spike came back on the comm. "Eight thousand."

Jet stared at the notice like he could burn woolongs out of it by force. "Is that what you have to contribute."

"I'm reading it."

"I'm going to kill both of you."

Ed scrolled farther down. "There's a customer feedback survey."

Faye made a sound somewhere between a cough and a laugh. "Send them a drawing."

Jet pointed at the console without looking away from the screen. "Plot a drift orbit. Engines cold as soon as the others dock. We're done burning fuel on this until I can see straight."

"Bebop's done zooming," Ed agreed.

But the grin stayed on Ed's face, because the trick was good. Cheap, mean, funny. Beacon on a freight drone. Chaff in the station lane. Tunnel too tight for the Swordfish. The sneaky fish pilot had put a beard on a robot and all the adults had gone after the beard with guns.

Ed liked adults best when they were competent. Adults were also very good when they were stupid in specific recognizable ways.

This had been the second kind.

* * *

By the time the Swordfish and the Redtail were back in the hangar, the Bebop had gone to cold drift above the basin. The engines were quiet. Without thrust underfoot the ship always felt a little too much like what it was, an old hull in space held together by fixes and habit.

Jet stood by the workbench with the summons printed out on orange thermal paper in his hand because he had not trusted the screen. The strip curled at the bottom like it wanted to roll itself into a cigarette. His thumb had left a dark grease mark over the word PENALTY.

The hangar smelled like hot metal, dust, and the bitter electrical stink of the chaff that had gotten into everything.

Spike came down the Swordfish ladder first, one hand sliding along the rail. Silver-gray metallic flecks clung to his shoulders and hair. He brushed some off, pinched the residue between finger and thumb, then wiped it on the leg of his suit. Faye climbed out of the Redtail on the far side with her jacket tied around her waist, flight undershirt stuck damp to her back, mascara smudged under one eye from rubbing at sweat with a dirty wrist. She hit the deck already talking.

"If anybody says one word about the shortcut, I'm not listening."

"Good," Jet said. "Because the shortcut isn't the only thing we're discussing."

"Really. I thought maybe the eight-thousand-woolong love note was enough."

Ed was upside down on top of a supply crate, the summons reflected in the lenses of the goggles pushed up on the forehead. Ein sat at the crate's base, ears twitching from voice to voice like this was one of the better shows the ship had offered lately.

Jet slapped the paper down on the workbench. "You broke formation."

"You brought a fishing trawler into a canyon chase."

"It's a pursuit-capable interplanetary vessel."

Faye barked out a laugh. "No, it isn't."

"It is when I'm flying it."

"You weren't the problem," Spike said, which was maybe meant to help and had the opposite effect.

Jet swung toward him. "No? Tell me what the problem was."

Spike scraped a line of bright dust off the workbench with the side of his thumb and let it gather beside the summons. "She didn't speed up."

"What?"

"Before the tunnel. Before the drone lit up with her tag. Three ships on her and she kept the same speed."

Faye crossed her arms. "So she's a good pilot. We knew that."

"Nobody asked you to dive under a freight hauler."

"I didn't hit it."

"Again," Jet said, "not the point."

"It's a little the point."

"No, the point is you went off your lane because you thought you could grab the kill yourself."

Jet heard how personal that sounded and did not take it back. The ore hauler's blunt nose was still lodged in his head, the Redtail slipping past with a strip of air so thin it barely counted. The number on the summons kept hitting the same place. Eight thousand woolongs for a chase they had blown and a near miss he could not stop replaying.

Her face changed by a degree, not much but enough.

"First of all, there was no kill. Unless you suddenly forgot why we're down here. Second, if I had waited for your perfect little box maneuver, she'd have been on the next ridge before you finished saying 'hold position.'"

"You don't know that."

"I know what I saw."

"You saw the nose of a hauler."

"And I missed it."

Jet threw both hands up. "You keep saying that like I should hand you a prize."

Spike shut the Swordfish hatch behind him with one flat push. "Jet."

"What."

"Yelling isn't going to make the money come back."

"No, but maybe it'll stop the next disaster before it starts."

Faye hit the Redtail strut with the heel of her hand and sent the next line at Spike instead of Jet. "You don't get to do quiet and superior. You lost her."

"Yeah."

"That's it? That's your defense?"

"Not a defense."

"Then what is it."

He shrugged once. It was a tiny movement. "Fact."

For a second the hangar settled into the ship's little sounds: cooling metal, a relay clicking somewhere in the wall, Ein's tags tapping the crate leg.

Then Ed said, cheerful as a knife, "Spike-person got sparkled."

Faye snorted despite herself. The chaff still dusted Spike's hairline like cheap glitter from a bad club. One shard clung stubbornly near his ear.

"You look ridiculous," she said.

"You nearly got flattened by industrial equipment."

"And yet I'm still the good-looking one."

Jet pinched the bridge of his nose with his flesh hand. The whole argument had dropped into grooves deep enough to steer itself. Faye got meaner when she was embarrassed, Spike went flat when he had already decided on the truth, and Jet got louder because somebody had to hold the shape of the mess while the woolongs bled out of it. "Can we do this after we talk about what actually happened."

"This is what happened," Faye said. "She took the tunnel, we lost visual, then we chased a beacon she'd strapped to a freight drone into a fuel stop and almost got cited into debt prison, and now you want to make it about my lane discipline."

"Because your lane discipline sucked."

"Your coordination sucked."

"Your-"

"Jet," Spike said again, same volume, and this time Jet stopped because there was something different in it, not sharper, just flatter.

Spike stepped to the bench, pinched the curling edge of the summons between two fingers, and let it spring back.

Jet had the stupid urge to snatch it back. The strip weighed almost nothing, but all he could see in it was fuel, docking fees, soy sauce gone low, one more meal turning into arithmetic.

"The chaff was cut up," he said.

Jet flattened the curling corner with his thumb. "What?"

"The stuff she dumped. Foil strips, cargo tags, insulation. All cut to size." He rubbed the glittering residue off his thumb. "You don't do that in the middle of a chase."

Faye pushed off the strut. "So she had it ready. Smugglers carry chaff."

"And the tunnel was too narrow for the Swordfish. She knew that."

Jet's jaw worked. "The drone too. Somebody wired that beacon by hand."

"Yeah. She set it up before we got here."

"In the middle of nowhere."

"Not nowhere. Settlement lane. Freight traffic. She knew somebody'd ignore a drone if it had the right tag."

Ed raised one hand from the crate. "Correct. Boring signals are invisible. Ed has been saying this for years."

"You haven't," Jet said.

"Ed has thought it very loudly."

Faye dragged a hand back through her hair. A line of red dust came away across her palm. "Fine. She planned the beacon. That doesn't explain how she kept staying ahead when we had three ships on her."

Spike didn't say anything. He picked at the chaff on his collar.

Jet pressed the summons flat against the bench. The answer was forming and he didn't like it. "She knew Faye would break the lane."

"Excuse me?" Faye said.

"The shortcut. She counted on it. And she knew I'd try to hold the middle."

Faye's mouth opened, then closed.

"And I went for the tunnel after the drone lit up with her tag," Spike said. He rubbed the residue between finger and thumb. "She had the chaff loaded for that."

Ed pointed at him. "Pattern recognition!"

Faye dragged both hands back through her hair and let the red dust fall. "That's insulting."

"Doesn't make it wrong," Jet said.

"Oh, fuck you."

"If she can read us like that," Jet said, "this wasn't just good flying. This was aimed at how we work."

Faye laughed once, ugly and brief. "Great. I love being profiled by a stranger."

Spike was still picking at the chaff on his collar. "I don't think she is one."

"Is what," Faye said.

"A stranger."

Jet straightened. "You saw her."

Spike nodded.

"And?"

He thumbed another shard of chaff off his sleeve toward the hangar drain. "Young. Dark hair. No helmet. Her hands were steady."

Faye's voice lost some of its edge, not much, just enough to notice. "How young."

"Twenties maybe. Not a kid."

"Armed?"

"Didn't see."

"Scared?"

Spike rubbed at the glitter by his ear and flicked it away. "No."

Jet picked up the summons again, then put it down because he had nothing to do with his hands. The pilot was a young woman, dark-haired and not scared. Up to now the pilot in his head had been a category: smuggler, bait setter, thief, old syndicate remnant, disgraced ex-cop with a grudge. A face should have made it easier. It did not. It made the whole stupid chase feel more deliberate.

"What else," he said.

Spike took a second.

Faye waited. "What."

"Like she was proving something."

"To us?"

"Maybe."

"That's not helpful."

"Didn't say it was."

Jet rubbed the thermal paper between finger and thumb until it squeaked. The ships ticked as they cooled. Ed hung upside down and delighted from the crate. Spike was still shedding metallic dust by the bench.

"If she wanted us lost," he said slowly, thinking it through as he said it, "really lost, she could've killed the drone beacon after the tunnel."

"Yeah," Spike said.

"Instead she leaves the beacon running on a freight drone."

"Yeah."

"Why."

Spike didn't answer for a second. "See what we'd do, maybe."

Ed kicked both feet lightly in the air. "It was funny, though."

Jet pointed at Ed without turning. "Not helping."

"Helping emotionally."

Faye's thumb grazed her collarbone once before she caught herself and dropped the hand. "So what, she's playing."

"She's not playing," Spike said. He rubbed the dust into his palm with his thumb.

"Then what."

"She's not trying to lose us."

Jet turned that over. Not fleeing. Not hiding. "She's running us," he said.

Spike gave one short nod. "Yeah."

The hangar lights hummed. The metallic dust on the deck threw back little dead sparks.

Jet felt his anger shift shape. It did not get smaller; it got cleaner, with less heat and more edge. "Running us" fit the canyon, the station, the drone with the hose-clamped beacon. It fit the six months of docking fees paid in cash behind Mama Yun's. It fit the dormant bounty that should not have existed, the signal that had woken one of Spike's dead names, and the waitress telling Faye she remembered a woman who could not have been her. Somebody had taken time with all of this, real time, maybe years, and down in the basin they had charged in like hunger itself, loud and easy to read.

Faye blew air through her nose and pushed away from the strut again. "I hate when he's right."

Spike opened his mouth.

"Don't," she said.

Jet almost answered, then did not. He folded the summons once, carefully, and tucked it into the breast pocket of his work shirt like putting the debt there would make it less embarrassing.

"Fine," he said. "We stop acting like we're chasing some random joyrider."

"Great," Faye said. "Now we're chasing a random joyrider who knows our personalities."

"That's still an improvement."

Ed slipped off the crate and landed barefoot without a sound. "Sneaky fish knows the Bebop song now."

Jet nudged his boot back when Ein nosed at it. "Maybe."

"Then Bebop should sing a different song."

That should have sounded childish. It did not. Not in the hangar, not with the basin spinning red under them and the fine in Jet's pocket and the glitter from the other pilot's cheap trick still clinging to Spike's sleeves.

Jet let the thought sit.

No one had anything useful after that, and for once they seemed to know it. Faye climbed back into the Redtail to check whether the near miss had scorched a port thruster gasket. Spike took a rag and started wiping chaff residue off the Swordfish's intake lip with the bored concentration he wore when something had gotten under his skin. Ed pulled the station map back up on a side monitor and began tracing routes with one finger, humming under his breath. Ein wandered from one set of legs to another, inspecting everybody's mood.

Jet stayed by the bench a little longer.

In another minute he'd have to go upstairs and count what was left in the galley, because food was a problem he could still solve with his hands.

Through the hangar's upper port he could see a sliver of Mars, dark rust and thin atmosphere, the rim lights of some settlement turning slow below. Somewhere down there was the station they had torn past, the ore hauler pilot probably telling the story already to anybody who would buy her a drink, the freight drone still waddling its programmed path with a fake beacon strapped to its back like a joke with good wiring.

And somewhere farther south, maybe already beyond the next range of canyons, the real ship was moving at whatever speed its pilot had chosen from the start.

It had not been fleeing. It had been setting tempo.


# Chapter 6

Jet stood in the galley with the cold-storage door open and took inventory like he was conducting an autopsy.

They'd been back six hours, and the Bebop already sounded poorer. With the ship cold, every small noise traveled. The hull ticked as it gave up heat. The life-support fans pushed a tired hum through the vents. Somewhere behind Jet's left shoulder, one loose spoon in the galley drawer kept tapping metal on metal every time the ship corrected its drift by a hair. Mars turned under them in a slow red sweep through the forward ports, the Hellas basin all rust scars and dark cuts, beautiful in the same useless way an expensive watch was beautiful when you couldn't afford lunch.

He had three bell peppers, and one of them was already wrinkling at the stem. Half an onion was wrapped in wax paper. There was a bottle of soy sauce with enough left to wet a pan and not much more, plus a packet of textured protein flakes that had gone stale so long ago they had become a geological feature. There was no beef.

The orange traffic summons lay under the salt tin so it wouldn't curl in the damp. `RECKLESS FLYING IN SETTLEMENT AIRSPACE`, it said in the neat bureaucratic block print of people who never had to choose between fuel and dinner. Eight thousand woolongs. Jet had turned it face down twice. It kept making its way back up like something alive.

He shut the cold-storage door with his hip, set the peppers on the board, and started cutting.

Across the common room, Spike was on the couch with one ankle over the other knee and a receiver balanced on his stomach. He kept rolling the dial with two fingers, slow, patient, like he was trying to tune in a station from another decade. What came out was mostly hiss with the occasional chop of a cargo marker or a burst of settlement weather. He'd said once, when Jet asked him why the hell he listened to static, that static helped him think. Jet had told him to think quieter.

Tonight Spike had been at it since they'd shut down the engines.

Faye sat at the table with one bare foot tucked under her, stripping chipped polish off her thumbnail with the edge of a card. The solvent smell mixed with oil, soy, old pepper, dog, and the stale trapped odor of four people living too close in a ship that had never been meant to be a home. Her hair was clipped up with a bent piece of wire because she'd lost the real clip again or pawned it or forgotten where she'd put it. With Faye all three explanations could be true.

Ed lay on the deck plates between the table and the couch, goggles up on forehead, talking to Ein while fitting bits of metallic chaff into the grooves of a dismantled toy drone.

"Sneaky ship confetti, confetti of deceit," Ed announced. "Ed will make a crown for Professor Ein, king of all canyons and also of all very rude lady pilots."

Ein sneezed because one filament of chaff had floated onto his nose.

"See?" Faye said. "Even the dog's offended."

Jet scraped the peppers into a bowl. "The dog's got more sense than the rest of you."

"The dog's got better seating than the rest of us," Faye said.

Ed sat up and set two strips of chaff across Ein's head between the ears. Ein held still with the patience of a creature who had accepted that this was his life now.

"Regal," Ed said. "Majestic. Slightly itchy."

Jet found the wok hanging where it always hung, blackened from years of too much heat and too little money, and set it on the burner. The gas caught on the second click. He added oil, then onion, then peppers, and the sound of them hitting hot metal was the best thing he'd heard all day.

Nobody spoke for a minute. The ship filled with the smell.

It should have been comforting. Usually it was. The first hit of frying onion, the pepper skin blistering, the soy going sharp in the heat. Usually that smell meant the day had stopped doing damage for at least twenty minutes.

Tonight the name kept moving through Jet's head anyway: Hosuke Takara.

Seventeen years old and stupid enough to think a cheap alias could save him from being himself. He had not heard the name out loud in almost twenty years. He had not written it in longer than that. He had seen it on a screen two days ago with a bounty number under it and a description of his own younger face, and now it was everywhere. In the scrape of the knife on the board. In the print on the summons. In the way his hand had gone stupid when Ed had said it, cheerful as a game-show answer.

He tipped the stale protein flakes into the pan. They landed light, barely a handful, and soaked up oil without turning into anything a decent person would call food.

Faye stared into the pan until the protein finished pretending, then tipped her chin toward Jet.

"You're kidding."

"Nope."

"That can't be the whole protein situation."

"That," Jet said, stirring, "is the whole protein situation."

Spike's gaze stayed on the receiver. "Looks optimistic."

"You wanna cook?"

"No."

"Then don't start."

Faye made a face at the pan. "If this is bell peppers and beef, I'm a nun."

Jet kept stirring. "You'd be a terrible nun."

"I'd be an excellent nun. I'd skim from the collection plate and sleep late."

"That's not being a nun."

"That's being any profession with a uniform."

Spike snorted without looking up.

Jet pointed the spoon at him. "Don't encourage her."

"Wasn't planning to."

"You already did."

"Too late," Faye said. "I'm encouraged."

The lines still came in the old order. Jet worked the burner. Faye took the first shot at the food. Spike answered from the couch without giving the room a whole sentence. Ed climbed in sideways and made everybody adjust around him. Usually the noise packed the galley tight. Tonight there were little snags in it, half-second gaps where nobody grabbed the next beat.

Ed crawled closer on elbows and knees, peering up into the wok. "Ed sees peppers. Ed sees onion. Ed sees fibrous lies."

"Ed sees dinner," Jet said.

"Dinner sees Ed."

Spike turned the dial a little farther. Static flared, then broke around the clipped voice of some freight dispatcher reading coordinates in a bored monotone. He let the bored voice finish the coordinates before moving on.

Jet plated the food into four bowls and a saucer for Ein. Each serving was mostly peppers with a little onion and brown scraps clinging where the protein had decided not to surrender all the way.

"All right," he said. "Eat."

Faye tipped her bowl toward the light, cut her eyes to the ticket, then back to the bowl. "You know what? I actually think the ticket tastes better."

"Good," Jet said. "You can eat that."

"I'm not paying it."

"Nobody said you were."

"You aimed that at me when you said eat."

"I was talking to everybody."

"No, you weren't. Your whole face was pointed at me."

Spike shifted one eye up from the receiver. "He did kind of aim it at you."

Jet set his own bowl down a little harder than he meant to. "Will both of you shut up and sit down before the peppers turn into wallpaper?"

That got them moving. Faye hooked the foot down, dragged her chair out with a squeal, and sat. Spike folded himself up from the couch and came over slow, receiver still in hand, trailing the cord until Jet told him if he dumped the thing in the food he could eat it. Spike planted himself at the end of the table and set the receiver by his elbow like it had a reserved place.

Ed sat cross-legged on the deck with bowl in lap. Ein circled once before settling next to him with the patience of the deeply resigned.

For a little while there was only the sound of eating.

It was not a happy sound. Bell peppers never made a happy sound. They squeaked against teeth. They released water at the wrong moment. The fake protein did what it always did, which was to suggest the memory of meat to nobody at the table.

Still, they ate fast. Broke had a way of making people grateful with their mouths and bitter everywhere else.

Faye chewed, swallowed, pointed chopsticks at the summons. "Eight thousand."

Jet kept his eyes on his bowl. "I know how to read."

"Do you? Because I want to make sure we're all appreciating it properly. Eight. Thousand."

"You can count, too. This is turning into a banner day."

"I'm serious."

"So am I."

"No, you're doing that thing where you act calm so you can have a stroke in private."

Spike reached for the soy bottle. It came out in two reluctant drops. "He doesn't need a stroke. He needs pilots."

Faye swung on him. "Oh, go to hell."

"You cut across settlement traffic."

"Because somebody boxed me out of the—" Faye set her chopsticks down. "You. You boxed me out of the clean line."

"There wasn't a clean line."

"There was until you decided canyon walls were optional."

Spike chewed, swallowed. "The tunnel closed the angle."

"There was no angle. There was a bad idea in a cheap suit."

Spike lifted his bowl and ate like they were discussing weather.

Jet rubbed two fingers over the bridge of his nose. "Can we not do the whole damn chase from the beginning?"

"No," Faye said. "I'd like to do it from the beginning. From the part where we left Mars broke and came back more broke because Captain Cool wanted to make friends with a cliff."

"I didn't hit the cliff."

"Congratulations. Put that on the wall."

"You almost hit an ore hauler."

"Almost isn't paperwork."

Jet slapped the summons with the back of his fingers. The paper made a dry snick on the table. "This is paperwork."

Ed raised a hand. "Ed would like the record to show that Ed said left-left-up and then small right and then whoosh, but grown-ups were busy doing loud ego rectangles."

"What the hell is an ego rectangle?" Jet said.

Ed shoved a pepper strip into mouth, thought about it, swallowed. "A rectangle made of ego."

Spike nodded once. "Accurate."

"Don't you start with accurate," Faye said. "You were the one out there playing lone wolf."

"It's a single-seat fighter."

"It's not a personality."

"You fly alone, too."

"Yeah, but I make it look good."

"You almost got vaporized by a freight hauler."

"Almost," Faye said again, pointing at him with chopsticks. "Not paperwork."

Jet could feel the argument trying to become one of the useful ones, the kind that actually sorted blame into neat little piles. It never worked. They all knew it never worked. The chase had failed the way Bebop jobs failed most often: everybody had been right about their own piece and wrong about the whole thing.

He shoveled peppers into his mouth so he wouldn't say what he was thinking, which was that he had seen the girl slip that ship through the maintenance cut on the sensor replay afterward and had felt, under the rage and the bill and the humiliation, one clean line of respect. She'd measured them better than they'd measured her. She had played them like a rhythm section that thought it was improvising.

He hated that he respected it.

Faye cut her eyes from Jet to Spike and back. "You know what's really pissing me off?"

"The peppers?" Spike said.

"Everything's pissing me off. Don't narrow it."

"Helpful if you specify."

"Fine. What's pissing me off right now is that none of us even got close enough to tag her. We burned fuel we didn't have, we got a ticket, and for what? For a drone. A stupid little freight drone with a light taped to it."

Ed brightened. "Very clever drone."

"I know it was clever, Ed."

"Beautiful deceitful drone."

"Still a drone."

Spike set his chopsticks down. "I got close."

Jet raised his head. "What?"

Spike shrugged. "For a second."

Faye leaned forward. "Close enough to what?"

"The cockpit."

Jet felt the room change by less than a degree.

"And?" he said.

Spike scraped one pepper through sauce with the tip of a chopstick. "Young."

"That's it?"

"Dark hair."

"That's very helpful," Faye said.

"Focused."

"That is even less helpful."

"Didn't look scared," Spike said.

He picked his chopsticks back up.
Jet saw the replay anyway: the other ship slipping into the maintenance cut without so much as a correction burn.

"Great," Faye said. "So we got humiliated by a child."

"She wasn't a child."

"You saw her for one second."

"Long enough."

"That's creepy."

"That's eyesight."

Jet waited for Spike to say more. He didn't. Of course he didn't. He went back to eating like he'd contributed his full legal share.

Faye watched him, annoyed on principle and because she could smell that there was something else there and wasn't going to get it. "You know, when normal people have useful information, they keep going after the first three words."

"I'm not normal people."

"No kidding."

Jet should have stepped in. Usually he did. He usually cut across the table before the tone took on that extra edge, before Faye went from entertainingly abrasive to properly mean and Spike decided mean was close enough to intimate to keep participating.

Instead he reached for his bowl again and found it empty.

Faye noticed. Faye noticed everything she could use and half the things she couldn't.

"You gonna say something," she asked, "or are you just going to stare holes in ceramic?"

Jet set the empty bowl down. "About what?"

"About the chase. About the ticket. About how this is somehow all our fault except yours."

"I didn't say that."

"You didn't have to."

"All right," Jet said. "You want my expert opinion? Here it is. We flew hot and dumb into terrain she knew better than we did. Spike tried to get cute, you tried to get ahead of the map, I kept the Bebop too close because I thought bulk would scare her into the open, and Ed was the only one giving useful information but none of us could understand it because Ed talks like a broken parade. Happy?"

Ed lifted chopsticks in salute. "Broken parade! Good band name."

Faye blinked once. "You left yourself in there."

"Yeah."

"Huh."

Jet reached for the summons, folded it once, then unfolded it again because folding it felt too much like giving up. "Doesn't change the number."

Nobody said anything. The summons sat between the soy bottle and the bowls like another dish nobody wanted.

Jet kept working the same crease in the summons with his thumb, flattening it and making it again. On any other night he would have been halfway through a rebuttal by now, tapping the table and walking them turn by turn through the chase until everybody else's mistakes outweighed his own. Instead the paper kept whispering under his thumb and nobody reached for anything on the table.

Faye eased back in her chair. "How much fuel?"

Jet gave her a look.

"What?"

"You care now?"

"I care if I have to eat this again tomorrow."

"We might be so lucky."

"Jet."

He sighed. "Enough to move if we have to. Not enough to go joyriding across half of Mars because somebody gets a feeling."

Spike pointed a chopstick at the summons. "Ticket before fuel?"

"Food before both, unless you want to start chewing insulation."

"I'm not doing that," Faye said.

"Shame. Might improve your disposition."

"My disposition's fine."

"Your disposition's armed."

Spike's mouth twitched.

Faye caught it. "What?"

"Nothing."

"Don't 'nothing' me when you're smiling."

"Wasn't smiling."

"You were absolutely smiling."

Spike picked up his chopsticks. "Maybe at the food."

Ed licked sauce from thumb and peered at Jet's bowl, then at the pan still on the burner. "Is there second dinner?"

"No."

"Tiny second dinner?"

"Also no."

Ed sighed with great theatrical sorrow and tipped two pepper strips from bowl to Ein's saucer. "Professor Ein, we suffer together."

Ein ate with the focus of a professional.

Jet got up and took the pan off the flame. There was enough stuck to the bottom for one bitter mouthful. He scraped it into his own bowl and stood at the sink longer than necessary, hands braced on either side, staring through the port over the faucet where Mars floated huge and wrong-colored and indifferent.

His reflection in the glass was faint, all beard and scar and the dark bulk of his shoulders. With the galley light behind him and the planet below, the double-image he hated clicked into place: the man he had built and the boy under the alias nobody was supposed to remember. Same shape at the jaw. Same stupid certainty in the posture. He turned away before the comparison finished forming.

"You know what we should do?" Faye said.

Jet rinsed the pan. "This ought to be good."

"Sell the receiver."

Spike didn't look up. "No."

"You love that thing more than people."

"It's still worth less than your makeup case."

"My makeup case has resale value."

"Everything you own has lipstick on it," Jet said. "That lowers the bid."

Faye found lipstick on the edge of the card in her hand and set it down. "Shut up."

Ed pointed at the summons. "Ed has solution. We mail them peppers."

"Mars traffic enforcement?" Jet said.

"Yes. With apology note. 'Dear sky police, please accept peppers in this difficult time.'"

Faye laughed despite herself, quick and unwilling. "I'd sign that."

"No one is mailing anybody peppers."

"Not even one pepper?" Ed asked.

"Especially not one pepper."

Spike rolled the receiver dial again. Another wash of static breathed over the room.

Jet dried the pan, hung it up, and said, "And for the record, nobody's selling anything until I say we're selling it."

Faye crossed her arms. "You don't get to say that like you're our landlord."

"Somebody has to keep this ship flying."

"It's not flying," Spike said. "It's falling in a controlled way."

"Thank you, Spike. That's exactly the kind of morale-building bullshit I was hoping for."

"Any time."

Faye slid her empty bowl toward the center. "So what's the plan?"

"The plan," Jet said, "is we stay cold, save fuel, and think."

"That's not a plan. That's being broke slowly."

"It's a better plan than burning another tank on pride."

"Maybe she'd double back."

"Maybe she'd paint her ship yellow and ask us politely to follow," Jet said. "Maybe we'd all grow wings. Eat your peppers and let me be realistic."

Faye opened her mouth, then shut it. Jet was rubbing the edge of the summons with one thumb again, not even seeing he was doing it.

Her eyes stayed on him a beat too long.

Jet reached for the nearest chore. "If you want something useful to do, scrape the bowls."

"There he is," Faye said.

"Scrape."

"You know, 'please' exists."

"So do sponges."

She made a face, but she stacked the bowls.

Spike rose with his own and reached for the summons on the way past. He flicked it with a finger. "Eight thousand for 'reckless.'"

"Read the rest."

Spike leaned in. "'In a settlement safety corridor with disregard for posted restrictions.'" He set it down. "That's a lot of words for bad luck."

"That wasn't bad luck," Jet said.

Spike flicked a look at him. Not long. Long enough.

Jet took the bowls from Faye and ran water into the sink. "No. It wasn't."

Water hissed into the sink. The receiver breathed static. Even Ed only hummed to Ein and lined the chaff strips along the dog's shoulders like epaulets.

After a minute Faye said, lighter on purpose, "If we all die because you made me eat peppers again, I'm haunting this kitchen first."

"Get in line," Spike said.

Jet snorted. "You'd all come back here. None of you know how to cook."

"I know how to cook," Faye said.

"No, you know how to set things on fire in oil."

"That's one school of cooking."

"It's not a good one."

"Depends what you're trying to kill," Spike said.

That got a small laugh out of Jet before he could stop it. It felt rusted from disuse.

Faye heard it and checked his face again, quick and sharp, like a laugh that rusty ought to leave a mark.

He gave her nothing back but the side of his face and the sound of bowls knocking in the sink.

By the time the dishes were done, the orange summons was the only bright thing left on the table.

Jet folded it once, exact and careful this time, and tucked it under the sugar jar with the kind of precision men used when there was no precision available anywhere that mattered.

***

Later, with the meal cleared and the common room lights dropped to the yellow half-power that made everybody look more tired than they were, the Bebop settled into its night positions.

Jet wiped down the counter a second time because the first time had not improved his mood enough. There were still pepper seeds in the grooves by the stove and a ring from the soy bottle on the table and a fine grit of Mars dust on the sill over the sink because dust on Mars did not respect doors, seals, or good intentions. He picked up each pepper seed with the tip of a damp cloth. He did not usually clean like this unless something had him by the back of the neck.

The name still did.

Not because he missed being that kid. He didn't. Hosuke Takara had been all appetite and no sense, a boy dumb enough to think petty cargo fraud was a future instead of a hallway with a locked door at the end. Jet had spent years deciding that boy was dead. Then one dirty database entry and there he was again, smirking through a physical description in an ISSP file nobody outside the system should have been able to touch.

He wrung out the cloth harder than the job required.

From the bathroom at the end of the corridor came the thin metal click of the medicine cabinet.

Faye stood in front of the mirror and leaned in until the bad fluorescent showed every rough edge in the ship's cheap glass. The mirror had a dark spot at one corner where the backing had started to go and a scratch down the middle that made it look like somebody had once taken a knife to their own reflection and quit halfway through.

She put two fingers against the hollow under her collarbone.

Then she took them away.

Then she put them back, slower this time, watching the movement in the mirror.

It wasn't even a gesture, not really. Not anything a sane person would call identifying. Miriam remembered a woman on Ganymede touching herself there when she had a good hand at cards. So what. People had habits. People touched their faces, their hair, the place their necklaces used to sit. She had probably touched that spot a thousand times before Miriam's tired Ganymede accent made it feel borrowed.

The skin there was warm from the ship and the bad heat, smoother than the chipped sink under her other hand. There was no answer in it. No hidden seam that opened. No tag stitched under the flesh reading MADE FOR SOMEONE ELSE.

Faye hated that she'd come in here for this. In the mirror her fingers landed at the notch above the breast, thumb tucked, elbow angled out. The movement looked casual, unthinking, familiar.

It was too familiar.

"Shit," she said softly to herself.

The word fogged the mirror, then thinned until her face came back through it.

She opened the cabinet. There was aspirin, one tube of expired antiseptic, and a lipstick worn down to a red stub. She looked anyway, as if one of them might explain how a stranger could carry your name around some moon while you slept on ice and came back owing half the solar system money you never spent.

There was nothing there, just the same bathroom they'd all used for years, smelling faintly of soap that never quite covered engine grease.

She shut the cabinet harder than she meant to, pulled the bent wire from her hair, then jammed it back in again because letting it down would feel like giving the mirror too much.

When she came out, she crossed the common room without looking at anybody and dropped into the chair by the porthole.

Spike was still with the receiver.

He'd moved from the table to the couch again and stretched out sideways, one foot on the arm, the old habit of taking up more room than a thin man had any right to. The receiver rested against his ribs. On low volume the static sounded less like noise than like weather far off, dry and intermittent. Every so often some dead-band handshake would try to form out of it and fail. It had a rhythm: three clipped tones, a burst, an empty carrier, another burst.

Nobody else in the room knew those patterns well enough to hear them as language.

Spike wished he didn't.

He kept telling himself he was checking once more so he could stop checking. That if the old syndicate band was quiet tonight maybe it would stay quiet tomorrow, and if it didn't stay quiet tomorrow he'd deal with that tomorrow, and if some remnant operator on the other end of the system had heard the handshake his alias had kicked loose then maybe they hadn't cared enough to write it down.

Maybe that was true.

The maybes were cheap. That was the problem with them.

Jet stacked plates in the drying rack with more care than plates needed.

"You gonna listen to that thing all night?" he asked without turning.

"Maybe."

"Sounds like a refrigerator dying."

"Comforting, then."

Jet half-turned at the sink. "If the refrigerator sounded like that, I'd have it in pieces on the counter."

"That's why it sounds different."

Faye made a face toward the porthole, not quite smiling. "You two should get a room and argue in full sentences."

"I'm arguing in full sentences," Jet said.

"You know what I mean."

"No, I don't."

"Of course you don't."

Spike rolled the dial. A clipped wash of coded pulse stuttered through before dissolving into hiss. His fingers stopped for a fraction of a beat.

Faye turned her head. "That music's terrible."

"It's not music."

"Really? I never would've guessed."

"Helps me think."

Jet reached for the dish towel again, found the counter already dry, and kept folding the towel anyway. "Thinking about what?"

Spike kept his eyes on the receiver instead of Jet. "Nothing useful."

Jet folded the dish towel into smaller and smaller rectangles and left it at that.

Ed had abandoned the drone parts and migrated to the open patch of deck in front of the couch with a deck of bent playing cards, one sock, and a strip of insulation foam that had somehow become part of the game. No one asked where the insulation had come from. Some questions were really just repair bills wearing mustaches.

Ein flopped onto his side while Ed dealt cards over the dog's ribs.

"Professor Ein has four queens," Ed informed the room. "This is mathematically suspicious, but Professor Ein rejects the tyranny of arithmetic."

Ein thumped his tail once.

"I don't think that's how poker works," Faye said.

"That is because Faye thinks too linearly."

"No, it's because I know what four queens means."

"Royal abundance."

"Cheating."

"Same family," Spike murmured.

Faye pointed at him. "See, now that was a smile."

He ignored her.

Jet checked the chrono over the stove. Late enough that normal people would have been asleep. Nothing on the Bebop happened at a normal hour unless it involved disaster.

He went to the storage locker under the bench for a clean dishcloth and found the folded wool blanket instead, shoved in with table linens because Ed had taken it to make a tent two days ago and no one had put it back. The blanket was thin from years of washing and rough in places where the weave had tightened. One corner had a brown half-moon singe from when Spike had dropped an ash on it months back and then claimed the ship was always attacking him unprovoked.

Jet left it where it was for the moment and took the cloth.

By the time he turned around again, Ed's card game had slowed.

That was how it usually happened. It was never a gradual bedtime or a normal kid's night routine. Ed moved at one speed until the body quit and then sleep took Ed like a switch being thrown. One minute Ed was still chattering, and the next Ed was gone.

Ed was on stomach now, cheek on crossed arms, one card stuck to forehead. The bare feet that were always in motion had gone still. Ein had rotated twice and wedged himself against Ed's stomach in the warm crescent under the bent knees. Every few breaths one of Ed's fingers twitched against the deck plate, as if finishing the game somewhere else.

"Ed has not conceded," Ed mumbled into the floor.

"Sure," Faye said softly.

"Strategic resting."

"Yeah."

Spike lowered the receiver volume without looking at the controls.

Jet stood still with the dishcloth in one hand.

The ship made its small old-ship noises around them. A vent clicked. Somewhere aft, metal settled with a hollow tick. The static at Spike's elbow breathed softer now. Mars turned under the portholes. The basin's darkness moved slow enough to look fixed.

Jet set the cloth down and went back to the bench locker.

He shook the blanket open with a practiced snap that made almost no sound. His prosthetic hand caught one edge, his right hand the other. He walked over and bent his knees carefully because a man built like him had to think about deck space or knock somebody flat.

Ein opened one eye as the blanket came down.

"Don't start," Jet murmured.

Ein considered this and allowed himself to be covered.

Jet laid the blanket over Ed first, then drew the lower edge over the dog, tucking it in around the flank where the ship's draft always found its way across the floor. The pilled wool dragged lightly over Ed's shoulder. One bit of red-orange hair stuck up through the fold until Jet, without really thinking about it, smoothed it down with the backs of his fingers.

Then he straightened.

Spike had lifted his head.

There was no smile on his face, and nothing on it Jet could use against him later. The receiver rested loose in his hand, tipped away from his ribs, static breathing back into the room.

Jet met his eyes.

Spike said nothing.

Jet didn't need him to.

Faye saw that exchange from the chair by the porthole.

Her attention tracked the whole thing: Jet moving, the blanket opening, Spike catching every second of it like the static had suddenly gone clear.

The cheap line rose in her mouth before she could spit it. Nice of you to notice. Nice domestic little act. Nice to know somebody on this ship can manage a basic mammal instinct.

The lines arrived ready-made and worthless.

She let them die.

Jet cleared his throat, low and rough, still watching the blanket. "He's gonna wake up freezing if I don't."

"Yeah," Spike said, eyes on the blanket. "Draft gets bad down there."

Faye turned back to the porthole before either of them could add another word. Mars sat huge and bruised below them. Behind her, Jet moved around the galley shutting things down one light at a time. The static stayed low. On the deck, under the singed blanket, Ed slept with mouth open and one card still stuck to forehead while Ein pressed his nose into the wool and dreamed whatever dogs with human-grade data in their heads dreamed.

Jet switched off the galley light.

The common room went softer.

Nobody said good night. They almost never did. The ship wasn't built for that kind of family and none of them had the nerve to retrofit it.

Still, the blanket stayed where Jet had put it, and the dishcloth stayed where Jet had dropped it by the sink. Faye sat by the porthole through the ship's false night, and when the first change in light found the ports she was still there.

The Bebop drifted over Hellas with its engines cold, ticket unpaid, pantry close to bare. Under the thin wool on the deck, two small bodies breathed in different rhythms and somehow made the room feel warmer than the heaters did.

Beyond the portholes, black cuts kept sliding through the red basin below.

Inside, the old ship ticked and settled around them for the night.


# Chapter 7

The Bebop's hangar still held the stale smell of engine grease and bell peppers when Faye slipped into the Redtail before Jet started coffee, before Ed woke up into a person-shaped weather system, before Spike finished pretending he had not noticed anything the night before.

She had not gone to bed.

Jet's dinner had gotten everywhere. Yesterday's look from Miriam still sat wrong under her ribs.

Miriam had put her at the Golden Ox.

There had been a blue dress.

There had been that touch at the collarbone.

Faye sealed the canopy and brought the systems up. The console lit her hands green. She did not look back at the Bebop once the clamps released. Looking back turned a solo errand into something you might explain later, and she had no interest in explaining anything. If she told Jet, he would ask practical questions in that careful voice that made it hard to tell whether he was worried or just organizing the day. If she told Spike, he would say "Yeah?" like it meant nothing and then file it away where he kept every dangerous thing. If Ed saw the launch prep, Ed would want to come, and Faye did not need company for this. She barely needed herself.

Mars swelled under the Redtail, wrong-colored and familiar anyway, a rusted skin under a thin, worked-at atmosphere. The settlements around Tharsis looked cheap from above, a scatter of lights and industrial ribs and old dreams with half the paint gone. She brought the ship down near the tram loop three districts over from Mama Yun's because even a quick landing cost money if you wanted not to get fined for it, and Faye had already had enough of paying for access to things that should have been hers.

Mama Yun had given her the address over comm without asking why, her voice arriving through the speaker with the scrape of a ladle on metal and the smoker's roughness she insisted came from broth steam. That had been almost worse than questions.

"Breakfast place," Mama Yun had said. "Miriam picks up the early shift there on Tuesdays and Thursdays. You still owe me for noodles."

"Put it on Jet."

"I always do."

That had been the whole exchange. Mama Yun knew when not to press. People who fed broke men for a living learned things like that.

The morning air on Mars had the dry bite of late autumn, dust and cold metal and fryer exhaust from a food stall opening too early. Faye zipped her jacket higher and cut across the block toward the diner, a narrow place under a dead strip of blue neon that read ORBITAL BREAKFAST except for the O, which had gone dark long enough ago that nobody had bothered fixing it. Through the window she could see the breakfast rush on its way out: two freight drivers in orange work jackets, a woman in clinic scrubs reading a hand terminal while she ate eggs one-handed, a schoolboy in a thick sweater pushing hash browns around his plate like they had offended him personally.

Miriam was behind the counter with a pot of coffee in one hand and a stack of plates on the other arm. She wore a gray cardigan under her apron, one elbow patched in darker yarn, and every time she passed the pie case she straightened the handwritten PEACH card even though the tray behind it held only a single wedge of something pale and collapsing. Tired eyes, same as yesterday at Mama Yun's. The accent was there too, that flat Ganymede drag on the vowels that made even small talk sound like it had crossed weather to get here.

Faye took a stool near the end of the counter and waited until Miriam came over.

"Coffee?" Miriam asked without looking up.

"Yeah."

The mug landed in front of her. Miriam poured, glanced up, and paused.

"Well," she said. "You again."

Faye wrapped both hands around the mug because they needed something to do. "Lucky you."

"Depends." Miriam set the pot down. "You want breakfast or trouble?"

"Just answers."

That got the smallest lift of Miriam's eyebrows. "Those cost more than breakfast."

"Put them on Jet too."

Miriam huffed once through her nose. Not a laugh exactly, but close enough to make room for the next thing.

"I wasn't trying to upset you yesterday," she said.

"You did anyway."

"Yeah. I figured."

Booth three waved an empty mug in the air. Miriam picked up the pot again.

"Sit there and be mad for a minute," she said. "I've got people who think coffee is a life support system."

Faye did not have anywhere better to be. She sat there and watched Miriam work the counter. She moved with the practiced economy of someone who had spent years carrying hot things to people who barely noticed. Her right knee was bad. You could see it in the half-hitch before she turned corners, the brief saving of weight when she thought nobody was looking. She called the schoolboy "captain" when he asked for more jam. She told the freight drivers they were not getting free refills on pie no matter what lies they had heard about charity. She straightened the PEACH card twice more.

The coffee was burnt enough to be almost a flavor of its own. Faye drank it anyway.

When the counter cleared down to the woman in scrubs and the boy with the jam, Miriam came back and leaned one hip against the register.

"All right," she said. "What answers."

Faye kept her voice flat. "The woman on Ganymede."

Miriam's mouth tightened a little, not with guilt, more with the annoyance of being right again. "The Golden Ox."

"You said you remembered me."

"I said I remembered a woman I thought was you."

"That isn't—"

"The same thing. No." Miriam picked up the pot again, put it down without pouring. "It isn't."

Faye looked past her at the pie case, at the stupid PEACH card standing in front of no peach pie at all. She could feel the question lodged in her throat like bad liquor.

"Tell me everything," she said.

Miriam tipped her head. "Everything's a lot."

"Then start with what you kept."

The woman in scrubs asked for the check. Miriam pushed off the register, ran the card, came back with the receipt tucked under two fingers. She did not hurry. Faye hated that. It made the whole thing feel ordinary, and ordinary was the part she could not get a grip on.

"The Golden Ox had a private room upstairs for the high rollers who wanted to pretend they weren't in a casino," Miriam said. "Blue carpet, terrible lighting, dealers who acted like they were priests. I worked the room three nights a week for almost a year. Mostly rich men from the wool trade and whatever women they were trying to impress."

"And me."

"Her."

Faye said nothing.

"She came in on a Thursday," Miriam went on. "Late. Not with the first wave. That was one reason I remember her. The serious players were already there, already dug in, already drinking like somebody else was paying. Then the host walked her up and started calling her Miss Valentine like she'd been there before or like he wanted everybody to think she had."

"Had she?"

"I don't know." Miriam shrugged. "I wasn't invited to the front office." She wiped a spot on the counter, eyes still on Faye, measuring how much honesty this woman could take. Then she kept going without waiting for the next question. "Enough like you that I put my foot in my mouth yesterday. Dark hair. Same eyes, or close enough in that light. Same mouth when you—" She caught herself. "When she wasn't smiling. She was older than you look now, maybe. No. Same age maybe, but life had got more miles out of her face. You know what I mean."

Faye knew exactly what she meant and hated her for saying it out loud.

"No," she said.

"Sure."

The schoolboy asked for more syrup. Miriam passed it over without taking her eyes off Faye.

"She wore a blue dress," Miriam said. "Not expensive expensive. It wanted to look expensive. Satin finish, maybe a blend, cut low in the back. The hem had been let down once and stitched by hand. I notice things like that."

"Why."

Miriam glanced at her patched elbow. "Because I own clothes."

That should not have landed, but it did. Faye pictured a blue dress under casino light, the kind of dress built to catch attention first and inspection second. Something chosen for effect because effect was cheaper than security.

"She played the high table?" Faye asked.

"Not like a tourist." Miriam nodded. "There was a wool trader there from Callisto. Big man, pink face, wedding ring he kept taking off before each hand like that made the odds cleaner. She let him underestimate her for maybe fifteen minutes, then took enough off him that he stopped laughing."

"How much."

"Enough that he sweated through his collar and started asking for a new dealer."

"That's not a number."

"No, honey, it isn't."

Faye opened her mouth, closed it again. Miriam had not called her honey in a condescending way. That was almost more irritating.

"How do you remember one customer out of a whole room?" Faye asked.

"Because I carried drinks for drunks and cheats for a living. You learn who's going to stiff you and who's going to make trouble for the dealer. She wasn't either one. That's what I noticed."

"That doesn't mean anything."

"It means she was quiet about it. Most women who came in dressed up, the men made a whole production. More drinks, bigger orders, showing off for the table. She didn't set any of that off. She just sat there stacking chips and touching right here every time a hand got ugly."

Miriam lifted two fingers to her own collarbone.

Faye's hand moved before she could stop it.

Both of them looked at it.

Miriam did not say I told you so. She was too old for that kind of cheap pleasure.

"Maybe everybody does that," Faye said.

"Maybe." Miriam's voice stayed even. "Everybody doesn't do it the same way."

The bad coffee turned bitter in Faye's mouth. She drank the rest because not drinking it would have looked like reacting.

"What way."

Miriam demonstrated again instead of answering, lighter this time. Two fingers, quick, at the collarbone and away. "Like checking a necklace that isn't there," she said. "She did it when she was bluffing. Or when she wanted the table to think she was bluffing. Hard to tell with good players."

"You said voice and laugh yesterday."

"I said I remembered both."

"So."

Miriam reached for the pot, saw Faye's cup was empty, filled it without asking. "Lower voice than yours," she said. "Not much. A little smoke in it. Laugh came out when she forgot herself, not when the men wanted it. That I remember too."

"You spent a lot of time remembering this woman."

"No." Miriam shook her head. "I spent a lot of time carrying coffee. She just stuck."

Faye watched the black surface of the refill settle in the mug. In it she could see a warped slice of her own face, the nose bent sideways by cheap ceramic and diner light.

"You said there was a scar," she said.

Miriam's eyes narrowed slightly, not suspicious, just careful. "I did."

"Where."

"Left shoulder blade."

The answer came so quick it was obvious she had never forgotten it.

"How do you know which side."

"Because she leaned forward to rake in chips and the strap slipped." Miriam touched her own back with the handle of the pot, indicating the spot with working-woman precision. "Not a fresh scar. Pale. Curved a little, like something cut and then healed badly. I remember thinking it must have hurt like hell when she got it."

The diner seemed to lengthen around Faye for a second, counter stretching away, the hum of the refrigeration unit too loud, somebody dropping silverware in the kitchen sounding like it had happened inside her own teeth.

"Hold this," Miriam said suddenly, and shoved the pot into her hands because booth two wanted the check and the schoolboy had tipped his syrup over and the woman in scrubs had stood up to leave. Faye held the pot while Miriam moved away with a dishcloth and a muttered, "Don't steal it. It isn't good coffee."

Faye put it back down the second Miriam was out of reach and slid off the stool.

The restroom was at the end of a short hall past a vending machine that sold headache tablets and mint gum. The mirror over the sink had a crack running through one corner and the fluorescent tube above it buzzed like an insect trapped in glass. Faye locked the door, pulled her jacket off one shoulder, then the strap of her top. She turned, tried to catch the angle in the mirror, cursed, shifted, used the smaller cracked strip of reflective metal bolted near the hand dryer to see more.

The skin there was smooth.

There was a faint line from her strap and a small old bruise going yellow near her ribs from a bad landing two days earlier. There was no scar.

Of course there was no scar. She knew her own body. She dressed it, sold it, patched it, hauled it in and out of ships and card rooms and bad decisions. She knew every place that marked easy and every place that marked slow. But she stood there anyway with one hand braced on the sink and her other fingers pressed hard into the flat skin over her left shoulder blade, as if pressure could raise something from underneath.

Nothing rose under her fingers.

When she came back out, Miriam had wiped the syrup off the counter and reset the jam rack. The PEACH card was straight again.

"You all right?" Miriam asked.

"Fine."

"You don't look fine."

"You don't look rich either. We all get burdens."

That one did earn the edge of a smile. Miriam poured herself half a cup of coffee she plainly did not want and drank it standing up.

"You said you wanted everything," she said.

Faye climbed back onto the stool. "Did she say where she was from."

"No."

"Who she was with."

"Nobody worth remembering."

"Did she use my name."

There it was, finally. Not the shape of the question, but the dirty center of it.

Miriam set her cup down. "I heard the host call her Miss Valentine. I saw her sign a marker slip Faye Valentine. You want me to lie and say maybe I read it wrong, I can't do that for you."

Faye looked at the register instead of the woman's face. Numbers glowed there in obedient little green blocks: add, subtract, tax, total. All the clean ways a machine could tell a story wrong.

"Why do you keep coming back to the money," she asked.

Miriam blinked. "What."

"The dress, the scar, the laugh. Fine. But every time you talk about her, you circle the tip. Why."

"How the hell would I know why."

"Take a guess."

Miriam rubbed one thumb against the side of her mug. Dry skin. Cracked cuticle. Someone who washed her hands too often and never got ahead of work.

"It wasn't theater," she said at last. "Men in that room threw cash when they wanted witness. She folded it under the saucer and kept walking. Didn't wait for thanks. That's somebody who's done a shift for tips."

Faye said nothing.

"That was part of it," Miriam went on. "The dress wanted one story. The tip wanted another. And when she got up to leave, the room came off her. Her shoulders dropped an inch. Like she'd been holding her face in place all evening and the door let it drop."

The freight drivers paid and lumbered out. The diner got quieter. Somebody in the kitchen turned down the music, which had been a radio station playing old Mars standards through too much static.

"Look," Miriam said. "I don't know what this is to you. I don't know if you're looking for her or trying not to. But I saw who I saw."

"You saw somebody wearing my name."

"Yeah."

Faye's fingers found her collarbone again. This time she made herself drop the hand.

"Maybe it wasn't mine first," she said before she could stop herself.

Miriam frowned. "What."

"Nothing."

"No, that wasn't nothing."

Faye pushed a few bills under the saucer though the coffee was barely worth the cup. "You did your good deed," she said. "Don't get ambitious."

Miriam glanced at the money, then back at her. "That isn't a tip. That's paying your tab."

"Then don't spend it all in one place."

She put her jacket on and headed for the door.

"Hey," Miriam called after her.

Faye stopped with one hand on the glass.

Miriam had the rag in one hand and the pot in the other. She opened her mouth. Then the kitchen bell went off behind her, loud, and somebody back there hollered something about the griddle.

"Goddammit— hold on," Miriam said, to the kitchen, not to Faye.

Faye didn't hold on. She pushed through the door.

Behind her she heard Miriam call something— "Tuesday" and something else— but the glass swung shut and the street took the rest of it.

Outside, the cold had sharpened. The tram line whined around the corner and the sky over the district had gone the dirty brass color Mars used when the day had not decided whether to die clean. Faye stood on the sidewalk with the smell of fryer grease on her clothes and the spot on her shoulder blade still throbbing from her own fingers.

There was no scar.

There had been a blue dress let out at the hem.

The tip had been real.

There had been two fingers at the collarbone.

That was more than Faye had ever gotten about the woman she had been before the ice, and every bit of it belonged to somebody else.

She turned toward the records office because if she did not go now she might talk herself into staying ignorant, and ignorance had never once paid what it promised.

***

The Mars Civil Identity Records Office sat in a low public building between a transit authority branch and a clinic that specialized in lung filtration. Faye had expected something hidden, or at least something guarded hard enough to make the corruption feel expensive. Instead she got a peeling sign, a line of bolted plastic chairs, and a ticket dispenser with one corner caved in from years of people hitting it too hard.

Window 4 handled historical verification. The woman behind it wore a wrist brace covered in marker drawings of planets and crooked stars, the kind of thing a bored child did when told not to touch the medical tape. She pushed her glasses up with the heel of her hand because the brace made her fingers clumsy, and she kept a jar of boiled sweets by the terminal that looked older than some governments. Faye took a number and sat under a vent that breathed directly onto her scalp.

The place smelled like dust, hot plastic, and old printer toner. A man in work boots argued softly with Window 2 about a death certificate. A teenager in school uniform practiced a speech under his breath for whatever transcript appeal had landed him here. A woman with twin toddlers tried to keep both from licking the base of a public charging station. Everybody in the room had brought some stupid bureaucratic hurt with them, and the room made no distinctions among them. Ticket numbers rose on the wall. A speaker crackled. Lives got reduced to documents, disputed, stamped, moved along.

Faye hated it on sight.

When her number finally flashed, she went to Window 4 and stood.

"Historical verification?" the clerk asked.

"Identity fraud."

"If it started in a suspended-patient file, that's still the same form." The clerk slid a pad across. "After civil consolidation the old cryo audits route through the claimant's current civil registry. You live on Mars now, it lands here. Fill out sections one through five. If the original records are off-world, check the jurisdiction box and write where from."

Faye looked at the form. It wanted legal name, current registry status, origin institution, basis for dispute, requested scope. Half the boxes were small enough to insult her on purpose.

"Basis for dispute," she read. "You want a paragraph or just the part where somebody stole my life."

The clerk's eyes came up, calm and unimpressed. "Either one fits if you write small."

Faye took the form to a side shelf and filled it out with a chained pen that dragged on the paper. Under basis for dispute she wrote possible unauthorized post-suspension use and then crossed out possible because if she had come this far she did not owe caution to a form. Under requested scope she checked full audit and stared at the line for supporting claim. She wrote current biometric match and let it stand. What else was she supposed to put there. Still me?

When she brought it back, the clerk scanned it, typed, and held out a handheld reader.

"Thumb."

Faye pressed her thumb to the glass.

"Retina."

She leaned toward the sensor ring. A soft tone acknowledged the match. The clerk turned the screen slightly and read.

"All right. Current biometric alignment is positive. You're the live match on the revived file and the current Mars registrant, so I can request the intersystem pull from the Earth-side cryogenic archive. If it clears, I still need your voice print and final signature before I can open the sealed review."

"How long is a while."

"Depends how bad their indexing was and whether the old med server wants to cooperate today."

"That's not a time."

"No." The clerk clicked through three more windows. "Sit down. Don't leave the building. If it clears, I need a voice print and final signature."

Faye did not ask about the fee until she was already reaching for the slot.

"There's a processing charge?"

"There is a charge for everything," the clerk said.

That, at least, sounded like Mars.

The amount on the screen was not ruinous. It was worse than that. It was petty. Small enough that refusing would have been ridiculous and large enough to irritate her for hours. She paid it and took the receipt, thin paper warm from the printer like the office was trying to hand back heat in place of justice.

She sat again.

The wait ate most of the afternoon. Numbers rose and reset. The toddlers fell asleep sideways across their mother's lap. The student got called, came back out looking relieved and embarrassed. The man in work boots left with three certified copies of somebody's ending tucked under his arm. Faye bought a can of citrus soda from the machine because the coffee from the diner had turned to battery acid in her stomach. She looked at the wall clock too often. She thought about leaving twice and did not.

At some point the clerk at Window 4 took one of the boiled sweets from the jar, cracked it between her teeth, and kept typing.

Finally Faye's number flashed again.

"Voice print," the clerk said when she stepped up. "State your legal name."

For half a second Faye almost said no just to prove the file could not make her do things. Then she looked at the emptying room, the dusty light in the windows, the ridiculous planets on the woman's wrist brace, and said, "Faye Valentine."

The terminal pinged. The clerk's expression did not change, but one corner of the screen filled with new text fast enough that even Faye could tell something big had unlocked.

"All right," the clerk said quietly. She was reading the screen now, not performing for the window. "It came through."

"And?"

The clerk kept scrolling.

"And sit down," she said. "This isn't one line."

Faye did not sit. The clerk looked at her, then at the room, which by now held only one old man asleep in a chair under the departure board.

"Fine," she said. "Stay standing."

She turned the screen enough for Faye to see.

Most of it was codes. Facility numbers, archival paths, jurisdiction flags, audit references. The top line carried her name in hard black letters and a date that meant nothing to Faye except that it was before the world she knew. Under that:

PATIENT STATUS: SUSPENDED

POST-SUSPENSION ACCESS REVIEW: VERIFIED IRREGULARITY

UNAUTHORIZED CREDENTIAL EXPORT: T plus 3Y 2M

CAUSE: EMPLOYEE MISCONDUCT / DATA RESALE

UTILIZATION WINDOW: 11Y 0M 17D

CURRENT STATE: DORMANT

Faye read the lines twice and still felt like the words had reached her in the wrong order.

"Data resale," she said.

"That's what it says."

"Meaning what."

The clerk clicked through something on the screen. "Meaning an employee at the facility exported your credential bundle—" She glanced at Faye. "Your identity file. Biometric profile, account hooks, medical access, whatever was linked at the time. And sold it."

Faye let out a breath that wanted to be a laugh and failed on the way up. "So while I was frozen, some asshole clerk sold me by the page."

"Looks that way."

"You say that like you're discussing the weather."

"I'm reading what the system returned." The clerk didn't look up. "If you want to file a formal objection to the audit language, there's a process for that. It doesn't change what came back."

Faye kept staring at the screen.

"How many people bought it."

"Doesn't show. Could've been direct, could've moved through brokers. A lot of these did."

"These."

"Stolen suspended-patient files."

The old man in the chair snored once, then settled deeper into sleep.

"I'm not special," Faye said.

The clerk gave a tiny shrug. "Not to an institution, no."

It was a brutal answer because it was not cruel. The woman was just narrowing scope, giving the factual piece and leaving the rest where it fell. Faye wanted to hate her and could not quite get there.

"Utilization window," Faye said. "Eleven years."

"Eleven years and seventeen days." The clerk didn't soften it. "After the first confirmed fraudulent use."

"Active doing what."

The clerk clicked another tab. More codes. More dates. She turned the screen another inch toward Faye, and Faye read faster than the clerk could narrate — transit registry, banking relay, medical intake, short-term lodging, and then a line that stopped her cold.

"Ganymede," Faye said. "Gaming license. Golden Ox."

The clerk glanced up. "That mean something to you?"

"Keep going."

"Debt collection flags. Off-world customs verification. Enough regular activity to establish a living identity rather than a one-time fraud." The clerk leaned back. "Whoever had the file used it as a life, not a quick theft."

Whoever had bought the file had used it as a life.

The phrase sat in Faye's chest and spread outward slowly, the way cold did in bad heating. Miriam's blue dress, let out by hand. A shoulder scar. A tip left by somebody who knew tips. Now this: transit, clinics, rooms, debts. Thumb on a scanner. Name on a room slip. Medicine slid across a counter. The long, ugly paperwork of staying alive.

"Who stopped it," Faye asked.

"Nobody necessarily stopped it. Dormant means it fell out of active use. Could be death. Could be abandonment. Could be a switch to another identity. Could be simple data decay if the user went off-grid."

"And the employee."

"Terminated after a later review." Another click. "Prosecuted in absentia, looks like. Record sealed after civil consolidation."

"Convenient."

"For somebody."

Faye leaned closer to the screen. Under the clean summary lines there was a dense list of access points and verification events. Most were unreadable to her beyond date and place, but the sheer number of them did more damage than the headline. Here a clinic intake. There a transit gate. Here a temporary license. There a denied claim. Eleven years did not arrive as one theft. They arrived as Tuesday afternoons, motel desks, customs lines, medication counters, rent notices, people behind glass asking for a thumb and a name and getting hers.

She had woken up to debt and a blank wall where the first part of her life should have been. Someone else had gotten paperwork. Someone else had gotten the ordinary weight of days.

"Can I print this."

"You can print the audit summary and certified access log. Detailed cross-system pulls require separate request orders."

"Fine."

The printer behind the clerk started up with a loud mechanical throat-clearing. Fanfold paper fed through, then cut sheets, then one stamped certification page with a red seal that looked theatrical in exactly the wrong way. The clerk squared the stack, stamped two places, signed one line, and slid it through the slot.

"Claimant confirmed by current biometric match," she said. "Historical credential misuse verified."

Faye looked at the top page. Claimant. Confirmed. Misuse. The language managed to make the whole thing sound like a dispute over luggage.

"I was frozen," she said.

The clerk looked at her over the stack. "Right."

"Then I wake up with no memory and debt collectors on my back and now this office is telling me some stranger got eleven years under my name."

"Yes."

"So why wasn't this sitting in the packet when they thawed me."

The clerk rested both hands on the desk, careful with the bad wrist. The marker planets flashed under the fluorescent light.

"Because revival intake isn't a fraud review," she said. "They confirm they woke the right body, unlock whatever care you need right then, and decide where the debt goes. Sealed misuse audits stay locked unless the living claimant asks for them or the stolen credentials are still active. Yours went dormant before revival."

"And the paper calls that misuse."

"That's the standard classification," she said. "Credential misuse covers everything from a one-time fraud to whatever yours shows. I don't write the categories."

Faye took the stack. It was heavier than it should have been. Three pages of summary, six of certified log, one receipt, one rights notice. Eleven years reduced to fewer sheets than a menu.

"Is there anything in there that says why?" she asked.

"No."

"Nothing."

"The system logs access events," the clerk said. "Time, location, type of use. It doesn't log motive. If you want that, it's an investigative referral, and that goes through a different office."

The office speaker announced closing in a bored recorded voice. The old man woke with a snort and shuffled toward the exit clutching his envelope. Window 2 shut down. Window 1 followed. The room changed shape as soon as the other terminals went dark. It got wider and emptier at the same time.

"We're closing public service in ten," the clerk said. "You can file for detailed pulls tomorrow if you want them."

"Tomorrow."

"That's how days work."

Faye almost smiled despite herself. It vanished before it fully formed.

"Sure," she said.

She took the papers to the waiting area and sat with them on her knees. The speaker made one more closing announcement. A janitorial cart rattled somewhere down the hall. The clerk at Window 4 shut down her terminal, stacked the boiled sweets into a drawer, and started gathering things into a soft-sided bag. Faye read the top page again. T plus 3Y 2M. 11Y 0M 17D. Dormant.

After a minute a shadow fell across the paper.

"I'm locking the main door," the clerk said. "You can stay a little if you're not looking to rob the place."

Faye looked up.

"You do this for everybody."

"No." The woman adjusted the strap of her bag with her good hand. "Most people leave."

"Maybe I'm special after all."

The clerk shifted her bag. "I've missed my tram," she said. "Don't move anything off the desks."

"Exit pad's by the inner door," she added, pulling her transit card from her pocket with her good hand. "Hit green when you leave. It logs you out."

"Of course it does."

"That's the system."

Then she left, limping slightly with the stiffness of someone whose brace did not actually solve the problem it was built for.

The lights dimmed by one bank.

Faye stayed where she was.

***

After closing, the records office sounded like a machine embarrassed to be overheard. The vent above the chairs kept breathing. Somewhere deeper in the building an old server rack hummed with a faint metallic whine. Through the high wired window by the ceiling, the Mars light went from brass to bruised violet while the cold started coming down. Faye opened the certified log again and turned past the summary to the denser pages with the bad toner and the certification stamp half bitten off at one corner.

Page four gave her two lines the headline hadn't. One from Tharsis: DUST RING ROOMS / identity verification passed / cash deposit waived. Another from seven months later: LOW MESA CLINIC / intake confirmed / claim denied, prior balance carried. The file didn't know a face or a reason. It knew a room, a clinic desk, a debt that kept following her name long after it stopped belonging to her alone.

She turned to the rights notice. Provider identifiers. Transit-gate images. Signed applications. Separate fee each. She slid that page into the inside pocket of her jacket instead of putting it back on the stack. Jet could ask practical questions later. Spike could go quiet later. Tonight the paper was hers. Everybody else could wait.

Her hand rose toward her collarbone and stopped. That stupid little touch was still the part she could not get past. If the other woman had been using the file years before Faye woke, then Faye had not learned the gesture from her in any ordinary way. Either it belonged to the lost version of Faye from before the ice, or habit lived somewhere memory could not reach, which was worse.

She reached back through the fabric of her top to the smooth skin over her left shoulder blade. Nothing. No raised line, no seam to match Miriam's memory. This body was hers. Those eleven years were not. The name sat between them anyway.

Outside, the city had gone full dark. Inside, the office kept humming around her. She did not cry. She held the top page flat with one hand and the unmarked place on her back with the other and let the vertigo stay ugly.


# Chapter 8

The reader liked his right eye better than the left. Most machines did.

Later that night, it took two passes before the locker clicked. The freight district had settled deeper into its night noises: a generator knocking somewhere off tempo, sheet metal pinging as the cold came down, dust hissing over concrete in long low sweeps. The loading bays along the lane glowed in strips. Everything else had gone black. Forklifts slept behind chain-link with their forks lowered like tired jaws.

Spike pulled the door open and stood there a second without going in.
