The place smelled the same as it had four years ago: old paper, oil gone gummy in its can, cardboard that had drunk too much dry air and given up. Memory moved. This place sat still. Just storage. A dead version of his life stacked and numbered and waiting for nobody.

He stepped inside, shut the door against the wind, and clicked on the penlight he had brought this time.

The beam found crates, binders, bundles of route sheets strapped with brittle bands. He'd come here the first time because the alias had flared to life and he needed to know what else might still be breathing with it. He'd expected danger and gotten paperwork. One manifest moved from the bottom of a stack to the top. One sentence in a hand he didn't know.

She needed this to live.

Five words. There was no name and no accusation. The absence needled him most. If whoever left it had written You did this, that would've been simple. If they'd written My mother or Help me or Even now, it would've been something he could push against. Instead it was just a fact. Facts had a way of sitting there until they got expensive.

He laid the annotated manifest on a crate under the light.

The paper was old enough to have gone soft at the folds. Carbon copy ghosting stained the back. Route code at the top. Tharsis freight annex to a basin clinic that only had a depot number, not a real name. Mid-column cargo descriptions in the syndicate's preferred language of concealment: machined housings, bolt assemblies, receiver blanks, lubricants, polymer wrap, coolant packs. Nothing that looked like a life in there unless you already knew where to look.

He knew now.

The medical line item sat halfway down the page, small as an apology. Refrigerated pharmaceuticals, sealed case, one unit. Special handling. Maintain cold chain. It had been tucked into a larger convoy under the same transit umbrella as rifle parts and ammunition. Standard enough. They hid all kinds of things inside weapons runs because weapons runs got where they were going. They had guards. They had fuel priority. They had people who got shot if they came up short. A clinic shipment on its own got taxed, skimmed, delayed, or robbed by the second checkpoint. A weapons convoy moved fast.

The ugly part was that the system worked.

He turned the page and found the attached handling sheet. Temperature tolerance. Transfer signatures. A remark from one of the depot clerks complaining about amperage draw from the refrigeration unit. A flat notation in his own hand authorizing the cold case to ride in the third truck and telling the dispatcher to stop whining.

He could see the moment without trying. A loading dock under yellow lamps. Dust in the corners where the sweepers never got it all. One driver bitching about battery pull. Spike taking the clipboard, looking at the clock instead of the cargo, writing three quick words and shoving it back. He had been younger and thinner and more careful with his hair. Morality had nothing to do with it. He'd been in a hurry.

At the time the refrigerated case had meant inconvenience. Different tie-downs. Different spacing from the hot crates. One more thing for idiots to mess up. He remembered that much. He didn't remember wondering what was in it, because he hadn't. The label had said pharmaceuticals and then turned into weight and volume and schedule. Somebody else's problem. The whole job was to turn people into categories, categories into movement.

He lifted the manifest and checked the stack under it.

There were more.

The clinic code was the same, and so was the route family. Monthly, near enough. One insulated case a month folded into shipments that paid better and mattered more to men with guns. Twenty-eight days between one and the next. Then thirty-one. Then twenty-nine. Somebody had been living in those gaps in the plainest sense. Someone's next four weeks arriving as a line item between ammunition belts and stamped receiver shells.

He flattened the second sheet with the heel of his hand.

On the delivery confirmation the receiving signature came as a courier mark, a quick angled slash with a loop under it. The kind of abbreviated sign-off that let men farther up the chain pretend they weren't moving goods through actual human hands. He found the same mark on the next month's sheet and the month after that, then a variation with the pen dragging at the end as if the truck had been moving or the signer had been tired.

It was the same courier on the same route, again and again.

He sat on the edge of a crate because there wasn't any point pretending he was just doing a quick check now.

His penlight made a hard white island in the dark. Beyond it the rest of the locker stayed full of shapes that used to mean something: spare transponders, old route books, a dead comm unit, invoices for shell companies that had dissolved before the ink dried. Lin Shao-Feng lived in those things; Spike didn't. Lin liked clean numbers and quiet drivers and routes so dependable nobody above him had to think about them. If you did logistics well, the cargo disappeared into inevitability. Men remembered gunfights. They remembered bosses. They didn't remember the truck that came on time every month.

Unless the truck stopped coming.

Spike set the manifest down and rubbed his thumb along the note the young woman had left. The pen strokes had pressed through the first page into the next two. Whoever wrote it had leaned hard, hard enough to emboss the pages underneath. There was no flourish in it, no show. Just bad paper and something mean enough behind the hand to bite through it.

She needed this to live.

He looked back at the line item. The drug names were there in print small enough to discourage curiosity. He knew enough to know what class they were. Anti-rejection. The kind you kept taking because if you stopped, your own body started acting like an occupied territory and tried to clear the invader out. He didn't know what organ. Kidney maybe. Liver. Miss enough doses and ankles puffed, fever climbed, the body started chewing at the graft like it was spoiled meat. Let the reefer unit drift warm for half a shift and the whole case might arrive too hot to trust. The point was maintenance, never a cure, never one good day in a hospital and done. Repetition. Delivery. Cold storage. Do it again next month. And the month after that.

He found himself counting how many times the route had repeated before he noticed he was doing it.

Nine confirmed runs in the stack. There might've been more somewhere else. Enough to say it wasn't a fluke. Enough to say the weapons convoy hadn't accidentally carried medicine once. The rifles paid the fuel. The insulated case got a ride. The route existed for violence and happened to make room for survival on the side. If the convoy had carried only guns, he could've filed it under the usual rot. Dirty was simple. This thing kept somebody alive.

He remembered the way the syndicate liked to talk about efficiency. It almost never came up in speeches. It came out in little irritated corrections. Don't waste a trip. Don't send half-full trucks. Don't separate loads if one set of muscle can cover both. Every decision shaved a risk somewhere and pushed it somewhere else. By the time cargo reached its destination, nobody at the top could remember which part had been the point.

He'd told himself for years that he hadn't been one of the men making bodies. He'd moved freight. He'd moved information. He'd moved money. The blood was always somewhere farther down the line, on somebody else's shoes.

The manifest let him keep saying that if he wanted. It just dirtied the line.

He went through the rest of the stack slower. There were no names. There never would've been names if his operation had been working the way it was built to. There were depot numbers, cargo weights, seal confirmations, the courier mark. Once, on a customs bypass sheet, a note that the basin runner preferred night handoff because daytime dust cooked the reefer unit. Another time, a surcharge approved because the route had to divert around a militia checkpoint that week. Mundane. Petty. The kind of details a person built a life around if staying alive depended on delivery windows and battery draw.

He could feel the old habits trying to help. Build the pattern. Strip it down. Ask who had access, who moved the paper, who knew the stash point, which old contact could identify the route mark. Do the part that was useful and skip the rest.

Useful was fine. Useful was what he had.

But there was an extra drag in it now, something he couldn't shake by standing up or lighting a cigarette. The manifests had made a person out of the sentence without giving him the person. He had regularity. A clinic code. A repeated sign-off. Someone whose life had depended on his routing math and who had never entered his head when he was doing it.

That part sat wrong.

He gathered the relevant sheets into one stack and tucked them into his jacket. The paper crackled under the lining.

There were only a handful of people left on Mars who would've remembered a courier mark from one basin feeder run years ago. Most of the competent ones were dead, jailed, or honest now. Honest was the rarest option. Hector Moro was still alive because he had built a life around remembering exactly enough and never looking like the sort of man who could matter.

Spike killed the penlight, opened the locker, and stepped back into the cold.

Dust moved along the lane like water with nowhere to go. He pulled the collar of his jacket up and headed for the alley quarter where the bars with bad liquor and no windows kept their lights on for men who didn't want witnesses.

***

The Radiant sat halfway down a service alley too narrow for delivery trucks. The sign over the door still worked only in pieces. RADI _ NT. Good enough. A red tube buzzed under the dead letters, painting the metal door the color of old meat.

From the street it looked closed. Closed was the point.

Inside, the light dropped another level. Two freight men in dust-scored coveralls hunched over dominoes at a back table without talking. A woman in a station cleaner's uniform drank beer and watched a muted race feed with the fixed concentration of someone trying not to go home yet. The speaker over the shelf coughed out trumpet through static. The room smelled like citrus cleaner losing a fight with spilled liquor.

Hector Moro stood behind the bar polishing the same glass too long.

He'd gone gray in the way some men did when the color didn't so much leave as fade under the dirt. Narrow shoulders. Liver spots on the backs of both hands. His shirt sleeves were rolled to the forearms, and on his right little finger he wore a green plastic ring the size and color of hard candy. Children's junk, the kind that came out of a vending machine for two woolongs. He turned it around the knuckle with his thumb while he worked, not enough to seem like nerves if you didn't know what nerves looked like.

He looked up when the door shut, and the glass stopped moving in his hand.

"Thought you were dead," he said.

Spike took the nearest stool. "Didn't take."

Hector set the glass down carefully. "Yeah. I can see that."

"You serving or just judging?"

"Mostly judging." Hector reached for a bottle anyway. He poured two fingers into a chipped glass and slid it over.

Spike took a swallow. It tasted like somebody had stored it in radiator parts. He set the glass down.

Hector huffed once through his nose, short and sour. "You don't come here for years and then walk in like you stepped out for a smoke."

"You want me to do an entrance?"

"No."

"Good."

The speaker crackled. One of the dominoes clicked against wood in the back. Hector picked the glass up again and resumed polishing, but his attention stayed on Spike the way a man watched a stove he'd stopped trusting.

Spike let the first minute go by without touching the subject. Hector respected patience more than pressure, and anyway there was no point pretending this was urgent in the dramatic sense. If the young pilot had found the locker, she had already been ahead of him for a while.

Hector finally said, "You using your own face on purpose these days?"

"It's what I had on me."

"Cute."

Spike drank again. "Nobody's said cute to me in a long time."

"That's because most people improve with age."

There it was. A little more acid than the room called for. Fear, then, aimed less at Spike himself than at the era Spike had dragged in with him.

He took the folded manifest from his jacket, opened it on the bar between them, and laid a finger on the line item.

Hector didn't look at the paper right away. He looked at Spike, then at the door, then at the silent race feed, then back at Spike.

"No," he said.

"You didn't even read it."

"I don't need to."

"Humor me."

"That habit got people killed."

"So did yours."

Hector's mouth twitched at that. He leaned in at last, glasses sliding down the bridge of his nose, and scanned the route header. The color left his face by degrees. He set the glass aside and put both hands on the bar as if the paper might tip.

"Where'd you get this?" he asked.

"Locker."

"Yours?"

"Used to be."

Hector looked at the note in blue ink. The green plastic ring stopped turning under his thumb.

"Hell," he said.

Spike watched him. "You know it."

"I know the route family."

"That's not what I asked."

Hector kept reading. His eyes moved down the monthly dates, the handling note, the receiving marks. "Somebody came through asking about this."

Spike's hand stayed where it was on the bar. "When?"

"Couple days ago. Maybe three. I've had a week."

"Young woman."

Hector looked up sharply. "So you do know."

"I know enough to ask."

"Then why're you asking me?"

"Because enough isn't the same as useful."

Hector pushed his glasses back up and reached for the bottle. He poured himself a little, drank it without bothering with a fresh glass, and wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.

"She wasn't syndicate," he said.

"How can you tell?"

"Because I'm still standing here."

Spike let that one pass.

Hector went on. "Syndicate people don't come in patient. They don't sit down and order one beer and ask if my knees are worse in winter now. They don't slide cash across like they're paying tuition. They come in knowing the answer already or not caring what the answer was."

"She paid well."

"Too well." Hector looked offended on principle. "Made the place look respectable."

Spike tipped the manifest toward him. "What'd she ask?"

Hector gave the room another quick scan. The domino men were still lost in their own slow war. The woman at the race feed hadn't turned. He lowered his voice anyway.

"Specific years. South basin feeders. Which loads got consolidated after the customs sweeps started. Who was dispatching cold cargo on arms routes. Names of depots that don't exist anymore. She had dates written down already. Real dates, not fishing."

"And?"

"And she knew enough that lying stupid would've been insulting."

"So you lied to her. Just not stupid."

"I retired, Spike. I serve poison to freight drivers and pretend I don't hear gambling debts." Hector's hand went flat on the bar. "I told her what I told her."

Spike looked at him over the rim of his glass and let that sit.

Hector scratched his jaw. The plastic ring flashed once in the low light. "She had paperwork."

Spike turned the glass on the bar. The liquor left a wet ring on the wood.

"I mean real paperwork," Hector said, like Spike had argued. "Route sheets. Clinic order numbers. Copies of — not a lot, but enough." He stopped. "One old transport abstract with your dispatch initials still on it."

Three block strokes, fast and ugly. Spike could see them without seeing the paper. He used to throw those initials anywhere a reefer problem needed to stop being his problem.

Spike said nothing.

Hector mistook that for surprise. "Yeah. She knew who she was asking around, even if she didn't say your name out loud."

"Did she say any name?"

"She never gave me hers."

"Mine."

Hector hesitated.

"Hector."

"I said it first," Hector said. "By accident."

Spike's gaze didn't change. On the paper between them the courier mark leaned the same way it had in the locker, low loop, drag at the end. Same hand over months. He had filed it under repetitive paperwork. That was getting harder.

Hector shifted, irritated with himself. "She put a paper down with — the route codes. Lin's old freight codes. The numbers, I mean. Anybody who worked the basin would've known whose they were." He rubbed his mouth. "I looked at the paper and said nobody's used those since Lin Shao-Feng was moving half the damn basin. Just said it. Like I was talking about somebody who wasn't in the room." He stared at his hand on the bar. "Then I wanted to bite my tongue off."

"You still have terrible instincts."

"You being here proves they haven't killed me yet."

He couldn't argue with that.

Spike took another drink. The whiskey had not improved. "What'd she do when you said it?"

"Nothing dramatic. That's what got me. She just nodded. Like she'd filled in one more square on a form. Then she asked about couriers."

That made Spike look up from the glass.

Hector saw it. "Yeah. She came in asking about couriers, not bosses or shooters or who owned what strip of dust. The people nobody asks about unless they already know where the bodies are."

Spike's voice stayed flat. "Which couriers."

"At first all of them on the Tharsis-Hellas line from that period. Then one in particular."

He reached for the manifest but stopped short of touching the note. "This route. These dates. She wanted to know who was carrying the clinic case when it wasn't moved by truck all the way through. Who did the last leg. Who signed the handoff."

Spike tapped the repeated slash mark on the confirmation sheet. "This one."

"Yeah."

He turned the page with one finger. Halfway down the handling sheet a pencil note sat in the margin, something he had skimmed past in the locker because it looked like dock noise: HOLD LAST LEG UNTIL 1900. His own approval mark sat under it, hard enough to bite the next page. He had not asked why.

"You know it?"

Hector didn't answer right away. He poured himself another little swallow, changed his mind, and shoved the bottle away instead. "I knew her."

Spike waited.

Hector kept his eyes on the paper. "Ayane."

The name sat there between them. Simple. Two short syllables. No help in it.

"Just Ayane?" Spike asked.

"Just Ayane to me. Kozuki on paper, when there was paper." Hector picked up the towel again, put it down. "She ran this route."

"I can see that."

"Off and on for years. She didn't do it because she loved freight." Hector snorted once. "Nobody does. She showed up on time, didn't skim, took the night legs when everybody else was whining about the basin roads. That was it."

Night legs. The penciled hold again. Nineteen hundred. He could hear a dispatcher years back calling one basin courier difficult because she would not take the clinic case before dark. Spike had signed under that complaint too and gone on with his night.

"Syndicate?"

"Choice didn't have much to do with it." Hector's mouth tightened. "Depending on how generous you were feeling, you might not even call it affiliation. She moved things because cash work was cash work and because after her husband got killed she didn't have the luxury of — " He waved a hand. "Principles people could see."

Spike looked back at the route sheets. The drag at the end of the courier mark made a different kind of sense now. End of shift. End of daylight. Someone hurrying because home was not optional.

"What husband?" he said.

"Dead one." Hector rubbed a thumb over the plastic ring until the skin around it whitened. "I don't know the whole story. Nobody down at my level ever does. Word was her man had mixed himself up with cops and syndicate both, which is — " He turned the ring. "One of those things that doesn't go well for anybody. Then he was gone and she had a kid and clinic bills and a face that made everybody think she was tougher than she was. That's what I heard."

Kid.

That word caught worse than husband had.

Spike set his glass down. "How old?"

Hector blinked, maybe at the question, maybe at the fact that Spike had asked one not strictly operational.

"The kid?" he said.

"Yeah."

"I don't know. Little enough that she talked about school pickups like they were emergency extractions. Old enough to draw, I guess."

That brought the pencil note all the way into focus. It was pickup time.

"Draw."

Hector finally touched the edge of the bar and pointed toward the bowl by the till. Paper matchbooks sat there under a film of dust, each printed with the bar's faded starburst logo.

"She used to steal those," he said. "One every trip. Thought I didn't notice. Said her kid liked the blank backs for doodling. Ships mostly. Once she showed me one by accident. Had booster flames bigger than the ship. Kid had ambitions."

Spike looked at the bowl.

"You remember that," he said.

"I remember weird things." Hector shrugged one shoulder. "Maybe because nobody ever asked me anything important after. Maybe because she always apologized when she took one. Who apologizes for a matchbook?"

The dominoes clicked again from the back table. Somebody on the speaker hit a trumpet note so thin it sounded like wire.

Spike said, "Quiet, you said."

"Yeah. Quiet." Hector glanced toward the woman at the race feed, checking himself. "She saved her words. If you asked her something, she'd answer straight. She just didn't waste a lot around it. Came in with dust on her boots and one sleeve usually dirty with coolant or freezer gel. Never sat with the loud ones. If the jukebox was playing too much garbage she'd unplug it without making a speech."

Spike looked at the third confirmation sheet. A thumb smear clouded the carbon near the courier slash. He remembered cursing it because it meant the clerk had to recopy the line. Coolant or freezer gel. Her sleeve, maybe. One more thing he had noticed only as damage to paperwork.

"Reliable."

"As hell." Hector's expression went strange for a second, somewhere between respect and annoyance. "That kind of reliable where you stop noticing the person because the job keeps getting done. If the clinic case had to get across and the road wasn't full of militia idiots, you could bet she'd make it. Men above her loved people like that. They got to count on them without learning anything."

Spike's fingers rested on the page. He didn't move them.

Hector looked at him and saw something. Remorse would've been too generous. But Spike was paying attention now.

"Money wasn't it with her," Hector said, quieter now. "That girl who came through. The young one." He picked up a glass, put it down without washing it. "She kept circling the break in the route. Which month. Which handoff. Blame speeches weren't the point."

Spike lifted his eyes. "You answer that?"

Hector's jaw worked. "I told her what I knew." He paused like he was going to explain why and then didn't. "She had the papers. She had the dates. She sat right there for two hours with one beer warming in front of her and I kept thinking she'll leave, nobody sits that long, but she — " He rubbed both hands over his face. "People like that don't stop. That's the thing."

Spike could picture it too easily: one hand around a sweating glass, the other flattening forms the way he had flattened them in the locker. In the canyon the pilot had looked amused. Maybe he had read stamina wrong.

"What'd she look like."

Hector thought about it. "Sharp face. Dark hair tied back with whatever she had on hand, more practical than neat. Mechanics' hands. Cuts on the knuckles, grease sunk into the lines no soap ever gets. Young. Younger than she'd want to seem. And tired in a specific way." He tapped under one eye. "Long-time tired. The kind that gets organized."

The pilot, then. Or close enough. Spike had only seen her in flashes through canyon glare and cockpit glass, but the edges fit.

"She ask about me?" he said.

"Indirectly." Hector waited. Spike drank instead of asking. "She asked who had authority to piggyback cold medical loads on arms shipments. I said authority is a generous word for what you people had." He spread his hands. "She said fine, who signed off. She had the papers, Spike."

"And you gave her my alias."

"By then I was mostly confirming what she knew."

"That's how it always starts."

Hector's eyes sharpened. "You want to get mad at me, fine. But she didn't get this from me, Spike. She already had it. You left it where somebody could find it."

Spike looked at the papers on the bar and didn't argue.

He folded one corner of the manifest over and flattened it again. "What happened to Ayane."

Hector leaned back. For the first time since Spike had walked in, he looked old in a way that had nothing to do with gray hair or spots on the hands. It was the look of a man being asked for an ending he had never bothered to earn.

"I don't know," he said.

"You knew her for years and you don't know."

"That's how years work down there. People run a route until they don't. A man gets shot, a clinic closes, somebody remarries, somebody ODs, somebody gets religion, somebody gets buried in a dust slide. You think every disappearance comes with paperwork?"

"No."

"Then don't ask like that."

Spike let the rebuke land. Hector deserved one. Probably more.

Hector sighed and dragged the towel off his shoulder. "Last I heard, she was still doing basin work and still trying to keep one foot out of the real ugly stuff. Which means she was already in it up to the knee. After that? Nothing. Maybe she got sick. Maybe she found a better line. Maybe there wasn't a better line." He looked at the note again. "Nobody sends bulletins around when a courier disappears. They just stop making the board."

"And the kid."

"I told you. I don't know." Hector's voice softened despite himself. "I never met the kid. I met the matchbooks and the stories about school and one time she bitched for five straight minutes because a teacher sent home a note saying the kid needed better pencils. Ayane said if the school wanted better pencils they could start paying courier rates. That's what I've got."

More than a route sheet now. More than a line item. Barely, but enough.

Spike finished the whiskey and set the glass down harder than he meant to. The cheap liquor bit up through his nose.

"You got anything else?" he asked.

"No."

"Think."

"I've been thinking since she came in, and it hasn't improved anything." Hector rubbed both hands over his face. "She asked one more thing."

Spike waited.

"She asked whether the clinic cargo ever rode without the weapons convoy."

"Did it."

"Only when your system was functioning."

Your system.

Hector heard it too. He winced but didn't take it back.

"I said no," he went on. "I said those clinic boxes made it because the guns were paying for the road and the guards and the bribed inspectors. I said if the convoy stopped, everything tucked inside it got stranded with the rest. She didn't say anything after that for a while."

Spike felt the paper edge under his thumb.

"Then what," he said.

"Then she thanked me. Left cash under the glass. Took one of the matchbooks. And before she went out the door she turned around and asked if I thought the men who ran routes ever knew what they were carrying."

Spike didn't move.

"What'd you tell her?" he asked.

Hector's mouth worked. "I said — " He stopped. Started again. "I told her the smart ones knew enough. That's what I said. That they always — " He rubbed the back of his neck. "That they'd gotten real good at not calling it that."

Spike didn't answer. The room stayed small around them.

At the back table a domino slapped down hard enough to make the woman at the race feed glance over, annoyed, then go back to her horses. Somebody laughed once in the alley outside and kept walking.

Hector took the manifest, folded it closed with more care than he had shown it at the start, and pushed it back across the bar.

"Drink up and go home," he said. "Or go wherever you people call home. I don't want this decade in here any longer than I need to."

"You close soon?"

"For you? Maybe."

"You always were hospitable."

"And you always did mistake bad habits for charm."

Spike almost said something back and didn't. There wasn't much point. Hector had already given him everything he had worth taking.

He slipped the folded papers into his jacket. "If she comes back."

"Yeah?"

"Call me."

Hector stared. "You got a number now?"

Spike took the receipt pad, wrote a comm frequency, and tore the strip off. Hector took it, looked at the numbers, and tucked them under the till without comment.

"If she comes back," Hector said, "I might call. If I think it helps."

"That's the idea."

"No," Hector said. "Helping you has never been the idea."

Spike stood, left enough cash to cover the drink and Hector's annoyance, and should've walked out then. Instead he sat back down when Hector moved to the far end of the bar and busied himself with the register.

The whiskey glass was empty. He gestured at it.

"One more."

Hector looked over. "You trying to punish yourself or me?"

"Depends what you've got left."

Hector poured anyway.

The second glass wasn't any better. Spike drank it slower.

***

Hector did his closing in pieces around him, not asking him to leave again but not pretending company either. He wiped tables. Turned the race feed off. Kicked the domino players out with the kind of insults that came from repetition, not feeling. The woman in the cleaner's uniform finished her beer and left without looking at Spike. The speaker died. The room got quieter but not calmer. Quiet in a bar after last call was just another kind of exposure.

By the time Hector disappeared into the back room with the day's cash box, Spike had the place to himself except for the humming fridge and the alley noise coming thin through the door.

He unfolded the manifest again.

The bar light was worse than the locker light. Everything on the page went yellow at the edges. The blue ink of the note stayed blue.

Ayane.

He tried, against his better judgment, to hang a face on the name.

Nothing stable came. A dark braid that might've belonged to somebody else. Hands with coolant dried in the lines. A shoulder in a cheap jacket, turned away. Every attempt felt stolen from women he had passed on docks without looking at long enough.

So he quit trying.

What stuck was the work.

Truck three on the south basin feeder because its reefer plug had been patched and held voltage better than the others. Night departures because daytime heat ate the coolant faster even in autumn. Guards in the front cab because the militia boys liked to stop the middle vehicles first. Pay the checkpoint at Marker Twelve in cash, not product, because the local commander had a brother with a pharmacy and got sentimental about medicine but not enough to let anything through for free.

He could remember the dock light on the cold-case latch. One guard smoking clove trash and bitching about battery draw. Spike taking the clipboard from him, writing THIRD TRUCK on the margin, then STOP WHINING under that, and shoving it back before the man finished talking.

He put two fingers on the dates. Twenty-eight. Thirty-one. Twenty-nine.

One month carried the pencil note: HOLD LAST LEG UNTIL 1900. Another had the courier slash dragged thin at the tail where the pen skipped. On one sheet the carbon had smeared under a thumbprint. At the time he had hated the mess. Too sloppy. Too human on the page. He had made a clerk recopy worse.

The whiskey went bitter halfway down his tongue.

He could hear himself on the dock, younger and cleaner, telling a dispatcher nobody was dumb enough to hit an arms convoy for medicine. He had liked how efficient it sounded. Then he had initialed the cold case into the same convoy and gone back to thinking about fuel.

That was the part he could not get around. His hand. His initials. The cold box riding beside gun parts because he had decided the road existed and that was enough.

He leaned back and looked around the empty room.

The Radiant had always been built for people who wanted to be overlooked. Low bulbs. No windows. Tables scarred beyond noticing one more scar. Even the starburst logo on the matchbooks had faded into something that looked more like a wound than a sun.

He turned the glass in his fingers. The cheap liquor clung to the sides in sluggish sheets. Somewhere in the back Hector dropped something metal and swore at it with real feeling. Spike almost smiled.

Then he looked at the bowl of matchbooks by the till.

One every trip, Hector had said.

He reached over and took one. The cardboard felt soft from old humidity. The front was printed with the bar's tired starburst. On the back there was nothing but a blank cream rectangle and a brown smudge from the stack.

He set the matchbook beside the manifest and stared at both.

He could see a kid leaning over the blank back with a dull pencil, making the fire too big because children usually got scale wrong. Or maybe kids got scale exactly right when they were hoping hard enough.

Borrowing grief would've been a lie. They'd never known each other. He probably would have passed her on a dock and remembered the reefer unit before her face.

He folded the matchbook open and shut once. The striker rasped softly.

Know enough.

He had known the cargo needed cold. He had known the convoy would carry it faster than any clinic runner could afford. He had known men with rifles made a better escort than mercy. He had known enough to sign.

The part where missing the name was supposed to count as innocence was dead.

On the back wall, the shelf mirror showed him in warped pieces: shoulder, jaw, one eye darker than the other, cigarette hair gone to shadow. A man sitting alone in a bar with old paperwork and a child's drawing surface. He looked less haunted than tired. That seemed fair.

He thought about the question Hector had repeated. Did the men who ran routes ever know what they were carrying.

Know enough.

Hector's answer still sat there. Smart men knew enough.

The taste in his mouth went metallic. He finished the whiskey anyway.

Hector came back out just as Spike was tucking the manifest under his arm.

"You sleeping here?" Hector asked.

"Didn't seem sanitary."

"Nothing about you has ever been sanitary."

Spike held up the matchbook. "Taking this."

Hector squinted at it, then at him. "They're free."

"Didn't ask."

Hector leaned one hip against the bar. Up close his eyes were more bloodshot than tired, and the green plastic ring looked ridiculous in the low light. He caught Spike looking at it and curled his hand in.

"Granddaughter," he said, annoyed. "Before you start."

"Wasn't going to."

"Good. Because you'd be wrong about whatever it was."

Spike slipped the matchbook into the same inner pocket as the manifest. The cardboard tapped once against the folded paper.

Hector heard it. So did Spike.

"You all right?" Hector asked, and then immediately seemed to regret having asked it.

Spike put a hand on the bar top. Sticky varnish. Old grooves under the palm.

"No," he said.

It came out so flat Hector almost missed it.

He didn't push. Part of living this long was knowing when to leave a thing alone.

"Yeah," Hector said after a moment. "Well. Join the club."

Spike left cash under the empty glass. Hector didn't count it in front of him.

At the door Spike stopped.

"If she comes back," he said without turning, "don't lie to her."

Hector made a small dry sound. "Yeah. That's not really what I do."

"Try anyway."

He went out into the alley before Hector could answer.

The air hit cold and thin. Somewhere above the roofs a cargo shuttle passed, low enough that the metal doors along the alley trembled in sequence after it. Red dust lifted, spun, settled again. Mars at night always sounded farther away than it was.

Spike stood there a second with one hand inside his jacket, fingertips resting against the folded manifest and the matchbook through the lining. Light things. Just paper and cardboard. Hardly anything. The port was a long walk off. By the time he got back to the Bebop, it'd be near dawn.

He could feel both of them all the way back to the ship.


# Chapter 9

The Bebop in fuel-save mode sounded different. With the engines down to a careful sip and half the lights cut, the ship stopped pretending to be dignified and let its old bones speak. Coolant ticked in the wall behind the galley. A vent fan worried the same dry bearing every seventh turn. The refrigerator gave a low irritated hum, quit, thought about it, and tried again.

Ed liked the stripped-down version of the ship. Fewer noises meant the hidden ones came forward.
Thin red dust still striped the floor by the couch where Spike had come in before dawn and stood for a while with one hand inside his jacket.

Data made noise too. Not to everybody. To Jet, a screen was a screen until it told him whether he could afford noodles. To Spike, a screen was something you glanced at for exits and trouble and then ignored on purpose. To Faye, a screen was debt, odds, a bill, a face, a lie. To Ed, the different systems all had their own little songs. Bounty archives were stiff and papery, all rustle and clack, like somebody riffling old cards with dry fingers. Port authority ledgers had a tinny beep to them, money-counting sounds. Hospital back-end systems lived in cold blue clicks. Obsolete syndicate freight code came in greasy, low, and red, the kind of sound that stuck to a wrench.

Tonight the songs were everywhere.

Ed had turned the common room floor into a nest. Two portable monitors from the engine room leaned against the couch. One stolen galley stool held a projector. Three hand terminals were spread open in a circle around Ed's bare feet. Thin cables ran under the table, over the arm of the couch, through the handle of a pot Jet had not found missing yet, and into the access panel by the main screen. Ein lay under the table with his nose on Ed's ankle, eyes mostly closed, ears too awake to count as sleeping.

The mysterious ship sat in the middle of all of it, not physically, because that would have been inconvenient, but as a shape. Registry number. Mass profile. Heat bloom. Thruster asymmetry. Custom weld on the starboard vector fin where somebody had patched a crack ugly and permanent instead of pretty and temporary. The ship had a face now. Not the outside face everybody saw behind Mama Yun's, sleek and shut down and rude. The underneath face, the one made of maintenance habits and docking fees and the way a pilot leaned on old systems when she didn't have to.

Ed hummed and dragged one window over another with two toes.

"Hmmmmm. No, no, no. That is fake-boring boring." Ed flicked a freight tax receipt into a trash cluster, pulled a customs wash from Earth into the center, then overlaid that with a security still from Hellas. Same weld scar. Same patched fin. Same ship. "Sneaky ship is still sneaky, but Ed is sneakier."

Ein thumped his tail once.

It had started as four separate piles because adults liked to scatter clues and then walk around injured that nobody understood their injuries. A stiff old bounty with a younger Jet face sat in one pile, yellowed and mean. The weird old syndicate handshake sat in another, all dead relays and obsolete packet language and names that had been burned out of public systems years ago. Faye's problem had started as not-Ed's-business and then become absolutely Ed's business when Ed noticed a cryogenic facility on Earth appear twice in the same travel history and once in a scrubbed public records cache. The ship's movements were the biggest pile of all, almost two years of berths and fees and fuel buys and one-night stays disguised as ordinary transit.

Individually the piles looked like what adults loved most, coincidence with paperwork.

Together they were beginning to look rude.

Ed pulled up the first clean thread and followed it sideways. The ship had been on Europa eleven months ago under a maintenance contractor shell that existed for six days and never bought parts. It docked two kilometers from an ISSP archive annex, long enough for three human meals and one data pull. The maintenance shell dissolved the next day. Boring if you liked boring. But the annex security software had been updated in layers instead of replaced, which meant one antique log still wrote timestamps in an older format. Ed loved older formats. Older formats got lonely. Lonely things always talked.

The old timestamp matched a dormant query run against a sealed records cluster on the same afternoon. Not the contents. Just the shape of the ask. Deep file. Restricted background. Personnel cross-reference. The kind of ask that brushed the dust off old names.

Ed rolled onto one hip and grinned at the old bounty photo. "Hello, old grump-face baby Jet."

The file photo looked enough like Jet to be funny and different enough to be offensive. More hair. Less beard. Same expression of a man who'd already had enough and had years left to prove it.

Footsteps sounded in the corridor. Heavy, unhurried, one rubber sole scuffing on the turn because Jet's left boot wore down there every time.

Jet came in carrying a mug big enough to be a threat. He was wearing sleep pants, a gray undershirt, and the expression of a man who had woken up because his ship had betrayed him. He stopped in the doorway and looked at the cable running across the galley threshold.

"If I die because you wired the coffee pot to the floor, I'm haunting you," he said.

"Good morning, very haunted Jet. Please step over Saturn."

Jet squinted down. "That's a charging cable."

"Today it's Saturn."

He stepped over it anyway. Jet always counted sugar taps into his coffee when he was half-awake, and even now he gave the spoon three absent little knocks against the mug before drinking. One, two, three. Enough. His beard on the right side had mashed flat from sleep. Ed noticed these things because everybody made patterns whether they wanted to or not.

Jet looked at the main screen, then at the monitors on the floor. "You've been up all night?"

"No, no. Ed was up all night. Different child."

"Right." Jet drank again. "Anything useful?"

Ed waggled one hand. "Useful is a grown-up word. Weird is a better word. Weird is stronger. Weird can lift more."

Jet stared at the old bounty photo for a fraction too long, then looked away like he'd meant to the whole time. "You say that about everything."

"Not about soap."

"You said it about the bathroom filter."

"Bathroom filter was weird." Ed pointed a toe at the Europa logs. "This is weirder. Ship goes to Europa, not for repairs, not for cargo, not for romance, because Europa is a bad place for romance, and then somebody asks a question in an ISSP archive that brushes exactly the right dust."

Jet went still in the way adults did when they didn't want children to know they were paying attention. It never worked. His good hand tightened on the mug. The metal fingers of the other flexed once and went quiet.

"Could be any archive pull," he said.

"Mmm, could be. Could also be the exact day the old sleepy bounty hiccuped and came back to life." Ed tapped the screen and let the dates stack themselves. Europa berth. Archive query. Reactivated bounty paperwork. "Same afternoon. Same sneaky ship. Same pilot with too much free time."

Jet let out air through his nose. "Don't say anything to the others yet."

Ed looked up. "Jet says that about everything too."

"Ed."

There it was, the father-voice without the name. Not angry. Just heavy.

Ed let it sit there a second. Then Ed nodded once because this part was still only one quarter of the rude shape. "Fine. Temporary silence. Not permanent silence. Permanent silence is how everybody gets itchy."

Jet rubbed a thumb against the mug handle. The flattened side of his beard bothered him now; he scratched at it with the back of his wrist and failed to fix it. "You eat anything?"

"Three crackers and an idea."

"Those are not the same food group."

"Depends on the idea."

Jet made a sound that wasn't quite a laugh and wasn't quite approval either. "I'll make something in a minute. Move the monitor off the stove before I forget and boil it."

"That would be educational."

"For me, maybe."

He moved toward the galley, stepping around the cables with annoyed care. Halfway there he paused and looked back at the Europa screen. His mouth did a small hard thing, a line tightening and then pretending it had always been that shape. Then he kept moving.

Ed filed that too.

By the time Jet had water heating and onions in the pan, Ed had pulled the second thread hard enough to make sparks. The syndicate frequency had always been wrong in an interesting way. Too clean for scavengers, too playful for a real dead network waking up. Now it had company. Six months before the ship appeared at Mama Yun's, the same transponder signature had ghosted over a dead freight relay on Mars, out in a district so chewed up by redevelopment and demolition that most city maps pretended it wasn't there anymore. An old logistics hub had sat there once. Most of it was gone now. Concrete, twisted dock arms, one cracked relay mast nobody bothered to haul away because the scrap metal wasn't worth the permit.

The ship had no reason to ping the dead mast. No practical one. There was nothing there to call back. But the pilot had pinged it anyway, twice, ten seconds apart, like tapping a glass to hear the note.

Spike shuffled in while Ed was listening to the relay packet for the fourth time.

He looked like he'd slept on the couch because he had. One side of his hair had been flattened and then rebelled into sharper angles. He had his cigarettes tucked into the breast pocket of his shirt and one sock on, which made him look less cool than he probably believed. Spike in the morning always stole the first cold thing that looked edible, and tonight's victim was a heel of bread Jet had left on a plate. He took it without asking, tore off a bite, and glanced at the nearest screen with one open eye.

"You find the owner's shopping list yet?" he asked.

"No shopping list. Old ghost tower instead." Ed replayed the dead-relay ping. "Listen."

Spike listened because he couldn't help it. He wouldn't admit that part. His face didn't change much, but his chewing slowed. That was enough.

"Old freight code," he said.

"Mmmhmm. Very old. Sticky old. Red old."

"You got colors for code now?"

Ed looked at him in outrage. "Now? Ed has always had colors. Spike is just late."

Spike leaned one shoulder on the galley bulkhead. "All right. What's the point?"

"Point is ship goes to dead place and knocks on dead door. Knock knock."

"Could be checking old hardware."

"Could be," Ed said cheerfully. "Then why fake the packet like somebody who knows the door used to matter?"

Spike took another bite of bread. "Maybe it did matter."

There was a lot inside that maybe, packed down flat and hidden under the lazy tone. Ed heard it. Ed heard everything if it sat still long enough.

"Mmm," Ed said, and did not push because pushing adults too early just made them roll under furniture.

Jet, at the stove, pointed the knife in Spike's direction without turning around. "If you're awake enough to stand there, you're awake enough to cut peppers."

"No, I'm not."

"Funny, because your hands work."

"Terrible rumor."

Jet shoved the cutting board down the counter with more force than the distance required. Spike caught it and, after pretending to think about not helping, started slicing. Thin, fast, exact. He always cut vegetables like he was proving something to nobody.

The ship's Earth route took longer because Earth systems were a mess on purpose and by neglect. Half the planet still ran on patched layers over disaster over military salvage over charity over fraud. Data there hid in plain sight by being ugly. Ed loved ugly data even more than lonely data. Ugly data never expected company.

The cryogenic facility appeared first as a maintenance invoice for a coolant pump that was never installed. Then as a security-camera reflection in a service tunnel window, just the nose of the ship and that crooked weld on the fin. Then as a gate toll using the same six-day shell company trick the Europa trip had used, only on Earth the shell had been dressed as medical waste hauling instead of repair work.

Three visits in fourteen months. Not enough to be a commuter. Too many to be casual.

Faye came in on the third cryo-facility hit, tying her hair up with one hand and still wearing the sleep mask pushed onto her forehead like a second set of eyebrows. She had pillow marks on one cheek. Those vanished the second she saw other people and remembered to be Faye.

"If that's breakfast, I'm filing a complaint," she said, eyeing the pan.

"Get in line," Jet said.

Faye plucked a pepper slice off the cutting board before Spike could stop her. "Why are there wires in my walking space?"

"Because Ed is making a giant brain."

"Make it somewhere else."

"No."

Faye followed a cable to the main screen and saw the cryogenic facility schematic. Her face didn't freeze. It didn't do anything that obvious. She just stopped reaching for a second pepper.

"What's that?" she asked.

Ed brightened automatically. "Cold-sleep castle. Sneaky ship went there three times. Same false company. Same hull. Same very ugly practical lying."

"Lots of people go to medical facilities," Faye said. "Some of them even have paperwork."

Ed rolled onto elbows and peered at the security still. "Lots of people don't bounce through a service entrance registered to a shell that dissolves before breakfast. Lots of people don't repeat exactly the same fake in three jurisdictions because they think nobody's looking sideways."

Faye gave a little shrug, too loose to be real. "Then somebody was looking for medicine."

Spike's knife slowed for a second, then picked up again.

Jet said, without turning, "Faye."

"What?" She crossed her arms. "I'm saying medical places do medical things. Wild theory."

Her voice had the extra edges it grew when she wanted distance. Ed marked that too. Faye's hand, the one not tucked under her elbow, went up to her collarbone and stopped itself halfway. Then she hooked a thumb under the mask instead, like that had been the plan all along.

Four piles. Three adults pretending the piles were separate because adults liked separate pain. Separate pain felt private. Private felt safe. Safe was a game people played until a kid with enough terminals ruined it.

Ed grinned so hard cheeks got involved. The shape was getting clearer now. Not a straight line. Straight lines were for bureaucrats and bullets. This was a web. Better. Ruder.

The orphanage showed up last because the orphanage had never expected anybody to care. No syndicate firewall. No ISSP archive protocols. No hospital compliance layer. Just a little Earth charity network patched together from grant money, secondhand hardware, and a local administrator who reused passwords and never updated image caches because image caches took up room. The pilot had visited six months ago. Paid a records fee in cash. Signed a paper ledger with a name the scanner blurred into static because the scanner was garbage. Asked for archived intake material from a sequence of years that narrowed, narrowed again, then landed on one file.

Ed found the access trail by accident first, then on purpose. A dock camera two streets over had caught the ship at dawn, half hidden behind a municipal water tower. The angle showed almost nothing. Wing root. Tail. The cut of the nose. Enough. Then the charity server showed a manual record retrieval from the same morning. Then the ancient admin cache coughed up a thumbnail that never should have survived, a waiting room, two plastic chairs, a wall painted cheerful once and then not again, and beyond the open office door a filing cabinet with the old label strip still stuck crooked.

Ed stopped moving.

Jet's onions hissed. Spike's knife tapped the board. Faye said something about coffee maybe, but the words were far away.

The thumbnail was tiny. Bad color. Bad light. The kind of picture adults looked at and dismissed because it wasn't useful enough. Ed didn't need more than that. The paint around the office door had been a green that always looked damp no matter how dry the wall was. One of the chairs had a split seam in the vinyl where yellow foam pushed out like a tongue. In the reflected glass of the office window, barely there, a line on the ceiling ran from the center beam toward the dormitory side in a bend Ed knew by heart.

That was the place.

Not home. Ed did not have a home before the Bebop in the way Jet meant home when he checked the pantry twice and the engine room once before sleep. But the orphanage was the place with the cracked ceiling and the hot metal swings and the smell of bleach on rainy afternoons and the little patch of dirt by the fence where nothing grew right.

Ed heard the blood in Ed's ears for one strange second. It sounded white, not red.

"Oh," Ed said.

Nobody looked over yet. It had been too quiet to register as danger.

Ed opened the record access log. Another dead-simple system. Date. Fee. Administrator ID. Requested file sequence. One sequence ended in a number Ed had not thought about in years because Ed usually didn't think about years at all. Years were for adults who saved things in boxes.

Ein had lifted his head now. He crawled out from under the table and pressed against Ed's side.

Ed looked at the tiny waiting room thumbnail again. "I know this one," Ed said.

This time Jet turned. "Know what one?"

Ed swallowed. Very annoying physical reflex. "Old Ceiling House."

Spike left the knife on the board with half a pepper still under it.

Faye frowned. "What are you talking about?"

Ed pointed at the thumbnail and because the words came out wrong in third person, came out too bouncy, Ed had to reach for straighter ones. "I lived there."

Faye's mouth opened for something sharper and came up empty.

Jet took one step away from the stove, leaving the onions to hiss on their own. "Ed."

He said it low. No orders in it. Just the name.

Ed hated that almost as much as pity. But it wasn't pity. It was attention, plain and heavy.

"The ship went there six months ago," Ed said, still looking at the screen because the screen was easier than faces. "Pilot paid cash and pulled intake records. Mine. Probably. Almost definitely mine. Ninety-six point..." Ed stopped. Numbers were safer too, which was exactly why they had become useless. "Mine."

For a second the ship answered for them: pan hiss, refrigerator grumble, Ein breathing against Ed's leg.

The onions started to burn. Jet smelled them before he saw them. "Ah, shit," he said to the pan, turning back hard enough to bump the counter with his hip. He scraped the darkening onions across the metal in short angry strokes, then shoved the heat lower. The ordinary motion helped. That was a Jet thing too. If pain showed up, he found something to stir.

Ed looked from the orphanage log to the Europa archive, from that to the dead relay, from that to the cryogenic facility. Four piles. Four adults, if Ed counted Ed as adult-adjacent, which on the Bebop depended mostly on the mess involved.

The shape did not snap cleanly yet. It leaned. It suggested. It stood in the corner with its hands in its pockets and acted like Ed should already know its name.

Too neat for random. Too crooked for simple.

Maybe a route.

Maybe a dare.

If it was planned, the pilot had been carrying them around in her head for a long time.

"Ohhhhhh," Ed said, longer this time, because delight and alarm were neighbors and Ed had never seen a reason to move them apart. "You sneaky, sneaky pilot lady. Maybe not caught. Maybe chased. Need more data for medium-fast conclusions."

Spike lit a cigarette off the stove burner without asking. Jet opened his mouth to object and then gave up because there were larger structural failures in the room.

Faye pushed the sleep mask up into her hair. "What medium-fast?"

Ed was already gathering terminals, unplugging one monitor, dragging the projector by its handle. The shock had not gone away. It had simply joined the work. That was easier.

"Meeting," Ed announced. "Common room emergency of pattern. Very educational. Bring eyeballs."

Jet looked around at the room they'd been standing in. "We are in the common room."

Ed pointed at the tangle of screens, the pan on the stove, the knife, the bread heel, the cigarette, the orphanage thumbnail, all of it. "No, this is pre-meeting chaos. Real meeting needs arrangement."

Spike exhaled smoke toward the vent. "He's got a point."

"Don't encourage this."

"Too late."

Ed scooped up the orphanage screen last and held it closer for one quick private second. Green paint. Split chair. Crooked label strip. Somebody had gone there and asked for Ed by the trail Ed left in paper as a child. Somebody had opened the old box and looked inside.

That was not funny.

The rest of it still could be.

***

By the time Jet had relocated breakfast to bowls and given up on saving the onions, the Bebop's common room had stopped looking habitable and started looking like Ed's brain had staged a coup.

String crossed the room in red, yellow, blue, and one length of fishing line Ed had stolen from Jet's tackle drawer because data deserved more than one texture. The main screen held a star map of the inner system with dots clustered and labeled in Ed's handwriting. Two smaller monitors showed rotating hull stills of the pilot's ship from different ports and one grainy frame from the Hellas chase. The coffee table had disappeared under notes, a dismantled projector housing, three mismatched mugs serving as anchor weights, and Jet's socket wrench set, which was now part of a chart whether Jet liked it or not.

Ein had been recruited as morale and sat in the middle of the floor web with the patient expression of a creature who had accepted that this was his life.

Jet set bowls down with the careful anger of a man who knew fury would only make him step into more string. He had put on a proper shirt but not bothered to button the top two buttons. "If one of you knocks this over, you're cleaning it."

Faye looked at the web, looked at her bowl, then chose the bowl. "I'm not climbing through that on an empty stomach."

"Don't climb through yellow lines," Ed said. "Yellow lines are chronology."

"Why is chronology on the floor?"

"Because time is a trap."

Spike, who had found the one patch of wall not currently in service as a graph, leaned against it and ate standing up. "That's actually the clearest thing he's said all morning."

Jet pointed his spoon at him. "You don't get to encourage the mutiny and then stand there like a houseplant."

"I'm not a houseplant."

"No, you'd have to contribute oxygen."

Faye snorted despite herself. That was useful. Low-level noises kept adults from thinking they were in a drama and doing stupid dramatic things.

Ed hopped onto the couch back, crouched there barefoot and balanced, and clapped twice. "Welcome, welcome, old people and medium-old people and one excellent dog. Today's lesson is called Sneaky Ship Is About All Of You. Subtitle, Nobody Gets To Be Special Alone."

"I hate this already," Faye said.

"Please save hating for the question section."

Jet blew on his coffee. "Start from the top."

"No."

"Ed."

"Top is fake. Top says ship appears, everybody yells, noodle shop, grr, very expensive parking. But that's not where the thing starts." Ed spread both hands at the web. "Thing starts before. Long before. So start is not top. Start is shape."

Jet looked like he wanted to argue. Then he looked at the strings and decided that even demanding linearity in this room was more work than he had fuel for. "Fine. Shape."

Ed beamed. "Excellent. First shape, same ship everywhere. Not similar ship. Not cousin ship. Same rude lady ship with ugly starboard weld and a replacement vent panel bought on Callisto six months before the Hellas chase. See?" Ed flicked a control and the hull stills rotated side by side. Dock camera on Europa. Security reflection on Earth. Fuel station image from Mars. Hellas chase freeze-frame. In each one the starboard fin carried the same crooked patch, too wide at one end, hand-laid, no shop symmetry. "Pilot fixes things for use, not beauty. Good mechanic. Not precious."

Spike's gaze sharpened at that without moving the rest of him. Mechanic's work always got his attention first. Human feelings, less so.

Ed dropped from the couch back to the arm, then to the floor, then popped up again on the other side because using the obvious path was for people with bad knees. "Now, if same ship same ship same ship, then where does same ship go? Lots of boring places. Fuel depots. Cheap berths. One place on Io with terrible coffee according to review aggregators. False noise. Padding. Good padding too. Makes the pattern fuzzy unless you pull on the right corners."

Faye twirled noodles around her fork. "And you pulled on the right corners because..."

"Because Ed is smarter than everyone in this room except maybe Ein and Ein is humble."

Ein's ears went up.

Jet said, "He's not disagreeing."

Ed pointed at the first cluster on the map, a pale gray dot with three lines running off it. "Europa. ISSP archive annex. Maintenance shell company. Six days alive, never buys one actual repair part. Ship parks nearby, somebody runs a deep background query through an antique terminal, and the same afternoon an old bounty with Jet's face and more hair twitches awake."

Jet's spoon stopped halfway to his mouth.

Faye looked from the screen to Jet. "What old bounty?"

Jet drank his coffee instead of answering.

"See?" Ed said, delighted and merciless at once. "Spoon freeze. Coffee dodge. Faye wants details. Good classroom."

"Ed," Jet said.

"Fine, fine. That part can wait." Ed flicked the Europa node smaller before Faye could keep digging. "But pilot on Europa is not asking a normal question. Query shape is broad. Background clusters, cross-reference clusters, all the dusty cabinets around the obvious cabinet."

That did not help Jet's face.

"So she wasn't just pulling one file," Faye said.

Jet set the mug down. "Keep going."

Ed did. "Segment two. Mars red ugly place." Ed tapped the rust-colored node hanging off a broken line. "Dead freight relay in a logistics district so demolished even the maps are tired. Ship pings obsolete code at a mast nobody uses anymore, twice, ten seconds apart. More interesting, the packet knock has the same old-handshake wobble the ship's ID had when it parked behind Mama Yun's."

Spike's bowl paused in his hand.

Jet turned a little. "Meaning?"

Spike took a second too long. "Meaning it wasn't random chatter."

Faye looked at him now. "You know what it was."

Spike shrugged one shoulder. "Address check. You knock on a dead door to prove you knew it used to be alive."

Usually Spike would've thrown a joke over that and kept walking. He let it sit there instead.

Ed rapped knuckles on the bulkhead twice, ten seconds apart. "Exactly. Door remembers your hand."

Faye squinted at the map. "So the ship went to a cop archive and an old syndicate relay on purpose. Great. Somebody likes ugly history."

"Selective ugly history," Ed said. "Important distinction." Ed pulled up the Earth line, a clean blue loop dropping toward the cryogenic facility. "Cold-sleep castle. Three visits in fourteen months. Same six-day shell trick as Europa, only here the shell wears medical-waste clothes. Ship comes in through service access, not front gate. Main records stay clean. Side systems twitch."

Faye had already set the bowl down.

"What side systems?" Jet asked.

"Verification channels. Public-facing dumb layer. Not the frozen-patient vault. The boring doorbell connected to it." Ed spread fingers at the screen. "Something under that identity stack got touched the same weeks the ship showed up."

Faye said, too fast, "Maybe somebody was checking a name."

Then she heard herself. So did everybody else.

Spike looked at her. Jet looked away from her because he was better at mercy than Spike and worse at pretending he wasn't doing it.

Ed pointed at Faye with both hands. "Aha. Bowl down, arms crossed, Spike looking now. Excellent."

"Shut up," Faye said, but without heat. She crossed her arms harder. "I mean it. Shut up."

Ed nodded because that was data too. "Yes, ma'am with the very obvious secret."

The strings on the floor had felt funny a minute ago. Improvised. Bossy. A little stupid. Now they looked like what they were, lines pulled tight through places nobody on the Bebop talked about unless cornered.

"And then," Ed said more quietly, ducking under a yellow line to the last blue-green square, "there's this one."

Jet's attention had never really left Ed since pre-meeting chaos. Now it narrowed further.

Ed touched the square with one finger.

"This one is mine."

Faye stopped with the fork halfway back to the bowl. Jet's hand stayed around his mug. Spike came off the wall a little.

Faye frowned. "Yours how?"

Ed kept a finger on the square because touching a thing made it easier to say. "Earth orphanage. Same settlement. Pilot goes there six months ago, pays records fee in cash, requests intake material from exactly the years Ed was there. Old camera catches the ship two streets over. Charity server saves a thumbnail because charity server is trash." Ed swallowed and hated it again. "She pulled my file."

Jet put his bowl down very carefully. "Ed."

He kept saying the name like maybe tone alone could make the floor safer.

Spike straightened a fraction from the wall.

Faye's anger came first because anger was easier to hold than anything else. "How the hell did she even know where to look?"

"That," Ed said, voice picking speed back up because stopping was worse, "is the right question. Also why Ed says deliberate, not sloppy."

Jet rubbed the bridge of his nose. "Sell me."

Ed spun one monitor toward them. Europa archive log. Dead relay packet. Cryo service reflection. Orphanage cache thumbnail. Four ugly little survivors from four different systems. "At each real stop she uses the oldest still-working side door and leaves one crumb there. Not the main logs. Those get washed or buried. The attached junk room. Europa antique terminal. Mars dead relay. Earth service layer. Orphanage image cache. Four systems, same habit."

Faye said, "That could still be coincidence."

"Once, maybe. Twice if the universe is drunk." Ed jabbed a finger at the screens one by one. "Four times in four different infrastructures? No. Accidents don't keep choosing the oldest door."

Spike said, "And the parking-lot signal."

Ed whirled on him, triumphant. "Yes! First day behind Mama Yun's, the ship ID came back dirty in the same way. Not broken-broken. Old-door broken. Same obsolete handshake wobble as the relay knock. She rang the bell there too. That's why Ed started sniffing at all."

Jet was staring at the four screens now, not at the string web. The math had moved from possibility into motive. "She planted the route and then she tripped the alarm."

"Medium-sized alarm," Ed corrected. "Enough to get Ed chewing. Not enough to make anybody run to ISSP and ruin the game."

Faye looked at Ed. "How'd she know there was an Ed to chew on it?"

Jet answered before Ed could. "She sat behind Yun's long enough, half the block would've told her who lives on this tub. Yun would've told her what we owe, what we eat, and that we've got a barefoot net diver climbing on the hull at stupid hours."

"Tragic fame," Ed said.

Faye's mouth tightened. "So she didn't need to know two years ago. She just needed the trail ready before she picked the fight."

"Yes," Ed said. "Homework first. Provocation later."

Jet asked, "How long?"

Ed tapped the timeline at the bottom of the main screen. "Twenty-three months from first meaningful stop to noodle-shop insult. Maybe longer if paper research came before ship research. Definite travel pattern across almost two years."

Faye looked at the line and then away from it. "Two years."

Spike crossed to the monitor with the dead relay packet. He didn't touch it. Just stood in front of it, cigarette forgotten between his fingers while ash lengthened. "Show me Europa again."

Ed did. Then the cryo reflection. Then the orphanage thumbnail. Then all four together.

Jet's voice had lost the leftover breakfast irritation and the parental nagging and most of its volume. It came out like a mechanic checking torque. "You said the Europa query was broad. Broad enough to brush whatever lived around that old bounty."

"Yep. Not one tidy question." Ed searched the room for an honest object, found Jet's spice tin, and opened it. Dried pepper flakes. Cumin dust. One bay leaf. "More like this. Reach in for one thing, come back smelling like all the others."

Jet stared at the spice tin, then at the map.

Faye said, "So what, exactly? She collects ruined paperwork? She likes messed-up people?"

"Maybe," Spike said.

Everybody looked at him because Spike volunteering a maybe was basically a speech.

He flicked ash into an empty mug. "Or she's tracing how the ship got built."

Jet frowned. "What?"

Spike kept his eyes on the data, not on them. "Not the actual ship. This one." He jerked the cigarette toward the floor, meaning the Bebop, the room, the people in it, the whole patched-together arrangement they lived inside and refused to define. "If she visited those places on purpose, she wasn't just digging. She was mapping."

Ed felt a thrill go through Ed's ribs, ugly and bright. There it was. The shape with its hands in its pockets finally giving up the trick.

"Mapping," Ed echoed. "Yes. Crew archaeology. Social spaghetti. A whole route built out of old paperwork and dead code and one very nosy lady ship. Not bounty money. Not salvage. Not random snooping. The points where all of you touched the world before Bebop happened."

Faye's hand went to her collarbone and stayed there.

Jet's gaze snagged there and cut away. Spike saw it too and studied the web instead. Faye left her fingers where they were, stubborn about it.

Jet looked back at the orphanage square. "And she included you."

That sounded like fact, which was kinder than comfort.

Ed nodded once.

"Why?" Faye asked.

Not sharp now. Flat. Almost annoyed that the question had come out sounding real.

"Unknown," Ed said. "Pattern says intention. Pattern says patience. Pattern says she wanted us moving after her at her speed. Pattern does not yet say why."

Spike looked at the web. "But it says this wasn't opportunistic."

"Nope," Ed said. "This was homework."

Nobody laughed.

Jet's cybernetic fingers clicked once against the table, a reflex he usually didn't notice. "Twenty-three months," he said again, more to himself than anybody.

Faye stared at the cryogenic-facility node like she could burn a hole through it by refusing to blink. "Two years of this and she parks in our spot like she's picking a fight over lunch."

"Maybe because that's how you get people out of the house," Ed said.

"Not people," Spike said.

That was when he stood up straight.

It was such a small change, two inches maybe, the lazy angle gone out of his spine. But Spike lived in slouches and doorframes and sideways leans. Seeing him vertical was like hearing a glass crack in another room and realizing it was your glass.

Jet saw it too. Faye did. Even Ein lifted his head.

Spike crushed the cigarette out in a saucer. "Us."

The vent fan turned.

The refrigerator hummed, failed, and quieted.

Jet's metal fingers quit clicking. Faye stared at Spike like he'd just named the thing she'd been living inside. Even Ein stayed upright, ears tipped forward.

Jet looked at the Europa node a long time. When he spoke again, the words came out flat and practical, which meant they had cost him. "If she wants us following the trail, fine. Tomorrow we stop following it blind. We answer the first knock and make her come the rest of the way."

Faye's eyes cut to him. "With what?"

Jet didn't look away from the screen. "Whatever she went looking for on Europa, she can come look at it in person."

Nobody jumped on him. On the Bebop that was already halfway to a plan.

Faye looked him over once, slow and merciless. "You're gonna need a hat."

"No," Jet said immediately.

Spike, still standing straight, said, "Yeah."

Faye set her bowl down and headed for storage before Jet could come up with a second no.


# Chapter 10

Jet hated the hat before he even put it on.

It sat in the middle of the Bebop's galley table like an accusation: cheap brown felt, pinched brim, one side going soft where somebody had once sweated through it and then left it in a locker with mothballs and a leaking bottle of engine degreaser. Faye had found it in storage and announced, with the confidence of a woman who had never had to wear a disguise that made her look like an off-brand cardsharp, that it was perfect.

"It says failed youth with ideas above his station," she said.

Jet stared at it. "That's not helpful."

"It isn't wrong."

"It's the hat," Spike said from the couch. "Maybe the hat is the criminal."

Ed, upside down on top of the backrest with a tablet hanging from one hand, peered at the hat as if it might produce useful data if insulted long enough. "Takara hat. Hosuke hat. Mystery hat with bad decisions in the sweatband."

Jet picked it up between two fingers. The felt left dust on his skin. The thing smelled like damp cardboard and the back end of a pawn shop. "The point," he said, "is not the hat."

"Then we're already in trouble," Faye said.

Outside the common room windows Mars burned under a thin amber haze. They had set the Bebop down in a wash half a kilometer from the settlement, where the hull blended well enough with rusted boulders and old mining scrap that nobody passing overhead would pay it much mind. The settlement itself sprawled along the rim beyond them, sheet-metal roofs and patched domes and a market strip wrapped around a refueling tower like barnacles around a pipe. Every gust of wind sent red dust sliding over the viewport in a dry hiss.

They were close enough to smell other people's generators when the air system cycled.

Jet set the hat down and braced both hands on the table. His cybernetic fingers clicked softly against the laminate. On the screen behind him Ed had pulled up a settlement map and painted routes across it in colors that meant something to Ed and only partly to the adults. Yellow for the market. Blue for sight lines. Green for gossip, which in Ed's system was apparently a measurable resource.

"One more time," Jet said.

"One more time," Ed echoed happily, then tapped the screen. A cluster of blinking symbols bloomed around the market square. "Public registry kiosk here. Old access node here. Gambling room here. Bar here. Roof roof roof, nice roof. Mister Spike gets roof. Faye-Faye gets card people and bar people and lying people. Jet-person gets hat and face and old criminal vibes. Ed gets the skies and the wires and all the boops. Ein gets moral support."

Ein barked once from under the table and resumed worrying at the edge of a frayed seat cushion.

"The pilot has been following old lines," Jet said. He kept his voice practical because that was the only way to say any of this without tasting the grit of it. "Places that matter to one of us. She used Daichi Kozuki's access codes to wake up an old bounty. She knows how to trip wires in systems nobody's touched in years. So we put one in front of her. We make it look like Hosuke Takara surfaced here, in a settlement where he used to move freight and scam inventory tallies, and if she's watching the same channels, she comes to take a look."

Spike's gaze stayed on the ceiling. "And if she already knows what you look like now?"

"Then she already knows what I look like now."

"Strong plan."

Jet ignored him. He was better at ignoring Spike when Spike was horizontal. "I don't need her to fall in love with the disguise. I need her to know the name is in play and come close enough to make a move."

Faye folded her arms. "And my part in this elegant operation is what, exactly? Because if you say 'be charming,' I'm leaving."

"Your part is to do what you always do when money might be in the room," Jet said. "Work the social layer. Bars, card tables, gossip. Find out who's new, who's paying cash, who's asking the wrong questions."

"So be charming."

"Be useful."

"I hate the way you say that like they're different."

"They're different," Spike said.

Faye turned on the couch hard enough to make the springs complain. "You know, if you want to help, you can keep helping from all the way over there."

Spike lifted one shoulder. "That's the roof's job."

Jet pointed at him with the hat. "You're on overwatch. West roofline above the market. If she comes in on foot, you see her. If she comes in with settlement security on her tail, you see that too. And if you get bored and wander off, I'm feeding your share to the dog."

"You keep threatening me with a thing the dog is going to do anyway."

Ed zoomed the map until the market became a jumble of rectangles and lane markers. "Pilot-person likes old routes and secondary exits and places where people can vanish into noodle steam. Ed likes all those things too. Ed has covered the sky and the old frequencies and the boring frequencies and the frequencies that pretend to be boring. If the ship says hello, Ed will hear it."

"Good," Jet said.

Nobody said what everyone in the room knew: they had tried speed already. They had tried surprise and formation flying and Spike's reflexes and Faye's nerve and Ed's eyes. The girl in the sleek ship had run them through a canyon, let them chase a freight drone for twenty minutes, and sent them back with an eight-thousand-woolong traffic summons they could not afford. Now they were trying theater.

It irritated Jet that theater suited this mess as well as it did.

He took the hat and pulled it on. Faye put a hand over her mouth like she was trying, unsuccessfully, not to laugh.

"No," she said. "Absolutely not."

"What?"

"You look like you're about to sell counterfeit heater filters out of a backpack."

"That was sort of the idea," Spike said.

"No, the idea was younger. Petty. This is middle-aged fraud."

Jet reached for the old jacket folded over the chair. It was brown too, because apparently his teenage self had once believed in blending into the woodwork by looking like the woodwork. The shoulders didn't sit right anymore. At seventeen he had been broad already, but not like this. Not with the prosthetic arm. Not with years on him. He looked at the mirror over the sink and saw something worse than a disguise that did not work.

He saw continuity.

Hosuke Takara had not gone away. He had put on a badge, later a bounty license, later a cook's apron in a ship galley, and none of it had erased the kid who knew which loading clerks could be bribed and which security doors latched badly in cold weather. Jet had spent a long time telling himself those versions had come one after the other, clean as train cars. The last few weeks had made a mess of that.

He tugged the hat lower. "We're burning daylight."
