She had a nicotine patch cut cleanly in half and stuck under one ear, as if she did not trust herself with the full dose. Her gray hair was pinned up with a hex key. Every few minutes she slid a racing form out from under the till, marked something with a golf pencil, and tucked it away again.

"No."

"Kitchen's dead anyway."

"Whiskey."

She poured without asking which kind because this was not that kind of place. Jet put cash on the bar. She looked at it, looked at him, took one bill, and pushed the rest back with two fingers.

"That's enough."

"For this?"

"For one."

Jet took the glass. "One's fine."

It was not good whiskey. That was fine too. He was not there for good. He was there because if he stayed on the Bebop he would start fixing things. The galley cabinet that stuck in damp weather. The coffee maker that needed the contact points cleaned. The loose hinge on Ed's console chair. He would do them one after another and call it productivity and never sit still long enough for the question to get where it wanted to go.

He drank a little. The bar's ceiling had been patched with three mismatched tiles and one square of sheet metal bolted in place crooked. Water had found the edges anyway. He counted the stains again.

Not was I responsible.

Responsibility, at least, could be argued. You could lay out the facts. He had not known Daichi Kozuki. He had never met the man, never taken his statement, never handed him a wire, never sent him into a room. His partner had handled informants off-book. That much Donovan had made clear. Jet could build a case for himself if he wanted to. Wrong department. Wrong desk. Wrong day. No direct hand on the knife.

The other question had no paperwork in it.

Did I choose not to see.

He drank again and tasted metal from the cheap sink water they had cut the bottle with, or maybe that was his own mouth. Across the room one of the dockworkers slapped cards onto the table and laughed through a cough that sounded wet enough to worry a doctor and cheap enough not to see one. The screen above the bar flashed a score. Someone swore at it without any real commitment. The room kept going. Jet kept looking up.

There had been things.

His partner coming in too pleased with himself on weeks no case had broken right. Expense on a salary that did not support expense. An informant file closed too fast. A report routed around him with a shrug and a, don't worry about it, Black, this one came down from upstairs. The specific look precinct cops got when they knew something dirty was moving through the building and had already decided whether it was their problem. Jet had known that look. He had worn it himself.

Not every time. Not always. But enough.

He had told himself the job was big and ugly and built on compromise. He had told himself you picked the piece in front of you and worked it clean because if you stood in the hallway screaming about every rotten thing in the structure nothing got done. He had told himself that staying useful was better than staying pure. He had told himself a lot of things that were not lies exactly. None of them were hard to say. They fit in the mouth too well.

The bartender drifted back down the bar, wiped a ring of moisture that was not there, then wiped it again.

"You keep staring at that ceiling," she said. "I already called about the tiles twice. Joro says he's got a guy."

Jet looked at his drink. "I wasn't thinking about the tiles."

"Suit yourself. That one drips straight into your glass when it rains."

She moved away. Jet went back to the ceiling. Eight stains, or nine. The count kept shifting.

He saw his old precinct as clearly as if he had stepped out the back door and walked there instead. The bad fluorescent light that flattened everybody's face. The coffee that tasted like pennies. The cracked vinyl on the interview room chairs. His partner with a tie loosened and a grin too easy for the hour, saying he'd take the informant meet because Jet had paperwork. Jet believing him because Jet did have paperwork. Jet being grateful, maybe, for one less dirty errand in a job made out of dirty errands. That gratitude was what kept snagging.

If he had pushed harder, if he had said no, if he had gone where he was not invited, he might have found something. He might have found Daichi Kozuki alive. Or nothing at all. There was no clean way to know now. He did not deserve even that mercy; the dead did not come back and tell you where the road forked.

Faye's voice came back to him from the Bebop common room, flat and mean because flat and mean was safer than the other thing. You two are standing here talking routes and precincts like that's the whole story. Like a dead woman borrowing my face is just paperwork. Jet had snapped back at her, because of course he had. Spike had gone hard and cold in that way of his that made a room feel smaller. Ed had gone quiet in the middle of it, which had been the worst part. They had all seen too much in one another at once, and none of them knew how to do that politely.

He lifted the glass, found it empty, set it down.

"Another?" the bartender asked.

Jet thought about the walk back. About climbing the Bebop's ramp with whiskey in him and words in his throat and no place to put either. He thought about staying until the freight shift turned over and the room got meaner. He thought about not going back at all, which lasted less than a second because there was nowhere else to go.

"Yeah," he said.

She poured. This time her half patch had started to peel at one corner. She smoothed it back with the side of her thumb.

"You work nights?" Jet asked, mostly to hear a human voice say something ordinary.

"I work when Joro's gout acts up." She jerked her chin toward a door marked PRIVATE. "Which is most nights when it rains and all nights when he thinks it might rain."

"That bad?"

"He says his feet can feel weather from two districts over. I say he's lazy."

Jet gave a short breath that might have been a laugh if anybody had asked too much of it.

"You from freight?" she asked.

"Used to be police."

She nodded once, with no visible interest either way. "That so."

"Used to be." He heard how he was repeating himself and stopped.

She set a bowl of pretzels near his elbow without comment. One was burned black on one side. Jet took it because refusing would make it strange. Salt hit his tongue. He had not realized he was hungry.

The thing about corruption, the real thing, was how little room it needed.

He saw one night clear as if the whiskey had polished it. Holding had been loud enough to shake the squad-room glass. He had just come back from helping drag two drunks apart, shirt cuff streaked with somebody else's blood and his bad knee singing, when his partner slapped a green source-transfer sheet across Jet's desk hard enough to tip coffee into the margin.

"Need your initials."

Jet had looked down. KOZUKI, DAICHI in the contact box. Under that, WIFE plus DEPENDENT CHILD. The motel field was blank. The escort field was blank too. A pink clinic voucher was stapled to the back crooked, cheap paper gone soft where somebody had held it too long. His partner said the source was jumpy, did not want uniforms near the family, wanted them moved quiet.

Jet had looked up. His partner had freight dust dried gray on one trouser knee and a tear at the cuff like he had climbed a fence in a hurry. From the hall came the lieutenant's voice, already sharp with somebody else's problem. A drunk in holding wanted a medic. Another wanted his phone call. The whole building wanted now.

"No escort?" Jet had asked.

His partner had shrugged. "You want him burned or you want him breathing? Sign it."

Jet had signed. Two blue initials, quick and irritated. The records clerk took the sheet through the slot and stamped SPECIAL HANDLING. Jet watched his partner peel the address attachment off before the packet went to copy. He saw the missing second page, the blank motel line, the wife and kid being moved into nowhere official. Then the lieutenant barked his name again and Jet stood up and went because the screaming drunk in holding was loud and immediate and the quiet wrong thing on his desk could still be called somebody else's call if he moved fast enough.

He had called that realism. Mature judgment. Knowing how the world worked.

Maybe it had been cowardice with a badge clipped over it.

He shut his eyes. When he opened them the ceiling stains had blurred together. Ten, maybe. Eleven. He had lost count.

Now Daichi came to him as the pressure of that blue ink drying under bad fluorescent light. Not a man he could picture. A name on a form. A wife and child translated into boxes. An address sheet his partner removed in front of him while Jet chose the louder fire. Somewhere that child had grown up inside the space Jet left open.

Jet rubbed at his beard. The skin under it felt raw.

Had he chosen not to see?

Yes. At least that once. Probably more than once. Not because he wanted anyone dead. Not because he was bought. Because the job had offered him a smaller ugliness he could handle right now, and he had taken it. He had let urgency do the moral thinking for him and still called himself one of the good ones.

It was not a dramatic answer. It stuck anyway.

He finished the second whiskey and stood. His stool legs scraped. One of the card players glanced over and then away.

"You leaving already?" the bartender asked.

"Yeah."

"Step's warped by the door. Watch it on the way out."

Jet raised two fingers off the bar. "Thanks."

Outside, the freight district air had the cold iron bite Mars got after midnight. The sky over Tharsis was the color of rust washed thin. At the corner, under a dead ad panel for a clinic that had probably closed years ago, Jet took out his communicator and called Donovan's desk.

The line rang long enough to make quitting easy. Then the machine picked up.

"It's Black," Jet said. "I need the Kozuki transfer sheet from that week. The original, not the cleaned summary. If it still exists, call me."

He ended the call before he could trim the request down into something safer, shoved the communicator back into his pocket, and started walking toward the paid docking lot, back toward the ship, back toward the people he had just spent an hour avoiding and would spend the rest of the night listening for anyway.

***

Spike took the stairs to the roof because the elevator smelled like piss and because climbing felt closer to earning the air.

The building was nine stories of old concrete and patched conduit. Up top, the city spread in stacked layers of light and dust, service roads threading between warehouse blocks, beacon towers blinking red through the haze. Beyond that there was only dark land and the wrong-colored sky. Mars never did stars right from inside a settlement. The atmosphere held on to too much dust, too much old industry, too much reflected light. The sky looked used.

He sat on the edge and let his legs hang. His shoes knocked once against the wall below and then went still. He lit a cigarette, drew deep, watched the tip burn down clean.

Ayane's name still did not attach to much. Hector's bar. Quiet, reliable, had a kid. Ran the Tharsis-Hellas route. A few facts and a note on a manifest, She needed this to live, written in a hand Spike had not recognized then and would not recognize now if it passed him on the street. If he shut his eyes he could not picture her. He got a blurred shape in coveralls, maybe, because that was what couriers wore, and then Julia pushed through anyway. Julia leaning against a doorframe with her hair pinned up loose. Julia in a red room. Julia turning her head half an inch before she smiled, like she was checking whether the smile was worth the trouble.

Julia came back easy. Vicious too. Mao. Annie. Men he had hit. Men who had tried to hit him. Rooms he had bled in. Guns. The color of certain hallways. The smell of gun oil in the back of a warehouse at three in the morning. His memory was excellent for the things he had believed were his story.

Ayane was not his story. Not then.

He flicked ash into the dark. It caught the rooftop wind and disappeared before it reached the alley.

He could still see the south depot loading bay. Blue plastic wrap shining under bad light. His alias on a clipboard in block letters. LIN SHAO-FENG. Long crates with the serials filed off. Two white cold boxes strapped on top with red tape and KEEP COLD stamped crooked across the side. He had signed for all of it. Counted seals. Waved the loader through.

One night a latch sprang when a pallet hit the floor too hard. Foil packs spilled out and slid into the heel of his shoe. A dock kid in orange gloves crouched to scoop them up and said, "Medicine?"

Spike had said, "Probably," because the truck was late and the bay chief was already yelling.

Then he had handed the clipboard back and kept the line moving.

He knew now what those boxes might have been. Something for Ayane. Something for somebody like Ayane. A body that needed a shipment to come through on time or it started eating itself. He could picture the boxes better than he could picture her.

He took another drag. The smoke scraped his throat. Good.

He did not feel grief for Ayane. He felt the work. The crate edge in his palms. The marker stink off the manifest. Julia laughing once because the alias on his papers sounded fake even for syndicate business. He felt how easy it had been to keep the pen moving and not ask. Work was what he had.

Julia had been enough to make him leave. Her face. Vicious's face. One woman, one man, one mess close enough to touch. He only looked that far out.

He looked up. No stars. Just haze and the reflected underglow of a city that could not stop burning fuel long enough to let the sky through.

If one of those boxes had been marked IMMUNOSUPPRESSANTS in letters big enough for him to read from across the room, would it have changed anything then?

Maybe not.

He hated how quickly the answer came.

He might have asked who they were for. Or laughed and said somebody upstairs had weird priorities. Or done exactly what he had always done, moved the shipment and kept breathing.

He remembered what he had actually looked at on those runs: dock numbers. Seal colors. Which men were armed. Whether the route stayed clear. The rest had gone past him.

He smoked to the filter and lit another from the first. Below him a tram hissed around a turn. Somewhere in the next block a couple were fighting with the window open, the words flattened by distance into pure cadence: accusation, defense, accusation, a long stretch of both talking at once. Life, continuing badly.

He thought about the Bebop without wanting to. Jet, probably not sleeping. Faye, somewhere being furious in private. Ed, maybe talking to the dog, maybe doing the thing where the silence in the ship got so loud it turned into a game first because thirteen-year-olds were smarter than adults and meaner to themselves. They had all gone separate because that was the only way any of them knew how to handle being seen. Still, he could feel the lines running back to the ship, taut as wire.

Family was too sentimental a word to use on himself. He did not use it. He pulled his communicator from his pocket, thumbed the Bebop channel live, and listened to the carrier hiss for a second.

"I'm out a while," he said. "Leave comms up."

The cigarette ember pulsed between his fingers. Julia, Ayane, the routes, the note on the manifest, the unseen hand arranging a trail through all of it. He could not retrofit compassion he had not felt. He could not decide, after the fact, to have been a different man.

He could stop saying it had nothing to do with him.

He ground the second cigarette out on the concrete beside him, then immediately regretted dirtying the roof of a building he did not live in. That was new too. He rubbed at the black mark with the sole of his shoe until it was only a smudge.

When he finally stood, his knees clicked. He put both hands in his pockets and looked out over the city one more time, as if the right answer might be written somewhere in warehouse lights.

It was not.

He headed down the stairs with the Bebop channel still open.

***

Faye chose the bath house because you could pay cash, because the lockers still took coins instead of identity taps, and because in a room full of naked women nobody had the energy to make a spectacle out of another body.

The lobby was all cracked cream tile and fogged plastic plants. A woman behind the desk wore a transparent green visor and had painted tiny silver stars around one eye, half of them flaking off. She was reading a folded romance magazine with the cover torn away. Without looking up she slid a locker key across the counter and said, "Towel costs extra."

"Of course it does," Faye said.

"Soap too."

"You're a criminal."

"Bath house," the woman said, still reading. "Close enough."

Faye paid. The key was attached to a square of chipped yellow plastic stamped 24. In the changing room she undressed without hurry and without performance, folding her clothes more neatly than she would have admitted to anybody. The air was warm enough to make the skin along her arms pebble anyway. Somebody down the row was humming off-key while she wrestled with a compression wrap around one knee. Somebody else had brought in a baby and was apologizing to nobody in particular every time it squeaked. The room smelled like steam, mineral salts, cheap soap, and wet wood.

She caught herself in the mirror over the sinks, saw the usual pieces line up. Dark hair damp at the temples from the heat. Green eyes. Collarbones like hooks. The body she lived in, the body other people noticed first, the body she had learned how to use because everything else about her past came in fragments or bills.

She turned, looked over her shoulder.

No scar on the left shoulder blade.

There never had been. She knew that now. Knew it with records and dates and a clerk's apology and Miriam's tired certainty and the stupid little fact of a blue dress at a gambling table on Ganymede while Faye Valentine, the original issue, had been on ice with a tube in her throat and debt accruing interest in her name.

She went through the swinging door into the bath room. Heat hit her face. The main pool was sunk below the tile lip, mineral water gone cloudy with steam. A row of shower heads ticked along one wall. Women sat around the edges with their shoulders shining wet, talking about food prices, somebody's son, a fungus problem in a hydroponics tower. Nothing anybody said mattered. It was perfect.

Faye eased down the steps until the water took her at the ribs, the waist, the chest. The heat was almost painful for a second. Then it turned into weightlessness. She let herself drift away from the edge and tipped her head back so her ears slid under. The room dulled at once. Voices became blurs. Water held her up without asking questions.

This was why she had come.

Not for cleanliness. The Bebop had water. Intermittent, rusty at the start of a cycle, but water. She had come because floating erased some of the hard border of the body. In water you got less exact. Less pinned down by seams and straps and the fit of your own skin. Tonight that seemed useful.

Steam had filmed the cedar planks above into a dark sheen. Condensation gathered, fattened, fell in slow drops that dimpled the surface near her cheek. Every ripple took the lights overhead and broke them into moving coins. When she let her eyes go soft, her reflection came apart and reassembled ten times a second.

The room kept tilting on one fact: she was not the only version. Amnesia had been one kind of damage. Bad enough. Waking up in the future broke, hunted by debt collectors, with a name and a face and almost nothing under them except paperwork and instinct. She had already spent years living with the fact that there had been a previous self she could not access. Some schoolgirl, some daughter, some woman who liked or hated things that meant nothing to the person Faye had become. One ghost already. Manageable, if you stayed mean and kept moving.

Another woman had been walking around under her name while Faye herself was not even awake to object. Another woman cashing chips, signing forms, tipping waitresses, maybe getting drunk, maybe getting laid, maybe laughing in a voice Miriam could still hear clear enough to swear by. Another Faye Valentine entering rooms and leaving impressions. More real, in some places, than the original.

If Miriam walked into this bath room right now, she might recognize Faye. Or hesitate because memory had been carrying a scar Faye did not have. Or say, no, not you, the other one. The one in the blue dress. The one who tipped. The one who touched her collarbone just so.

Faye's hand moved before she could stop it. Two fingers to the notch above her sternum.

She snatched it away.

It had been hers. Hadn't it? Or maybe it had only become hers because somebody at some point told her she used to do it, or because she saw it in a record, or because she watched someone watching her and learned a habit from the watching. How many things in a person were original? How many were theft? How many were just repetition with the source lost?

"Too hot?" a woman near the edge asked.

Faye turned her head. The speaker was maybe sixty, maybe forty hard years made into sixty, with a waterproof hearing aid tucked behind one ear and a tattoo of a tiny wrench on the inside of her wrist. She was holding a soaked washcloth over the back of her neck.

"No."

"You had a look." The woman wrung the washcloth out on the tile edge. "They changed something in the feed last month. It's hotter now, or the minerals are different. My knees can always tell."

"My first time here."

"Ah." She resettled the cloth on her neck. "It's better after eight. Fewer kids. Tuesdays are the worst."

Faye didn't answer. The woman went back to soaking. It helped, weirdly. Someone with opinions about mineral feeds and crowd schedules, none of it aimed at Faye. Ordinary talk, going nowhere. Proof that not everything in the world had arranged itself around her crisis.

An attendant padded through in rubber sandals with a skimmer net, fishing hair from the corners. In the hot room beyond, somebody laughed so hard she started coughing. A child splashed, got hushed, splashed again. Faye drifted another few inches and let the back of her head bump tile.

The woman who had used her name was dead.

Faye still did not have a real shape for her. Not a name, not one that felt solid, just a dead woman whose body had rejected itself because the drugs keeping it in line had stopped coming regular. Faye tried to imagine anger clean enough to hold that fact and failed. The theft was real. The violation was real. Somebody had sold Faye's life out from under her while she slept and another woman had climbed into it because the alternative was what, exactly, dying sooner? Staying herself and getting turned away from clinics? Taking her child by the hand and admitting there was no legal version of her left that could survive?

It was wrong, and that still did not make it simple.

She hated that it was not simple.

She slid lower until the water touched her lower lip. Warm mineral taste. A little bitter. Not unpleasant. The ceiling above her blurred again. Three planks darker than the others where steam had gotten in deep. Beyond them, structure. Pipes. Nails. Somebody's idea of support.

She wondered what the woman had looked like when nobody was calling her Faye Valentine. Not the blue dress. Not the gambling table. Not the role. Did she walk differently alone? Did she peel the name off at night like makeup, or had it stuck after enough years that even in private she turned when somebody said Faye?

Other people knew pieces of that answer.

The vertigo wasn't just the fact of another woman living under her name. It was other people carrying memories of her that belonged to somebody else. Miriam's certainty. The young woman's face in the courtyard. Clerks, gamblers, maybe lovers, maybe nurses, maybe a landlord somewhere with a file folder full of receipts. Pieces of her spread out in strangers' heads. Faye did not own even the mystery of herself anymore.

Her throat tightened, and she hated that too because it was such a cheap body response, so generic, so available to every bad actress in every bad serial. She swallowed it down and focused instead on the tile against her shoulder blade, the real one, the scarless one, cool above the waterline. Specific. This body. This room. This night.

When she stood, water sheeted off her in one heavy curtain. The room air hit cold. She walked back to the showers and rinsed mineral salt from her skin. At the sinks she caught herself in the mirror again, blurred by steam this time, doubled by the fogged edge of old glass.

For one nasty second she saw two women.

Then the image cleared. Just her. Which was somehow not reassuring.

She dressed slowly. In the locker room a teenage girl with a shaved head was painting careful black lines around her eyes using the mirror on a compact that kept sticking half closed. The woman with the wrapped knee had moved on to arguing with herself about bus schedules. The baby had finally gone quiet. Life again, ordinary and indifferent.

At the desk, the attendant held out Faye's change without looking away from her magazine.

"You forgot your soap refund," she said.

"There's a soap refund?"

"If you don't steal it." She counted coins from a dish without looking up. "There's a tracking form now. Management's idea."

Faye took the coins. "You're wasting your talents here."

"Yeah, well." She licked a thumb and turned a page.

Outside, the cold bit hard after the steam. Faye tucked her hands under her arms and stood for a moment under the bath house awning, not moving, just watching her breath turn to vapor and vanish. She had come for anonymity and warmth and a place to set her body down without anybody wanting anything from it. She had gotten that. She had also gotten the clear sick fact that her name had once belonged to more than one life and might always.

The Bebop channel was already live when she thumbed her communicator open. Wind hissed. Somebody's silence sat on the other end.

"I'm coming back," she said. "Nobody talk to me tonight."

She cut the line and started toward the Redtail. Halfway across the lot she found herself touching her collarbone again, gentler this time, as if checking whether the gesture still fit.

***

The Bebop sounded different when three people were gone.

Not quieter exactly. Ships were never quiet. There was always a fan somewhere half a bearing away from failure, a coolant line ticking in the walls, the low refrigerator hum from the galley, the occasional deep settling thunk from the hull as temperature shifted outside. But the human noise was missing. No Jet opening cabinets too hard because he was pretending not to be angry. No Spike's shoes dragging in that lazy way that was only half laziness. No Faye talking to herself like she wanted witnesses. The ship's mechanical sounds had all the space.

Ed sat backward in the common room chair with knees hooked over the back and goggles pushed up into wild hair. Ein lay under the table in a loaf shape, ears alert, eyes open. Data windows floated across the main screen in blue stacks and green threads and one aggressively cheerful orange map Ed had made for no reason except the data liked orange tonight. In one corner a narrow comm bar glowed live. Spike had opened the ship channel and left it there. Faye had cut in once from somewhere wet and echoing to say nobody talk to her tonight, then gone away again. Every so often wind brushed the mic and was gone.

"See, see, the path-path-path is not random, Ein-buddy-person," Ed said. "Random is one thing and selected is a different thing and grown-up people always think messy means random, but messy counts as a pattern category too, yes it does, yes it does. Mystery-ship person didn't go boop anywhere. She went to all the places and left tiny digital breadcrumbs, tiny tiny crunchy ones, but not too crunchy, because if the breadcrumbs are too crunchy then Jet-person hears them and says why are there crumbs in my console, Edward."

Ein blinked.

Ed swiveled the chair a few degrees with a bare foot. "Jet-person is in beverage sadness mode. Spike-person is on a roof doing cigarette geometry. Faye-person is in hot water probably insulting someone whose job is to hand out towels. Adults are very good at feelings if the feeling is anger. Adults are extremely weird if the feeling is another feeling wearing anger as a hat."

Ein's ears tipped forward at the word hot. Or at the word towel. Hard to say.

"Yes, that too."

Ed tapped a key. The orange map dissolved into a file tree, then an access log, then a scan of orphanage intake records from Earth that looked like they had been digitized by somebody who hated the concept of preservation. Lines skewed. Dates clipped at the edges. Metadata glued on later by a different system with different ideas. The whole archive smelled, in Ed's imagination, like dust and old plastic and school lunch steam.

Ed had been all over it earlier, fast and bright and proud. Look what the ship did. Look where the pilot went. Look how all the strands make a nice ugly web. The adults had stared and gone pale in different flavors. After that came the chip and the arguing and the not-arguing after the argument, which was worse.

Now it was just Ed and Ein and the record.

"This one is personal data," Ed told the dog, softer. "Not just pattern data. Personal data is sticky."

Ein got up, stretched long enough to make his paws tremble, and put his chin on Ed's ankle.

"No, no, it's okay. Ed is merely observing. Observing is one hundred percent safe and emotionally neutral and definitely not a trick category."

That got no bark at all. Ein had learned skepticism somewhere.

Ed clicked into the access history the pilot had pulled six months earlier. Not because Ed had to. Because the line of thought kept curving back there no matter what other file Ed opened. The access trace was clean. The pilot had known what to ask for and how not to trip alerts. Intake ledger. Placement notes. One medical incident. No outbound deletion, no copy corruption, no vandalism. Just looking. Just reading. Just someone, somewhere on Mars, deciding Edward Wong Hau Pepelu Tivrusky IV belonged on the map with everybody else.

The entry opened.

EDWARD WONG HAU PEPELU TIVRUSKY IV, AGE 7, ADMITTED.

Below that, smaller text with dates and signatures and institutional abbreviations. A line for guardian status. A line for dietary restrictions left blank. A line for observed temperament written by an adult who had not known Ed at all and had still felt entitled to summarize.

Ed looked at the first line and stopped moving.

The ship hummed around the pause. Somewhere aft, a pipe clicked twice as it cooled.

Seven was a number. Ed liked numbers. Numbers behaved. Seven was prime. Seven was clean. Seven was also small, but not baby-small. Old enough to know when you had been put somewhere and nobody had said for how long. Old enough to decide a bunk was not home and might never be. Old enough to learn the sound of institutional shoes in hallways and the smell of bleach on blankets and which adults meant well and which adults meant schedule.

Ed's mouth went dry.

"Hmm," Ed said, but the sound came out thin.

Ein put one paw on Ed's knee.

It was not a dramatic gesture. Dogs did not do drama unless food was involved. It was just weight. Warmth. Contact. Here.

Ed reached down and scratched behind one of Ein's ears, slow enough that the fur shifted in layers under Ed's fingers.

"I was so little," Ed said.

The live comm in the corner gave a soft scrape and then nothing.

The words landed in the room and stayed there.

No third person. No joke. Just the sentence.

Ed kept looking at the record because looking away would make it stranger. Age 7. Admitted. There should have been more, maybe. Some emotional fireworks. Some big cinematic flash of memory. Instead there was a fluorescent hum in the back of Ed's head and the texture of a blanket that had been washed too many times and one meal tray with peas rolling into the compartment for potatoes. Not even a whole day. A few pieces. Memory behaved like bad file recovery. You got fragments and checksum errors and the occasional thing that came back sharper than it had any right to.

Pilot-person had looked at this. Sat somewhere, maybe with grease on her hands from an engine job, maybe with a bad cup of coffee, maybe with a heater rattling nearby, and opened the line about a seven-year-old Ed. Not to hurt, probably. Not exactly. To know. To complete the shape. That was almost weirder.

Adults thought the past was a locked box. Ed had never thought that. The past was active code. Ugly old code, undocumented, still running in the background and throwing errors into the present when you least expected it. The adults on the Bebop spent so much time pretending their background processes were not eating memory. Then a pilot parked in the wrong docking spot and suddenly everybody's hidden programs were visible on the main display.

Ed snorted once, not because it was funny. Because it was true.

"Bad architecture," Ed told Ein.

Ein thumped his tail one time.

Ed closed the intake record, then opened it again, then closed it for real. The common room reflected back from the darkened edge of the screen. Jet's mug still in the sink. Faye's red jacket thrown over the couch arm like she had tried to make it look accidental and failed. One of Spike's cigarettes on the table where Jet would complain about it in the morning if morning happened normally. Evidence everywhere that the ship was full even when it wasn't.

Ed slid off the chair and onto the floor. Ein followed at once, circling once before settling with his side against Ed's leg. From down there the common room ceiling looked lower. Old paint. A hairline seam above the light fixture. Nothing special. Ship ceiling.

Home ceiling, though.

That thought arrived without permission. Ed let it sit.

Ed got up long enough to pad to the entry panel and kill the night lock. Then Ed reached up, killed the main screen, and left only the galley light on. The room went amber and soft around the edges. The Bebop kept making its old mechanical noises, holding together the way it always had, by vibration and patches and stubbornness. The live comm window stayed pinned in one corner, a dim blue line that said somebody was still out there.

Ed leaned against the couch and listened for the others, for the airlock cycle, and for the occasional brush of sound on the open channel, the way you listened for weather through hull metal, not because you could control it, just because you wanted to know when it was getting close.


# Chapter 13

Jet opened the refrigerator and leaned in. The galley light was still on.

Under the amber spill, the shelves offered up their little insults. Half an onion wrapped in a wet paper towel. One egg with a crack running through the shell like a hair in glass. A jar of mustard with more yellow dried around the lid than left inside. Soy sauce. A bell pepper that had gone soft at the stem. No meat. No coffee. No beer. Cold air rolled over Jet's knuckles and smelled faintly of mildew and old scallions. He shut the door before the motor could waste any more power pretending there was something worth cooling.

The traffic ticket was waiting for him on the galley screen, red and patient.

MARS MUNICIPAL ENFORCEMENT
OUTSTANDING VIOLATION
8,000 WOOLONGS
STATUS: OVERDUE

He hit dismiss. The screen chimed and brought it right back.

The Bebop sounded hungover. Hull ticking as the morning cold eased out of the metal. Life support humming in the walls. Somewhere below, a loose vent grille rattled every few seconds with the stubborn regularity of something Jet had meant to fix three days ago and now resented on principle. The whole ship smelled like engine grease, stale air, and the soup he had made two nights ago from the last of the mushrooms and a hope that had not lasted past dinner.

They had all come back at different hours.

Jet knew because he had heard the airlock cycle three separate times after midnight and because nobody was where they usually were this morning. Spike was stretched on the common-room couch without taking off his jacket, one forearm across his eyes, one foot on the floor like he had sat down for a minute and lost the argument with gravity. He smelled of cold rooftop wind and old smoke. Faye had not shown up until almost dawn. He knew that from the sound of her heels in the corridor and the brief sharp smell of public soap when she passed his door. Ed was already up because Ed was almost always up, cross-legged on the floor in front of the main screen with three terminal windows open and Ein leaning against one bony thigh.

Ed looked over as Jet stood there with the refrigerator's failure still in his hand.

"Sad box says no breakfast," Ed announced. "But bounty box says maybe breakfast. Maybe breakfast and also fine-paying. Fine-unpaying. One of those."

On the main screen, a mugshot rotated slowly under municipal graphics. SAL PERETTI. BAIL JUMP. COUNTERFEITING. FRAUDULENT SEALS, TRANSIT PAPERS, CERTIFIED ID CHIPS. REWARD: 30,000 WOOLONGS.

Peretti in the photo looked like the kind of man who ironed a shirt before he got arrested. Narrow face. Black hair combed down too carefully. A burgundy scarf tucked into the collar of a market jacket that had probably cost more than anything Jet was wearing. His mustache was just a shade too dark for the hair over his ears, which meant vanity or panic, maybe both.

Jet squinted at the details scrolling beneath. Last confirmed sighting: Tharsis bazaar district. Possible local protection. Likely armed. Bond posted by a private guarantor with a long record of regrettable judgment. Review petition pending. Municipal docket window: 12:00.

"How fresh?" Jet asked.

"Eight hours if the sleepy municipal people are not lying, which they might be, because sleepy people are chaos goblins." Ed tapped a window with one toe. "Peretti buys fake papers, sells fake papers, makes fake papers, likes the bazaar because everybody there already thinks somebody else is cheating. Natural habitat. Also the thirty's on a timer. Bondsman filed at first bell to get the number cut. If Peretti isn't in intake before noon, some judge gets to decide he's a harmless little paperwork rat with an aunt and a prayer."

Ein thumped his tail once. That was optimism in dog form.

Jet opened a cabinet, found two packets of instant broth, and decided hot salt water counted as leadership under the circumstances. He set the kettle on.

Spike said, from under his arm, "If that's breakfast, put me back to sleep."

Jet did not turn. "You're awake enough to complain. You're awake."

"Tragic."

The corridor door slid open. Faye came in still buttoning her jacket, dark hair damp at the ends, eyes flat from too little sleep or too much thought. Public bath soap clung to her for maybe two seconds before the ship's normal stink took it back. She stopped when she saw the screen.

"Thirty thousand?" she said.

Jet nodded. "If we beat noon."

Faye's mouth tightened. "Pending review. That's not thirty. That's thirty with a fuse on it."

"Yeah."

"That's funny."

Jet turned from the kettle then, because if he kept looking at the red ticket warning he was going to start talking to it. "What's funny is eight thousand overdue to traffic enforcement, a fuel gauge that might as well be decorative, and whatever this is." He held up the bell pepper. It sagged in his fingers. "This is not food. This is a cry for help."

Faye dropped into a chair at the table. "You don't need to sell me on being broke. I live here."

"Do you?" Spike asked without moving his arm.

She looked at him. "Start with me this early and I'll sell your cigarettes."

Spike lowered the arm enough to look back at her with one sleepy eye. "You'd smoke half of them on the way to the buyer."

"I'd charge myself retail."

Faye's chair legs scraped the deck when she sat back. Spike let his forearm fall over his eyes again. Jet poured hot water into four chipped mugs and set them in their usual places. Steam climbed off them. Faye took hers without looking up.

He carried his mug to the table, thumbed the Peretti file larger, and said, "We're taking him anyway."

Faye blew across the surface of what was basically seasoned steam. "Who's we."

Jet looked at the screen again, then at the ticket notice, then at the refrigerator as if it might develop character and solve something for him. "Unless one of you found eight thousand woolongs under a cushion overnight, it's we. If the court cuts him before we book him, then we work faster."

Ed brightened. "Ed found three buttons, half a playing card, and a noodle fossil under the couch. No woolongs. Tragedy."

Spike sat up slowly. The couch gave a tired squeak under him. "Bazaar's a bad place to tail a paranoid guy. Too many doors. Too many cousins."

"I know where the bazaar is," Jet said.

"Do you." Spike rubbed at one eye with the heel of his hand. "Because the last time you took the Bebop into a Tharsis side lane we got a letter from traffic enforcement with a picture."

Jet held up one finger. "We're not doing this."

Faye gave a short laugh that had no humor in it. "That's optimistic."

Jet planted both hands on the table and looked at all of them in turn. He did not want a speech. He especially did not want a family speech, not after last night, not after the week they had been having, not with a mug of hot broth standing in for breakfast like an insult with steam coming off it. What he wanted was simple, practical, mercenary, and therefore maybe possible.

"Thirty on the poster," he said. "Eight of that's already the ticket. If the review doesn't cut him before noon." He looked at the refrigerator. "We catch him, pay the fine, buy food."

Faye stared into her mug. When she set it down, the thin liquid slapped the chipped sides and kept trembling after her hand was still.

Usually that would have been the meeting. He would have said the name, the price, maybe one note about location, and the rest would have arranged itself. Faye took social. Spike took approach. Jet took exits. Ed took comms and the invisible electronic layer under everything else. They had done it a hundred times.

This morning Jet had to say every part out loud.

"Faye," he said, hating how formal it sounded already, "I need you in the stalls. Food row, tea houses, gambling corners if they're open this early. Peretti buys local cover. He doesn't grow it himself. Talk to the vendors, the runners, the people who notice him because he pays late or complains too much. Find out where he's sleeping and who's watching for him."

Faye looked up. "My cut comes off the top."

"We always split it."

"No, Jet. I mean off the top. Before you decide the ship needs twelve gallons of something and a new valve and a replacement screw nobody can live without." She set the mug down. "My share first. In my hand."

Jet felt the old reflex rise in him, the one that wanted to argue about shares and fairness and the invisible accounting by which he fed them all and kept the ship alive while they treated both facts like weather. He shoved it back down because he did not have room for pride with half an onion in the refrigerator.

"Fine," he said. "You get your quarter."

She narrowed her eyes. "A quarter of thirty?"

"A quarter of what we actually get."

"There you go."

Spike took a sip of broth and made a face like the ship itself had offended him. "Inspiring stuff."

Jet ignored him. "Spike. Foot tail. Not roofline unless you have to. Bazaar awnings are rotten and half the catwalk bolts in spice row are original settlement junk. He gets spooked, he'll run for storage or loading access. I want eyes on him before he knows he's being pushed."

Spike leaned back against the couch cushion and crossed one ankle over the other. He looked awake now, which on Spike mostly meant his expression had flattened into attention. "South service lane's no good for the Bebop."

Jet stopped. "I didn't say south service lane."

"You were about to."

Jet hated that he was right. "South service lane gives me loading access and two exits."

"South service lane is six meters on paper and five if the kebab guys put their extra propane out early."

"Then I'll squeeze."

"Then you'll get stuck and he'll walk past your nose."

Faye looked between them. "Can we save the divorce for after lunch."

"We're not married," Jet said.

"Thank God."

Spike did not bother with the joke. "North access. Park wide by the generator bank. You can cover the lane on foot and swing the ship down only if he breaks for the warehouses."

Jet hated that too, because it was better. "North puts the ship farther from the market center."

"Which is why he won't clock it."

Jet rubbed at the scar on his cheek with the heel of his hand. "And if he bolts south?"

Spike shrugged. "Then I push him south and Faye makes noise on the east side."

Faye pointed at him with two fingers. "Make noise means what, exactly."

"Exactly what you're good at."

"You really know how to ask for help."

"I'm not asking."

"Then fuck you."

"Kids," Jet said, louder than he meant to. The word hit the room and lay there. Faye's face shut a little more. Spike's expression did not change at all, which was worse. Jet exhaled through his nose and amended, "We don't have time."

Ed had been listening with bright, shameless interest, chin propped on both hands. "Ed has watcher map," Ed said into the silence. "Two confirmed maybe three. One is red-cap boy on dried-fruit row. He has a school tie in his pocket and keeps touching it when he thinks nobody sees, so probably borrowed cousin labor. One is tea auntie with the gold tooth, lane three. She knits tiny socks between customers and taps her ear when something moves that shouldn't. Third watcher maybe maybe not. Old man at the prayer bead stall with one bad knee and a bad poker face."

Jet took a breath. "Thank you, Ed."

"Ed is very useful."

"Yeah."

The kettle clicked as its base cooled. Somewhere in the corridor, a pipe knocked once and settled.

Jet sat down finally and pulled the bazaar map up from memory more than need, two fingers spreading the grid. "Ed, you stay on comms and camera pulls. I want market feeds, traffic feeds, private security if you can get it, stall cams if you're feeling illegal, and the live court docket. If that review stamps before we have him, I want the exact second."

Ed looked offended. "Ed is always feeling illegal."

That got a sound out of Spike that was almost a laugh.

Jet went on before the room could remember how to breathe. "I'll take the Bebop north, stay wide. He breaks warehouse side, I block. He runs east, I close it. Faye, anything near transit, you call it — don't let him get on anything that moves."

Faye rested one elbow on the table. "And if I get to him first?"

"Call it in."

"He's a paper guy with bad lungs, Jet."

Jet didn't answer right away.

"Just call it in," he said.

She picked at the rim of her mug. "Fine."

Spike set his empty mug down on the floor by the couch instead of on the table like a civilized person. "If he sees me, the chase goes bad fast."

"I know."

"Then don't talk in my ear unless you have something."

Jet almost said since when has that stopped you. He let it go. "Fine."

Ed raised a hand. "Peretti has a Thursday candy habit."

Jet's hand stalled on the back of his chair. Faye blinked at Ed. Spike looked over without lifting anything but his eyes.

Ed blinked. "Fennel seeds from the striped cart on lane six. Sweet breath for a liar's mouth. It's not Thursday, but habits bend around panic. Also he hates garlic sauce. Left two complaints under fake names last month. Ed found reviews."

Faye snorted despite herself. "You hacked kebab reviews."

"Public data is lonely if no one looks at it."

The room eased half an inch. Faye's snort stayed loose in the air, and even Spike's mouth twitched before it went flat again.

Jet pushed away from the table. "Gear up."

The mug in Faye's hand clicked once against the table. Spike rolled forward off the couch and stood, stretching as he went. Faye finished the broth in one annoyed swallow and headed for the corridor to get her sidearm and a jacket with deeper pockets. Ed swung around toward the console, fingers already flying. Ein sneezed once and trotted after the sound of movement, nails clicking over deck plate.

Jet stayed where he was long enough to look at the refrigerator one more time.

Half an onion. One bad egg. A soft pepper. There were people who said money was abstract. Those people had never had to open a door and count the shape of lunch.

He grabbed his coat, killed the ticket alert again, and followed the others out.

* * *

The Tharsis bazaar always felt one loud noise away from collapse.

Canvas roofs sagged over metal frames older than half the people selling under them. Extension cords looped from generator to generator in black knots that would have made any fire inspector cry if Mars had funded enough fire inspectors to cry properly. Charcoal smoke hung low over kebab row. Dust moved underfoot in thin red films, pushed around by boots and cart wheels and the drag of too many coats. Somebody was shouting over the price of dried apricots. Somebody else was shouting because shouting worked even when the price didn't change. Somewhere deeper in the market, a child laughed like a thrown bolt hitting steel.

Faye moved through it in sunglasses and a pale jacket that made her look richer than she was if nobody got close enough to check the hem. Close enough happened all the time. She used that too.

"Lane six, striped cart," Jet said in her ear. "You heard Ed."

"I heard Ed. Try not to narrate my legs while I'm using them."

Jet muttered something that got lost under market noise. Good.

Faye cut between a stall selling bootleg cartridges and a counter stacked with oranges that had been polished harder than the vendor's teeth. She smelled lamb fat, old fryer oil, coffee so burnt it had gone sweet again, cumin, coriander, hot metal, human breath. The bazaar was tight in the shoulders. You did not walk through it so much as let it push you in the direction you had decided to pretend was yours.

The striped cart sat where Ed said it would, white-and-blue awning patched with clear tape. The man behind it was turning skewers with one hand and patting his chest with the other like he had just remembered he owned lungs. He was in his fifties maybe, beard gone mostly gray, a silver thumbnail on his left hand where some kid had painted it and he had never bothered to scrub it off. An inhaler hung from a cord around his neck and bounced against his apron when he moved.

Faye stopped at the counter and gave him the smile she used on men who wanted to feel chosen for something.

"You selling food or smoke," she asked, "because I only have time for one disappointment."

He looked up, took her in, and grinned despite the coughing edge in his chest. "Depends what you order."

"Dealer's choice."

"That is how people get fed organs they did not ask for."

"You always talk like this before noon?"

"Only when I can tell it's being appreciated." He turned a skewer and winced as smoke came back at him. "What do you want?"

Faye leaned on the counter, close enough to suggest interest, not close enough to promise any. "Man in a burgundy scarf. Too pretty to let grease near him. Complains for sport."

The vendor barked out a laugh. "Peretti."

"So you've met."

"Everybody's met. He eats here and then tells me I don't understand restraint." The man touched his inhaler, frowned at it as if it had failed him personally, and lowered his voice. "No garlic sauce, extra napkins, asks if I have filtered water like we're in a hotel."

"Where is he."

The vendor's eyes slid left, then right with the fast market sweep of a man used to checking corners before he lowered his voice. "Why."

"Because his face annoys me."

"That is not a shortage."

Faye took a folded bill from her pocket and set it beneath the edge of a metal tray. It was tea money, smoke money, enough to pay for a favor without dressing it up as trust.

He covered the bill with his hand and sighed through his nose. "He went through tea lane ten minutes ago. Headed toward spice row. Had red-cap boy drifting behind and old Litsa at the samovar watching his back. He's jumpy today. Kept wiping his fingers after he touched the counter. Ink man who hates his own hands dirty. Imagine."

"I don't have to."

"If you find him," the vendor said, then hesitated.

Faye waited.

He shrugged. "Tell him he still owes me for Tuesday. Eight woolongs."

He tucked her bill under the tray with two smoke-stained fingers and turned the skewer before it burned.

Faye smiled without warmth this time. "What's your name."

"Rafi."

"If I see him, I'll mention it."

She pushed off the counter and kept moving before the moment could think it meant anything. "Tea lane. Spice row. You were right."

"I know," Ed sang back at her through static and market noise. "Red-cap boy shifted. Tea auntie also shifted. Peretti is noodle-wriggling. Twenty-six minutes to noon chop."

Spike's voice came in low and flat. "I see red cap."

Jet said, "Where."

"Above dried fruit. Three stalls east of tea lane."

"That's not where Faye is."

"I can count, Jet."

Faye turned at the sound of a tea glass shattering somewhere behind her and caught the movement three bodies ahead. Burgundy scarf. Market jacket. One hand at the collar, the other brushing at his cuff with a folded square of white cloth that came away faintly blue at one corner. Sal Peretti moved like a man trying to look casual in a place that punished effort. He stepped around a puddle, gave a basket of eels a disgusted glance, and paused by the tea lane samovar only long enough for the old woman behind it to tap one finger against her ear.

Litsa, apparently. Sixty if a day. Thick wrists. Gold tooth. Knitting two tiny yellow socks between pours with the indifference of someone who had seen police, gangsters, tax collectors, and drunks do their little dances in front of her for twenty years and found none of them worth dropping a stitch for.

Faye kept walking. If she stopped, the pattern stopped. If the pattern stopped, Peretti's head would turn.

"I've got him," she said.

Jet came back too fast. "Confirm."

She bit down on the first answer that rose in her throat. "Burgundy scarf. Dark mustache dye job. White handkerchief with ink on it. He looks like he filed a complaint against gravity. That enough for you?"

Spike said, "It's him."

The old familiarity of that irritated her more than if he had disagreed.

Peretti took the mouth of spice row without looking behind him. He had the confidence of a man who trusted other people's eyes more than his own. Red-cap boy moved off the dried-fruit stall roof and melted into the crowd. Litsa kept knitting.

Jet said, "I'm moving north access."

There was a burst of engine noise in the distance as the Bebop shifted from idle to offended motion.

Faye shadowed Peretti past sacks of lentils and hanging strips of cured meat. The air changed as spice row took over: warmer, drier, sharper. Paprika. Turmeric. Ground coriander. Cardamom. Cumin thick enough to taste on the back of the tongue before she was close to the warehouse doors.

Peretti stopped at the striped candy cart on lane six.

Ed made a triumphant noise in her ear. "Habit!"

Peretti bought a paper twist of sugared fennel, tipped a few seeds into his mouth, and checked the reflection in a polished samovar side as he chewed. He was not handsome. He was a man with handsome ambitions and the budget to support them badly. His scarf was real silk or wanted to be. The handkerchief was monogrammed S.P. in the corner. He cleaned ink off his fingers with the devotion other men gave religion.

Faye slowed near a spice merchant pretending to argue over fenugreek and watched him without watching him. He lifted his head, suddenly still, eyes tracking past her shoulder. Red-cap boy had reached Litsa. Litsa had looked up from the baby sock. A pulse went through the row.

"He knows," Faye said.

"Push him south," Jet said. "We don't have the clock for subtle."

"I'm not a broom."

"Faye."

But she was already moving.

She cut left and grabbed the elbow of a man carrying a tray of tea glasses. "Sorry, sweetheart," she said, and sent the tray tilting just enough for hot tea to slosh across the lane in front of Peretti's shoes.

He recoiled with a hiss, more offended than alarmed. "Watch it!"

He looked up. Saw her. Saw nothing, maybe. A woman in sunglasses swearing at a tea seller. Market noise. Bad luck. He turned right instead of left.

Right was south.

"He's moving," Spike said.

Faye did not look for him. She knew where he would be because she knew how he hunted. Somewhere above eye level, somewhere lazy-looking and exact.

Red-cap boy tried to angle after Peretti and Faye stepped into his path hard enough to make him bounce back a pace. Up close he was younger than Ed's guess, maybe sixteen, acne under the brim and a school tie stuffed in one pocket of his jacket exactly where Ed said it would be.

"Sorry," Faye said with perfect insincerity.

"Hey-"

"Go home."

He blinked at her, startled enough to miss Peretti's next turn. Then he remembered his job and shoved past. Faye caught his sleeve, twisted, and sent him shoulder-first into a stack of flattened boxes. The whole bundle slithered out from under him with a dry cardboard crash.

"Watcher down," she said.

"Temporarily," the kid yelled behind her.

"Good for you."

Spike had Peretti now. He dropped from a low service ladder onto the top of a refrigeration unit, crossed it in three silent steps, and watched the man slide through spice row faster than his shoes wanted to go. Peretti was not built for running. He was built for desks and printers and whispered transactions over good tea. But fear made sprinters out of people whose lungs objected, and now he was moving with his scarf half out and his precious cuffs forgotten.

He glanced back once. That was enough.

His eyes caught Spike above the lane and widened, not in recognition but in the ancient animal understanding that someone faster had entered the frame.

Peretti bolted.

"Warehouse side," Spike said.

"I'm cutting him off," Jet answered.

Through gaps in awnings, Spike heard the Bebop's engines rise to a low ugly growl, enough to make every loose sign and hanging light in the lane start trembling. A few heads turned upward with the resigned fury of market people seeing a ship where ships had no business being.

Spike dropped to ground level and went after the scarf.

Peretti hit a stack of burlap sacks with one hip, rebounded, cursed, and kept going. He ran with both hands out from his body like he couldn't bear them brushing the dirty walls. Fennel seeds flew from the paper twist still clenched in one fist.

"Left," Ed said in Spike's ear. "No, his left. Your other left. Good job."

Spike cut through a narrow lane between cardamom bins and came out parallel to Peretti just as the man burst into loading access.

The Bebop's shadow crossed the lane first. Then the ship itself nosed down into the opening like an insult too large for the room. Jet brought her in crooked, ugly, perfect enough. Market vendors scattered with crates in their arms, shouting. A drone rose up from somewhere under the eaves and began issuing legal objections in a flat machine voice.

"Unlawful docking posture. Unlawful docking posture."

"Get in line," Jet snapped at it over comms.

Peretti saw the ship, swore, and veered again, this time through the half-open sliding door of a spice warehouse.

Faye reached the loading lane in time to see him disappear inside. Her breath burned. Her hair had come loose under the jacket collar. Somewhere behind her, Litsa was still shouting in a language that probably predated half the laws being broken in front of her.

"Back door?" Jet said.

Faye was already there, circling the warehouse wall. "On it."

Inside, the air went thick at once.

Spice warehouses never looked like much from outside. Inside they were caves built out of smell. Sacks piled shoulder-high. Wooden pallets. Narrow aisles. Light coming in dusty through vents near the ceiling. Cumin, coriander, chili, turmeric, cinnamon, dried pepper skins. Enough scent to coat the mouth and sit there.

Peretti slipped on loose powder at the first turn and caught himself with both hands against a sack. "Shit."

Spike saw him recover and knew exactly where the man's next three choices were going to be because panic made people simple.

Right aisle blocked by stacked bins. Left too narrow. Straight through the center lane, toward the back door Faye was already taking.

Spike ran.

Peretti glanced back again and made the mistake of trying to dodge before he had room. He cut sideways around a pallet of cumin sacks, one foot skidding on spilled seed, shoulder clipping the stack. Spike hit him low and hard a breath later.

They went into the sacks together.

One split with a muffled pop. Then another. A brown-yellow cloud burst up around them, hot and bitter and dry enough to scrape the wet out of Spike's nose. Cumin filled his mouth. His eyes. The back of his throat. It got under his collar and into the sweat at his neck. Peretti screamed, not from the tackle itself but from seeing both hands disappear wrist-deep into spice.

"Not the hands," he yelled, coughing. "Jesus Christ, not the hands."

Spike pinned his right arm, sneezed twice into his shoulder, and got a knee between Peretti's shoulder blades. The floor was slick with powder. Everything smelled like somebody had ground up a whole kitchen and fired it out of a cannon.

"Stop moving."

"I'm not moving, I'm dying."

"Not yet."

Peretti twisted anyway. Spice caked the burgundy scarf into a dirty rope. The monogram on his handkerchief was gone under dust. He looked less like a careful man now and more like what he was: a thin, middle-aged crook with pretty habits and bad lungs.

The back door slammed open.

Faye came in with her gun up and stopped dead at the sight of them half-buried in cumin. For one clean second she just stared. Then a laugh escaped her, sharp and involuntary.

"Oh, that's incredible."

"Cuffs," Spike said.

"Ask nicely."

Jet thundered in from the loading side before Spike could answer. His boots threw spice prints. His coat smelled like cold ship metal and engine wash. He took in the scene in one sweep and said, "Of course it's cumin."

The drone outside was still reciting the law to anyone with the patience to be corrected by a machine.

Faye tossed restraints. Jet caught them and knelt, cybernetic fingers snapping one cuff closed around Peretti's wrist before the man could remember he had options.

Peretti jerked. "Wait, wait, I can make you money."

"You're making us money now," Jet said.

"No, more money. Papers. Transit seals. Medical clearance chips. Freight stamps. Whatever you need."

Faye crouched beside him, looked at his spice-packed cuffs, and said, "If you could print groceries, you'd be out of here already."

"Lady, I printed a clinic pass for a bishop last month."

"Good for the bishop."

Jet hauled his arms back and clicked the second cuff. "Save it."

Peretti coughed so hard his whole body folded around the sound. When he spat to clear his throat, the spit came out yellow-brown with cumin dust. "Rafi still owes me change," he muttered.

Faye looked at him. "Rafi says you owe him eight woolongs."

For the first time since the chase started, genuine indignation broke through his fear. "That is a lie. I owed him six."

Spike sat back on his heels, sneezed again, and wiped his nose on a sleeve that was already lost to spice. The whole warehouse had gone hazy. Cumin lay over the floor in drifts. It would be in the folds of his jacket for days. He could feel grains grinding between his teeth.

An old woman in a quilted vest appeared at the end of the aisle holding a ledger pad against one hip. Half-moon glasses hung low on her nose. One hearing aid whistled faintly before she slapped it once and made it behave.

"You break it," she said, looking at the burst sacks, "you bought it."

Jet got to his feet with Peretti in tow. "Put it on his tab."

"His tab is terrible."

"Then put it on municipal justice."

The woman sniffed the air as if testing whether sarcasm had a market price. "That one never pays either."

Peretti twisted to look back at the ruined sacks with genuine sorrow. "This was imported."

Faye stood and brushed cumin off her knees. "You forged transit seals for a living. Consider it cultural exchange."

They got him out into the lane with the drone still complaining and half the market pretending not to stare. Red-cap boy had vanished. Litsa at the tea stand gave Peretti one long look, clicked her knitting needles together, and went back to the tiny yellow sock in her hands as if all this proved something she had been saying for years.

Jet marched Peretti toward the holding transport call point. Spike followed, smelling like a spice grinder. Faye walked on the other side with her jacket open and her gun hidden again, face blank now that the laugh had gotten out of her system.

Cumin shook off Spike's jacket every time he moved. A yellow-brown stripe ran down Jet's coat sleeve where he had hauled Peretti upright. Faye wiped her knee with two fingers and only smeared the spice wider.

* * *

The bounty office smelled like paper dust, overheated plastic, and old disappointment.

Peretti sat on a holding bench with his wrists sealed to a ring in the wall and kept trying to brush cumin out of his sleeves with his fingertips. It was hopeless. Every time he thought he had won, a fresh brown smear bloomed across the fabric. He looked sick with it.

At the counter, the clerk took one look at the capture paperwork, one look at Peretti in the bench area, and one look at the spice all over Jet's coat and said, "Warehouse arrest."

Jet set the forms down. "Yeah."

The clerk was a small woman with compression gloves cut off at the knuckles and a peppermint antacid parked in one cheek hard enough to change the line of her face. A strand of silver tinsel from some forgotten holiday decoration was tied around her wrist like she had found it on the floor and never made time to remove it.

She keyed the intake number, frowned at the return, and clicked her tongue. "Oh, that's ugly."

Faye leaned one shoulder against the counter. "How ugly."

"Missed-the-noon-review ugly."

Jet looked at the wall clock. Twelve twenty-three.

"Bond got reviewed down at eleven forty-seven," the clerk said. "Petition's been sitting on the public docket all morning. Poster never caught up."

"Poster says thirty," he said.

"Poster was true when somebody printed it." The clerk sucked the peppermint to one side of her mouth and clicked again. "If you'd walked him in before the review stamped, you would've gotten thirty. You didn't. Judge knocked him to fifteen-eight on nonviolent and community-ties grounds."

Peretti called from the bench, offended all over again. "My aunt was in the room."

The clerk did not look at him. "Your aunt cries on command. Everybody knows that."

Faye closed her eyes briefly. "Fifteen-eight."

"Afraid so."

Jet put both hands on the counter. "He had armed watchers."

"Did he shoot you."

"No."

"Then the judge called it nonviolent." She printed the receipt with a mechanical cough. "Take it up with democracy."

There were arguments worth having and arguments that only cost breath. Jet took the chit when it came out of the machine and felt the weight of nothing in it. Fifteen thousand eight hundred woolongs. It would wipe out the ticket and buy food, but fuel and repairs could keep standing in line.

Spike, sitting on the windowsill with one boot heel against the wall, said, "Better than six."

Jet looked at him.

Spike shrugged. Cumin dust lifted off his jacket in a faint cloud. "Rafi's version."

Even Jet laughed at that, once, against his own will.

It went away fast.

They stopped at the enforcement kiosk on the walk out because if Jet carried that ticket another day he was going to start seeing its red warning every time he blinked. The kiosk stood between a payday loan office and a machine that sold hot canned coffee, tucked under a plastic awning stained orange by dust and age.

Jet fed the bounty chit into the slot. The screen flashed the ticket back at him with bureaucratic cheer.

OUTSTANDING VIOLATION
8,000 WOOLONGS
PAY NOW?

"No," Faye said flatly behind him. "We just came here for the scenery."

Jet pressed YES hard enough to make the panel complain.

The machine thought about it for a long time. Then:

PAYMENT ACCEPTED
THANK YOU FOR SUPPORTING SAFE MUNICIPAL AIRSPACE

"Liar," Jet said.

"Language," Ed said in his ear from the ship, pleased to be included from afar. "Ed is monitoring the illegal parking count. We're at two warnings, one citation threat, and one very stern drone poem."

"Can it wait."

"Everything waits if you ignore it long enough."

"Not helping."

"Helping spiritually."

They went to the market with what was left and the mood of people doing math they already knew would end badly.

Jet carried the number in his head the whole time. Fifteen-eight minus eight. Seven-eight. Docking fee later. Fuel, not today. Repairs, not today. Coffee, probably not. Food, if they kept their heads. Food and enough left over to let the ship keep insulting them tomorrow.

The grocery stalls nearest the paid docks sold in layers: what you wanted at eye level, what you could afford lower down, what even the broke people avoided in the bins by the ground. Jet worked the shelves from bottom up.

Rice. Dried noodles. Eggs, the cheaper tray with two already cracked and discounted for honesty. Bell peppers because he could still turn those into something that tasted like dinner even when the protein situation was an argument with reality. Green onions. Cabbage. A squat bottle of cooking oil. Cheap protein strips made from soy and lies. Dog food that smelled so bad through the sealed bag he could feel Ein judging him from half a ship away.

He stopped in front of the beef case and did not bother pretending. Thin shaved strips, red under the lights, more expensive by the gram than common sense. He stood there a second too long anyway.

Spike came up beside him, hands in pockets. He still smelled like cumin. So did Jet. So did Faye. The whole day had settled into their clothes.

"Move along," Spike said. "It only encourages them."

Jet snorted and took a pack of tofu instead.

Faye was two stalls over with a basket on one hip and an expression like she was offended by every price tag on principle. She picked up a can of peaches in syrup, turned it to read the cost, and set it back with careful contempt. Then she grabbed three onions and a packet of cigarettes, held both a second, and put the cigarettes back too.

Jet saw. He did not say anything.

In their line of work, dignity about money usually lasted until the cashier named the total. After that you put things back and kept your face still.

At the register, the total came up, they all did the same silent subtraction, and Jet pulled two things off the pile before the cashier could say the number again: the better oil and one tray of eggs. The cashier, a girl with freckles across her nose and a bandage on one knuckle, did not give them pity. Jet appreciated that more than kindness.

"You still want the scallions?" she asked.

Jet looked at the pile.

Faye said, "Keep the scallions."

He glanced at her.

She was looking at the register, not him. "What, you want to eat gray food."

Jet left the scallions on the counter.

The paid dock was two blocks away. By the time they turned onto the second one, the afternoon had gone colder. Mars did that without ceremony. One minute the air was merely thin and mean. The next it slipped under your collar and started collecting rent.

Jet took the heavier bags because of course he did. One loop cut into the fingers of his right hand. The left, with the prosthetic, barely registered weight. Spike carried rice and dog food with the bored posture of a man pretending not to carry anything. Faye had a bag of vegetables and the oil. Ed kept talking in their ears from the ship about docking threats, a salvage forum argument, and the fact that Ein had tried to eat a receipt.

Mostly, though, they walked in silence.

Their boots scraped dust. Plastic bag handles whispered and stretched. A transit tram groaned somewhere overhead. They had been up too long, gotten what they went for, and still felt cheated by arithmetic.

Spike said, after a block, "Rafi really said eight?"

Faye kept her eyes on the pavement. "With feeling."

"He's getting soft."

"He has asthma."

"That's not what I meant."

"I know."

A tram rattled over the cross street. Spike hitched the rice higher against his hip. Faye kept her eyes on the pavement, but one corner of her mouth moved before it went flat again. Their steps hit the dust in the same beat for the rest of the block.

Jet shifted the groceries in his hand. One of the cheap bags gave a small plastic complaint. He looked down. The corner of the rice package had started sawing through the seam.

He should have doubled it at the stall. He had been doing the numbers in his head instead.

The Bebop's paid berth came into view between two cargo fences, old hull paint dulled by dust, one docking light flickering in a way that had definitely moved from tomorrow's problem to tonight's. Jet watched it blink and felt the day lining itself up into the next day already: unload, cook, patch the light, see what else broke while they were gone, decide whether fifteen hundred woolongs counted as hope or insult.

He heard himself speak before he had decided to.

"New rule," he said. "Next poster number means nothing till Ed checks the live docket and we count the split at the table before the ship takes a bite. Nobody disappears before the count."

Faye shifted the vegetables higher on her hip. "Good. My money gets to be money before it turns into one of your valves."

Jet glanced at her. "Fine. Ship rule."

"Same time tomorrow, or are we still not talking?"

Spike made a small sound that could have been a laugh or a cough dragged backward through his nose. He did not answer.

Faye didn't either.

She stepped closer, took the splitting bag out of Jet's right hand, and twisted the plastic around the rice until the seam stopped whining. The handle left a red line across her palm.

Jet let go.

The bags whispered against their coats after that. They kept walking toward the ship with the groceries between them and cumin still caught in the cuffs of their coats.


# Chapter 14

The Bebop sounded bigger when nobody was using it right.

It had the same hull tick, the same breathy push of life support through the vents, the same loose spoon in the sink tapping metal every time the dock clamps took a strain and let it go. But since they'd hauled the groceries back aboard and counted the split at the table two nights ago, the spaces between the sounds had stretched out. They stood up taller. The ship felt less like a room and more like one of those old Mars wells with the water gone out of them, deep and lined in rust and not meant for children.

Ed sat cross-legged on the common-room floor in the middle of a drift of printouts and thermal-paper curls, bare heel pressed against the warm housing of the main console. A map of Mars turned on the central screen in slow red bands. Little windows opened and closed over it like blinking fish: a licensing board, a settlement clinic ledger, a dead informant's code tree that should not still have had live branches, docking manifests, fuel purchases, work tickets, a school absence roll from a settlement that had since caved in and been bulldozed flat.

Ein lay half under the table with his chin on Ed's ankle, one eye open. Every time the printer spit out another strip, one of his ears flicked back in annoyance and then settled again.

Ed had not slept much. That part was ordinary. The ordinary part ended around dawn, when three names that had been orbiting one another for days finally stopped moving and locked together hard enough to hurt.

I found her, Ed had told the empty room first, just to hear if it sounded true.

It had.

Now the problem was the grown-ups.

Jet came in first because Jet always came in first when the ship made a noise it wasn't supposed to make, and the printer had been whining off and on for an hour. He still had yesterday's stubble on yesterday's face and a gray work shirt with one sleeve rolled and the other hanging right because he never rolled the prosthetic side unless he needed the joints clear. He took two steps into the room, stopped short at the paper on the floor, and squinted at Ed.

"If you've printed the net again, I'm charging you for it."

Ed looked up. Jet was aiming for normal. He missed by a mile.

"I found her name," Ed said.

Jet did not move for half a second. Then he set his mug down on the table too carefully. "You found the pilot."

"Mm-hm."

Spike spoke from the couch without lifting his head. "You could've started with that."

Ed had known Spike was awake. Spike had the wrong stillness for sleep. He lay on his back with one ankle over the other, one arm flung over his eyes, hair sticking every direction except a useful one. The cigarette at the corner of his mouth was unlit. He had probably gone horizontal an hour ago and never closed either eye, the real one or the other one.

Faye came in from the hallway a moment later, drawn by voices or instinct or the fact that nothing private stayed private on the Bebop for long. She had Jet's old sweatshirt thrown over her usual clothes, sleeves hanging past her hands, and the look on her face said she was prepared to be irritated before anybody had given her a reason. She took in the paper on the floor, the red map on the screen, Jet standing rigid by the table, Spike not bothering to pretend anymore, and her expression changed by one degree.

"What now."

Ed patted the floor in front of the main screen. "Come see. Ed made the messy parts hold still."

The floor stayed Ed's territory. That was also ordinary. Jet dragged a chair around backward and straddled it, forearms across the backrest. Faye leaned against the counter and crossed her arms inside the sweatshirt. Spike sat up and let his feet find the deck without any hurry in them. Ein got up, circled once around Ed's pile, and resettled with a grunt.

Ed turned back to the screen. It felt strange to run the line straight through. Usually the fun part was the bounce, the boop-boop between points, the part where the others had to keep up or drown. This one needed edges. This one needed doors with labels on them.

Ed tapped the screen. A license photo filled the left side.

A young woman stared out of the license photo. Her dark hair was pulled back so hard it changed the shape of her brow. Her coveralls were zipped to the sternum, and the collar was stained darker where someone had wiped grease with the back of a wrist and only moved it around. She had a sharp face and a tired mouth. The form below the photo read KOZUKI, REN above Independent mechanic certification, Mars Free Trades Board. She was twenty-two.
