The thumbprint box had been rejected once. TOO SMUDGED, a clerk had stamped over it in pale red. On the second scan there was still a dark crescent of sealant lodged under the thumbnail.

"Ren Kozuki," Ed said. "Licensed mechanic. Paid-up smallcraft rating. Three lapsed docking permits, one settlement fine for doing an unauthorized engine swap in a public lane, which is funny but also stupid. She uses her real name on work orders when she wants to get paid and no name when she doesn't."

Jet's gaze stayed on the photo. "Kozuki."

There it was. The word had weight now. Before, it had been a code attached to a dead informant in an archive. Now it had a face under it and sealant under one nail.

Ed nodded and brought up the next pane. "Daichi Kozuki. That's the father. ISSP confidential informant classification buried under three seal layers and one lazy dead folder. Not dead enough, though. The access tree still twitched when Ren kicked it."

Jet leaned forward. The movement was small, but the chair squeaked under him.

On the screen, Daichi's file was mostly blocks of black, whole paragraphs drowned in redaction bars. But the precinct stamp in the upper corner showed through. So did the district number. So did the date on the deactivation notice. Jet's gaze caught on the stamp and stayed there.

"That's my precinct," he said.

The room stayed on the precinct stamp in the upper corner. Gray copy toner made the proof ugly and flat and impossible to argue with.

Ed moved the cursor lower. "The bounty on Hosuke Takara came back because somebody filed a sighting report through Daichi Kozuki's old code path. Not forged. Not hacked from the outside. Just... used. The branch never got cut off all the way."

Jet's prosthetic fingers tightened on the chair back until the joint servos gave a faint insect whine. "I know that part."

"Ed knows. The part you didn't know is this part." Ed expanded the access history. Dates climbed down the screen. "Ren didn't wake the code up to collect the bounty. She used it to get the bounty in front of us. The report was filed two days before the Bebop came home. She timed it with our docking pattern. That's premeditated sneaky."

Spike reached out at last and held his hand out wordlessly for the thermal copy nearest Ed's knee. Ed passed it over. Spike looked at the timestamps once, then set the sheet on the table without comment. His face did not change. His shoulders did, barely. They went stiller.

Faye uncrossed one arm enough to point at the screen. "So she's Daichi Kozuki's daughter. Fine. That gets her Jet's file. It doesn't get her the rest."

"No," Ed said. "The rest is the mother."

He put Ayane on the screen.

There was no license photo for Ayane Kozuki. What Ed had was uglier and more intimate: a surgical intake record from a Mars settlement clinic, decades-old formatting preserved inside a newer records shell because clinics never threw data away if they could bill for it later. AYANE KOZUKI first. Then, farther down the chain and much more often, FAYE VALENTINE.

The intake image was grainy. Not a portrait, just the freeze-frame from an admissions cam: a woman turning away from somebody out of frame, one shoulder bare where the gown had slipped. On the skin over the left shoulder blade was a pale older scar, the shape wrong for anything clean.

Faye's hand went to her own shoulder. She caught the motion halfway and dropped it.

Ed kept going because stopping would only make the room worse.

"Ayane Kozuki. Later alias Faye Valentine. Mars clinic use, transit use, cash work, gambling use at least once on Ganymede because Miriam isn't making that up. The biometric authorization doesn't start right away. It starts three years after the real Faye got frozen and it stays active for eleven years. Same identity sale window the records office showed. Same medical profile. Same debt shell, except she was using it for treatment instead of bills."

Faye stared at the name on the chart as if hard enough looking might make the letters admit they belonged to somebody else. "She got care under my file."

"Some care," Ed said. "Not enough care. Here."

The next documents were refill histories. Rows of prescription authorizations had once been regular enough to make a rhythm, landing every twenty-eight days until the gaps opened: thirty-six days, then forty-one, then fifty-three. One emergency partial fill showed up in a clinic out by the Hellas freight spine. Then nothing came for seventy-two days.

Spike lifted his head a little more. That was all.

Ed did not look at him yet. "Immunosuppressants. Anti-rejection drugs. Same package family as the manifest in the locker. Same shipment code cluster. Same clinic endpoint."

Jet turned to Spike. It was not accusation. Not yet. More like the swivel of a searchlight.

Spike rubbed the bridge of his nose with one finger. "Yeah."

The single syllable sat there. Jet's prosthetic thumb stopped on the chair seam. Faye's finger hovered over the screen a second too long. Even Ed waited a beat before opening the next file. That was new.

Ed opened the clinic notes because if they were going to know, then they were going to know all the way.

The room got smaller around the screen.

Patient reports missed doses due to supply interruption.

Patient arrived with edema in ankles and hands.

Vomited twice in triage sink.

Requested lights off because of headache.

Asked whether daughter had eaten.

The note about the daughter was in a different hand, less tidy than the rest, squeezed into the margin beside a billing code. Somebody in that clinic had thought it mattered enough to write down.

Faye looked away first. Not because of the bloodwork numbers or the rejection markers or the neat thin cruelty of the phrase supply interruption. It was the line in the margin. Ed could tell.

"Jesus," Jet said quietly.

Spike did not say anything.

Ed zoomed once, just enough to bring up the final page. The alias sat at the top in clean machine type. FAYE VALENTINE. Beneath it the discharge field had not been used. The death certificate had.

Faye laughed once. No humor in it at all. "So she died as me."

Ed made himself say it plain. "She died under your name."

The sweatshirt sleeve had ridden down Faye's wrist. She shoved it back with more force than the fabric required. "That's disgusting."

"Yeah," Jet said.

"No, not just her. All of it." Faye jabbed a finger toward the screen and missed by an inch. "A clerk sells a frozen girl's file, a clinic runs with it because the pills clear, some woman spends eleven years pretending to be me, and then she dies with it on a bracelet because that's the part the system knows how to read. That's disgusting."

Faye's words stayed in the room with the chart glow on them. Jet's jaw worked once. Spike looked back at the refill gaps.

Ed took a breath and brought up the last cluster. This was the part the adults had not yet learned how to look at. They kept seeing the chase, the forged signals, the baited spot behind Mama Yun's. They kept seeing a pilot. Ed had spent the night inside smaller records. Kids left traces everywhere if nobody bothered to clean them properly.

The big screen filled with address changes, settlement schools, dockside food-assistance lists, apprenticeship rejections, one approved work permit, a mechanic-yard invoice paid partly in cash and partly in scrap copper, and a foster-network intake scan so old the corners had gone fuzzy.

Ren Kozuki, age 9.

Personal effects: one shirt, one pair boots, one cough-drop tin containing sorted bolts and screws. Child became upset when staff attempted to discard tin.

Jet made a sound in his throat and stopped it.

Ed touched the next note.

Child took apart desk fan in sleeping room using spoon handle. Reassembled incorrectly but improved motor noise.

On another line, years later, one of Ren's work chits from a bay outside Tharsis: CUSTOMER REPORTS "GHOST SOUND" FROM PORT THRUSTER. R. KOZUKI NOTE: NOT A GHOST. BEARING.

That one almost looked like a joke. It probably had been, or as close as Ren got.

"She moved a lot," Ed said. "Seven addresses in nine years that can be found and probably more that can't. Informal foster placements, settlement crash couches, yard bunks, work bays. She started taking mechanic jobs early because foster networks like kids better when kids bring money in."

Jet looked at the nine on the intake form, then at the twenty-two on the license photo. He did not blink enough.

"How old was she when Ayane died?" he asked.

Ed put the death certificate and the intake scan side by side.

The math did not need doing. It sat right there in two boxes.

"Nine."

Spike swore under his breath, soft enough that the word almost dissolved into the ship noise.

Ed finally turned to look at all of them instead of the screen.

"Ren grew up in the same mess she's been showing us," Ed said. The words came out cleaner than Ed's words usually did. Not prettier. Just cleaner. "Daichi used your precinct, Jet. Ayane used Spike's routes and Faye's name. Ren inherited all three and then went digging. That's the web. That's why the bounty. That's why the signal. That's why the records. It wasn't random-random. It was built."

Faye dragged a hand over her face. "Built for what."

Ed thought about the parking spot behind Mama Yun's. Six months had been paid in cash. A ship had sat dark and waiting exactly where Jet would feel it, where all of them would feel it. It had the feel of a note left on the galley table under Jet's coffee mug so everyone had to see it. It felt petty, sad, patient.

"To make us look," Ed said.

Spike's eyes lifted to Ed's then, full on for the first time all morning.

"You sure about that?"

Ed looked back at the screen. At the note about the cough-drop tin. At the thumbprint stamped TOO SMUDGED. At the line in the clinic chart where Ayane had asked whether her daughter had eaten. At the old orphanage file Ed had found last week with age 7 beside Ed's own name, the way a system could flatten a life to a line item and still hold enough of it to wound.

"If Ren wanted us dead," Ed said, "the parking plan was very slow."

Spike looked down again. "Yeah."

Jet stood up so abruptly the chair legs scraped hard across the deck. For a second Ed thought he was going to start issuing orders because that was Jet's favorite way to keep from bleeding in public. Instead he walked to the screen, picked up the printout with Daichi's precinct stamp, and read it standing there like he had forgotten chairs existed.

Faye pushed off the counter and came closer too. Not near Jet. Near the clinic notes. She did not touch them. The color had worn off the middle of her lower lip where she'd been worrying it with her teeth.

"Did she know?" she asked.

Ed knew who she meant. "Ayane?"

"Ren."

"The whole whole thing? Not when she was little. The records start getting organized later. But by eighteen? Yeah. Maybe before."

Faye nodded once, like she was signing for a package she didn't want.

Jet kept reading. "There's no relocation completion. There should've been a relocation completion. If he had a kid and a wife there should've been..." He stopped. Put the page down. Picked it up again. "Christ."

Spike's cigarette was still unlit between his fingers. He rolled it once over his knuckles. "Routes collapsed after I left," he said to the room, the floor, nobody. "A lot of things did."

The printer fan whined soft and the unlit cigarette turned once between Spike's fingers. The words stayed where they'd landed. It didn't need answering yet.

The printer, which had been quiet for nearly five minutes, gave a chirp and started spitting again. It kicked out another work order, another address, another little life detail too boring for anybody but Ed to love. Ein stood, sneezed at the noise, and crossed the room to put his chin on Ed's bare foot.

Jet was still staring at Daichi's stamp. Faye was still staring at the name on the death certificate that had once been hers and wasn't, then was again. Spike sat with the unlit cigarette and looked like he had forgotten what it was for.

The ship sounded small now. Or maybe it only sounded full.

The paper kept their hands busy instead. Jet rubbed one corner of the precinct printout soft with his thumb. Faye kept her eyes on the death certificate. Spike turned the dead cigarette once between two fingers and didn't lift it.

Jet finally looked away from the paper. "I'm making food," he said, which was not a conclusion but was the only thing in the room he knew how to do next.

He carried the Daichi printout with him into the galley.

***

Jet cut the cabbage too thin the first time and had to go slower with the second half.

That annoyed him more than it should have, which meant he was exactly as rattled as he felt. He had bought one green cabbage, a bundle of scallions, a sack of cheap noodles, six eggs, and the smallest package of pork scraps in the freezer bin because thirty thousand woolongs turned into a lot less than thirty thousand after the ticket and fuel and the little humiliations that came attached to being broke in public. The pork looked like offcuts shaved from the edges of something a richer person got to buy whole. He browned it anyway. Fat still rendered from scraps.

The galley warmed fast. Oil popped in the pan. Steam filmed the nearest porthole. Someone had left the bag of bean sprouts too close to the stove and the plastic had softened at one corner. Jet moved it with the back of his wrist and set the pot to boil.

Usually this part brought the crew back to themselves. Somebody complained about the smell, or stole something from the cutting board, or asked how much of an ingredient they were allowed to eat before Jet called it sabotage. Usually Jet met them halfway because the complaining was how the room got blood moving again.

Tonight they came together without the usual static.

Spike took the chair by the table and stayed there. That was the first sign. He should have drifted to the couch or the deck or nowhere obvious. Instead he sat with one knee bent and a beer he had not opened and watched the pork take color in the pan like it was a job he had been assigned. The cigarette was gone. That was good.

Faye sat on the counter by the sink with her feet on the rung of a lower cabinet, one of Jet's chipped bowls upside down in her lap. She turned it around and around by the rim, not looking at anybody while still somehow staying in the middle of the room.

Ed had moved the papers into stacks only Ed understood and occupied the far end of the table with the screen turned so everyone could see the southern stretch of Mars. Dry Well sat there now in a blinking box, little more than an old bay designation and a cluster of dead infrastructure tags. Ein stationed himself under the table in his most political position, centered where crumbs were likely and emotions might fall through.

Jet slid the cabbage into the pan. It hissed and shrank at once.

"Don't feed him onion," Faye said after a while.

Jet glanced over. "I know not to feed him onion."

"I'm just saying."

"You don't have to say every fact that enters your head."

She turned the bowl in her lap one more time. "Then we'd all have a much quieter life."

That would have been the place where the old pattern picked up, two more lines at least, Spike tossing in something lazy from the side, Ed making it worse on purpose or by accident. The bowl kept turning in Faye's lap for half a turn more, then stopped. Steam from the pan filled the space where the next jab should've gone. The old pattern missed its cue and stayed missed.

Jet stirred the pork, threw in the white parts of the scallions, and said, "Ed. Show me where she is."

Ed rotated the small monitor on the table and crawled under one elbow to point at it. "Last live transponder echo here-here." A finger tapped south, farther down the basin. "Then she went dark because Ren isn't dumb. But Dry Well ate some maintenance orders under her mechanic number six months ago, and an old fuel account tied to one of Ayane's settlement clinics bought two filters at a supply yard twelve days back, and Spike knows this place."

Jet looked at Spike.

Spike lifted a shoulder. "Waystation. Used to be syndicate freight country. One functioning bay if it's the same as it was. Wind all day. Solar panels cracked to hell." He paused. "Remote enough to stay missed."

Faye kept her eyes on the bowl. "So she's there."

"Probably," Ed said.

"Probably isn't there," Faye said.

Ed nodded agreeably. "Correct. But it's also maybe enough."

Jet dropped the noodles into the pot. They bent slowly, then all at once. "It's enough."

The noodles slipped under the broth. Faye only rolled the bowl rim under her thumbs, and Spike kept his eyes on the pot.

That unsettled him even more than the silence had.

He skimmed foam with a spoon and tried to line the facts up in a row where they could be handled: a dead informant in his precinct, a courier in Spike's routes, a woman dying under Faye's name, a kid with a cough-drop tin full of bolts growing into a mechanic who had spent years feeding them pieces of their own past until they finally had to look. Facts ought to have been the safe part. They were how he had once made a living. Tonight the facts felt like tools somebody else had put back in the wrong drawer.

"We can make Dry Well on what we've got," he said. "If nobody burns fuel being stupid."

That at least got him a glance from Spike.

"I wasn't planning on a dogfight."

"Good."

"Didn't say you were."

"You looked like you were about to."

Spike's mouth twitched without reaching humor. "Maybe that's just my face."

Faye snorted. It was small, but it cracked something loose in the air.

Jet ladled broth into the pot with the noodles, tasted it, added the packet of cheap soy that always tasted like metal first and beans second, then cracked the eggs one-handed while the other hand braced the bowl. The shells broke cleaner than his thoughts.

"We're not going down there blind," he said. "We talk this through first."

Faye's gaze came up. "Talk what through."

Jet scraped the shell fragments out of the pan with a thumb. "What we're doing when we get there."

"Looking at her, apparently."

"Faye."

"What? That's what this is." She set the bowl aside. "You heard Ed. She wanted us to look."

Jet turned the heat down and faced the room, towel in one hand. "Yeah. I did. That's why I want to know if we're going down there to drag answers out of a twenty-two-year-old mechanic or—" He jerked the towel toward the monitor, the paper stacks, the galley. "I keep calling this her problem. It's not her problem. Half that file is the building I worked in."

The burner hissed low. Faye pinched the bowl rim between thumb and forefinger. Spike studied the beer label without opening it.

Jet turned back to the stove before whatever he'd just said could settle into something he'd have to stand behind.

"If Daichi worked out of my precinct and got burned there," he said, "I don't get to put that in somebody else's pile. I wore a badge in that building. I knew how informants got used. I knew enough to know better, anyway."

The pork smell had deepened. It was good pork, or maybe it was only real pork and that counted for good enough. Jet dropped the green tops of the scallions in last so they stayed bright. He kept his eyes on the pan.

"We told ourselves they chose it," he said. "Sometimes maybe they did. Sometimes they needed money. Sometimes they needed a door opened, or a warrant buried, or somebody to leave their brother alone for a week. We called it cooperation because that made the paperwork read nice. If Daichi was in the building, then he was somebody's problem to keep alive. Mine too. Doesn't matter if his name never crossed my desk."

Spike said, "It matters some."

Jet looked over his shoulder. "Not enough."

Spike tipped his chair back on two legs, let it hang there, then set it down again. "No," he said. "Not enough."

It had no joke wrapped around it and no shrug to soften it.

Jet gave him a second and then another. Spike would talk or he wouldn't.

He did.

"Clinics bought through syndicate routes all the time," Spike said, eyes on the unopened beer. "Not openly. Stuff got folded into other manifests. You'd see a clinic code on freight you weren't supposed to look at and you'd sign it through." His thumb ran along the edge of the label. "That was it."

Faye's fingers stopped on the rim of the bowl.

Spike went on because stopping now would have been worse. "When the routes broke, they broke ugly. People stopped getting paid. Warehouses got torched. Small cargo disappeared first. Things nobody shot over because they were too busy shooting over the big stuff." He lifted the beer at last, looked at it, put it back down unopened. "So yeah. If those meds were coming through my old lines, then they stopped coming through them too."

Jet asked, "You knew?"

"I knew enough." Spike stared at the table. "I didn't know her. I didn't know Ayane. I knew the manifests had clinic codes and I never looked at what was under them." He paused. "Didn't want to."

The galley was quiet except for the pan cooling on the burner, metal ticking as it contracted.

Faye slid off the counter. Her bare feet touched the deck without sound. She took two steps toward the stove, stopped short, glanced at Jet, and stayed by the end of the table instead.

"I keep trying to decide how mad I'm allowed to be at a woman who stole my name," she said.

Jet said nothing.

"No, that's not right." She pressed the heel of one hand to her forehead, annoyed with herself for having started soft. "I'm mad. That's not the problem. The problem is every time I get a nice clean version of it going in my head, I see some clerk calling out Faye Valentine in a clinic and if Ayane doesn't answer, she doesn't get the pills. Kind of ruins the simple part."

Ed watched her without moving.

Faye laughed again, quieter this time. "And now all I can think about is some bracelet on a dead wrist with my stupid name on it. That's sick. That's really sick."

Jet turned off the flame under the pan. The quiet after the burner died felt immediate and raw.

"Yeah," he said.

"Don't 'yeah' me like that."

"I'm agreeing with you."

"I know." She folded her arms tight. "I'm just saying don't do it like you're handling evidence."

That was fair enough that Jet let it stand.

Ed had been quiet for too long, which on Ed was the same as an alarm. Jet glanced over.

Ed sat with hands flat on the table. "Ren knows the boring parts too," Ed said. "Not just the dead parts. Work tickets, bad addresses, school slips, the cough-drop tin, all the ordinary pieces. That's how Ed knows she wasn't just building a case against the crew. She kept the little things."

Jet looked at the monitor again. The work order note still sat there: NOT A GHOST. BEARING.

Faye saw it too. "She writes like an asshole mechanic."

"Yeah," Spike said, and this time there was the faintest trace of dry in it. "That's pretty convincing."

That got a real laugh out of Faye, one quick breath of it. She rubbed her thumb along her collarbone afterward as if embarrassed to have made the sound.

Jet started filling bowls because the food would turn to paste if he kept talking at it. He made one bowl for Ed, heaviest on noodles; one for Faye, with less cabbage because she always claimed she hated cabbage and always ate it anyway; one for Spike, who would complain there wasn't enough meat no matter how much meat there was; and one for himself. He slid a fifth empty saucer to the floor for Ein after fishing the onions back out of a spoonful by hand.

He handed out bowls into a room that accepted them with both hands. Chopsticks clicked against ceramic. Steam lifted across their faces. Faye took the cabbage without complaint. It should have felt unnatural. Instead it felt like a room listening to itself.

They ate.

The first minute was all chopsticks, broth, and dog snuffling at the saucer. Steam dampened their faces, and the table stayed warm under their forearms. Jet had not realized how hungry he was until the food hit his mouth and his body leaned into it harder than his pride liked.

Faye broke the silence with, "If we go down there and she tries to give us another scavenger hunt, I'm leaving."

Jet swallowed. "Fair."

"I'm serious."

"I know."

Spike twirled noodles around his chopsticks and said, "Don't think she will."

Faye looked over. "Why."

Spike shrugged. "Too much work to get us this far. If she wanted one more puzzle, she'd have kept hiding."

Ed nodded with his mouth full and then remembered manners only after the fact. "Mm-hm."

Jet set his bowl down. There it was, the part that had been circling since he'd said talk this through.

"When we find her," he said, "we're not going to confront her."

The word sounded wrong the second it was out. Too much cop in it. Too much suspect across a table.

Faye heard it too. "Good."

Jet corrected course before pride could get in the way. "No. That's not... We go there and we listen first. Ask after. Understand what she wants from us, if anything. But we listen first."

The pause sat there with broth steam between them. Faye looked down into her bowl. Spike kept eating. Jet let it sit. He'd said it badly, but he'd said it.

Spike ate a little more, then wiped his mouth with the back of his wrist. "Works for me."

Faye stared into her bowl. "I don't know what I'd even say to her."

"You don't have to know now," Jet said.

"That's helpful."

"It's true."

She took another bite, chewed it, and said, "I know."

Ed spun the monitor a few degrees so everyone could see the southern basin line. "Dry Well has one good bay and ugly crosswinds. Ren probably parked nose-out because the old bay doors stick on the west track. Also the south panel field still gives about sixty-two percent if you kick relay three." Ed tapped the map. "Ed can get us there."

Jet blinked at the sudden practical turn and was grateful for it. "How do you know about the relay."

"Repair ticket."

"Of course."

"Ren charged too little for it," Ed added. "Thirty percent under fair market unless the customer supplied parts. That is bad business."

Spike's bowl paused halfway to his mouth. "She built that ship on mechanic pay."

"And side work," Ed said. "And patience patience patience."

Jet leaned back and felt the chair complain under him. He could hear the rest of it already before it happened: fuel checks, route calculations, weather over the basin, whether the port stabilizer was going to keep pretending it wasn't due for service. Practical things. Things with bolts and numbers and finite failure points. Relief moved through him so fast it almost embarrassed him.

He looked around the table.

Spike was still here.

Faye was still here.

Ed had broth on one wrist and did not seem to care.

Ein licked the saucer clean and then looked up as if prepared to negotiate for jurisdiction over any dropped pork.

Jet said, "We leave in the morning."

Spike glanced at the dark porthole. "Could leave tonight."

"No."

"Why not."

"Because you look like hell, Ed's been awake all night, and I'd like one trip this week where we're not making decisions because we're rattled."

Spike opened his mouth, shut it, and gave a short nod. "Morning, then."

Another small thing, but it landed.

Faye pushed cabbage around in the bottom of her bowl. "If she says she did all this just to make us feel bad, I'm walking back to Mars."

"That's a long walk," Spike said.

"Then I'll steal a ship."

"There she is," Jet muttered.

Faye pointed a chopstick at him. "Don't start sounding relieved. It's weird."

Jet looked down at his bowl so they wouldn't see it too clearly. "Eat your noodles."

She did.

For a while that was all any of them did. They ate, reached for the soy, and passed it along. Faye took one of Spike's pork pieces without asking and Spike, after noticing, chose not to dignify it. Ed slurped too loud. Jet told Ed to slow down. Ed slowed down for maybe twelve seconds. The ship ticked and breathed around them. Outside the porthole, the Mars dock lights smeared in the thin night haze.

Daichi's precinct stamp sat by Jet's elbow with a wet ring from his bowl inching toward it. Ayane's death certificate was still half under Faye's bowl, with one corner gone limp from the damp. Dry Well kept blinking on Ed's little screen.
Spike asked for the soy without looking up, and Faye handed it over before he had to reach.
Ed fished the last pork scrap out of the pot. Jet saw it and let it go.
The dock clamps clicked once out in the berth. Then again. Nobody stood up.

Jet got up first when he was done and carried his bowl to the sink. He waited for chair legs to scrape and doors to open behind him. He ran water, set the bowl down, and reached for the sponge.

When he glanced back over his shoulder, water ran over his knuckles and the chairs were still occupied.

Spike sat with one arm thrown over the chair back, looking at the Dry Well marker on the little screen as if he could memorize the route by force. Faye had tucked one foot up under her on the chair and was rereading the work ticket with the ghost-sound note, expression impossible to read but not closed. Ed was feeding coordinates into a nav string and humming under the breath instead of over it. Ein had rolled onto his side directly in the walkway, certain the universe would route itself around him.

Jet rinsed one bowl, then another.

"Hey," Faye said after a while, not looking up from the ticket.

"What."

"The noodles are good."

Jet scrubbed at a spot that wasn't there. "They're noodles."

"Yeah, well. They're good noodles."

He could have made a thing out of that. Usually he would have. Tonight he just said, "There are more in the pot if anybody wants them."

Spike stood, took his own bowl to the sink, and set it down beside Jet's instead of on some random flat surface for Jet to find later. "I do."

Jet handed him the ladle without comment.

That, more than anything else, was how the trip south got decided.


# Chapter 15

Ed's crooked little crown floated over Dry Well on the nav glass, yellow grease pencil trembling every time Jet corrected the yoke. He flew the Bebop lower than he needed to, low enough that the ship's shadow kept finding the broken ground and sliding over it like spilled oil. South of Tharsis the settlements thinned fast. Towers got shorter. Roads stopped pretending to go somewhere. Old terraforming hardware sat out in the open where everyone could see how much money had died there. Mirror fields lay in cracked silver strips across the flats. Pump stations leaned into the dust with their intake mouths packed solid. A canal cut straight through the plain for thirty kilometers and then simply ended, as if the planet had gotten tired of being argued with.

The cockpit glass held the whole wrong-colored afternoon. Mars wasn't red from up close. It was rust, brick, old blood, oxidized machinery, dust blown thin over black stone. The light turned everything into some variation of worn metal. Jet kept one hand on the controls and one hand near the trim, feeling the age of the ship through the small corrections it wanted from him. The Bebop had a loose sympathy in the yoke on long flights. It never flew exactly straight unless you kept reminding it.

One bare foot hooked over the arm of the chair. The toes flexed in time with a tune only Ed could hear. The goggles sat up on Ed's forehead, held there by habit and static, and a measuring spoon from Jet's galley had somehow ended up tucked through the hair like an ornament.

"Dead greenhouse at two o'clock," Ed said. "No, correction, dead greenhouse cluster. Big crunchy salad bowl. Nobody watered the tomato kingdom."

Jet glanced left. Ribbed domes hunched out of the flats, half collapsed, their translucent skins torn open and packed with red drifts. A service road ran between them, visible only because the dust lay slightly shallower there.

"I see it."

"Do you think there are ghost tomatoes?" Ed asked.

From the common room, Faye said, "If there are, I hope they die again before dinner."

She was stretched on the couch with one arm over her eyes and a magazine open on her stomach, not turning pages. She had put on one of Jet's old sweatshirts over her usual clothes because the heating kept shifting between weak and nonexistent. It swallowed her wrists. She would rather have died than admit it was comfortable. Ein had claimed the warm stripe under the avionics rack and slept on his side with one hind leg twitching in short offended jerks, as if even in dreams somebody was breaking a rule.

Spike stood in the hatch between cockpit and common room, one shoulder against the frame, looking as if he'd just wandered there by accident and might wander away again. He had been doing that for an hour. The ship was too small for real avoidance on a flight like this. You could leave a conversation, but you couldn't leave the fact of other people breathing.

"How much longer?" Faye asked.

Ed peered at the display upside down. "Two hours thirteen minutes if the headwind stops being dramatic. Two hours thirty if Mars remains committed to the bit."

"Mars is always committed to the bit," Jet said.

Faye lowered her arm enough to squint toward the front windows. "All this for one settlement."

"Dry Well isn't much of a settlement anymore."

"Good. I was worried we'd miss the nightlife."

Jet let that sit. The truth was he preferred the empty part of Mars. Cities gave you too many stories all at once. Out here the damage was older and cleaner. You could see the exact shape of what had failed. A weather tower stood by itself on a rise, blades gone, only the central mast turning slow and pointless in the wind. Farther south, a line of black irrigation pipe surfaced from the dirt, ran fifty meters, and vanished back under, like something trying to crawl away.

No place on Mars looked empty if you knew what to look for. Failure had architecture. It left footings. It left service roads. It left paperwork in cabinets nobody cleared out. He thought of precinct rooms and informant files and a name he had never known while the man carrying it was still alive. He kept his eyes on the plain and eased the nose a little lower.

Ed hummed, stopped humming, and tapped the screen with a dirty thumbnail. "Basin lip in nineteen minutes. Then fun geology. Then more geology. Then old syndicate nonsense. Then Dry Well. Ed made color codes."

"I noticed," Jet said.

"Yellow means suspicious dirt. Blue means historical bad decisions."

"What's red mean?" Faye asked.

Ed looked delighted that she'd asked. "Red means Mars."

Faye made a small noise that could have been a laugh if she'd meant it more.

Spike pushed off the hatch and came forward at last. He took the copilot seat without asking, slouched in it, and looked out through the glass as if the planet had mildly inconvenienced him. "You're coming in low."

"Yeah."

"Wind'll throw you around along the basin wall."

"Probably."

Spike glanced at the display Ed had painted with lines and symbols and the tiny crooked crown. "West side's cleaner once you hit the cut. Less old hardware to scrape the hull on."

Jet nodded. That was the kind of thing Spike could say without either of them having to touch the rest. Practical. Observable. A route, not a reckoning.

He took it anyway. "You remember the approaches."

Spike kept watching the ground. "Some of them."

Faye turned a page she hadn't read. The paper crackled too loud in the ship, and after it there was only the engine note and one dry tick from the hull as temperature shifted. The Bebop moved south over abandoned pump stations, over the broken circles of survey camps, over dry riverbeds the terraformers had named before they had water for them.

Ed leaned forward so far the chair squealed. "Oh. Oh, look at that. Mirror field. Huge one. Wow."

The mirrors had once tracked sunlight into a central receiver tower. Now half of them were shattered and the other half pointed in random directions, flashing dumb bright pieces of afternoon into the cockpit.

"That project was called Helios Twelve," Ed said. "Ed found the file. It promised seasonal cloud generation and local agriculture expansion and a thirty-one percent increase in regional morale."

"How'd that work out?" Faye asked.

Ed watched the dead tower roll under them. "Regional morale remains unverified."

This time Faye did laugh, once, against her will. Jet heard it and didn't look back. On a ship this size you learned the value of not turning toward every soft sound. Some things only happened if you let them think nobody had noticed.

They crossed the basin lip just as the light began to flatten. The ground broke open beneath them in long oxide canyons and shelves of exposed black stone. Old freight tracks clung to ridgelines and then vanished where slides had taken them. Dust moved through the deeper cuts in pale streamers. Far off, the ruins of a relay station stood on three legs instead of four, the whole structure listing like a drunk trying to stay dignified.

Jet felt the ship answer the terrain. The air changed. The wind came harder off the stone. He worked the controls, small movements, steady pressure. Behind him the ship held its shape: Faye on the couch, Spike in the right seat, Ed pouring out route updates and nonsense and the occasional fact too sharp to count as nonsense.

"Old roadbed below," Ed said. "That one connected a clinic run, three freight depots, and a fuel cache that exploded in '66. Also, a man filed forty-seven maintenance complaints about the road surface before he quit. Ed respects him."

"You would," Jet said.

Ed rested chin on the back of the chair. "The first twelve had diagrams. Number thirty just said 'road still bad, see previous.' Ed read them all — all forty-seven went to the same depot office that approved the road."

The line sat in the cockpit a second longer than the others had. Jet adjusted trim he didn't need to adjust. Out the glass, the basin went on opening, one scar after another, heading toward the south where Dry Well waited in a place the maps treated like an afterthought.

Ed's words hung in the cockpit a beat, then the engines swallowed them. The ship kept going.

* * *

By the time the sun had dropped low enough to turn the cockpit windows copper, the Bebop felt smaller than it had that morning. Faye noticed because she had been waiting for somebody to finally vanish into a room and stay there. They were all still within earshot. Even Spike's exits had been half-hearted. He went to check something, or pretended to, and came back with the same face he wore when he didn't want to explain himself.

She came out of the head toweling her hair with one of Jet's least disgusting rags and found Jet and Spike at the common table. Ed had pulled an old topographic survey over the main screen and layered it with ghost routes in three colors. Dry Well sat near the bottom edge, a blinking square under Ed's ridiculous crown. Jet braced one hand on the table and the black fingers of his prosthetic clicked once against the metal rim. Spike sat opposite him with his chair tipped back on two legs, looking bored in the exact way he looked when he was paying complete attention.

Faye almost kept walking. Then she didn't. She dropped onto the couch with the towel around her shoulders and let the shape of the room do the listening for her.

Jet touched one of the old lines on the screen. "This feeder route. The clinic supply ran off this one?"

Spike leaned forward. The chair legs came down. "Sometimes. Depends what year."

"Try me."

"Fuel, machine parts, ammo, food if somebody up the chain got sentimental. Medical crates when the settlements got loud enough."

"And you knew that."

Spike's eyes stayed on the screen. "I knew crates got marked priority."

"That's not what I asked."

Faye looked from one to the other. Jet's voice was level, which was new and somehow worse than when he yelled. Yelling burned off heat. This just sat there.

Spike rubbed his thumb along the edge of the table. "Yeah. Sometimes I knew medicine was in the mix."

Jet waited.

"I didn't know whose," Spike said. "I knew weight. Timing. Handoff codes. Fuel. You miss a stop, somebody on the line starts screaming over comms. That was the part I dealt with."

Jet's jaw shifted once. "And that was enough for you."

"It was the job."

"Yeah."

There was no snap in it, no cheap victory. Jet looked tired in a way that had nothing to do with sleep. Faye knew that look better than she liked. It was the face people made when the explanation they finally got turned out to be exactly as bad as they had guessed.

Ed, upside down now from the ladder to the upper bunks, said, "Route Four had radiation spikes in '65. Also mold. Nobody liked Route Four except mold."

One of the galley cabinet latches clicked. Ed did not seem offended.

Jet dragged two fingers across the screen until a second line lit up, thinner, official. "My side looked cleaner on paper. Daichi Kozuki turned into handler initials, reimbursement codes, a transfer request stamped twice and buried. All trimmed down so nobody had to think about the body attached to any of it."

Spike said nothing.

Jet looked at him at last. "I let my partner keep that side of the wall because it was easier. I told myself that's how the precinct worked. Need to know, chain of command, all that bullshit. He had names, I had cases, and if I didn't push too hard then I got to keep pretending I was doing the decent part of the job."

Spike leaned back again, not far. "You were doing your job."

Jet barked a humorless laugh. "That's your defense?"

"No." Spike's voice flattened. "It's what I told myself too."

The room went quiet enough that Faye could hear the little rattle in the vent behind the galley shelf. Jet's prosthetic hand stayed on the table. The other one curled around nothing.

"When the chip came out," Jet said, "I wanted a clean line. I wanted my partner on one end and you on the other and me somewhere off to the side where I could say I wasn't part of it except by bad luck."

Spike's mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "Find one?"

"No."

"Yeah."

Jet looked back at the map. The old routes crossed the basin like scars from different knives. "I wasn't asking for absolution."

"Good."

Jet's head lifted. "Don't start."

"I'm not." Spike spoke without force, almost without inflection. "There isn't any. Not for me, not for you. I kept my eyes on what made the line move. You kept yours on what made the paperwork hold together. Same trick."

Faye pulled the towel tighter around her shoulders.

Jet exhaled through his nose. "You ever wonder who was at the other end of those deliveries. Not the route code. The room."

Spike took a second with it. "Not enough."

Jet nodded once. He had probably wanted that answer for a while. Faye couldn't tell if getting it helped.

Spike went on, quieter. "If a clinic got missed, I knew because somebody bitched about rescheduling. If a courier disappeared, I knew because another one got assigned. I thought that was me keeping out of the rest."

"It wasn't."

"No."

Ed swung gently from the ladder and said, "The route manifests and the precinct transfer logs have nine dates that match within the same hour. Ed made a chart. It looked like a zipper."

Faye turned her head. "Could you not."

Ed considered that. "Nope."

Jet's mouth pulled to one side despite himself. It vanished fast. He pushed off the table. "I'm heating something."

Spike stood when Jet did, as if some agreement had ended. "Wake me if the approach changes."

"It always changes."

"Then wake me."

He headed for the hall. Faye said, "Elegant contribution."

Spike lifted one hand without turning around and kept going.

Jet took a packet from the galley locker, frowned at it, put it back, took another. He had the look of a man furious at dry goods for not solving a problem. Faye stayed where she was a beat longer, then got up and came over because there was no point pretending she'd missed all of that.

Jet filled a pan with recycled water and set it on the burner. "If you're going to say I went easy on him, save it."

"Wasn't going to."

"You were thinking it."

"I think a lot of things."

"That's new."

She leaned a hip against the counter. "See, this is why nobody tells you anything nice."

Jet snorted. The burner clicked, caught, and filled the galley with a faint hot-metal smell before the broth powder hit the water. He started cutting preserved greens into smaller pieces that absolutely did not need to be smaller. His left hand pinned the board. His right worked the knife. The movements were too precise to be casual.

Faye watched him a moment. Then she asked, "What was it like?"

Jet didn't look up. "What was what like?"

"Being a cop."

The knife stopped. "You've asked me a lot of things. That's a new one."

"I know."

"Why."

Faye looked past him at the porthole over the sink. Mars moved there in a slow red band, all stone and shadow. "That picture on the chip. Your precinct in the back. Him with the ISSP shoulders." She shrugged. "I keep trying to picture what a regular day looked like around all that. That's all."

Jet went back to the greens. "Regular day."

"Yeah. Not the corruption, not the dramatic downfall, not the part where you drink in bars and stare into the middle distance. Just the job."

"I don't stare into the middle distance."

"You absolutely do."

"Fine." He scraped the greens into the pan. "Most days it was reports. Coffee that tasted like the machine hated you. Radios going off when you finally sat down. A lot of waiting on paperwork that should've cleared an hour ago. A lot of people lying badly."

Faye folded her arms. "That's every institution I've ever met."

"Pretty much." He opened a cabinet, frowned into it, came up with soy sauce. "The report room smelled like toner and wet coats in winter. Hallway always smelled like somebody's noodles because night shift kept eating at the desk. We had one vending machine that stole your coins unless you kicked it low on the right side."

She didn't realize until then that she had wanted details like that, dumb details. Something with no moral attached. Something a dead woman might have walked past on the way to use her stolen name.

Jet kept going, not sentimental, not bitter, just inventorying. "You'd get sent out to the same blocks over and over. Same domestic calls, same landlords, same guys who thought they were smarter than everyone because they changed the door code after every arrest. You knew which stairwells smelled like piss and which ones smelled like machine grease because somebody was running a shop out of their apartment. You knew who'd talk if you brought coffee and who'd only talk if you left."

"Did you like it?"

He gave that the respect of thinking before answering. "Parts of it. I liked knowing where I was supposed to be. I liked that if a kid went missing there were cars to put on it, forms to file, people to wake up. Most of it was slow and stupid, but sometimes being there mattered for ten minutes. Sometimes that was enough."

The broth began to simmer. Jet stirred it, then frowned at the powder packet as if it had insulted him personally.

"And the rest?" Faye asked.

"The rest was how easy it got to turn people into categories." He kept stirring. "Witness. Suspect. Informant. Transient. Body in an alley. You put the right words on the page and the page starts feeling more real than the person. That's the bad part. That's the part that sticks."

Faye looked down at the counter seam under her hands. "Yeah."

Jet slid two bowls out and set them down harder than necessary. "Why are you asking me this now?"

She shrugged once. It felt inadequate. "Because I spent half a day in a records office looking at my own name attached to somebody else's prescriptions and signatures and clinic visits. And I still can't get a grip on her because records don't breathe." She glanced at him. "I figured maybe a precinct does."

Jet's face changed almost imperceptibly. Not softer. Just less defended. "You never told me what that place was like."

Faye laughed without humor. "A records office? Sexy stuff."

"Faye."

She hated that tone because it worked. She picked at a chip in the counter laminate with one nail. "There was a clerk. Mid-forties, shirt buttoned wrong, coffee stain old enough to vote. Wedding ring worn flat on one side. He kept saying they were closing and I kept sitting there and then he turned the sign to CLOSED and left me with the terminal anyway."

Jet set the spoon down.

"The air unit made this awful knocking sound every twenty seconds," she said. "Like somebody trying to get out of a pipe. Screen kept timing out and asking if I was still there. I must've hit yes fifty times." She gave a small, ugly smile. "That's probably the most commitment I've shown anyone in years."

Jet huffed a laugh before he could stop it.

"He let you stay," he said.

"Yeah."

"That was decent."

"Weirdly, yes."

She could still see the room if she let herself, the flat blue light on the terminal, the nicotine yellow in the ceiling tiles, the way the clerk had made a point of not looking at her too hard on his way out. Not kindness exactly. Recognition, maybe. The ugly little courtesy of letting somebody come apart in private.

"I didn't cry," she said, and then, because that sounded like she thought it mattered, "Not that I was trying not to. I just sat there."

Jet ladled broth into the bowls. "Sometimes that's what there is."

Ed dropped from the ladder and landed barefoot on the deck with a slap. "Question for former cop Jet. Did cop Jet have a lunch box?"

Jet blinked. "What."

"A lunch box. Sandwich container. Soup cylinder. Sad pickle chamber."

Faye barked a laugh. Jet looked offended by the whole line of inquiry.

"I had a thermos," he said.

Ed gasped. "Metal or plastic?"

"Metal."

"Was there tea?"

"Coffee."

"Did it leak?"

Jet pointed a spoon at Ed. "Every damn day."

Ed nodded with grave satisfaction. "Excellent. Authentic."

Ein, awake now, trotted in and sat hopefully by Jet's knee. Jet tore a strip from the preserved greens packet and gave it to him without breaking rhythm. Faye took her bowl when Jet handed it over, burned her fingers, swore, and took it anyway. The broth was too salty and too thin. It helped.

Ed had already moved on to the screen. "Dry Well beacon just woke up."

Both adults looked over.

Ed tapped the blinking square. "Somebody brought one more heater online in the main bay. Tiny power draw, very consistent. Also an exterior transponder ping, very low strength, like a polite cough. So. Home stretch."

The room held still around that.

"She's there," Faye said.

"Very probably," Ed said. Then, with cheerful irrelevance or maybe not, "Also Ein stole Spike's sock."

Ein looked innocent in the aggressive way only a dog could.

Jet took his own bowl and leaned against the counter. "How long?"

"Forty-nine minutes if the wind keeps behaving. Fifty-six if Mars remembers it has opinions."

Faye ate another spoonful. It tasted like salt and metal and whatever passed for greens after too long in storage. Outside the porthole the last of the light dragged across the stone. Inside, nobody said anything grand. Jet drank broth. Ed hunted signal noise. Ein crunched stolen evidence under the table.

After a minute Faye put her bowl down and said, to no one in particular, "I'm going up."

Jet nodded once. He didn't ask where. On the Bebop that counted as courtesy.

* * *

Spike was already on the observation deck.

Of course he was. There were only so many places on the ship where a person could go to be alone without making a statement about it, and the observation deck was the best of them. Too cold, too exposed, not comfortable enough for lingering unless you meant it.

He stood with his hands in his pockets, looking out through the forward glass. Night had not fully come down yet. Mars below was a long basin of dark rust and black stone with strips of old infrastructure catching the last light. A relay mast flashed once and went dark. Far ahead, too far to make anything of, one square on the scope marked Dry Well.

He heard Faye before the door finished opening. Not loud. Just the particular annoyed economy of her footsteps when she was carrying a hot bowl and refusing to admit it.

"You hiding?" she asked.

"No."

"Looks like it."

"Then I'm doing it wrong."

She came to stand beside him, not close enough to touch. She had ditched the bowl somewhere on the way up and kept the sweatshirt. The collar sat crooked, exposing one shoulder. She didn't bother fixing it. The glass put a faint copper stripe across her face.

For a while they looked out without talking. The observation deck always gave the planet too much room. From here the canyons looked carved and deliberate, like somebody had taken a tool to the whole basin and then walked off mid-job. Spike watched the shadowed shelves roll under them and tried, not for the first time, to imagine Ayane. He never got farther than a pair of hands on a crate and a name attached after the fact. Julia came easier. Julia always came easier. That was part of the problem and maybe all of it.

Faye rubbed one thumb against the heel of her hand. "Ed says she's got the bay heated."

"Yeah."

"That's almost hospitable."

"Maybe she doesn't want us freezing while we make asses of ourselves."

Faye snorted. "There's the confidence."

Another stretch of silence. The ship hummed around them. Somewhere aft, a panel ticked as it cooled and reheated. Through the glass, a dried reservoir flashed past, its retaining wall split open so the basin floor below showed in pale, useless terraces.

Then Faye said, "What you told Jet down there, that can't be the whole landing plan."

Spike kept his eyes on the glass. "Didn't know I had one."

"You do now." She turned toward him. "When we get down there, don't give her that weight-timing-fuel routine and act like that's the end of your part in it."

He watched the reservoir slip behind them. "Back then that was enough. Weight. Timing. Fuel. Names on a handoff. If I kept it there, I didn't have to think about the people at the far end."

Faye waited.

"It doesn't work now," he said. "Not with Dry Well right in front of us."

She studied his face as if looking for the exit in it. "So stay in the room."

He glanced at her.

"I'm serious," she said. "If she asks what you knew, answer her. Don't go drifting off the second it gets ugly."

Spike took a second. "Yeah."

"That yes or your fake yes."

He looked back out at the dark basin. "I'll stay. If she asks, I'll answer."

Faye looked at him a beat longer than she meant to, then turned back to the glass.

"She died in my name," Faye said after a moment. "That's still a hell of a thing to fit in my head."

"I know."

"No, you don't."

Spike glanced at her. She was not looking at him now. Her fingers had gone to her collarbone and stopped there, two knuckles pressing the fabric down like she was checking for something under it.

"No," he said. "Not that part."

That seemed to satisfy her, or at least not irritate her further. On the Bebop those were close cousins.

"Jet told me what the precinct smelled like," she said.

"That sounds like Jet."

"Wet coats. Bad coffee. Copier heat." She breathed out through her nose. "I wanted something ordinary. I got sick of records."

"Records are easier."

"For who?"

He thought about it. "Nobody, probably."

Faye gave him a look sideways, quick and sharp. "Look at you, saying almost-human things."

"Don't spread it around."

"I won't. Ruin your reputation."

The corner of his mouth moved. That was all. Under them the basin began to rise again toward a line of broken ridges. The scope square for Dry Well brightened by a fraction as distance closed. Somewhere behind them, Ed whooped at some private victory and then immediately started humming again.

Faye said, quieter, "I keep thinking if I'd remembered anything real, anything from before, maybe this whole thing would've landed different."

Spike leaned one shoulder against the frame. "Would it?"

"I don't know." She sounded annoyed by the question. "Maybe I'd hate her more. Maybe less. Maybe I'd know if the gesture was mine first."

"Does it matter?"

"To me? Today? Yeah, a little."

He let that stand. Then he said, because there was no cleaner way into it, "For what it's worth, I don't think any of this was waiting on your memory. Or mine. Or Jet's. It was already moving."

She looked at him again. "That's not you trying to make me feel better, is it?"

"No."

"Good."

The door slid open behind them. Ed poked in sideways, hair worse than usual, Spike's missing sock hanging from one hand like evidence in a trial only Ed understood.

"Observation deck people," Ed announced. "Dry Well has one ship, one active bay heater, two exterior lamps, and a structural groan that Ed does not enjoy. Also arrival in eleven minutes."

Ed peered from one adult face to the other and added, matter of fact, "Adults get extra weird right before landing."

"Thanks, Ed," Faye said.

"You're welcome." Ed considered the sock. "Ein says this belongs to the tall sulky one."

"That's me?"

"Today yes."

Ed left as abruptly as Ed had arrived. The door shut. Faye looked down at the sock in Spike's hand because apparently Ed had transferred it at some point during the announcement.

"You gonna wear that," she said, "or frame it."

"Haven't decided."

She laughed, softer this time. It faded without embarrassment.

From the cockpit Jet's voice came over the ship intercom. "Ten minutes. Strap in if you want to keep your teeth."

Faye pushed off the glass. "There's the charm."

Spike stayed one second longer, looking out at the weak square of light ahead. Dry Well was still too far to see with the naked eye, just signal and distance and the knowledge of someone waiting. He thought of Jet at the table, of Faye in the records office hitting yes over and over, of a courier he couldn't picture and an informant he had never known existed while he was alive. None of it arranged itself into anything useful. It just stayed there, weight with no good place to set it down.

He turned and went inside.

The ship folded them back into place. Jet was at the controls. Spike took up station behind Jet's seat and didn't bother pretending he was only there for the approach. Faye slid into the jump seat behind the cockpit and braced a hand against the strap. Ed had already strapped in upside down somehow, still humming. Ein circled twice under the table and settled. On the main screen, the distance count kept falling and the approach box tightened over the basin.

Faye kept her hand planted on the strap. Ed's humming thinned to breath through his teeth. Spike said nothing. No one said anything. The only thing that changed in the room was the engine pitch dropping under Jet's hand while the weak square ahead got bright enough to count as a destination.


# Chapter 16

Dry Well looked like somebody had tried to build a town out of scrap hull and bad intentions, then got tired halfway through and let the dust finish the job.

Jet brought the Bebop down slow over the settlement's one clear patch of ground, if a hardpan lot ringed with busted piping could still count as clear. The old waystation sat in a shallow cut of red stone with the wind worrying at it from all sides. Three low buildings hunched against one another as if they had spent years bracing for a blow that never quite stopped coming. Cracked solar panels leaned on rusted frames. A dead condenser tower stood out back like a snapped neck. One ship bay still had power. Ren's ship sat inside it, clean and dark and very much at home in a place that had no business looking lived in.

Spike stood just behind Jet's shoulder with one hand in his pocket, staring through the forward glass. "Used to be a syndicate fuel stop."

Jet kept his eyes on the settling numbers. "You recognize it."

"Recognize the type." Spike's voice was flat. "Places like this were all over the basin. Cheap walls, bad wiring, one room for cargo and one room for lies."

Ed lay upside down across the co-pilot chair, goggles crooked on her forehead, bare feet hooked over the headrest. "Dry Well has one human ping outside, one warm machinery nest inside, no sneaky turret blips, no beep-beep death gifts. Dry Well is rude but not armed."

Faye, strapped into the jump seat behind them, said, "That's comforting."

"Moderately comforting," Ed amended. "Mars-standard comforting."

Jet brought the Bebop lower. Dust skated out from under the thrusters in long red sheets, then curled back on itself and slapped the bay doors. Through the haze he made out a person standing beside an engine block on a waist-high stand. Dark hair tied back. Coveralls. Rag in one hand, wrench in the other. She had been bent over the engine a second ago. Now she was upright, looking at them come in.

She did not run.

What Jet had been braced for, without admitting it to himself, was motion: a sprint for the ship, a slammed hatch, the old familiar shape of a chase. Instead the woman only stepped back far enough to keep the wash off her face and watched the Bebop descend with the patient expression of a mechanic waiting for somebody else to finish doing a job badly.

Jet set the ship down. The deck thudded under them. For a second all he heard was the cooling tick of old metal and the soft whine of systems settling.

Nobody moved first.

Then Ed unbuckled in a single boneless slide and dropped to the deck. "Adventure cave."

"Not alone," Jet said.

"Jet-person says that every time there is an adventure cave."

"And every time I'm right."

Faye was already on her feet, smoothing a hand down the front of her yellow shorts as if she had dressed for a confrontation she did not intend to take seriously. "If she makes us walk into some sentimental death trap, I'm haunting all of you."

"You'll fit right in," Spike said.

Faye gave him a look without any heat in it. The trip south had worn some of the sharp edges off them, or maybe it had only made the sharpness less useful.

Jet hit the ramp controls. "Nobody starts anything."

"That aimed at me?" Faye asked.

"If the shoe fits."

"You mean if the tiny impossible pair of stupid shoes fits."

Ed, already halfway to the hatch, said, "Ed wears no shoes. Ed is innocent."

The ramp opened on cold air with iron in it. Mars in the south had a different bite than Tharsis. The wind came cleaner out here, not because the basin was pure but because there was less of everything human to blunt it. Dust struck the metal ramp in fast dry whispers. Somewhere in the settlement a loose panel clanged on a rhythm too uneven to become background.

Jet stepped down first. The ground gave a little under his boots, packed dust over old concrete. Spike came after him, loose as always, though Jet knew better than to trust what Spike looked like. Faye descended with one hand near the pistol at the small of her back. Ed came last because Ed had stopped halfway down to peer under the ramp at a line of blackened pipe running into the dirt. Ein trotted after her, nose busy, little body low against the wind.

The woman by the engine wiped her hands on the rag and tossed it over one shoulder onto an open toolbox. She was younger up close than the trouble she had caused. Early twenties, maybe. Lean, medium height. Sharp face. The resemblance Faye had flinched from on the chip was there if a person let their eyes blur for it, but it did not survive attention. This woman looked like she had been built by long work and thin meals, not cryogenic chance. Her hands were what Jet noticed most. Grease in the cuts, calluses thick across the base of the fingers, one thumbnail split and growing out crooked. Hands that made a living by getting skinned.

She took them in, one by one. Jet. Spike. Faye. Ed crouched by the pipe. Ein sniffing a bolt. Her attention stopped nowhere long enough to become a challenge.

"You made good time," she said.

Her voice was steady and lower than Jet expected, carrying the settled patience of somebody who had long ago accepted that conversations of consequence happened whether a person felt ready or not.

Jet said, "Ren Kozuki."

"Yeah."

Faye folded her arms. "You couldn't have just sent a note?"

Ren tipped her chin toward the Bebop behind them. "You would've ignored a note."

"Try me."

"I did." Ren nodded toward the ship. "That was the note."

Jet ought to have been angry. Part of him still was. Eight thousand woolongs in fines, fuel burned, old files dragged up from places he had buried them with both hands. But standing in front of the woman who had done it, with the engine stand between them and the wind kicking grit at his cuffs, anger had nowhere clean to go. There was too much face on the thing now. Too much actual life.

Spike nodded toward the engine block on the stand. "You were really fixing that."

Ren followed his gaze. "Starter assembly's been chewing itself. It wasn't going to improve because you showed up."

That got the faintest twitch at one corner of Spike's mouth. It vanished before it was a smile.

Ed straightened from the pipe and pointed into the building. "Dry Well has old signal bones in the walls. They are crunchy."

"They are," Ren said. "Don't touch the junction box in the common room unless you want the lights to die."

Ed nodded solemnly, as if receiving sacred law.

Jet had been waiting for the next move, the reveal of the gunmen on the roof, the lock dropping on the bay, something. Ren only picked up the rag again and wiped a streak of oil from the back of her wrist.

"If you're here to talk," she said, "come inside. If you're here to drag me out in cuffs, I don't have any, and I'd rather not do it in the dust. It gets into the bearings."

Faye said, "You always this hospitable?"

"No." Ren slung the rag into the toolbox and snapped it shut with her heel. "Just practical."

She turned and headed toward the building without checking whether they followed. Years of having only herself to rely on had put a hard confidence in the way she moved. Jet exchanged a look with Spike. Spike gave the smallest shrug. Faye rolled her eyes at both of them and started after Ren. Ed was already gone, following her in a crooked line with Ein weaving around her ankles.

The bay door groaned open on manual tracks. Inside, the air held the layered smell of hot dust, machine oil, old insulation, and something boiled to death in recycled water. Ren's ship took up most of the bay, its hull scarred now that Jet could see it without wanting to kill it on sight. Tools hung in neat shadow shapes along one wall. Parts were sorted into old food tins and labeled in blocky marker. Somebody had made this place work by refusing to care that it had not been built to.

The common room opened off the bay through a narrow pressure door with a bad seal. Jet felt the draft before he saw the room. A cot against the back wall. A workbench under the only intact strip light. Two mismatched chairs, one with the stuffing showing through the cracked vinyl. A kettle on a hot plate. And across the largest wall, from shoulder height nearly to the ceiling, a spread of paper and plastic sleeves and copied photos and route maps and handwritten notes pinned so tightly together that at first Jet could not tell where one ended and the next began.

Ed stopped dead in the doorway.

"Oh," she said.

The sound had none of her usual bounce in it.

It was not a detective's board. It was worse than that. Better, maybe, if a person respected labor. No theatrical loops of string. No red circles drawn by somebody in love with accusation. Just years of patient accumulation. An ISSP report with a precinct stamp Jet knew in his bones. A syndicate cargo manifest Spike had once held in his own hands. A clinic intake form with Faye Valentine's name typed across the top and a corner torn away where some other identifier had been removed. Old transit maps of the Hellas corridor. A photograph of a dark-haired woman with a little of Faye in the shape of the mouth and none of her in the eyes, standing under a clinic awning with one arm folded tight across her waist. Another photo, older and grainier, of a man in cheap civilian clothes holding a toddler on his hip while looking over his own shoulder as if the camera might be trouble.

Jet knew the paper before he could read it. ISSP stock had a grain to it. Even copied three times, it kept the look of institutional cheapness.

Nobody said anything.

Ren set the toolbox down by the workbench. "The water takes a minute if anybody wants tea."

Jet kept staring at the wall. "Start talking."

* * *

The line she had planned to begin with was gone by the time the kettle started to tremble.

Ren had built the first ten minutes of this conversation a hundred times in her head over the years. On bus rides. Under ships. Half asleep with a light still on because she had fallen into records again. The order had always seemed important. Start with the files. No, start with her father because he came first. No, start with her mother because the face would make Faye listen. Keep it clean. Keep it factual. Do not let them turn you into a stray problem they can solve by deciding what you feel.

Then the five of them were in the room, taking up more space than they had any right to, and nothing held shape.

Jet stood closest to the wall, close enough that he would have had to shift if he wanted room to pace. Big shoulders set, weight balanced wrong, one hand on his hip near the old familiar place a badge used to ride. Spike leaned against the doorframe into the bay, eyes on the route maps first and only then on Ren. Faye had taken the chair with the torn vinyl and sat on the edge of it, one knee bouncing once before she shut it down. Ed crouched on the floor under the document wall with her hands on her ankles, looking up. Ein curled beside her foot, already choosing comfort over history.

Ren poured hot water over a tea brick shaved into a dented pot. The smell that rose was bitter and old and barely tea at all.

"It's bad," she said. "But it's warm."

"That's a ringing endorsement," Faye muttered.

She took a cup anyway when Ren handed them around. So did Jet. Spike shook his head, then changed his mind and took one after all. Ed accepted hers with both hands and sniffed it like a suspicious engineer.

Jet did not drink. "You said start talking."

Ren leaned back against the workbench, cup warming her palm. "All right."

She looked at the photo of the man with the toddler because looking at his face was easier than looking at theirs.

"My father's name was Daichi Kozuki," she said. "You already know that part. He was an informant for ISSP out of your precinct."

Jet's jaw moved once. "I know the name."

"You knew the access codes. That's not the same thing."

His eyes shifted to her. Surprise showed first. Then something smaller crossed his face and was gone before she could name it.

Ren set her cup down. "He didn't work for ISSP because he loved the law. He worked for ISSP because he thought information would buy him room. That's all he ever wanted, really. Room. My mother said he hated owing anybody money and hated owing anybody favors even more. He kept copies of everything because he figured if somebody tried to bury him, he'd at least leave a mess behind."

She crossed to the wall and tapped a row of plastic sleeves near the middle. Old paper. Handwritten tabs. Green, blue, yellow, all cut from cheap file dividers.

"He color-coded by mood," she said. "Green if he thought something was dangerous. Blue if he thought it was stupid. Yellow if he thought it was both. He wrote complaints in the margins. Mostly about coffee. Sometimes about people's ties."

Ed tilted her head. "Organized grudge system."

"Yeah," Ren said. "Pretty much."

The room changed by a hair. Jet's gaze caught on the colored tabs. Faye looked again at the photo and stayed there a beat longer than before.

Ren went on. "He worked routes in and out of the basin. Freight gossip, small syndicate movements, names of couriers, where shipments got thin because somebody in the middle had taken a cut. He fed pieces upward and thought that if he made himself useful enough, ISSP would protect us if things got bad."

Spike finally sipped the tea and made a face. "That's one mistake."

"He knew it wasn't clean," Ren said. "He didn't think it had to be clean. He thought it had to be better than the other side."

Jet spoke without looking away from the papers. "Who was handling him."

The words landed flat. Ren heard what sat under them. A test, maybe. Or a wish that there would still be some room left for uncertainty.

"Your partner," she said. "Not you."

Jet turned to her then, fully. "You sure."

He had not put a question mark on it. Jet's voice, when pain got near it, flattened out instead of rising. Ren had expected anger. The stillness was worse.

She moved two steps down the wall and pulled a copied ledger page from behind a route map. Another sheet came after it, then a photo with a precinct timestamp burned into one corner.

"These were in my father's storage unit," she said. "Along with a key to a dead drop box that still had the bank notices in it. Small payments. Irregular. Your partner's shell account on one side. A freight concern tied to Mars syndicate people on the other. Money coming in after my father turned over route updates, not on every exchange but often enough."

Jet took the pages from her. His metal fingers were careful on the paper. More careful than flesh had ever made him.

"Could be coincidence," he said. The sentence came out flat, like something he had already used on uglier facts.

"It could be," Ren said. "If it was only that."

She tapped the photograph. Two men outside a precinct service entrance. One of them turned half away from the camera. The other was Daichi, thinner than in the family photo, shoulders up around his ears. Fear had a posture once a person learned to recognize it.

"This was two days before he died. The timestamp puts it after an off-book meeting. The next report he handed over was a corridor map for the Hellas run. Forty-eight hours later the syndicate moved on one of the safe transfer points he had just flagged. That told him the leak was inside ISSP, not out on the street. He wrote that down too."

She handed Jet another copy, this one of her father's own note. Block letters, cramped. NOISE INSIDE HOUSE. WATCH HANDLER.

Jet read it. His eyes did not move for a while.

Ren heard the hot plate tick as it cycled off.

"How was he killed?" Jet asked.

Faye shut her eyes for half a second and pinched the bridge of her nose.

Ren could have softened it. She knew how. Years around clinics had taught everybody in the basin the house language for horror. Complications. Incident. Acute event. Loss. Words that dressed the body before it was cold.

She was tired of those words.

"He went to a meet point because your partner told him ISSP extraction would be there," she said. "It wasn't. Three syndicate men were."

The room held still.

"The report says blunt-force trauma and asphyxiation," Ren said. "The scene photos say they broke his right hand first, then two fingers on the left, then most of his teeth. He still didn't give them the backup box. They used a wire after that."

Ed's hand came down to rest on Ein's back and stayed there, still.

Jet set the pages carefully on the chair arm beside him, like paper could break louder than a person if he mishandled it. Ren watched his throat work once. He looked back at the wall instead of at her.

"He had a daughter," he said.

"Yeah."

"And he still kept copies."

Ren almost smiled. He had gone straight to the practical failure in it, same way he had inventoried the room on sight: a daughter, a storage unit, a pile of risk. That was how it sounded to her, anyway.

"He thought the copies were what would keep us safe," she said. "He wasn't wrong about how the world worked. He was wrong about which side would use them first."

Jet finally drank the tea. It had gone lukewarm enough to stop pretending.

Faye set her cup down untouched. "And your mother."

Ren had expected Faye to do that, cut across the room and drag the wound into the middle where everybody had to look at it. Some people did that to hurt. With Faye, to Ren, it looked more like self-defense.

She turned to the clinic form with the typed name.

"Ayane Kozuki," she said. "My mother."

The picture there had been taken under bad weather light. Even copied, the resemblance reached across paper. The line of the jaw. The mouth when it was tired. The eyes never matched.

"She ran courier work before my father died and after," Ren said. "Mostly medicine, machine parts, small freight. Sometimes guns if the route came attached to the rest and saying no meant no more work at all. She wasn't syndicate because she believed in it. She was syndicate because Mars has a lot of jobs that become syndicate jobs if you get poor enough."

Spike's cup stopped halfway to his mouth. He lowered it without drinking.

Ren kept going. "She'd had a kidney transplant before I was born. I don't know all the reasons. She barely talked about before. She hated being looked at like a lesson. What mattered was the meds after. Immunosuppressants. Anti-rejection drugs. Regular bloodwork if she could get it, which mostly she couldn't."

Faye's voice was hard and level. "And she got those with my name."

"Yes."

The borrowed name sat between them now, ugly and exact. Ren let it stay there.

"After my father died, Kozuki wasn't a usable name anymore," she said. "Too visible to the wrong people, not visible enough to the right ones. Clinics saw a widow tied to a dead informant and a courier network and found reasons to lose paperwork. Landlords saw trouble. Employers saw it too. A clean identity packet came through a clerk at the cryogenic facility. My mother bought it because it worked."

Faye laughed once, and there was nothing amused in it. "Identity packet. That's nice."

"I know."

"Do you?"

There was a bite in it, but not a plea. Faye stayed back in the chair, chin up, waiting. It looked to Ren like she wanted the word said plain.

"It was theft," Ren said. "It kept her alive and it was theft."

Faye sat with that for a long beat, searching for some slick little adjustment in the language. Ren gave her none. After a second Faye leaned back in the chair and crossed one leg over the other, a motion too sharp to be casual.

"Go on," she said.

Ren nodded.

"The records office packet had biometrics, financial history, medical access, some intake footage. Enough to pass under pressure if nobody was checking too hard. My mother learned the name fast. Faye Valentine got better service than Ayane Kozuki. Faye Valentine got seen. Faye Valentine could stand at a clinic desk and have somebody look at the chart instead of the woman."

She paused, then added, because if she was going to say it she was going to say it all the way, "She wasn't good at being your kind of reckless. Too careful. But she got good at the parts of you that opened doors."

Faye's fingers moved once toward her own collarbone, then stopped in the air and closed into a fist instead.

Spike said, "The medication. My route."

He laid the words on the table like a weight and let them sit there.

Ren pulled the annotated manifest off the wall and held it out to him. He did not take it right away. When he did, his thumb went to the note in the corner before the rest of the page.
