"Most of the shipments she relied on came through a corridor your alias helped standardize," Ren said. "Enough that when I started mapping shortages against route changes, your name kept showing up where the lines tightened."

Spike read the manifest without expression. The absence of expression was expression with him.

"I wrote that note," Ren said.

"Figured." His voice was quiet.

"My mother told me about the locker. Said people like your bosses always kept extra nests in case they needed to vanish from each other. She'd used other waystations and knew the pattern. When I found out it was still yours, I put the manifest where you'd see it."

Spike looked up from the page. "Why."

Ren frowned a little. "Because it was yours."

"That's not an answer."

"It is if you think about it for five seconds."

For the first time since they'd come in, a little heat moved in the room, enough to show that Ren had edges and wasn't interested in sanding them down because the people in front of her carried old damage elegantly.

Spike's mouth flattened. Jet glanced between them and did not step in.

Ren set her cup aside. Her hands wanted a tool. There were none between them but paper, so she folded her arms instead.

"You asked why," she said. "Because if I'd left it on a public board or mailed it to the Bebop, it would've been one more weird thing in a pile of weird things. You would've treated it like bait, and you would've been right to." She turned the sleeve over once. "I needed you to find your own piece first. Your locker, the precinct file—so when the rest showed up, you'd know your part wasn't forged."

Spike looked back down at the manifest.

"She needed it to live," Ren said, softer now, because that sentence always came out softer no matter how she meant it. "Every day. Every week. It was pills cut in halves with a kitchen razor. It was praying a courier didn't skim the cold storage for resale. It was my mother sitting at the table with a bowl under her chin because the nausea hit before she could stand up. It was her ankles swelling over the tops of her boots so bad she stopped wearing boots. It was the skin around her eyes going yellow-gray. It was her pissing twice in a day and both times it smelled like pennies."

Nobody interrupted.

"When the route held, she looked almost fine," Ren said. "Tired, but fine. When it didn't, she shook. She'd sit on the edge of the cot and hold her own wrists because her hands wouldn't stop. By the end she had sores in her mouth and a taste like battery metal all the time. She'd spit into the sink and rinse and tell me the clinic lights were making her sick because that sounded less final than what was happening."

Faye had gone very still. Only her thumb moved once against the chair arm. Her posture stayed hard enough to crack a tooth on. Ren couldn't know what sentence Faye had built out of it. She could see what it cost her to keep that sentence off her face.

Jet rubbed the heel of his hand once against his beard. "How old were you."

"Nine when she died."

"And before that."

"Old enough to count pills." Ren shrugged. "Old enough to know what a clinic clerk's face looked like when the chart said Faye Valentine and the woman at the counter didn't look expensive enough for it."

Faye's laugh came out low this time, almost not there. "That sounds about right."

Ren heard the opening in it and left it alone.

"What was she like," Faye asked after a while, and then, with immediate irritation at her own question, "No, not the file version. Don't give me some research answer."

Ren turned back to the photo under the awning and tried to pick one thing that belonged to her mother alone. There were too many. There were not enough.

"She read the last page of detective magazines first," Ren said. "Said if the ending was stupid there was no reason to waste the middle. She couldn't keep a pair of coveralls intact at the knees because she was always dropping to the floor instead of bending like a normal person. She hated oranges. Said they made a room smell like hospital cleanser. She touched her collarbone when she was thinking or bluffing or trying not to let somebody see she was scared."

Faye's eyes lifted to hers.

"That last one," Faye said. "How."

Ren let out a breath through her nose. Faye had finally asked the thing underneath all the other questions.

"I don't know," she said.

Faye looked unconvinced and deserved better than a shrug, so Ren gave her the whole answer.

"The packet had a short intake clip from before you were frozen. Maybe she picked it up there. Maybe she saw it in some other part of the records and kept it because it helped the impersonation. Maybe it was hers already and she only made you doubt it because she was wearing your face when somebody saw her do it." Ren's mouth tightened. "I grew up watching her do that. Then I saw you do it in the market and I still don't know which of you I was looking at for a second. I can't tell you anything cleaner than that."

Faye looked away first, toward the clinic form on the wall. Her hand did go to her collarbone this time, not a performance, not even a conscious motion. She caught it there and dropped it to her thigh as if somebody else had moved it for her.

Ed spoke into the quiet. "Why the orphanage, then?"

All the adults turned. Ed hadn't looked up from Ein, but her voice had lost all its usual decorative bounce.

"Why baby-Ed papers," she said. "Ed was not in your dead-parent math."

Ren had expected the question less than she should have. She had spent so many years looking at the main lines of the web that she had still, sometimes, to remind herself that the shape of the Bebop now was not the shape it had been when her parents were alive.

"No," she said. "You weren't."

Ed waited.

"But you were on the ship." Ren glanced at the wall, at the places where the later notes were pinned in a slightly different pattern because those had been the years when the research stopped being about three names and started being about a crew. "I needed to know who was there, who stayed, who mattered."

"Ed mattered."

"Yeah."

Ed nodded like a person confirming a number against a record. "Okay."

That was all. Ren felt something in her chest loosen half an inch and hated that the kid had done it by being simpler than the adults.

Jet had moved closer to the photograph of Daichi with the toddler. He was studying it with the expression he used on crime scenes back when he still had reason to think looking hard enough could fix the world.

"I never knew him," he said.

Ren answered the sentence he meant, not the one he had said. "I know."

"I should've."

"Maybe." She was careful here because this was the ground where people tried to make themselves innocent by making themselves central. Jet did not strike her as that kind of man, but grief and guilt both made idiots out of people. "Or maybe the whole program was built so you wouldn't. Informants were supposed to be segmented. Need-to-know. That's what all the training docs say, anyway. My father thought that meant he was protected. Mostly it meant if he got killed, nobody on the floor had to feel it with a face attached."

Jet let out a breath that almost became a laugh and died before it found any humor. "That sounds like ISSP."

"Yeah." Ren leaned a shoulder against the workbench. "That's kind of the point."

He turned then, slower than before. "You keep saying point. All right. Let's get to it. You parked in our spot. You lit up dead files, old frequencies, records offices, half the wrong people on Mars. You chased us all over the basin. Now we're here. What was the point."

He'd finally stripped the whole thing down to one question he could hold.

Ren had thought she would be ready for this part. She had practiced it most. In every version of the conversation there was a line, neat and spare, something she could put down between them like a wrench and let the weight of it do the rest.

Instead she took one of the yellow-tabbed sleeves off the wall and held it by the corner. "I wanted you to get your own prints on it first," she said.

Jet's brow went down. "Meaning what."

"Meaning if I'd walked onto the Bebop and dumped all this in your lap, you'd spend ten minutes deciding whether I'd forged it and another twenty deciding which part was grief and which part was evidence. This way you already know your own pieces are real. The bounty. The alias. The records office. I needed the room past that."

Faye cut in before Jet answered. "He means if you wanted blood, you should say blood. If you wanted an apology, say that. If you wanted us miserable, congratulations, but don't make it sound noble."

Spike was still looking at the manifest. "If you want to say one of us killed her, say that too."

Ren looked at him. He hadn't raised his voice. If anything it had gone flatter. His face gave her almost nothing, just that flatness and the way he held the manifest too still. It looked less like a dare than a man done listening to people step around the point.

"No," she said.

Spike's eyes lifted.

"No what."

"No, I don't want to say that." Ren heard the roughness enter her own voice now, the tiny looseness that meant she was past the rehearsed part. She lifted the sleeve a little. "Look at the tabs. Green means dangerous. Blue means stupid. Yellow means both. Most of this wall is yellow. My father took cop money because he thought it bought one more week. My mother took syndicate work because the meds didn't care who stamped the invoice. A clerk sold a frozen woman's file because she could. Your partner sold names. You moved crates and didn't ask what was staying cold inside them."

She pushed off the bench and crossed to the wall again, not because the movement would make the argument better but because her hands had started to itch. She tapped the wall once with two fingers. Paper clicked softly under the nail.

"I kept trying to pin it to one body," she said. "Then another page would pull loose."

Jet's shoulders tightened. His thumb went still on the paper. The whole wall had changed shape on him: not one loose part knocking in the housing but the whole assembly grinding wrong.

Faye said, quieter now, "And us."

"Yes."

"Why us, specifically. Plenty of people were in that system."

"Because you were all on one ship." The answer came out more tired than sharp. "At first I thought one of you had built it that way. Then I started pulling the dumb little records around the edges. Docking fines. Permit renewals. Parts orders. Fuel debt. Bounty claims so small they barely paid for dinner. Nobody designed that." She rubbed at her thumbnail. "You just kept showing up in the same records. Same port, same—roof."

Jet blinked. "My what."

Ed made a small surprised sound that might have been a laugh trying not to get noticed.

Ren felt heat rise in her face for the first time all day. "I read what I could."

"Apparently."

The corner of Faye's mouth twitched despite herself. It was gone almost at once, but the room had shifted half a degree. That was all grief usually allowed.

Spike said, "That's your big revelation. Paperwork did it."

It would have been a dismissal from anybody else. Coming from him, to Ren, it sounded more like disgust at how ordinary the answer was.

Ren met his gaze. "Start with the paperwork if you want. You'll hit bribes and storms and greed soon enough."

He looked away first.

Jet had not moved. "You could've told us."

He'd finally hauled the real complaint into the room. Not just why did you do this. Why this way.

Ren nodded because that part, at least, was fair.

"I could've," she said. "I thought about it. Walk up to the Bebop and say hello, my father died because your precinct was rotten, my mother died wearing her name, your dead alias hauled her medicine for a while, and I need you to listen."

Faye snorted despite herself.

"Yeah," Ren said. "Exactly."

Jet dragged a hand over his beard. "We would've listened."

Ren did not call him a liar. He wasn't one. Not about that.

"Maybe you would've," she said. "For a few minutes. Then you'd ask where I got the files, split up after the parts that matched your own faces, and I'd still be here with a wall."

She took them in again, one by one.

"So I used the parts," she said. "Your docking spot. The Takara file. The old syndicate ping. Faye in the market. One shove wasn't enough. I needed all of you moving toward the same room, already holding your own proof."

Ed raised a hand from the floor as if in class. "Parking rage was ignition fuel."

Ren looked at Ed and, before she could stop it, laughed once, small but real.

"Yes," she said.

Jet's mouth pulled sideways. "You spent six months and good money on my berth because you needed ignition fuel."

"I prepaid because Mama Yun doesn't trust promises," Ren said. "And because I wasn't doing all this just to watch you leave at the hatch."

Faye let out a breath through her nose. "That's either commitment or brain damage."

"Probably both."

Spike said, "Hell."

Jet shook his head. Not laughter, but close enough to hear the same tired humor in it. "You could've just said you were serious."

"I did," Ren said. "You just don't read that kind of note."

Jet's eyes went again to the pages in his hand. "My partner."

The room dropped with the subject. Ren felt it in her knees.

"He's dead," she said.

Jet did not look up. "How."

"Syndicate dispute." Ren kept the words plain. "Years later. Over money from a warehouse split, not my father. He bled out in a loading yard because the people he was cheating got there first."

Jet let that sit. His eyes stayed on the copies. The line of his mouth gave nothing except that the answer hadn't helped.

"What's his name," Faye asked suddenly.

Jet's head came up. He was already half turned toward the precinct section. The copied ledger crackled in his hand.

Ren said, "No. Not like that."

Faye stared. "Seriously."

Ren pointed at Jet's fist. "Because if I say it now, he goes straight for that wall, you go straight for the name, and this whole room shrinks down to one dead bastard. He already took enough. The name's there. Read it when you can read the rest with it."

Jet only seemed to notice his own hand when the page edge bit into his palm. He let it flatten, slow and angry.

Faye said, "That's a hell of a way to ration information."

"It's five more minutes," Ren said. "You can survive it."

Faye broke the silence. "So what now."

Ren frowned. "What do you mean."

"I mean congratulations, we're informed. Now what."

Ren crouched by the lower shelf under the workbench and pulled out three clipped bundles of copies. She set them on the table hard enough to make the cups jump.

"Now this," she said.

Jet touched the top bundle with one metal fingertip. "You made sets."

"I made working copies. Tape fails."

Faye shook her head. "That's not what I meant."

"I know." Ren put her hand on the clinic packet. "I didn't bring you here to make you fix anything. There's no repair." She rubbed her thumb once along the clipped edge. "I just—my father kept one coat until the elbows wore through. My mother turned off any song with words when she was sick because she said voices crowded her. He cut the crusts off my bread because I told him the corners hurt." Her thumb stopped on the clip. "That's—I wanted them to have that. Not just the intake numbers."

The bay panel outside banged in the wind. Nobody flinched. The room had narrowed too far for that.

Jet put the pages down very carefully. He looked older than he had on the trip south, all of it settling as weight.

"And what do we do with it," he said.

"Read it. Don't. Ask me things. Leave in the morning. I don't care what order you do it in. Just don't tell me they were only paperwork."

"You said you don't blame us," he said.

Ren nodded.

"I don't know what that means."

That, at least, she had an answer for. "I blamed you some," she said. "And him. And the clerk. And the doctor who cut her dose. And the couriers skimming cold storage. And myself because nine is old enough to know something's wrong and too young to stop it. It moved around. That's all I mean."

Jet's mouth pulled to one side. "That's not much comfort."

"I didn't say comfort."

Ed rolled onto her side and squinted up at the wall. "Cop papers keep leaning at Jet-person. Clinic papers are biting Faye-person. Route maps are stuck to Spike-person's shoes. Very grabby wall."

For the first time since landing, the room rearranged itself without Ren pushing it. Jet took the precinct bundle and sat. Spike peeled the route copies from the clip without comment. Faye picked up the clinic packet and carried it to the wall as if she needed the original in sight.

Faye stopped in front of the clinic intake form, close enough that Ren could see her eyes moving line by line. Name. Height. Weight. Biometric confirmation. Prescription access. Beneath the typed words a clerk's pen had marked one dosage change in blue, then another in red. Faye read those marks twice, jaw tight, one fingernail riding the edge of the paper.

"Did she use it everywhere," Faye asked without turning.

"No. Just where she had to. Clinics. Contracts that wanted a clean ID. The Golden Ox once or twice because a respectable woman with money got watched differently than a courier from the basin."

Faye touched the edge of the form with one fingernail. "Was she good."

Ren knew what she meant.

"At cards? Good enough to leave before the table got angry. Better at reading who'd fold from boredom. Worse at reading who'd follow her into an alley. She once won three hands in a row and spent the whole walk home furious because she tipped a waitress too much."

That finally pulled Faye around. "She tipped."

"Yeah."

"Nobody tips at a place like that."

"I know. She said the woman looked like she'd had a long shift and bad shoes."

Faye's expression shifted again, not softening exactly but losing the clean shape of anger. Miriam had told her that part. Ren saw Faye's mouth pull tight and stay there.

"Damn it," Faye said, mostly to the form.

Ren did not answer. Some emotions were better left without witness until they chose to have one.

Spike pushed off the doorframe at last and came to the wall. He stood beside a map of the Tharsis-Hellas corridor with old syndicate route marks copied over in black pencil.

"This line," he said, touching one fork in the map. "It was cut because the pass got unstable after a slide. Not because I left."

Ren stepped beside him. "For a while, yes. Then the reroute started getting hit."

"That would've happened anyway."

"Maybe."

He glanced down at her. "You don't have to protect me from it."

"I'm not." Ren followed the route line with her finger. "I'm telling you not to make yourself the hero of the tragedy either. It was bigger than one departure. The corridor got worse after you burned your alias because nobody in the chain knew who was stealing what anymore. Smaller crews. Sloppier cold storage. More middlemen. That hurt her. So did the dust storms that grounded flights. So did clinic shortages. So did a doctor who cut her dose to stretch inventory. That's what I mean."

Spike stayed with the wall a long time. "Messier than murder."

"Yeah."

"Worse."

Ren considered that. "Longer, anyway."

He let out a breath. "Hell."

Jet lifted his head. "You have records from the clinic."

"Some."

"And the precinct."

"Some."

"And the routes."

"Enough."

He rubbed at his brow. "I'm staying up all night, aren't I."

"Probably," Ren said.

Faye looked over. "You're not the only one."

That landed in the room without anybody trying to make it sentimental. One fact among others.

Outside, the wind rose and shoved at the bay with both hands. The strip light flickered once. Ed looked up toward the ceiling and pointed. "Crunchy junction box is thinking about betrayal."

Ren went automatically to the wall switch, slapped the side panel twice, and the light steadied.

"It does that," she said.

Jet watched her. "You live here."

"Sometimes."

"By yourself."

"Mostly."

"Why here."

She glanced toward the bay door, toward the shape of her ship through the narrow window. "Because my mother picked up supply drops here. Because the rent is the work. Because nobody wants a half-dead syndicate stop at the bottom of Hellas unless they have a reason."

Faye said, "And your reason is the murder scrapbook."

Ren looked back at the wall. "Research," she said.

"Right. Sorry. The grief archive."

That one came out meaner. Faye heard it too. Her mouth tightened.

Ren did not let herself flinch from it. "Yeah," she said. "That too."

Something tight and raw crossed Faye's face for half a second, then she covered it with annoyance. "You could at least have better tea."

Ren snorted. "If I'd had better tea, I would've sold it for fuel."

Jet's eyes moved to the empty pot. "You got food."

"Some."

"Enough for six."

Ren almost laughed. Even here, with the wall full of bodies and paperwork between them, he was counting food and mouths. Maybe it was just habit. Maybe habit was all care ever got to look like on him.

"Enough for tonight," she said.

He grunted. The sound held more acceptance than the word yes ever would have.

No one said they were staying. The weather outside said it for them, and the hour, and the way the room had shifted from confrontation to the slow uglier work of sitting with what had been uncovered.

Faye went back to the chair but did not sit. Spike remained at the wall, manifest in hand. Ed had started tracing the pattern of documents in the air without touching them, following lines only she could see. Jet sat with his elbows on his knees, staring at the photo of Daichi and the copied notation beside it and some place beyond both.

Ren felt the adrenaline go out of her body in strips. What was left behind was exhaustion so sudden it almost made her laugh. She had spent years reaching for this exact room and had not really believed in it until the hatch opened and there they were, boots on her floor, carrying all their own history in with them.

Jet broke the silence first.

"You should've just told us your name at the start," he said.

Ren leaned back against the bench. "You should've looked harder at your own records twenty years ago."

His head came up. For a moment she thought he might take offense at last and the whole fragile thing might break along its oldest seam.

Instead he gave one short nod. "Fair enough."

Faye said, "I'm not saying that."

"Nobody asked," Spike said.

"Shut up."

Ed, still on the floor, said, "Bebop people are doing the low-information fighting now. Healthy sign."

That did it. The laugh that moved through the room was brief and ragged and unwilling, but it was a laugh. It left behind a quieter kind of silence.

Ren looked from one of them to the next. Jet had the precinct bundle open across his knees. Spike held the route pages in one hand like they might still bite. Faye had the clinic packet hanging loose from two fingers. Ed sat cross-legged under the wall, goggles crooked, tracking patterns in the air. From a distance Ren had spent years making them look like a design. Up close they were just people in a cold room with paper in their hands.

"I don't need anything from you," Ren said, because that mattered, maybe most. "Money wouldn't help. Revenge wouldn't either. And I'm not here for some speech about being sorry."

Jet let out a tired breath. "Good. Because we're not exactly stocked for speeches."

"I noticed."

"Rude."

"True."

He looked at her then, and for the first time since landing there was no challenge in it. Only a battered sort of attention.

"What if we still have questions," he said.

Ren glanced at the wall, at the cot, at the bad kettle and the door to the bay and the night settling hard outside. "Then ask them."

Jet asked for the timestamp on the service-entrance photo.

After that the room found a rougher rhythm. Faye wanted the dosage changes in order. Spike wanted the route fork pinned to a season instead of a map. Ed wanted the dates where the wall changed pattern. Ren answered what she could, went out once to clamp a charger rig onto the Bebop, then came back with cold dust in the seams of her coveralls and kept going.

By the time the hour stopped counting as evening, nobody had said they were staying. Nobody reached for the hatch either.


# Chapter 17

The waystation settled after midnight in layers.

First the questions stopped. Then the voices ran out. Then the charger rig Ren had clamped to the Bebop dropped from a hard industrial whine to a low steady hum that lived in the floor. Then the wind took the whole place back. It came along the outer walls in long dry scrapes, lifted dust into the seams around the door, and worried the loose metal skin of the old bay until it ticked and shivered. Dry Well had been built for men who thought a shipment schedule was the same thing as a future. Half the rooms were dead now, sealed with plate and old foam that had gone the color of teeth. The common room still held heat if nobody opened the door too often.

Ren had given them that room with the same economy she'd used for everything else. The cot was yours, the couch was yours, the floor was flat enough if Ed didn't mind it, there were blankets in the locker, the bay light switch stuck halfway and needed a push. Then she'd taken her own bedding, a thin rolled pad and two rough blankets, and carried them back out to her ship without waiting to see whether any of them would object.

Jet didn't.

He ought to have. A young woman turning her home over to a crew she'd spent years tracking across the system should have made him feel something clear and named. Gratitude. Shame. Protective irritation. Instead he felt tired in a way that had weight to it. Tired in the knees, in the base of his skull, in the hand that wasn't metal. Too tired to argue about sleeping arrangements. Too tired to lie to himself about what kind of room this was.

Ed had gone down first, as if the day had simply run out underneath him. He lay in a shallow crescent on the floor with a route map under one cheek and Ein jammed up against his stomach, one arm flung over a stack of copied manifests like he'd claimed them by touch. Spike had made a circuit of the room without seeming to look at anything, found the couch, sat on the arm of it for a while, then stood back up and gone outside with a cigarette tucked behind one ear. Faye had taken the cot, boots still on, one forearm over her eyes. She wasn't asleep. Jet could tell by the way she held still for it, too deliberate by half.

Jet stood at Ren's wall and read.

The wall took up most of the room opposite the rusted heater. It wasn't arranged for drama. There were no strings, no neat little circles around faces, none of the cheap detective nonsense you saw in cop shows made by people who'd never touched a case file. Ren had built it like somebody rebuilding an engine from salvage. Documents overlapped in practical layers. Route maps were pinned first, then photocopied clinic bills, then ISSP forms, then photos, then notes in a hand compact enough to fit between printed lines. Old black magnets held corners flat. Washers had been used where the paper wanted to curl. Dates were written on strips of masking tape. Some pages had grease on the edges where she'd handled them with work hands.

Jet knew the forms before he got close enough to read them. His body knew them. The width of the boxes. The blunt gray type. The cheap paper ISSP bought in bulk because cheap paper made cheap institutions look efficient from a distance. Informant contact logs. Routing memos. Internal requests. Disciplinary notices. Dead paper, most of it. The kind of paperwork men filled out because a building asked for paper and not because anyone thought a human life sat inside it.

He reached for one page with his prosthetic hand, saw the clamp of polished fingers against the flimsy edge, and stopped. The metal could hold it, but it couldn't feel when the paper wanted to tear. He used his other hand instead. Flesh thumb, flesh forefinger, toner dusting the ridges of his skin.

Confidential Informant Handling Review. Mars Precinct Sector 4. Partial. Copy.

The name block had been blacked out on the original and then copied twice, so the redaction showed as a thick charcoal bar. A note had been added below in Ren's hand: DAICHI KOZUKI. He already knew that. He read anyway.

Risk exposure elevated due to suspected leak in internal chain.

Recommended transfer pending supervisor approval.

Pending.

Pending.

Pending, over three separate dates, each one initialed, each one routed onward, each one stamped and moved and never completed. He knew those initials too. Not all of them, but enough. A lieutenant who transferred to Venus. A records clerk who'd once cried in the break room because her ex had cleared out the apartment while she was on shift. His partner's signature on the third page, slashing across the line where the review had been acknowledged. A note in the margin about resource limitations. Somebody had written hold in blue pen and circled it so hard the paper puckered around the mark.

Jet set the page back carefully and reached for the next.

Incident summary. Unofficial copy retained by source.

Male, civilian, deceased.

He read the words too fast at first, skidding over them because he knew where they were going. Alley behind a freight exchange. Multiple wounds. Compromised cover. No arrest at scene. Follow-up deferred pending internal review.

Deferred.

He remembered that phrase. People loved that phrase. It let a department act like time itself was a meaningful response. Defer, reroute, escalate, close. The verbs of a building where death turned into routing decisions before the blood dried.

He went through the next six pages standing straight-backed, like posture might do something. Expense vouchers. A reassignment request that had never been processed. A precinct roster with his own name halfway down the page, BLACK, J., shift code next to it. Then he found the thing that made him stop reading and simply look.

Officer Fitness Review.

He'd filled out a hundred of them over the years, first as the younger man being judged, later as the older man doing the judging. This one was his. His own block letters in the self-assessment field. His own neat irritation in the notes section where he'd written that the range calibration system was drifting and needed replacement. His own signature at the bottom, heavy from the cheap pen they'd issued that quarter. His partner's approval signature beneath it.

Marksmanship: excellent.

Vehicle handling: excellent.

Administrative compliance: satisfactory.

Areas for growth: patience with civilian procedure.

Jet stared at that line until the words lost shape.

He could see the desk where he'd written it. Green laminate chipped at the corner. Coffee ring at the upper left. The fan in records squeaking every fourth turn because no one wanted to fill out the maintenance ticket. His partner leaning in the doorway with mustard on his tie from a vending machine sandwich, joking about somebody in lockup singing all night. Jet annoyed at the paperwork, annoyed at the range note, annoyed in the ordinary petty way a man could be annoyed inside a functional workday.

Same week.

The dates might as well have been touching.

While Daichi Kozuki was trying to get moved before the leak reached him, Jet had been checking boxes about his own performance and complaining about the damn fan.

He lowered himself onto the metal chair by the workbench because his legs had started to feel less than certain. The chair bit cold through his pants. One of Ren's notes had come loose from the wall and was stuck to the sole of his boot. He peeled it off and set it on the bench. It held nothing more explosive than a list of dates cross-checked against route schedules and clinic purchases. Her hand was cramped but clean. No dramatics there either. Just sequence. This happened, then this, then this, and because of that, this.

The heater coughed twice and kept failing to become useful.

Jet rubbed his face. The room smelled like dust, paper, machine oil, and the ghost of broth from some older time when this station had fed men between runs. On the floor, Ed shifted, smacked his lips, and dragged one sheet halfway under his shoulder. Ein's back foot twitched against the map. Faye moved on the cot and muttered something too soft to catch. The room was full of the crew's sleeping noises, or almost-sleeping noises, and behind them all was the wall.

He read until the pages began to blur together: Ayane's clinic charts under Faye Valentine's name, partial manifests from routes Spike had worked, copies of old bounty data, settlement school enrollment records with Ren's name changing handwriting halfway through as if one clerk had cared and the next hadn't. That was almost the worst part. The whole thing had been built out of a thousand small permissions. A clerk who sold frozen patients' data. A department that treated informants as consumable. A syndicate route that carried medicine because it also carried guns. A man who did his job and asked the exact number of questions required to keep doing it.

He found another page with his own handwriting on it and didn't pick it up. He left it where it was pinned and looked at it from three feet away as if distance still meant anything.

The door clicked.

Jet turned. Faye had shifted onto one elbow on the cot, hair in her face, eyes open now.

"Are you planning to read till dawn," she said, voice thick with not-sleep, "or are you going to pick one document and marry it?"

Jet looked back at the wall. "Go to sleep."

"That's not an answer."

"Didn't realize I was taking questions."

Faye watched him for another second. In the heater light her face looked younger and worse for it, stripped of most of the sharpness she used on purpose. She followed his gaze to the fitness review, to the precinct forms, and her mouth tightened.

"Bad one?"

Jet let out a breath through his nose. "No. Ordinary one."

Faye sat up the rest of the way, slowly, then stopped with one hand flat on the cot.

"Right," she said.

"Yeah."

She flopped back down and put the forearm over her eyes again. "If you start apologizing to the wall, I'm leaving."

Jet almost smiled. Almost. "Wouldn't dream of it."

After a minute her breathing leveled out for real, or close enough. Jet stayed in the chair until his back locked. Then he stood, set the fitness review flat against the wall again, and went outside because the room had run out of air.

The cold hit fast.

Dry Well at night looked unfinished. The dust darkened toward brown where the moonlight caught it. The settlement buildings made a crooked black row against the plain, each one with a few stubborn lines of old sealant still pale around the joints. Farther out, the ground fell away into a wash of rock and rust that used to mean convoy exposure and now meant nothing at all. Ren's ship sat in the open bay with its hull light off, just a darker shape under the frame. The charger cable to the Bebop glowed at one connection point where the insulation had split and been taped.

Spike was twenty yards from the door, sitting on an overturned intake housing, one elbow on one knee. Cigarette ember, then dark, then ember again when he drew. The wind kept trying to steal the smoke before it rose.

Jet didn't say anything at first. He came up beside him and looked out at the empty dark because that was what the dark was for.

After a while Spike said, "You found something?"

"Too much of it."

Spike nodded once. He flicked ash. It blew back against his cuff and clung there.

Jet stuffed his hands under his arms against the cold. "You know her face?"

Spike took a second. "No."

"Yeah." Jet looked down at the hard ground by his boots. Dust had piled against the intake housing in little scalloped ridges. "Me neither."

That was all, and it was enough.

They stood in it together for a while. The wind scraped over sheet metal. Somewhere in the bay, a loose chain knocked against a strut, stopped, knocked again. Spike finished the cigarette and lit another off the stub of it, more habit than need.

When he spoke again his voice had dropped into the flatter place it got when he wasn't bothering to make it sound like anything.

"I had a cargo code. Drop schedule." He rubbed his thumb across the filter. "Delay notice that sat in a box for—I don't know. Months."

Jet glanced over.

Spike's eyes stayed on the dark. "That's what I had. Codes and a schedule and some paperwork I didn't read." He stopped, dragged on the cigarette, held it. The smoke went sideways in the wind. "Nobody opens the crates."

Jet didn't say anything to that.

Spike rubbed ash from his thumb with the side of it. "I left for my reasons. None of them had a damn thing to do with somebody's meds."

Jet heard the rough edge in that and left it alone for half a breath too long.

"I know."

"Do you?"

The question landed more like a tired correction than a challenge. Jet took it as one anyway because he wasn't built for anything gentler in moments like this.

"No," he said, and then, because he was too old to dodge a fair hit, "No. I don't."

Spike nodded once more. The nod wasn't agreement. It was acceptance of the answer's limits.

Jet looked back toward the common room window. From here the heater light was a dull square behind dirty glass. He could just make out the wall, pale strips of paper, the shape of the chair he'd left crooked.

"I found my performance review in there," he said.

Spike said nothing.

"Same week."

Still nothing for a beat. Then Spike took the cigarette from his mouth and tapped it against the housing.

"Middle of the day," he said. He turned the cigarette in his fingers. "Some guy's filing something. Lunch. A crate goes on a truck."

The fan in records. The cheap pen. The mustard tie.

"Yeah."

Spike put the cigarette back between his lips.

The wind changed direction. It carried a smell out of the bay, old coolant and hot metal, and under that the iron smell dust got when night cold tightened it. Jet stayed until his knees hurt and the cold found the seam at the wrist of his sleeve. He could have said more. He could have said something about informants, about partners, about not seeing what sat three desks over if the paperwork that came to you looked ordinary enough. He didn't. Spike wasn't the man to hear it, and Jet wasn't the man to say it cleanly.

When he finally headed back inside, Spike didn't look over.

Jet stopped in the doorway and glanced once more toward Ren's ship. No light showed through the cockpit glass. She might have been asleep. She might have been lying there awake with both hands under her head, listening to the same wind and wondering what the hell she'd expected from any of this after the telling was done. There was no paperwork for that part. No form to route it.

Inside, the room had gone close and stale again. Faye really was asleep now, one boot hanging off the cot. Ed had rolled onto his back and somehow pulled a precinct roster over his chest like a blanket. Jet bent, took the paper from Ed carefully, folded it once along its old crease, and put it back on the bench.

Then he turned the heater up even though it wouldn't help much and sat in the chair by the wall until the dark outside thinned.

***

Faye woke because somebody was tapping metal in a patient little rhythm that refused to become part of her dream.

She lay on the cot and listened to it for a while with both eyes shut. Tap. Turn. Tap. A pause. Then the soft clink of something small set down in order. The room had gone that gray color right before dawn, not light exactly, just less dark. Jet was slumped in the chair by the wall with his chin on his chest. He wasn't sleeping so much as losing a fight with gravity. Ed had not moved except to acquire another page. Spike wasn't in the room.

Tap. Turn. Tap.

Faye sat up, swore under her breath when her neck answered with a crack, and swung her legs off the cot. The floor was cold enough to bite through her socks. She stepped over Ed, over one of Ren's copied maps, and made it to the door without waking anybody.

The bay smelled different from the common room. Colder. Cleaner in spots, dirtier in others. Machine oil, sealant, metal filings, dust that had come in under the big doors and settled on everything the way Mars always did. A work light hung over the open panel on Ren's ship and threw a white hard circle across the floor. Ren stood inside that circle with one boot braced on a crate, sleeves shoved to the elbows, bent over a coolant pump housing spread open on a rag.

She didn't look up right away. She had all the fasteners lined in a row from longest to shortest, each one turned so the heads faced the same way. Every time she lifted a part free she touched the line with the knuckle of her little finger as if counting without wanting to count aloud. Off to one side sat a dented tin with faded peaches on the lid, full of washers and seals. There was grease on her wrist and a pencil tucked behind one ear worn down nearly to the metal band.

Faye leaned against the bay frame. "You always tear into machinery before sunrise, or are we getting special treatment?"

Ren looked up then, blinked once as if she'd had to come back from farther away than the inside of an engine, and straightened a little.

"It was making a noise."

"That's not a normal answer."

"It's the answer."

Faye almost told her that was annoying. She let it go because it was also true.

"What kind of noise?"

"High whine under load. Bearing's going." Ren lifted the housing and tipped it so Faye could see the dark ring of worn metal inside. "I can get another month out of it if I clean it and shim it, but I'd rather not."

Faye came two steps closer despite herself. "You talk about engines like people talk about dogs."

"Dogs are less predictable."

Faye looked at the laid-out parts, the clean order of them. "You sleep at all?"

Ren put the housing down. "A little."

"Liar."

"Moderate liar."

Faye snorted before she meant to.

The bay door was cracked open an inch at the bottom, enough to let a blade of cold air slide through. Outside, morning hadn't quite happened. The sky was still the wrong side of black. Somewhere beyond the ship, a loose panel kept ticking in the wind. Ren picked up a thin brush and started cleaning old grease from the bearing race, careful, even strokes.

Faye watched her work for a few seconds longer than made sense, then said the thing she'd come out here to ask before she could talk herself out of it.

"What was she like?"

Ren didn't ask who. She set the brush down, wiped two fingers on a rag, and considered the pump housing like it had become a harder repair.

"Which version?"

Faye folded her arms. The motion brought her own hand near her collarbone and she moved it away on purpose. "The one that used my name."

"That's not different enough to be a version."

"You know what I mean."

"Yeah." Ren looked at the open panel, not at Faye. "She was careful in public. She knew what people expected when they heard the name, so she gave them enough of it to stop questions."

"What'd they expect?"

Ren lifted one shoulder. "Money, style, some kind of easy confidence. She could do the confidence part."

The pause before style was small but real. Ren didn't seem like somebody who wore words for decoration if she didn't trust them.

Faye shifted her weight. "And the rest."

"Borrowed." Ren reached for the dented peach tin and tipped out a new seal into her palm. "She practiced the signature on receipts. She'd get angry if the F looked too neat. Said rich people didn't write carefully because they were used to getting another sheet of paper."

That got Faye in the stomach somewhere.

"That sounds stupid."

"It was stupid."

"Did it work?"

"Mostly."

Ren fit the seal into place and ran her thumb around the groove. Faye tracked the thumbnail, cracked at one edge and black with grease.

"Miriam said blue dress," Faye said.

Ren nodded. "There was one. Synthetic satin. Cheap up close. She bought it used from a woman in Tharsis who sold clothes out of her apartment and cigarettes one at a time. It was too tight through the ribs and she wore it anyway."

Faye rubbed the heel of her hand once under her collarbone and let it drop.

"And the scar on the left shoulder blade."

Ren's mouth moved, not quite a smile. "It was a welding burn on the left shoulder blade. She got lazy with a torch when she was sixteen. If she was telling the story to someone she wanted to impress, it became a knife fight. If she was telling it to me, it was the torch."

There it was, sudden and plain, a woman appearing not as theft or medical file or route casualty but as somebody who lied different depending on her audience. Faye hated how much that helped.

"Great," she said. "So she had range."

"Yeah."

Low information. Still conversation.

Ren picked up the bearing and frowned at it. Faye stayed where she was. The light buzzed overhead. Dust moved in the beam every time the wind found the door gap. After a little while Ren settled the bearing in her palm and worked the brush into the groove. "She couldn't keep cards square when she was tired. That's what I remember from those nights. She'd come back late and empty her pockets on the table and if she was really worn out the deck would go crooked in her hands."

Faye blinked.

"You watched."

"I was there."

"That's not what I asked."

The brush stopped against the metal. "No. I watched."

The honesty of it made Faye want to pick a fight. She did what she always did when the ground shifted under her and went for the angle with teeth on it.

"Did she ever feel bad?"

Ren didn't flinch. "About using your name."

"Yeah."

"Yes."

"That's a fast answer."

"It was a fast fact."

Faye pushed off the frame and paced once across the bay, three steps, turn, three steps back. There wasn't room for more than that without hitting a crate. The place was full of old syndicate hardware stripped for parts, lengths of hose, a busted compressor, a bucket of bolts with dust half-filling it. Ren had made herself a life out of leftovers and labor. Faye hated that, too, because it made anger feel expensive.

"You don't have to say that because I'm standing here," Faye said.

"I'm not saying it because you're standing here."

"Then why say it?"

Ren set the bearing down and wiped both hands on the rag slowly, like she wanted them empty before she answered.

"Because it was wrong." Ren scrubbed the heel of one hand with the rag though there was nothing on it but clean skin. "It kept her alive, and it still—" The rag bunched in her fist. "It was somebody else's name. She knew that. I knew it too. I was old enough to know."

Faye laughed once, no humor in it. "Old enough to know and nobody stopped."

"Nobody could afford to stop."

Faye reached out and nudged the longest bolt half a turn out of line with her fingernail, then set it straight again.

The bay held the silence without ceremony. They stayed where they were. The pump housing sat open between them like a third thing in the conversation, something with pieces that had to go back where they belonged whether or not people got sorted the same way.

Faye found an overturned crate and sat.

"I keep trying to figure out what exactly she took," she said after a while. "I don't mean the legal answer. I know that one. Data, account access, medical clearance, whatever. I mean the actual thing." She spread both hands on her knees as if that might make the shape of it clearer. "I was frozen. I didn't know my own damn name half the time when I woke up. And meanwhile she's out there being Faye Valentine better than I was because I wasn't even there."

Ren listened the way she did everything else, all the way to the end before touching it.

"I don't think she was better at it."

Faye barked another laugh. "You weren't at the table with Miriam."

"No." Ren picked up the brush, put it down again. "But I saw her come home after. That's different."

"Different how?"

Ren twisted the rag between both hands for a second, wringing grease deeper into the weave.

"Like wearing shoes that fit if you don't stand still too long. She could hold it for an evening, a clinic visit—maybe a week if the paperwork needed to match." Ren wiped her hand on the rag. "Then she'd come home and start taking it off. Jewelry first. She'd stop talking like that, stop standing like that." One shoulder moved. "By the time she changed clothes she was just herself again. That was the hard part, I think."

Faye dragged one thumbnail over the splintered edge of the crate. That was too close to understanding. It made her skin itch.

"Don't make her noble," she said.

"I'm not."

"Because she wasn't."

"I know."

"How?"

Ren gave a small impatient breath and reached for the bearing again, more force than the part required.

"Because I lived with her after," she said. "Because I saw what fear does to a person. Because I saw her take your name and I saw her hate that she needed it and I saw her keep using it anyway. You want me to make it cleaner than that, I can't."

Faye folded both arms over her middle and held on. Something in her chest wanted to crack open and she had spent too many years making sure it didn't know how.

"I don't want clean," she said.

Ren's hands went still on the bearing. "Okay."

"I want..." Faye stopped, angry now because the sentence had reached a place without floor. She tried again. "I want one version of this that isn't rotten."

Ren didn't answer right away. She turned the bearing over in her hands. "She loved me," she said. "I don't know if that helps. But it's what I've got."

Faye pressed her thumbnail into the seam of her jeans until it hurt. She could hear Jet snore once in the common room and then catch himself. Somewhere outside the wind shifted grit against the bay door with a dry hiss.

"Yeah," she said.

After a moment Ren added, "And she stole your name."

Faye nodded once.

"Yeah."

Ren picked up the housing again. "Can you hold this?"

Her voice stayed flat at the end, all need. Faye stood, stepped into the light, and took the pump housing when Ren handed it over. It was heavier than it looked, cold on one side, still a little warm near the motor where it had been running earlier. Grease smeared onto Faye's fingers.

"Don't drop it," Ren said.

"I know how gravity works."

"Good."

They worked like that for a while. Ren seated the bearing, adjusted the shim, ran a thin line of sealant with steady pressure from the tube. Faye held the housing where she was told and asked the occasional question because that was easier than not talking at all.

"You learn all this from your mother?"

"Some of it."

"Who taught you the rest?"

"An old woman in Hellas who fixed haulers and cheated at dominoes." Ren tightened one fastener, then another. "She had one eye and hated sentimental music. She'd throw a wrench at the radio if a singer dragged a note."

There it was, another life attached to Ren that had nothing to do with the Bebop crew and everything to do with being a person who had mornings before this one. Faye held the housing steady and let that settle too.

"Sounds charming."

"She was mean as hell."

"Better."

The last bolt went in. Ren tested the spin with two fingers and listened, head tilted, that mechanic's stillness people had when their ears did better work than gauges.

"That'll do," she said.

Faye set the housing down on the rag. Her hands were filthy now. She rubbed thumb against forefinger, felt the grease tack there.

"You can wash up inside," Ren said. "Sink on the left sticks. Kick the pipe under it."

Faye didn't move yet. "What did she call you?"

Ren looked over, not understanding for one beat.

"Your mother?"

"Ren."

"No, when she was mad."

The answer came quick enough that it had been living near the surface already.

"My whole name."

Faye nodded. She could work with that. A woman yelling across a room, full name, the oldest trick in the world. The symbol and the ghost dropped away. What stayed was a mother with a daughter and too much fear in the house.

"Right," Faye said.

Ren picked up the rag, folded it once, twice. "Yeah."

Faye went to wash her hands. She kicked the pipe under the sink when it stuck, just like Ren said. The water that came out was brown for half a second and then clear.

***

Ren had never trusted quiet kitchens.

In settlement houses quiet usually meant the food had run out, or somebody was sick, or the adults were saving the bad news till there were enough bodies in the room to spread it around. A kitchen was supposed to make noise. A pan on heat. A knife on board. Somebody complaining before the first bite had even happened. She woke with that old expectation lodged under the ribs and lay in her bunk for a minute staring up at the low ceiling of her ship until she realized the noise hadn't started because she hadn't started it.

The sky outside had turned from charcoal to a flat rust gray. Morning.

She dressed, tied her hair back, and crossed to the waystation galley with the inventory in her head. Half a sack of flour. One onion. Dried peppers. A jar of preserved tomatoes she'd been saving for no good reason. Chickpea meal. Two cans of cheap fish. Tea bricks. Enough oil. Enough salt if she was careful. Food that wanted apology if handled badly and something closer to breakfast if handled right.

By the time the first pan warmed, the room had begun to smell alive.

Onion hit oil and gave back sweetness. Pepper darkened. Chickpea batter thickened in the bowl under the fork. She cut the preserved tomatoes into the pan with the fish and let them go until the red deepened and the edges started to catch. It was settlement food, straightforward and unforgiving. The kind that depended less on ingredients than on not being afraid of heat.

She was sliding the third flat cake onto a tray when she heard footsteps behind her and knew from the weight of them it was Jet.

He stopped in the galley entrance and just stood there for a second, broad and sleep-creased and already offended on principle.

"What's that smell," he said.

Ren kept working. "Breakfast."

"I can see that."

"Then we're ahead of schedule."

He came in farther, peered into the pan like it might be committing a procedural violation, and folded his arms. His beard had a flattened patch on one side from sleeping in a chair. He looked like a man who'd been insulted in his own church.

"You started the fish too early."

"No, I didn't."

"It's going to dry out."

"It's in tomato."

Jet leaned over the pan and had the decency to wait until he smelled it properly before he looked more annoyed.

"That doesn't make you right."

"No," Ren said. "The smell does."

From behind him Faye's voice came, rough with sleep and not enough patience. "If you're mad because breakfast smells edible, that's a personal problem."

Faye stepped into the galley pulling her jacket on over yesterday's shirt. There was still a dark grease mark on one thumb from the pump housing despite the wash. She saw Ren notice and did not acknowledge it. Spike came in after her with the look of a man who had slept less than anybody and would deny it if asked. Ed arrived last at a low trot with a piece of paper still stuck to one side of his face and Ein pacing hopefully around his ankles.

"Morning biscuits of destiny," Ed announced, then stopped and sniffed harder. "No, wait. Flat circles. Crunch disks. Smells very legal."

"Nothing in this room is legal," Faye said.

"Speak for yourself," Jet muttered.

He was still staring into the pan.

Ren slid the last cake onto the tray and turned the heat off. "Plates are on the shelf."

Jet did not move.

"You know where the plates are," Faye said.

"I know where the plates are."

"Then why are you standing there like she broke into your house?"

"I'm assessing damage."

Spike pulled a plate from the shelf and handed it past Jet without comment. Jet took it automatically and kept staring into the pan like he had not noticed his own hand move.

They assembled themselves around the narrow table and every available flat surface with the clumsy efficiency of people who had done it too many times to think about it. Jet took the seat nearest the stove because of course he did. Spike leaned against the wall till Faye stole that spot too by setting her hip there first, so he ended up on an overturned crate. Ed sat cross-legged on the floor with a plate balanced on one knee. Ein hovered under the table in a state of focused spiritual commitment.

Ren put the food down in the middle and reached for the tea kettle.

"No coffee?" Jet said, suspicious already.

"No coffee."

"So this is punishment."

"It's tea," Ren said.

Faye took a mug, sniffed it, and made a face. "This smells like a ship part."

"Then don't drink it," Jet said.

She drank it anyway.

For thirty full seconds nobody mentioned Daichi. Or Ayane. Or clinics, precincts, syndicates, stolen names, or any of the other things that had been sitting on all of them since yesterday. They ate because the food was there and because hunger was simpler than moral history.

The first bite made Jet's eyebrows move despite him.

Faye saw it and pounced. "Oh, that's beautiful."

"What is?"

"That look."

"I didn't make a look."

"You made exactly a look." She pointed with half a flat cake. "That's the look you make when something annoys you by being good."

Jet chewed, swallowed, and aimed a glare across the table that would have worked on somebody who wasn't Faye.

"Needs more salt."

Spike took another bite before speaking. "No, it doesn't."

Jet turned on him. "You don't know."

"I'm eating it."

"That's not analysis."

"It's enough of it."

Ed had both hands in the food now, one holding a cake, the other scooping tomato and fish with complete disregard for the plate's intended geometry.

"Crunch disk number one," Ed said around a full mouth. "Very successful. Sauce level also successful. Tiny fish army defeated."

Ein put both front paws on Ed's knee. Ed fed him one illicit scrap under the table with the speed of long practice.

"Don't feed the dog from the table," Jet said automatically.

Ed looked around. "This is not a table. This is a multi-surface meal event."

Faye snorted tea through her nose and coughed.

While Jet was distracted by telling Ed to chew before talking, she leaned over and lifted a strip of fish straight off Spike's plate.

Spike looked down at the theft, then at her. "You have your own."

"I had my own. Now I've got yours too."

"You could've asked."

"And missed the fun."

Jet pointed at her with his fork. "Can you not do that for one meal?"

"No."

"At least she's honest," Spike said.

"Oh, shut up."

The argument had no heat in it. It moved on old rails. Yesterday they had sat in this same station with the whole ugly web spread out plain between them. This morning Jet was trying not to admit somebody else had seasoned a pan better than he would've, Faye was stealing off Spike's plate because Spike always let her get away with that amount and no more, Ed was making a private religion out of the crispy bits, and nobody seemed confused by the pattern of any of it.

Ren poured tea into the last mug and realized everybody else was already eating.

Ed looked up first. There was a red line from a folded document pressed into his cheek.

"Ren forgot Ren-food."

"I didn't forget."

"Then why is the plate empty?"

"Because I was pouring tea."

"That's a technical excuse."

Faye nudged the tray toward her with two fingers. "Sit down before he writes you a citation."

Jet, still chewing his resentment and his breakfast together, jerked his chin toward the only open spot, the edge of the bench near the wall. "Yeah. Sit."

It was casual, which might have been what made it harder. Breakfast had slid into invitation without stopping to announce itself.

Ren took a plate and sat on the bench because refusing would have turned it into something larger than breakfast. The wall of documents was at her back. From here she could see all of them at once: Jet guarding the pan even now, Spike folded into himself around the mug, Faye sprawled in defiance of available furniture, Ed all elbows and appetite, Ein making hopeful passes under the table like a practiced thief. The charger hum from outside still lived under the floor. Wind scraped the far wall. Somebody had left a wrench by the sink and a damp towel over the chair back. Morning had come all the way in.

Ed hooked one bare heel under a copied roster and started dragging it toward a drip of tomato without noticing.

Ren set her mug down.

Jet leaned over before she could move, caught the page by one corner, and folded it along the old crease. He set it on the bench beside Ren's plate without looking at the header.

"Later," he said.

Ed peered up at him. "Paper delay?"

"Yeah."

Jet sat back down and took another flat cake off the tray on the way, hunger plain now. Nobody bothered making him defend it.

Ren ate too. The food was good because she'd cooked it hot enough and long enough, and because old poor ingredients stopped being ashamed of themselves if you treated them like they were meant to be dinner.

After a minute Jet said, not looking at her, "What'd you cut the batter with?"

"Hot water."

He chewed. "That's it?"

"And chickpea meal. Oil hotter than the stove looks like it can manage."

Jet grunted once and kept eating.

Faye looked over the rim of her mug, saw Jet still staring at his plate, and didn't say a word.

Breakfast settled around that.

Spike asked for the peppers. Ed fed the dog when Jet wasn't looking. Faye complained that the tea still tasted like machinery and drank the rest of it anyway. Jet let his own mug go cold while he reached for another flat cake. The knowledge stayed in the room, but it stayed the way dust stayed in Mars light, visible when the angle caught it, otherwise just part of breathing.

Ren looked down at her plate so they would not make a subject out of whatever was on her face; she could sit in the meal, but the years that made it easy were still theirs.

She ate while the wall waited at her back, the tea went metallic in the mugs, and nobody reached for the papers.


# Chapter 18

After breakfast, Jet's tea had gone cold before Ren rolled the Dry Well bay doors wide enough for her ship to clear, and the wind had put a skin of red dust across it. He drank it anyway. It tasted like tannin and metal and the grit Mars worked into everything it owned for more than five minutes.

Ren moved around her ship with a rag hanging from one back pocket and a checklist that existed nowhere but her hands. She checked each landing strut with her boot, ran her palm along the seam she'd patched on the starboard fin, looked up at the sky, then checked the seam again. The right knee of her coveralls had been mended with blue thread that did not match the rest of the fabric. She kept wiping her thumb across that stitched patch as if the grease on her skin bothered her more than the mismatch did.

Dry Well looked flatter by daylight than it had the day before, as if the place had taken its shape from the story Ren told in it and was now shrinking back into sheet metal and dust. The cracked solar panels along the far building threw back a weak white glare. Somewhere behind the storage shed a loose panel knocked once, then again, in the wind. The old waystation still smelled like hot wiring, stale air, and the iron tang of sand grinding itself finer.

Jet stood with one shoulder against the bay wall and watched Ren do a walk-around she did not need anyone for. That, more than anything she had said, made the morning feel final. People who were staying asked for a hand. They asked where the good wrench was. They said hold this, would you. Ren checked her own seals and her own fuel couplings and did not look up.

Ed sat on an overturned crate with Ein between her bare feet, chin propped on both fists, following Ren like she was a puzzle with moving parts. Faye leaned against a support post with her arms folded, jacket half-zipped against the cold. Her hair was still a little damp from the wash basin and had started curling at the ends. Spike stayed near the door track, coat on, cigarette unlit between two fingers as if he'd forgotten to commit to it.

Jet set the cup on the floor and pushed off the wall. "You want me to take a look at that patch before you go?"

Ren glanced at the seam on her fin, then at him. "It'll hold to the basin rim."

"That's not exactly the part I asked."

"It's the part that matters right now." She gave the seam one more press with her thumb. "There's a machine shop two settlements north that keeps decent gasket stock. If they haven't gone under."

That was Ren all over. Ask a bigger question and she answered the piece with bolts in it. Jet had gotten used to the shape of it fast enough to hear the edges. He nodded once.

"Your portside condenser bracket is loose," she said. "Third mount back. It was singing when you came in yesterday."

Jet looked over at the Bebop on reflex, as if he might hear it from where he stood now.

"You heard that from inside the bay?"

"It wasn't subtle."

Faye snorted. "Nothing on this ship is subtle."

"Thanks," Jet said.

"You're welcome," Faye said at once, like she'd been waiting.

Ed raised a hand. "Ren-person, if Ren-person goes to a terminal with very friendly wires, will Ren-person send a message to Ed and Professor Ein? Not a location if location is dramatic, just a boop maybe. One boop. Two boops if snacks are available."

Ren's mouth shifted, not all the way into a smile. "I might send a schematic."

"That's better than a boop," Ed said solemnly.

"Debatable," Faye muttered.

Ren climbed the ladder to her cockpit, then climbed back down because she'd forgotten a metal lunch tin from the workbench. She tucked it under one arm and stood for a second with the hatch open above her, looking at the Bebop crew like she was taking a final measurement. Jet could not tell if she was memorizing them or just making sure none of them had wandered under her thrusters.

"You don't have to leave in a hurry," he said, because the thing he wanted to ask came out wrong the first time. "The bay's yours till nightfall."

Ren shifted the lunch tin to her other hand and looked past him into the bay. "If I stay till nightfall, somebody decides I should stay till morning."

Jet's mouth worked once around a better line and found none.

Faye uncrossed her arms, then crossed them again. "You always this good at making everybody feel like they're in the way, or is this a special occasion?"

Ren looked at her. "You want me to drag it out?"

Faye's shoulders moved under the jacket. "I want you to stop acting like we're cargo."

Ren's hand tightened on the ladder rung. "You're not."

That got a brief sound out of Spike, not quite a laugh. He looked down at the dead cigarette in his hand like it had surprised him by staying unlit.

Jet rubbed the heel of his hand against the scar at his cheek. There were things he could have said then. About Daichi. About the reports on the wall. About the fact that he had spent half the night reading his own handwriting next to a dead man's file until the words stopped being language and turned back into ink. He could have said he was sorry, though he did not know what weight that word had left in it, used this late. He could have offered money they did not have, help she had not asked for, the ship's frequency, a real invitation north to Mars and the spot behind Mama Yun's and bowls of noodles nobody could afford properly.

What came out instead was, "I should've seen something sooner."

Ren hooked the lunch tin into a cargo net by the hatch and had to redo the first loop when the tin knocked against the frame. "Maybe. Maybe there wasn't anything to see from where you were standing."

Jet let that sit.

Then she added, still looking at the knot, "And your heat exchanger is running hot."

"That's because this ship is old."

"No," she said. "It's because the exchanger is running hot."

Faye laughed once through her nose. Jet let it go because she was right and because Ren was right and because he was not going to spend the morning defending a heat exchanger to a twenty-two-year-old mechanic who had already listened to his ship breathe.

Ren put one boot on the ladder rung and paused. "Mama Yun's refrigerator is short-cycling."

Jet blinked. "What?"

"The compressor kicks out too fast and then starts again." She nodded toward the north, toward Mars, toward the lot behind the noodle shop like it was just outside the bay instead of half a day's flight away. "You can hear it through the wall if you're trying to sleep."

"You never slept there."

"No," Ren said. "My mother did, once or twice. In the ship."

That shut the air down for a beat. Ayane, in that lot. Ayane close enough to hear the refrigerator behind the noodle shop. Ayane under Faye's name, needing medicine, parking where Jet's ship would later come home as if the universe had been quietly laying cable behind the walls the whole time.

Faye looked away first. She did it by turning toward the open desert, as if there were something out there worth seeing that had not been there thirty seconds ago.

Ren went up the ladder and disappeared into the cockpit. The canopy came down. Systems whined to life under the hull in ascending layers, one high and thin, one lower and rougher, then the steady vibration of power coming online all the way through the frame. Dust skittered across the bay floor in little red snakes.

Ed slid off the crate and came closer, one hand on Ein's head to keep him from deciding this was the moment to investigate rocket exhaust. Jet stepped back with the others until they were in a line he would have denied was a line if anybody had said so.

The ship's running lights came up amber against the dull morning. Ren did not wave from the cockpit. Jet had not expected her to. She had spent years arranging an encounter none of them had known enough to ask for. A cheery hand out the canopy would have been the wrong instrument.

The ship lifted on a controlled burst that hammered the dust flat, then kicked it back up in a wall. Jet turned his face into his shoulder. His cup went over and rolled, spilling the last brown swallow into the grit. The hull cleared the threshold by a meter, two, rose into the washed-out Martian light, and hung there a moment while the engines adjusted pitch.

Ed had to raise her voice over it. "Where is Ren-person going?"

Nobody answered right away. They all looked up as if direction might be written on the underside of the ship.

Spike spoke without taking his eyes off the canopy. "She didn't ask us to do anything."

The ship banked out over the basin, a clean turn, not hurried.

Spike flicked the dead cigarette into the dust and added, "That's the worst part."

Jet felt that settle in him harder than anything from the night before. If Ren had asked for money, he could have counted what they did not have. If she had asked for names, he could have found records. If she had wanted the corrupt bastard who sold out her father, he could have told her the man was already dead. A demand gave shape to a day. It gave you something to put your hands on.

Ren had handed them a map of what had already happened and left them to carry it.

The ship dwindled fast in the thin air. For a second the sunlight caught on the patched seam by the starboard fin, a sharp white flash, and then it was just another moving point against the haze. Another ship going somewhere on Mars. Another life peeling away from theirs at an angle.

Ed lifted one arm and wagged it anyway, long after the ship was too far off to see a person behind the canopy. Ein barked once because Ed was doing it. Faye shoved both hands into her jacket pockets. Jet bent, picked up the empty cup, and brushed dust from the rim with his thumb though there was no point in it.

"All right," he said. His voice came out rougher than he'd meant. He cleared it. "Let's get this tub in the air before the afternoon crosswind starts getting ideas."

No one argued. That was its own kind of wrongness too.

They moved because there was nothing else to do. Jet was halfway to the Bebop when he looked back once more at the open sky over Dry Well. The air was already empty where Ren had been.

***

The Bebop lifted out of Dry Well heavy and complaining, the way it always did when the tanks were less full than Jet wanted and the ship had decided the morning was not to its taste. He rode the complaint with both hands on the controls, one flesh, one metal, feeling the old trawler's shudder smooth out as the engines found the climb.

Mars opened beneath them in layers of red stone and bad memory. Dry Well became a scratch in the basin, then less than that. The southern country spread away under a haze the color of old bruises, canyon lines cut hard as knife marks, stretches of failed terraforming hardware rusting into the land where somebody had once spent real money trying to make weather happen on purpose.

Jet kept the nose steady and let the autopilot take the finer corrections only after he'd bullied the Bebop into the altitude he trusted. He never liked handing full control over too early. Machines lied less than people but more often than they admitted.

Behind him the ship settled into its own sounds. The life-support hum thickened when they leveled out. Something in the galley tapped twice with each mild vibration until Faye got up, found it, and shoved a towel under it without saying anything. The hull pinged here and there as the temperature changed around the frame. Ein's nails clicked across deck plates, followed by Ed's bare slap-slap pace as she chased him and then gave up on the chase in favor of collapsing crosswise on the common-room floor.

Spike went to sleep.

He skipped the fake half-doze he put on when he wanted out of a conversation, skipped the sprawled one-eye-open thing that said he could be vertical in two seconds if trouble came through the hatch. He lay down on the couch with one arm crooked over his face and was gone so fast Jet almost looked around for the trick in it. His legs were too long for the couch, boots hanging off one end. The unlit cigarette from the bay sat behind his ear like he'd tucked it there without noticing.

Jet turned his attention back to the forward glass before the sight of it could mean more than it had to. Spike slept like that when something had run all the way to the end and left nothing but quiet behind it. There was a difference between that and peace.

Faye had dug out the same cheap magazine she'd been carrying around the ship for at least a week. The cover featured a smiling woman in a silver dress standing beside a casino cruiser nobody on the Bebop would ever afford to board, much less lose money on. The spine had cracked so many times it no longer knew what page order meant. Faye kept flipping back to an article halfway through and reading it again as if the words might have improved from the last pass.

Jet glanced over his shoulder. "You know how that story ends."

"Yeah," Faye said without looking up. "With a yacht and a divorce."

"Then why are you reading it again?"

"Because it's here."

That was good enough. It was more honest than most reasons people gave for anything.

Ed had both the game console and the main auxiliary screen alive at the same time. On the console, a blue frog in a tiny cape kept dying at an acid river. On the auxiliary screen, file trees bloomed and collapsed under Ed's hands so fast Jet could only track them by color and the occasional familiar word.

"Are you playing," he asked, "or are you working?"

"Both both both." Ed did not look up. "Professor Frog has very poor timing but excellent moral fiber. Also packet refinement is at sixty-two percent. Maybe sixty-three. Depends if checksum goblins count."

"Checksum goblins don't count," Faye said, turning a page she had already turned twice.

"This is anti-goblin prejudice."

Jet let the corner of his mouth move. "Packet for who?"

Ed finally looked up, goggles shoved into her hair, face serious in the middle of all the rest of her. "For Ren-person. Anonymous present. No sender, no snack crumbs, no metadata tails, very clean. Ed fixed the crooked timestamps and put all the folders in nice polite rows. Researcher gift etiquette."

Jet took that in and watched the ridge line ahead at the same time. "You strip the origin tags?"

"Obviously. Ed is not a baby criminal."

"Not a baby anything," Faye murmured.

"A mature criminal?" Ed considered. "No. A citizen scientist."

That got a little sound out of Faye, almost not worth calling a laugh, but it changed the air in the room by a degree. Jet heard it because the whole ship had gone so quiet otherwise.

He adjusted course two points east to avoid a bank of dust that would not hurt them but would sand the paint another layer thinner. The Bebop's starboard side already looked like old teeth. The red country moved under them in a slow scroll.

He thought about Ren saying Ayane had slept in her ship behind Mama Yun's. That one detail kept catching in his head because no report would waste a line on what a woman heard through a thin wall when she was trying not to think about how much medicine she had left.

Jet reached for the autopilot confirmation switch with his mechanical hand. The metal fingertips clicked softly against the housing.

"Send it when we're back on the Tharsis relays," he said.

Ed's fingers hovered. "Understood by Captain Caution."

"I mean it."

"Yes, yes. No packet flung into the raw screaming void. Only respectable routing through boring infrastructure."

Spike spoke from the couch without moving his arm. "Try not to get her arrested with kindness."

Jet looked back. One of Spike's boots had shifted. That was all.

"You were asleep," Faye said.

"Was."

"Helpful."

Spike let the word pass without picking it up. A minute later he was breathing deep again.

Faye kept reading. She licked a thumb, turned another page, then stared at the same paragraph long enough that Jet knew she was not seeing it anymore. He knew because he was not really seeing the instruments either. Their eyes kept doing the work, but the mind behind it lagged, catching on the same few scraps and worrying them.

He knew this part. The return flight. The part after the thing when the body had not caught up to the fact that there was nowhere else to be.

He remembered other return flights. Bad busts. Empty holds. Blood on a sleeve not necessarily his. The old ISSP shuttle smell of gun oil and cold coffee. Those rides had always been louder than the action before them because everybody on board wanted to prove, by talking or cursing or making plans for the paperwork, that they were still in motion.

This was not that. Nobody on the Bebop had anything to prove. They were just wearing the same ship around themselves again and letting it settle where it always settled.

Ed's hands slowed. The game music on the handheld chirped in tinny defeat as Professor Frog died once more.

"There." Ed sat up straighter and hit three keys on the auxiliary board with ceremonial precision. "Gone gone gone. Wrapped in seven layers of boring. Anonymous to everyone except people who deserve to know that the indexing is too elegant to be governmental."

"That's not anonymous," Faye said.

"It's spiritually anonymous."

"That doesn't mean anything."

"It means if Ren-person sees the folder architecture, Ren-person will know it's from a genius and maybe a frog."
